# For Freedom!

From my conversations with Count Carlo di Rudio, accomplice of Felice Orsini

**Cesare Crespi** (1913)

Translated from the Italian

---

## Preface

<!-- pages:7-8 -->

THE PRESENT volume might offer some contribution to History; but it is not history in the sense in which that word is today understood and written... Something less for those who demand the methodical concatenation of facts and the cold judgment of men and events; something more for those who, regarding events as a mere resultant, might seek to grasp "the soul of the times," rummaging among the passions that stirred them.

The Count di Rudio, so distant, in time and in space, from the events he recounts, frequently protests that the miseries of politics no longer move him... Doubtless he deceives himself. Like a Minos who

"Judges and sentences according to how he coils"

he might have, in the estimation of many of my countrymen, a value altogether relative; yet he acquires a great one as a "human document," and it is precisely as such that he ought especially to be considered.

Why did I resolve to write of him?

Consider: In this far Western land, in this country of vulgar commerce,... to find a specimen of that era, now swept away by the centuries, in which the third Italy had its gestation; to find him pulsing with intense life, still distilling strong poetry, still capable of indignation and of tears... How could I have failed to be tempted, and well-nigh subdued? I wrote because my own existence was thereby enriched with edifying and pleasurable sensations.

And I have not cast these papers to the bottom of a trunk, especially if not exclusively, out of regard for the Count di Rudio, whom I know to be either calumniated or forgotten. In the torrent of men and events that poured forth from the European revolution which preceded my time, he possessed virtues that predestined him to be swept under. The triumph of political men is almost always a consequence of their most wretched qualities. It neither dazzles nor moves me. My sympathies are with those who fall with manly grace.

I sensed that this book would see the light when di Rudio would be no more. So that no doubt might arise as to its authenticity, I not only solicited written approval of each individual chapter; but I also asked and obtained that every page likely to give rise to controversy be authenticated by di Rudio with his own signature.

I might have softened certain discordant notes, made more jarring by so much high and virile language; might have erased certain heterogeneous traces that crept into the volume through the influence of those authors whom di Rudio most cherished... I did not. To adulterate the words that issued from lips now silenced forever may be a venial sin for a Minister of Public Instruction; for a simple mortal it is cowardice.

I have nothing else to preface. *Salute!*

Cesare Crespi.


### Chapter One

<!-- pages:8-14 -->



LOS ANGELES, on a splendid summer morning. I had arrived only a few hours before, on a train that had put me in mind of those which, from every corner of France, converge upon the shrine at Lourdes.

For the metropolis of southern California, situated near the ocean, between the desert and the orange groves, is a great centre of attraction for all those who have the misfortune of living beyond the Rockies: where, in summer, the sun prostrates men and herds alike, like withered grass; where the frost of the long winter makes the earth rigid and impenetrable even to the blows of a pickaxe; where cyclones sweep through the immense valleys, uprooting trees and demolishing houses.

The wretched souls whom consumption gnaws at their hollow chests find in the "City of Angels" the deeply azure sky and the balmy air that give the illusion and the hope.

How many of them I had encountered in the streets during those few hours! They pass quietly, light as beings come from the kingdom of shadows, and they smile at the children and smile at the flowers. In their optimism, which here becomes extraordinarily tenacious, they accept as indisputable truth the merciful lie that their loved ones murmur in their ear with every fit of coughing.

They go to sit in the shade of the flowering orange trees, and sometimes, while they cling in reverie to the sweetnesses of life; while the waxen corollas, all unknowing, breathe above their heads their invisible, most piercing fragrance... the waxen flesh breathes out, all unknowing, its invisible last breath... The breeze stirs from the sea and, mingling that breath and that fragrance, carries them away across the green countryside, away across the arid desert, away into the infinite mystery!..

But I was not in Los Angeles to prolong an agony, and when, after a long walk, I judged the opportune moment had come, I stopped before one of those little wooden houses that one cannot conceive of, in California, without associating with them the idea of a flora altogether particular, altogether domestic... the flora that embraces and almost possesses the house!

A rubber hose coiled in spirals upon the green grass was driving from its metal tip, into the air, a fan of minute droplets which the sun painted with the colours of the rainbow. A hummingbird, with the glint of emerald, was assailing with swift, persistent darts of flight a scarlet flower...

Beyond the climbing plants, within the walls of that little house, caught up in the social vortex, a man had sought refuge — a man who represented the last, glorious remnants of an era. Through the deep peace adorned with green, I could sense the suppressed longing of conspiracies, the fever of battles... and, in that picturesque corner of the far West, I suddenly recovered the emotions of my youth, invoked in vain for so many years... Then I breathed deep, warmly, warmly, as if it were a breath of the Homeland, and the fibres of my heart exhaled a word of thanks.

But a subtler thought succeeded it. Three-quarters of a century; an unceasing sequence of struggles, dangers, sufferings... what could have survived? Ought I to regard this refuge as a temple enclosing a living flame; or as the final resting-place of a long pilgrimage, the dolorous threshold of a sepulchre?

I advanced resolutely... Before I could raise my hand to the electric bell, a figure took shape in the half-shadow of the corridor, beyond the glazed door.

I found myself face to face with a stout lady, with a slight tendency toward plumpness. Her hair was of a fairness that was fading into white and had already begun to yield; but the face was still fresh and rosy and, beneath the prominent brow, shone eyes of blue, gentle and assured. The mouth and chin revealed to me a creature accustomed to confronting obstacles with resolution.

She asked me severely what I wished.

"Pardon me... Major di Rudio?" And I presented my visiting card.

Her expression brightened. "Ah! ah! that name is familiar to us. The Major will be pleased to see you. But he is still in bed!"

I thought: "The infirmities of old age... the threshold of the sepulchre!" And I was about to tell her I would return; but she anticipated me. "Come in, come in! This is precisely the hour at which the Major rises; he is methodical... he cannot be long. Is that so? From San Francisco? But then a cup of coffee can do no harm. Precisely! I am the Major's wife!"

— Signora Elisa!

And I offered her my right hand with the familiarity of one who, though never having seen a person, holds them dear because they have long occupied one's thoughts. Then a fancy took me and, as I followed her along the corridor, I asked her why my name was familiar to her.

"What shall I say? The Major reads whatever you write, and he grows animated and beats his fist on the table. Do sit down. The water is boiling and in four seconds..."

She left me alone. I was in the dining room. The foliage trembling before the one large window tempered the light within. A green tint pervaded the room, and the crockery on the sideboard reflected it; upon the table there glowed, in a vessel of blue glass, a bunch of amaryllis.

The dining room gave onto a study from which it was distinguished, more than divided, by a simple cornice. Through the opening, nearly as wide as the entire wall, I could discern in the background another broad, solitary window, likewise obscured by an arabesque of trembling foliage. In the air, the same green tint, restful and tranquil. At length I made out clearly several simple and massive pieces of furniture in the Mission style. I reflected that, amidst that artistic antiquity, I might well have found a place for myself; all the more so because a bookcase crammed with volumes had at once caused my old instinct as a library mouse to prevail. I found myself with my hands clasped behind my back, before those volumes, intent on scanning the gilded lettering pressed into their brown leather spines. History, philosophy... works stamped with freedom of thought.

I looked about me... From one wall there smiled upon me the pensive likeness of a man known to the People and to its tyrants in every part of the civilised world: Giuseppe Mazzini. From the adjoining wall there hung a picture bearing the effigy of a martyr less universally known, but no less great in spirit: Fortunato Calvi. And I exclaimed: The house reveals the man!

"Beg your pardon!"... It was Signora Elisa, just then returned. She had supposed I was speaking to her. She had unfolded a napkin and was laying the place setting, assisted by another lady who struck me by her extreme pallor, her very dark eyes, and her slender, slight figure.

"My daughter... Italia Adair," said the Major's wife, and the young woman confined herself to a nod of the head.

— Your only daughter?

— Not in the least! We have two others: Roma Scott and America Vickery. I am expecting them today and you shall meet them. For I hope you will be one of our party today. I also have a son, the firstborn, born in England and trained in his first steps — you are not unaware of this, I suppose — in tragic moments. But he is far away, in Arizona. A kind of fever for the unknown has taken hold of him, and he passes his life on horseback, in the solitude, exploring.

The refreshment was ready. The gracious lady had added to the coffee some toast and a dish of fresh figs. I protested. I was not accustomed to touching food before midday, and so much kindness was superfluous. She insisted. The eggs had been taken that very morning from the henhouse... she kept many hens; it was her particular care. As for the figs, Italia had picked them with her own hands, just at that spot. They were ripe and delicious.

She asked to be excused and went off about her affairs... I remained alone with the other lady. I was sipping the coffee and felt somewhat ill at ease. That appearance of suffering... perhaps my presence would prove unwelcome to her. I took advantage of the brief moments in which she was occupied in arranging some trifle or other to observe her minutely, without allowing it to appear, without committing any rudeness. She had a mass of hair of a lustrous black, perhaps too voluminous for her graceful head; thin brows, superbly arched; an aristocratic profile; an ethereal little person. But it was not long before I noticed a certain energy of movement that contrasted, in a strange fashion, with her invalid appearance.

I resolved to bring into play all my resources as a reporter seasoned in the craft. She grew animated by degrees, and from her lips there issued thoughts such as I had not expected.

I learned that she had been brought to the last extremity by appendicitis and that the surgeons had saved her by resort to the knife... a few hours' delay would have proved fatal to her. Children? One only! In homage to Uncle Sam, the eternal and glorious Uncle Sam, she had given him the name of Samuel. She wished to guide him toward a military career. At West Point, where she hoped to have him admitted, he would have the benefit of one of the finest educations in the world. She had spent a good part of her youth with her father, at the Regiment. A beautiful, magnificent life, the military life! Discipline... servitude?.. She was joking. The life of the Regiment was boundless freedom; at the mere thought of it she felt a tremor of energy stir within her. Not a moment of idleness, not a moment of tedium! True, there were days of painful anxiety. The regiment was often called out to drive back into their reservation the Indians, who committed some raid or other. It would remain absent for three, four, eight months. But when the courier, white with dust, burst into the camp and announced its return aloud, joy was in the air and stole through one's entire being. In the twinkling of an eye, life redoubled in intensity. The ladies hastened to communicate the good news to one another; the children doubled their leaping on the grass; the rose-bushes were stripped of their flowers; a coming and going of orderlies putting everything in order, everything afresh. And from that noisy bustle we would launch ourselves at full gallop, away in a race... ten, fifteen miles, to be the first to greet the column of the returning men. "When the dust, gleaming with arms, announced them from afar, on both sides there were cries to shame a horde of Bedouins. What moments, what embraces! Upon their faces, alongside the joy of homecoming, the serene pride of having suppressed barbarism, of having snatched our bold pioneers from torture and destruction. Upon ours, alongside the joy of seeing them again, the serene pride of being the daughters and wives of those strong men. Wherever the horses of Uncle Sam advance, they leave behind them the road cleared for honest labour and for civilised life!..

"You are mistaken, you are mistaken! Few bonds are so cordial as those that bind soldiers and officers together. The roughness is entirely apparent. When a subordinate receives a dressing-down, he looks his superior in the eye and can always read there a phrase very different from the one the lips have uttered. Severity is reserved solely for one who shows signs of cowardice. But have you ever heard of cowards in the army of Uncle Sam?

"Intemperate? Intemperate! I will tell you; there are birds whose natural state is flight. To set foot on the ground and never again to rise upon the wing is, for them, to be finished, and what is worse, they walk with a clumsiness that moves one to pity. The natural state of the Anglo-American race is motion, incessant, intense labour. The exuberance of life that does not confront arduous and glorious enterprises wastes away in taverns and in the gutter. And the American never does things by halves; not even evil. But at the rising of a difficulty, at the mere idea of danger, in the trial of strength... what a transformation! Where would one find a body of men of greater dash, quicker in expedients, more spontaneous in sacrifice, more steely in temper? And what enemy, at parity of numbers, has ever known how to withstand them?"

I listened, full of wonder and almost disconcerted. That harmonious voice, which a residue of weakness rendered more caressing, expressed thoughts that I had until that moment believed the exclusive province of virile minds, of folk born to face the perils of battle, whole and content only amidst anger and blood. But if strength bows before grace and sometimes adores it, why should grace not admire strength?

She bent her slender little figure over the table and set her white hand busily to arranging the bunch of amaryllis glowing in the blue vase. Then, answering a fresh question of mine:

"No, the Indian raids are by now, save the rarest exceptions, a memory of the past. Now Uncle Sam keeps arms and spirits bright with drills and frequent orders of transfer. One never leaves a place where one has lived happily without feeling a sense of sadness; but it is a sadness that vanishes, in a little while, in the wholesome emotions of the journey. For the ladies the journey is a festivity and, I am tempted to say, a regeneration. On the last one in which I took part, we covered, on horseback, more than seven hundred miles, through a marvellous country. It is a most strange sensation. One's being, forgetting its own individuality, feels itself transformed and almost part of the elements themselves. The force, the infinite motion — that is us. One goes on and on, in an atmosphere of sunlight, and we raced with the wind."

She fell silent, lowered her eyes, then resumed. "No, the true life is not in the great cities. The great city makes us small. An elegant prison, the calls of ceremony; a childish babbling of gowns and bonnets. If this is civilisation... I hate it."

I was no longer surprised. The impetuous nature, the need for action of the grandfather had passed into the father, and from the father into that slight lady, so ethereal that, as she walked, she seemed barely to brush the carpet. What might she have become in one of those confined environments in which virtue bursts its banks and runs headlong to victory or to martyrdom?

The mother returned. She had caught the thread of the conversation and added much of her own. She concluded the series of vivid evocations with this melancholy regret: "Happy days... all too sadly gone. For us they will return no more!"

Among the episodes she narrated was the following: Captain di Rudio (he was a captain at the time) showed in the execution of orders a meticulous precision. On one occasion, so as not to delay the departure by a few seconds, he cut clean off with his sabre the sleeves of a magnificent gown that had been left protruding from a trunk. For several years Signora Elisa had preserved them, like silent denouncers of an unheard-of crime...

The good lady, laughing it off, ended by leading me into the courtyard. She walked along throwing handfuls of grain to a flock of cackling hens, and I followed her step by step, heeding, now and then, a learned lesson in poultry-keeping. My readers, I imagine, take no interest in cocks and hens that are not roasted with a fork in their flesh. But an incident which, in due course, I shall relate, compels me to introduce into this opening passage those poor feathered bipeds as well... of pure breed, moreover!

We came to a corner, sheltered in which a white brooding hen sat upon her eggs in a basket. And the lady said: "Today or tomorrow the clutch will break from the shell. How much maternal love beneath that fistful of feathers! Is it not strange that the great affections, in which we take such pride, should be contained and powerful even in hearts so tiny? I am fond of creatures that have no speech."

At this point she set down the grain-vessel and moved away with a brisk step.

Standing on the landing, standing out against the dark background of the kitchen, framed almost by the doorposts, I made out an old man dressed in a grey coat, a tuft of white hair upon his brow, moustaches pointed, and the small beard of other lands and other times. The sun, beating full upon his face, threw into strong relief his angular features, as though carved in bronze. He was tapping the floor nervously with his heel, while his deep-set but most lively eyes scanned the scene beyond the bushes, beyond the plants that shadowed the courtyard.

There exist in California isolated groves of ancient vegetation which the botanists call *sequoie*, but which the people call simply "great trees." They rise straight, without boughs, like immense columns of a temple raised, in the night of ages, by some race of giants. The tallest among the pinnacles of the Lombard cathedral would seem, by comparison, a slender reed. They were already withstanding the wind when the Almighty created, according to the Scriptures, the Sun and the Stars. The prehistoric man who danced his bestial round-dance, devouring his fellow man, has vanished; the patriarchs who, wandering through the solitudes, traced the first paths for young humanity, have vanished; the Brahmins passed the lighted torch of civilisation to the Egyptian... and vanished; the Egyptians passed it to the Greeks... and vanished; the Greeks to the Romans... and vanished. What remains of the mighty Roman Empire? Race upon race has pressed upon race; States have hurled States into the void, through vicissitudes so long that men have lost within them even the memory of their own origins... And those trees stand!

With invisible eyes they behold the pygmies of ephemeral life who flock from every corner of the world to their feet and there stammer out their wonder. If it be so... how much disdain in their austere rigidity!

But their high summits, adorned with an evergreen crest, tremble in the wind of the Sierra and, perhaps, murmur ancient stories to the sky.

I was thinking of those trees, when the Count Carlo di Rudio and the companion of so many of his vicissitudes, standing together on the landing, heedless of my presence, pressed upon each other's cheeks the morning kiss.


### Chapter Two

<!-- pages:14-23 -->



It was an hour of debate in the study, beneath the likenesses of Mazzini and Fortunato Calvi, who seemed to be listening to us. And I had already grown almost disposed to abandon my purpose, when, to forestall future regrets, I said with some vehemence: "But do you not understand, Major, that this is a duty to be fulfilled? The event had its historical motive and to History it belongs. Why not clarify its circumstances and its intentions? Why permit inexactitude and falsehood to overlay the truth? If you persist in silence, the last word will remain unchallenged with your political adversaries, and you would make yourself a willing accomplice in their lies. It would be a grave wrong against the martyrs whose memory you are bound to defend; against your children, to whom you owe the transmission, in all its integrity, of the honourable name received from your father."

The Major passed a hand across his brow and ground his teeth. It was his way of giving expression to powerful emotions. Suddenly he fixed his gaze upon my face and began:

I was born in Belluno on the 26th of August, 1832, the son of Count Ercole and Countess Elisabetta de Domini.

My family has old roots in that uttermost extremity of Italy. Angelo Biave wrote of it — and wrote, bless his soul, in Latin hexameters. A great-uncle of mine took the trouble to render them into the vernacular, thereby preparing an instrument of torture for his great-nephews. For every escapade of ours of any consequence, when punishment came it was mild enough for the times, yet terribly tedious: to commit to memory a translated passage from Biave. Papa, in such fashion, presumed to temper one anew in the glory of one's ancestors. I recall the opening still:

*"I gird myself for an arduous enterprise; I narrate of ancient personages, descended from illustrious lineage, who gave to the Peoples most just laws.*

*"In the ninth century, the Emperor Otto the Great ordained that the celebrated Nosadana family should govern Belluno and endow it with a wise order of Laws. A just reward accorded by Caesar to virtue.*

*"For a long time the Nosadani governed the city which our ancestors, with good reason, called Belluno; for there Bellona proves herself, and the citizens are valiant in arms, undaunted in the face of danger.*

*"The Nosadana family boasts personages of high renown in war and in counsel. It changed its name and was called Rudia."*

The poet then proceeds to recount the deeds of a goodly company of Nicolai, of Ercoli, of Eustachi — some of them podestàs of the native city; others celebrated for books dripping with doctrine; others again pre-eminent as advocates of the common people, or as commanders of Venetian infantry, or for grey hairs made venerable by holy works and the bishop's mitre...

But let us leave the ancestors to sleep in peace, and speak of my father; from whom I believe I inherited something more than blood and name. One anecdote to make my meaning plain:

At the beginning of 1848, when the eyes and hopes of Italian patriots converged upon Rome and popular songs echoed the praises of Mastai-Ferretti, the citizens of Belluno organised a demonstration in homage to the "liberating Pontiff." They paraded through the streets, bearing in triumph the bust of Pius IX.

My father was seated at a café table. He sees, he trembles, and planting himself boldly before the demonstrators, shouts at them: "O deluded souls, you carry in triumph the greatest enemy of our Fatherland." And without adding another word, with a powerful blow of his walking-stick, he sends the idol of the multitude flying in splinters.

Intense was the excitement that followed the momentary stupefaction, and my father owed it to his well-established popularity that he did not live out, there and then, his last quarter of an hour.

Pius IX, by disavowing the holy crusade for the independence of his Country and fornicating with the foreigner, undertook, shortly afterwards, to strengthen that popularity and to demonstrate the perspicacity of the old and proud patrician.

A man of such temper was bound to be hateful to Austria, gloomy and suspicious as she was. The fact remains that he was never granted permission to practise as a lawyer, though deeply versed in legal learning. He consoled himself with the sweetness of paternity and had fourteen children. I was the fifth-born.

They called me *il Moretto* — the swarthy one — from the colour of my skin. I was the favourite, and I do not preface this with "modesty aside," because I believe I was so more for my mischievous tricks than for my virtues. My maternal grandfather, Fortunato de Domini, Colonel of the Austrian infantry, blinded by his deep affection, believed he could discern in me the makings of something worthwhile; and in order to shield me from the influence of the liberal ideas that had infiltrated the family, he insisted on keeping me constantly by his side. First in the barracks at Belluno, an old building which had in its better days resounded with the psalmody of friars; then in the fortress of Palmanova, to which he had been transferred. The good man died before he had time to realise that liberal ideas were more nimble than his vigilance, and stronger than the walls of any fortress.

The simple and rough education he had imparted to me was afterwards continued at the Military Academy of San Luca in Milan, which was then destined for the cadets of the Kingdom of Lombardy-Venetia. And I should have remained there, together with my brother Achille, until my studies were complete, had there not suddenly arisen to sweep me away the fierce tempest which, having gathered in the North, crossed the Alps and swept through and drenched all Italy in blood.

Here, at the risk of recounting things already too well known to some, I think it opportune to summarise the Italian question as it presented itself at that eventful epoch. Memory is more frail than one is inclined to suppose among the fervent Latin race, and the recalling of the afflictions that weighed upon us will make it better understood why those who desired a United and Independent Italy neither could nor should have hesitated in the face of the greater or lesser correctness of the means.

Milan was the first centre where our wrath erupted. No plan prepared long in advance, no understanding among the citizens. The news of the insurrection in Vienna had disposed men's minds to venture something new; a yellow and black cloth displayed at an inopportune moment on her windowsill by a woman of easy virtue fanned, as though stirred by destiny, the flame that refines men and transforms them into heroes.

He who had long prepared the fuel to feed the insurrection was the aggressor himself; it was the obtuseness of the sagacious Metternich.

That grim incarnation of subtle arts already accounted himself the most powerful intellect to have appeared for centuries upon the stage of the world, even before the day when, taking advantage of the embarrassment of Napoleon the Great, he had contrived to leap at his throat and to hasten his ruin. One can well imagine how, once Napoleon had been struck down from his eminence, he had treated the peoples as a flock of sheep; why should he not have been able to do the same again?

Thus the one and the other, through excess of confidence in their own strength, fell, by different paths, into the same fatal error.

Napoleon I, by the very nature of his genius, having subjugated a People, redoubled their activity, fomented their ambition. Not for nothing does one trample upon the prejudice of birth and reserve the highest offices of State for positive merit. Son of the Revolution, he had usurped its thunderbolts; but only through it, and upon the ruins of the past, had he been able to raise himself a giant.

Wherever his eagles appeared, there blew a powerful breath of modernity; wherever his victorious armies passed, he scattered, of necessity, the ferment of new ideas that smoothed the road to the future. And moreover, his entire life was a good lesson: "one must grow strong and be bold, in order to defeat the strong."

Thus within the very yoke that he imposed lay the seed of liberty and redemption; the very good that accompanied his evil works must — strange to say! — undermine his power and hurl him into the abyss of Leipzig.

Metternich was too fine an observer not to perceive that weakness. In his titanic enterprise of driving the human flock back towards the past, he took every care to be tyrant, grim tyrant and nothing else.

In 1814, when the power of Napoleon, though declining rapidly, still appeared threatening and the aid of the peoples seemed indispensable in order to forestall surprises, Metternich and the other European diplomatists who took their cue from him made the most sweeping promises of liberty to the Italians. One had only to show oneself worthy of it by helping the Holy Alliance to shatter the bloody yoke of the French adventurer; by rallying around a banner that signified the happiness, the regeneration of their Country.

Once the Napoleonic peril had passed, the deluded, though divided in their choice of a prince, showed themselves nonetheless agreed in openly demanding their own independence.

The replies which came, at long intervals, from Metternich's lips mark the various phases through which the nefarious work of subjugation passed, and would of themselves suffice to arouse hatred in the heart of any people possessed of feeling.

— The affairs of the Italians will be decided in Vienna.

— Austria will never consent to renounce her rights of conquest.

— Italy is a geographical expression.

Unfortunately, Metternich was able to give force to his assertions not only through diplomatic intrigues and the arts of the Church, but also and above all through the armies of the Holy Alliance.

The debt of gratitude towards the peoples being treated as non-existent, there remained Austria's debt towards the princes. And perhaps, striking a line through even this, she would have appropriated half the Peninsula had there not arisen, at a fortunate moment, to play the game of the petty tyrants to some degree, the jealousies of Prussia and of Czar Alexander I.

Compelled to divide Italy in morsels in order to settle that debt, Metternich took care to create around himself a group of satellites that would of necessity gravitate towards the great centre of Vienna. A partial exception he chose to make for Murat, to whom he reserved the kingdom of Naples. The wife of Murat, Napoleon's sister, had regaled him with kisses that were worth a kingdom... but the impulsiveness of the French general, who prematurely chose to strike the pose of a champion of the Italians, deprived Metternich of the opportunity to carry out the sole luminous design of his entire political life.

The kingdom of Naples fell to Ferdinand I, who had, moreover, prepared the ground for himself by lavishing nine million francs in the antechambers of the Congress of Vienna. It was an Austrian army that accompanied him in triumph to his capital.

Rome and the Legations fell to Pius VII, the restorer of Jesuit power; the future compiler of the Bull *"Ecclesiam Jesu Christo,"* which excommunicated all those faithful who, on knowing a member of the Carboneria, did not degrade themselves so far as to denounce him, to play the spy.

The Grand Duchy of Tuscany and the Principality of Piombino fell to Ferdinand III, an Austrian archduke; the Duchy of Modena to Francesco IV, surnamed by his subjects "the executioner." Maria Luisa, daughter of the Emperor of Austria and wife of Napoleon I, had to content herself with holding sway at Parma; while the other Maria Luisa, Infanta of Spain, was placed over the Principality of Lucca.

Genoa, despite its protests and the eloquence of the Marchese Brignole, who endeavoured to invoke its long centuries of liberty and its former greatness, was incorporated into Piedmont; upon whose throne, returning from his refuge in Sardinia, sat Vittorio Emanuele I.

Austria retained under her direct dominion Lombardy, Venetia, the Tyrol, Istria and Dalmatia.

Having assured Austrian preponderance by surrounding the empire with weak States, Metternich set himself to increase its power ever further by spreading a dense network of secret treaties. And by imposing, together with the obligation to adopt reactionary measures, the further obligation of mutual aid in case of disturbances, he reserved for himself, in substance, the right to intervene with arms in the affairs of each individual State.

The sovereigns of Italy, so arrogant and cruel towards their subjects, had, merely in order to reign, accepted the role of assassins in Austria's service. Austria, having seen the direct dominion of the entire Peninsula taken from her by the jealousy of her loyal allies, had turned the difficulty and had contrived to secure an indirect dominion, yet no less substantial. And the chains that weighed upon the wrists of the august Latin Mother were none the less heavy and none the less firm for that.

By virtue of those Treaties, every Italian movement, even peaceful, designed to provoke liberal reforms, was smothered in blood through the nefarious agency of the army in white uniforms and pomaded moustaches; nor did the barbaric deluge spare even Piedmont itself, whose Kings, not lacking in courage, had nonetheless given some fine proof of manly independence.

In the great periods of history, joyous or sorrowful, there unfold incidents that epitomise them and may serve art in portraying them.

The symbol of Italy in those times was as it were embodied and set in motion in the punishment devised under the diabolical inspiration of the Bourbon of Naples. A rash word, a suspicion, the hatred of a denouncer — the more prone to calumny in that accuser and accused were never confronted — and behold an innocent man torn from his family, stripped to the waist, bound astride a donkey, and driven through the streets of the city. A company of Austrian soldiers preceded him to the sound of drum, another followed; at his side was the gaoler whirling the whip armed with nails, and at every step there were blows that penetrated the flesh, that tore it away in shreds. The torture was suspended when the wretch, scarlet with blood, dropped his pallid face and seemed on the point of breathing his last... Suspended, but to greater torment. It recommenced the moment a groan, a convulsive movement of the muscles betrayed the reawakening of sensation. The passers-by did not react; they covered their faces and fled in horror... At the end of the long Calvary, the gallows! It was the suffering of Christ daily renewed by the agency of most Christian Kings allied, under the auspices of the Pontiff, in holy alliance!

More refined, but no less inexorable, was the oppression weighing upon those Italians subject to the direct dominion of the Empire. The air, steeped in lies, began to envelope tender childhood, which was taught, as the highest duty, as a religious obligation, servile submission to the tyrant; it penetrated the lungs of the university student, which institution was required to furnish the State not so much with learned men as with faithful subjects; it hung, subtle and corrosive, over every phase of social life. The beloved subjects of His Majesty were free to move from house to house, from place to place; to attend to their affairs upon whose profits the Exchequer extended its rapacious claws... slaves in all else; men resigned or seething with wrath... citizens never!

Nor for this reason did the repression of refractory spirits proceed without due respect for the forms prescribed by strict legality — an easy task, where Laws are promulgated by despotism without the collaboration and assent of the governed. And of generous Italians, lawfully stricken, the fortress of the Spielberg in Moravia was populated — a place which, for the stifled groans within its walls, for the noble intellects that were withered and extinguished there, for the crimes against humanity that it shrouded in the darkness of its dungeons, will rise up terrible in the memory of men even when the last of the Habsburgs shall have been for centuries confounded with the dust.

To furnish that Austrian Bastille — and the pillory and flagellation which had been restored — with innocent flesh, there served admirably the flood of spies, high and low, that inundated the Kingdom. Bearing no distinguishing marks; concealed beneath plausible occupations, they insinuated themselves into every family, drew up reports on the tendencies, the habits, the conversations of every individual, even on the style of hat preferred; they violated postal secrecy; they wove plots; they besmirched, or attempted to besmirch, the reputation of the most notable and authoritative men, whose word might have incited minds to the virile vindication of rights.

The population of Lombardy-Venetia, gaunt and eroded by a monstrous fiscality monstrously applied, was required to provide not only for the expenses of the local Administration, but to replenish abundantly the imperial coffers as well. So true it is that, scrape the surface of the tyrant, and the brigand comes to light!

And the brigand cast aside his refined and serpentine methods in order to resort, like the other oppressors of Italy, to those barbarically ferocious, the moment the Milanese, refusing to smoke and to wear cloth manufactured by the foreigner, ventured to injure him in his purse. The doors of the prisons were flung open and the dregs of society, mingled with the constables, swept through the streets of the metropolis, smoking and compelling others to smoke. The bold ones who refused to set between their lips the leaf steeped in German spittle were mutilated or run through with steel; neither sex nor age afforded protection.

Martial law became ordinary law; treason, punishable with harsh penalties, to wear cloth of certain colours, to sing certain songs, to applaud certain phrases in this or that play; to wear a beard or hair cut in a particular fashion; to lend oneself to any act which the Authorities, at their own absolute discretion, might deem a demonstration of a subversive character. "Three days of slaughter ensure thirty years of peace," said old Marshal Radetzky. And the pretext for perpetrating slaughter was sought with the pertinacity of one who pushes the craving for dominion to the point of lust; of one who knows no other inspiration save the sinister interests of the dynasty.

Such were the delights that the trim and smiling Metternich dispensed, with a more or less liberal hand, to a great part of Europe. And the Peoples escaped him by virtue inherent in the very arts that kept them enslaved. For those arts necessarily aroused hatred, which is a regenerating energy that drives men towards the luminous affirmations of Right as powerfully as, and more powerfully than, love.

And so intensely did this energy stir in the hearts of the Milanese that they found themselves on the 18th of March 1848 united as one in a supreme resolve; without distinction of class, without distinction of sex, without distinction of age... to kill or to be killed; to conquer or to remain buried beneath the ruins of their own city.

No exhortations were needed, no password, no leaders. The hundred and sixty thousand inhabitants of the city became a terrible unity... the PEOPLE; just as the billions of grains of gunpowder constitute, in their explosion, the terrible unity of the magazine.

The weapons?

They were drawn from underground, from the innards of walls, from the hiding-places where the spirited, yearning for the day of vengeance, had secreted them... old implements of every kind, rusted; to which the armourers, emptying their shops, soon added a supply of hunting rifles. Then came the clubs, the broadswords, the halberds once gripped by mercenary soldiers; the great two-handed swords that had perhaps broken the impetus of Barbarossa in the glorious days of the Lombard League... the past emerging from the half-shadow of silent Museums, hurling itself into the vortex of the uprising, bringing its contribution to the redemption of a People.

That formidable tumult of surging passions did not shatter against the walls of the Military Academy of San Luca; instead, penetrating within, it aroused in our young souls a fever that, though suppressed, lost nothing of its intensity.

On the first day of the insurrection, the Officers spoke to us contemptuously of a handful of brigands whom the Austrian force and the plain common sense of the "respectable" population had soon enough brought to heel. Then... a hard face and silence. But that silence, concentrated, was worth the eloquence of many volumes.

The energy that stirred the living and made them greater than themselves had passed suddenly into things. In little more than a night, more than a thousand barricades arose and blocked the narrow streets of the city. The pavement had yielded its stones; from the palaces came crashing down the artistic furnishings; the homes of the working people furnished their household goods, their mattresses, their pillows. Hallowed by the feverish hand of a people that willed it, even the humblest utensils had been transformed into a bulwark of liberty... Shapeless bulwarks; their disorder revealed the fury of men's souls.

The fighter who, pale, clutching his carbine, awaits his post, recovers emotions almost refined to their purest essence. It was among that furniture that fathers, while chastising him, had taught him to whisper secretly the name of Italy. And now that name he would shout at the appearance of the enemy as the supreme provocation, as defiance unto death; now he would demonstrate that the ancient valour may lie dormant in Italian fibre, but can never be extinguished. Victor or vanquished, he would have accomplished a work not devoid of greatness.

The assault came, and at a hundred scattered points simultaneously. But the Austrian found everywhere men whom desperation had taught the art of defence. The barricades creak and change form beneath the fury of attackers and attacked; they are drenched in the blood of both... blood... but they hurl back the curses of the barbarian who falls, the battle-cry of the citizen who triumphs.

While the patriots fight in the front ranks, the mothers, the sisters, the children share the care of the wounded and prepare new means of assault. Cobblestones and tiles, heaped upon the rooftops, are transformed into deadly projectiles; the web of barricades extends and presses close about the barracks, the isolated garrisons. Besieged by the oppressor, the Milanese become in their turn, in part, besiegers. And beneath the leaden sky, more insistent, more resonant than the battle itself, spread the tolling of the bells sounded in alarm. Across the broad plain they diffuse the certainty that Milan is not conquered, that she still hopes and fights.

On the morning of the fourth day, we knew that the garrisons within the circle of the Naviglio had surrendered; that Radetzky was compelled to confine himself to the outskirts of the city; that a Provisional Government had been constituted; that the insurgents, certain of victory and impatient to achieve it, had rejected the offer of an armistice; that the wind and the balloons — one of which had flown above our very Academy — had scattered across the countryside incitements to the uprising; that the cities of Lombardy and Venetia were in full revolt; that the Piedmontese army was already in motion to lend its strength to the Italian cause; that armed bands were sweeping through the countryside and rendering even the problem of retreat arduous for the old marshal.

In the afternoon of that same day, a committee of citizens, preceded by a white flag, came to the College and demanded the return to their families of the Milanese cadets. Then discipline availed no longer to restrain our enthusiasm. Those who departed and those who remained embraced one another as brothers, and not without tears. Brief and solemn promises were murmured which not a few among those beardless youths were afterwards to seal with their blood.

Not many hours had passed before we were concentrated at Monte Tabor, near the Porta Romana. The order was to fall in behind the imperial army as it filed along the ramparts. Radetzky, the iron Radetzky, despite all the resources of his ingenuity, found himself compelled to avert utter ruin by a retreat — dissembled, but headlong.

Five days had the clash of virtue against the barbaric oppressor endured. He, maddened by his own unexpected impotence, slaughtered the old and the young, buried prisoners alive, bound couples together and burned them living, lovers and spouses; he bloodied the Castello degli Sforza, hacking off for sport the limbs and the breasts of the gentle and stately women of Lombardy.

The Milanese people, by conquering, had set in motion the period of history overflowing with enthusiasms and disillusions, with victories and defeats, with tumults and silences, from which, as from a travailing womb, our Nation was born to new life, to the tremors of Liberty and of Glory!


### Chapter Three

<!-- pages:23-26 -->



ON THE VERY DAY of our *fuga* — our flight — I had my first plunge into life lived at full intensity... and it was to prove decisive.

The Germans were filing along the ramparts that run from Porta San Celso to Porta Romana, weary, dejected, and sullen all at once. And since we were obliged to fall in at their rear, we stood our ground and watched their forced withdrawal. From the ramparts one commanded a view of the whole city. Here and there houses could be seen in flames; amid the rooftops, the white cathedral towered aloft, and the Germans shook their fists at the tricolour that flew from its tallest pinnacle. The bells went on ringing the tocsin.

Between the ramparts and the dense mass of houses there stretched, in those days, an expanse of kitchen gardens, still green... a strange quietude held sway there, in that great city swept and re-swept by the impetuous tide of passion. Solitary beside the ramparts stood the farmstead of the market-gardeners. A child, serene as the patch of earth that had in all likelihood seen him born, came running out of it and, clambering up the grassy slope, stopped in silent contemplation beneath the horse-chestnuts that overhang the walls. Then, as though drawn by the marvellous world of small things, he bent down, and his slender fingers played among the little stones that glittered at his feet.

The troops continued to file past, each company preceded by three Croatians who went on ahead as scouts. One of these glanced sidelong at the child, lowered his rifle and, drawing it back the better to put force behind the thrust, drove a lunge at him as though the object were to skewer a wild creature on a spit. The bayonet entered the child's flank and came out through his back; the innocent boy, lifted clean off the ground by the ferocious impetus, opened his mouth wide. But the cry, intercepted by death, never came.

The little one's mother was at a window of the solitary farmstead and she saw it and screamed for him. She came running, struggling to clamber up the green slope — her belly, heavy and swollen with new life, made every step a burden. The wretched woman did not reach the child in time to gather his tender body in her arms. No sooner had she arrived than the tormentors of Italy drove in another bayonet thrust, and she fell backwards, a corpse with a corpse within her womb.

I was seized with fever; I was seized with delirium. On the point of levelling my rifle blindly at the assassins, I felt the iron hand of my brother Achille holding me back. He whispered a few words in my ear which, though I could not make them out, I understood. Then, instinctively, I lifted my face skyward, as though I were awaiting vengeance from on high. The sky was leaden, motionless... God, if He exists, must have been absorbed in the affairs of some better world.

It was then that in my heart the conviction began to take hold that the wretched have no salvation save in the awakening of their own rebellious energies. "To fools," I concluded, "the feeble prayer; to the strong who will with strength, action."

Our retreat from Milan to Verona was not of a nature to weaken that terrible impression. Radetzky had ordered the garrisons stationed here and there throughout the various parts of the Kingdom to contract upon the main body of the army. At the same time, to guard against the danger of being harried by an enemy that might decimate his forces and slip through his fingers, elusive as a swarm of mosquitoes, he commanded the evacuation of every town and village, large or small, lying along his line of march. At the least sign of resistance — cannon fire and the horrors of the sack!

The various garrisons came down almost unmolested, arriving in time to replenish Radetzky's forces, which had been weakened by incessant desertions. The confusion, the panic among the Imperialists, was such that, hardly having reached Lodi, all their discipline appeared ephemeral. Had Carlo Alberto, instead of wasting precious time manoeuvring to satisfy the demands of Diplomacy, crossed the Ticino like a thunderbolt, the retreat would have degenerated into a rout, and perhaps Austria, having lost her army in Italy, would never have recovered!

As for the population — unarmed, misled by uncertain and contradictory reports — they neither knew how nor could bring themselves to resolve upon great deeds. Nevertheless, inveterate hatred and a virile spirit broke forth here and there. Melegnano, which had flattered itself that it might bar the enemy's road, was swiftly reduced to submission by fifty pieces of artillery. I saw the Croatians looting it — drunk and savage — hurling dishevelled women and defenceless children from the windows.

Not a day passed but I was forced to witness fierce and cowardly scenes, often wholly unprovoked, and the repressed fury all the more rapidly tempered my patriotism.

It found its first explosion at Desenzano. The garrison of Brescia, commanded by a certain de Domini — my uncle on my mother's side — had effected its junction with Radetzky at that place. Having learned of the presence of his two nephews, he wished to see them. He questioned us on our political opinions. The uncle was a rabid *austriacante* — an Austrian partisan — and I was well aware of it. I expressed myself in language vibrant and free.

The old soldier turned pale and interrupted me. "These," said he, "are the accursed sentiments of your father. He has infused them into your blood. Poor fool! In what do you trust? In what do you hope? Do you believe that four peasants armed with pitchforks and cudgels will suffice to frighten the most battle-hardened army in the world; to put the stability of the Empire in jeopardy?"

I contented myself with a cold allusion to Radetzky's retreat; to the revolution bursting forth from one end to the other of the usurped dominion.

"Ah, if you were not my nephew—" he muttered through his teeth. "Thank your mother; she is your salvation! Be that as it may... be that as it may... sooner or later you will lose your head to the executioner!"

And I, smiling: "I would sooner face the executioner a hundred times than be the vile renegade that you are!"

He departed fuming and I never saw him again.

But those were mere words. Another terrible incident soon came to fill the measure, to give the fatal direction to my existence.

The army had left Peschiera behind when informers arrived to announce that the little town of Castelnuovo was daring to raise barricades. Its spirited inhabitants, reasoning correctly that Radetzky could not reach the safe shelter of Verona without passing through their locality, heeded Carlo Alberto's appeal to obstruct the retreat, and resolved to set their breasts against the enemy's arms, to offer themselves up as a burnt offering to the Fatherland.

In those days of heroic enthusiasm, one did not deliberate overmuch on the probability of success. The greater and more certain the sacrifice, the stronger the goad that drove men to embrace it.

The Imperial artillery made short work of Italian patriotism. Castelnuovo, riddled with shot and in flames, was abandoned to the lusts of the soldiery. We, cadets of the Academy, witnessed that carnage drawn up near the gate of the old walls, at the far end of an elliptical square. And as the Croatians, in their blind fury, were not taking careful aim, we had sought cover behind several abandoned carriages.

All at once I saw a young girl emerge from a burning house — her hair unbound, barefoot, scarcely covered by a simple shift — evidently a sick girl surprised by the fury of the fire and of the men. And then, from that same house, a foul Croatian burst forth with weapon in hand, in pursuit of her.

I thought: "If that dog reaches her, he will cut her throat." And following an irresistible impulse, I was already launching myself forward to go to her aid, when another of those ruffians, heedless that in his own country too there were mothers and sisters trembling with anxiety, levelled his carbine and fired. The girl fell, and a scarlet streak stained the white linen that wrapped her.

A curse, a leap, and I was at her side, trembling with pity and with rage. At the touch of my hands lifting her head, she opened her eyes; but the moment she caught sight of the hated uniform, she shuddered and turned her face away — her pale face, whereon, more telling than the deep marks of the end that was upon her, there reigned an expression of uttermost revulsion.

"Do not be afraid. I am one of your own; I too am Italian!" and I was touching her body to find the wound.

The unhappy girl pointed to her heart, gave a sigh, and her young limbs were overtaken by the ineffable loosening of death. I stood and gazed upon her. She was beautiful with a beauty rendered sublime in my eyes by the aureole of martyrdom. Then it was as though those lips spoke to me in a language passionate and vibrant. My emotion grew overwhelming. I suffered as though I were taking into my soul all the griefs of the Fatherland, and a surge of new resolves broke suddenly forth from my depths. Never again, after that day, did I invoke, in the moments of danger, the sacred ideal of Liberty but that, to renew that surge, there did not reappear before me, distinct in my mind, the delicate features of that young girl driven into the eternal silence by the oppressor's bullet — unconsciously great, tragic, and beautiful.

Night fell, and it was the last night for certain tormentors who had expected to savour yet a while longer the juice of our clusters. Livid lips kept no secrets. Nor was the matter probed too closely. To what end, in times of inextricable confusion? But I can speak now without fear and without bravado. It was that night that my hands and Achille's came to know blood for the first time. An act in defence of my reawakening race, an act of Justice! The pregnant Lombard mother and the young girl of Castelnuovo did not remain long unavenged!


### Chapter Four

<!-- pages:26-31 -->





ARRIVED safe and sound in Verona, the old Marshal offered the cadets the option of entering the regular army as ranked soldiers, granting them the privilege of choosing their own Regiment. Not one accepted. We were interned as far as Gratz and incorporated into that Military Academy; until the Minister of War, accepting our petition, permitted us to return to our homes.

September arrived. The valiant Piedmontese army, abandoned to itself through the defection of the Italian princes, its impetus broken by the mental infirmities of its own King, defeated more by the deficiencies of its own organisation than by Austrian valour, had been compelled to abandon Venetia, the Duchies, Lombardy; to retreat behind the line of the Ticino. Milan had fallen once again into the possession of the oppressors... With the royal war virtually ended, there continued, in the expression then in vogue, that of the People.

Among those individuals stripped of every official distinction who distinguished themselves most that year, by valour and by integrity of spirit, was a patriot most dear to my family, a certain Fortunato Calvi...

With a handful of mountain men and arms that were nearly primitive, he had defended the Cadore from the first days of May to the first days of June, inscribing in the annals of our epic the names of Ampezzo, of Termine, of Rivalgo, of Chiusa di Venas, of the pass of Death, of Selva, of Rindimera, and the fateful Mauria.

In this stupendous campaign, which certain historians skip over with a swiftness yet more stupendous, Pietro Fortunato Calvi repelled the attacks — at first separate, then combined — launched by the Austrians against the three natural gateways of the Cadore. When, at the third phase of the war, he laid down arms, it was a necessity created by the complete exhaustion of provisions and ammunition. Nor in that era, so ready to attribute reverses to treachery, did a single voice arise to diminish the glory of the hero. He made his way, by unfrequented routes, to Venice, and wherever he appeared he was received not as a man defeated, but as a conqueror.

In his footsteps, by way of Portogruaro, the only road still open, I entered Venice as well, together with my brother Achille. Calvi at once admitted us to serve in that legion of the Cacciatori delle Alpi, whose name became synonymous with heroism and glory.

Through the final months of 1848 and the early months of the following year, I had thus the opportunity to witness a marvellous spectacle. In my long existence I have known no other more powerful to exalt the human soul... A people which, by sheer force of will, retempers its own fibre, scours away in hardship, in the grip of hunger, in the horrors of pestilence, in the bath of its own blood, the shame of centuries of languor, the cowardly resignation to bondage.

In destruction, the gleams of life renewing itself, of greatness reaffirming itself.

In those months, beyond my share of the common sufferings, I endured a grief intimate and intense. My brother Achille had his saintly resolves and his fervent hopes inexorably cut short by the pestilence. I reached him in time to press my lips to his cold brow, and it seemed to me that at the touch of my lips something like a remnant of feeling stirred in the inert corpse. I believe that only from that moment did the young patriot wholly cease to tremble.

Personally, I took part in but a single action of importance: the sortie towards Mestre which resupplied the city with forage and provisions. On that occasion I saw, for the first time, without speaking with him, a man who was to have a great influence upon my future: Felice Orsini.

Two grave infractions of discipline removed me prematurely from that theatre of sacrifice and grandeur. I had been assigned to the defence of the forts of Marghera, lunette number thirteen. A tedious duty; for Cavedalis, the Minister of War, on the pretext that ammunition was wanting, had ordered us to hold our fire. We looked on in idle inaction at the feverish labours of the Austrians who, concentrated at Mestre, were digging their parallels and preparing for the bombardment. Our inaction had so emboldened them that one day they advanced along the canal to within musket-shot of the forts... and from there they wounded the sentry of my lunette in the arm.

The orders of the Minister, and the Minister himself, vanished from my mind, to say nothing of the face of the globe... I snatched the fuse from the gunner's hands and the shot was fired. Immediately, as though my fury had spread with the swiftness of electricity, all the cannon of the forts thundered... the few enemies who remained standing took to their heels.

Pietro Fortunato Calvi himself pleaded my cause before the Council of War. He urged in my defence my ignorance of the orders, my youth, my love of the Fatherland, the irresistible impulse of the moment, and for that time I was spared. But at the second infraction, political animosities rendered my situation untenable.

The fatal dualism between republicans and monarchists, which in 1848–49 was so great a part of our reverses, had necessarily its repercussions in Venice as well. Republican at first, then annexed to monarchical Piedmont, the city had once again assumed republican government when the royal war seemed irremediably lost. The upper bourgeoisie, which even then arrogated to itself the exclusive possession of practical good sense, did not accommodate itself readily to the popular verdict, and several Ministers lent it their support.

Hence, hybrid measures that irritated the multitude and frequently impelled it to gather in the Piazza San Marco and make its tumult heard.

In March of 1849 (the precise day I do not recall) exasperation, having reached its height, seemed on the verge of degenerating into open revolt. To forestall it, Cavedalis gave the order to Pietro Fortunato Calvi to disperse the crowd at all costs, and Calvi, detaching several patrols of his legion, laid the thankless duty upon the non-commissioned officers. Yet at the moment of our departure he drew us aside and, certain of being understood, spoke these very words: "Remember that in Venice, the Croats no longer give the orders."

I led my patrol through a good stretch of the city without encountering any hindrance; but no sooner had I emerged upon the Piazza San Marco than I found before me a tempestuous sea of humanity. Our green uniform trimmed with white braid and our drooping plumes at once drew general attention, and a vibrant voice cried out: "Look at them! They've come to shoot us down!"

I placed my *kepi* on the point of my sword and, waving it in the manner of a salute, cried at the top of my lungs: "We are here to defend the People, not to tyrannise them!"

Farewell to repression! The crowd parted to give us free passage and, its mood transformed in an instant, roared from a thousand and a thousand throats: "*Evviva*! *Evviva* i Cacciatori delle Alpi!" Lost and as though absorbed into that stormy sea, we ended by becoming an integral part of it.

By good fortune, where our arms fell short, authority and eloquence were supplied by Daniele Manin. I see him still before my eyes, his countenance resembling, as one drop of water resembles another, that of Cavour, though his spirit was so far removed from all diplomatic duplicity. The thoughts he expressed I can repeat today still, almost in his very words. He exhorted the crowd not to exacerbate the miseries of the Fatherland, which in its hour of extremity called for the concord of all its sons. To divide was to diminish its strength; to divide was a crime.

The urgent, the supreme task was to vanquish the foreigner, to shatter the fetters being forged for the Republic. There would be time enough afterwards to compose internal dissensions; when the glorious banner should once again unfurl over free lands and free men. What might not a people then achieve, conscious of its own rights, drunk with enthusiasm, invigorated by victory? What intrigue, what blandishment could avail to wrest from it, even in part, the fruit of so many sacrifices? "Return to your homes, refresh yourselves in the embrace of your mothers, your wives, your anxious children... poor great People, so hardly tried in the school of adversity! Reflect that the cannon may at any moment tear you from those embraces and summon you to where the fighting is, where men die. No Italian worthy of the name should refuse to present himself, unblemished and without remorse, to the ultimate sacrifice!"

He fell silent, and that strong People, and that good People, little by little dispersed. Of how much greater glory would the wise, the humane word be to those Governors, even today, who so lightly prefer it to fratricidal arms!

The following day, Colonel Calvi sent for me. I found him more sorrowful than stern. "The Minister Cavedalis," he said to me, "is in a towering rage. Venice is full of informers, as in the days of the Signoria, and he has learned, chapter and verse, all that... all that has occurred. He demands the names of the non-commissioned officers who commanded the patrols; above all — do you understand me? — the name of a beardless sergeant who uttered seditious phrases and whom he wishes to see punished. The escapade is dealt with in the military regulations..."

I made ready to cobble together a defence; but he silenced me with a gesture. The superior officer would not and could not enter into that discussion; but the patriot extended his right hand to the patriot and gave me a clasp that spoke more than an entire volume.

"It is the second of its kind," he continued, "and there would be no remission. Sergeant de Rudio, you must leave Venice!... You will find enemies to fight in every corner of Italy. Rome has need of defenders, and the Government of the Republic, at the solicitation of those Triumvirs, is sending thither the legionaries of the Unione, who came among us after the volte-face of Pope Pio. Venice has more need of bread than of soldiers, and Colonel Ferrara, who leads the legionaries, will set sail tomorrow from Chioggia. Here is a letter for him... you will be well received. Go — I command it!"

I gave the military salute and departed. The unforeseen awaited me. The Minister Cavedalis had already sent to Chioggia the order to arrest and return to Venice any soldiers who neither belonged to the Unione nor could produce a pass signed by the commandant of the Place. I did not stroll long through the streets of Chioggia...

The barracks in which they confined me flanks a street that leads down to the shore. A single second sufficed to hurl oneself from the gateway and turn the corner into that street; four bounds like a squirrel would carry one along it to the beach. In the courtyard of the barracks there strolled about, in no particular order, legionaries of every region and every uniform... at the wide-open gate stood a sentry on guard — or rather a great lout who, judging by appearances, seemed to be dreaming of the good soups of his homeland. Slowly, slowly, as though my only care were to amuse myself a little by watching the passers-by, I drew up to his side. Then I waved my arms and called out to him in a muffled voice: "Look out! Run... run, there's someone at that window!"

He looks to the left, I spring to the right, round the corner, out of range... Oh, the blue waters of the Adriatic, oh the dark *trabaccoli* [coastal trading vessels], swaying gently on the waves, light as nutshells!... the *trabaccoli* grazed by the wing of the gulls, resounding with the sounds of toil and of song! Before me lay some forty of them, already crowded with legionaries, already manoeuvring for imminent departure.

I flatten myself in a small boat which, driven by two sturdy oarsmen, cuts through the waves swift as an arrow. But as I clamber up the side of a vessel, an officer caught sight, from the shore, of the green uniform trimmed with white braid. Providential for me, in that critical moment, was the manoeuvre of displacement carried out by the mobile flotilla. The officer had undoubtedly fixed his eye on the *trabaccolo* in which I had sought refuge; but in the passage across, he lost his bearings and boarded, in his search, the vessel where I was not. He persisted in inspecting a second ship, then a third, and arrived at last upon mine... but by then I had already found means to put on a uniform of the Unione. I fell into line with the other legionaries and he, passing within a hand's breadth of my face, scarcely deigned to glance at me.

Nevertheless I was not to set foot in Rome without first traversing a wide circuit. Numerous combatants had, like myself, left Venice by stealth — not from cowardice, but because drawn by various considerations towards the heart of Italy. However, upon reaching Ravenna, Colonel Ferrara, invoking I know not what peremptory orders, refused to enlist us and, having formed us into a detachment, had us escorted as far as Bologna under close guard, almost as though we were Austrian prisoners. Our appointed destination: Venice. But I was conscious of having taken up arms to break chains, not to have them imposed upon me... I resolved to face the hazards of a new flight.

As the sole officer among us, I had been entrusted with the task of drawing rations for the entire detachment... one fine morning I took advantage of this to bid farewell to the barracks and to San Petronio. The soldier Angeli, whom I had persuaded to follow me, placed at my disposal a few coins so that I might procure a disguise; but Venetian coinage, unhappily, had no currency in Bologna, and an extreme expedient imposed itself: to strip off my green uniform trimmed with white braid, which I had put on at Ravenna, and exchange it for a suit of *frustano* [coarse fustian cloth]. Deep was the sense of bitterness this caused me; but I found consolation at last in the thought that the working-man's dress is an honoured garb too — the honest and unfortunate *paria* [pariah] of my Country. Besides, the heart that yearns for Liberty, the heart that wins battles, I carried always, unchanged, within my breast.

At one o'clock in the morning, that same night, we arrived without incident at the walls of ancient Ferrara. The gates were shut; in the deep darkness, on the far bank of the Po, in the distance, the fires of the Austrian encampment gleamed. Save for the fortress, the town was in our hands. I knock at the gate.

— Who goes there?

— Italians who have deserted from the Austrian camp. We wish to fight for the cause of independence.

We are admitted, received with open arms, escorted to headquarters, overwhelmed with questions. Our intelligence, gleaned here and there from newspapers along the road, was found plausible... besides, we had passed ourselves off as common soldiers and no one expected revelations of high strategy. It was concluded by advancing us as far as Cento, where Major Mambrini enrolled us in the Legione Garibaldi.

Italy's most glorious sun was shining when, from the heights of Civita Castellana, I saw Michelangelo's dome spring out vigorously against the blue. My weariness vanished as if by enchantment and our arms stretched out towards the plain, towards that place where the great city was stirring — the city which had filled the world with its power and its wisdom. In those days, among the vestiges of her ancient civilisation, among the proud creations of the Middle Ages, the symbol of the new idea blazed forth in the red shirt.

It was the last days of April. On the 30th, the troops of the French invader broke their own onset against our breasts, and the hearts of Italians opened once more to hope!


### Chapter Five

<!-- pages:31-34 -->

THE SIEGE OF ROME is indissolubly linked to the name of a man fatal to Liberty: that of Luigi Napoleone. The Carbonari, who had received him into their bosom, regarded him with suspicion as early as 1831; for at that time there had spread abroad the rumour that he had done away with his brother (Napoleone Luigi), who, having been born four years before him, stood as a barrier across his path to greatness.

Our revolutionary movements seemed then to open new and adventurous horizons, and the two sons of Hortense, taking part in those movements, sought in the Peninsula a field favourable to their ambitions; a compensation, perhaps, for the loss of the French throne, then warmed for the moment by Luigi Filippo. This train of ideas lent weight to the sinister rumour, which, moreover, could never be thoroughly investigated.

The majority of historians who have addressed the matter state simply that Napoleone Luigi died of measles. Others go so far as to admit that the truth is no longer certain, while vaguely suggesting that, if a crime was indeed perpetrated, it must be attributed to the fanatics of some *Vendita* — a Carbonari lodge.

Be that as it may, the distrust was attenuated by time and by the humanitarian poses of the prince. Moreover, the subversives clearly understood the immense advantage they might derive from a man whose very name was a whole programme of war against the old order, and who could not have emerged from his semi-obscurity save on condition of assuming the full responsibility of that programme.

His hopes of making his fortune in Italy having vanished, Luigi Napoleone turned his eyes toward France. He attempted first the coup de main of Strasbourg, which failed principally through his own want of resolution; then that of Boulogne, which led him straight to the prison cells of the fortress of Ham.

It was then that the Princess Cristina Belgioioso Trivulzio, deeply versed in the affairs of Young Italy, was induced to make use of her own influence at the Court of Luigi Filippo in order to obtain an audience with the prisoner.

The substance of that meeting required no lengthy preamble. The noblewoman offered the support of the liberals of France and Italy to effect an escape and to assist the man in keeping afloat upon the stormy sea of politics. Once he had attained power, he would repay the debt of gratitude by working toward the promulgation of democratic laws and championing our independence.

Luigi Napoleone's hesitation led the illustrious Italian lady to grant him two months for reflection. In the meantime, so as not to be unprepared for events, his friends would set about organising the plan of an escape.

The two months elapsed and a second meeting took place. The prisoner listened without committing himself; then expressed his deep sympathy for our Nation; but concluded by declaring that, for the present, he did not feel disposed to act — whether because he distrusted the accomplished and most cultivated noblewoman, or because he hoped for assistance from parties of ideas more akin to his own instincts and his own secret designs.

The plan seemed to have been abandoned when, like a thunderbolt, the news spread through our circles that Napoleone, suddenly changing his mind, had given his consent; and shortly afterwards, equally swift, came the news of his disguise and his flight — from France to Belgium, from Belgium to England.

At the Brunswick Hotel, he saw the Belgioioso again and sealed, so to speak, the pact: he would aid Italy as soon as affairs in France had been settled.

Events moved swiftly. Luigi Filippo was driven from the throne, and Napoleone requested permission to return home. To overcome the parliamentary opposition, Luigi Blanc arose and, with a single speech, changed the course of France's history as much as ten battles might have done. Napoleone became a candidate for the National Assembly for the Department of the Seine, and triumphantly set foot upon French soil.

A period of melancholy fame! The parties contending for mastery of the country flew at one another's throats and unleashed upon Paris the tempest of blood that prefaces every great political upheaval. Cavaignac emerged grimly from the carnage and aspired to the Presidency of the Republic, which the vanquished contested against him by desperately advancing the candidacy of Ledru Rollin. The resultant of those two forces, foreseen and facilitated by our secret associations, carried to victory a third candidacy: that of Luigi Napoleone. On the 20th of December, 1848, he became the Supreme Magistrate of the glorious Nation.

And his first act was to requite his friends by working, with arms and with treachery, to restore in the very heart of our Peninsula the most feeble, the most pernicious, the most hated of Governments — the government of the Pope!

This diabolical design was so cunningly dissembled as to induce the Italians to welcome with enthusiastic, fraternal demonstrations the landing of the troops of General Oudinot, who had appeared without warning in the waters of Civitavecchia.

In France, suspicions arose more promptly. The indignant protests against the suspected destruction of a sister Republic, to the advantage of the element most hostile to all liberty, spread from the halls of Parliament down among the multitudes, assuming here and there the proportions of full-scale uprisings. And perhaps the energy of the people would at last have imposed honest intentions, when news spread of the reverse suffered, on the 30th of April, by the arms of the invader.

A shudder of stern patriotism ran from one end of France to the other. "The prestige of the flag must be restored; the national honour must be vindicated at every point." As though the shame of a People derived not from the crime itself, but from its failure to be carried through! Luigi Napoleone thus found, unexpectedly, a willing accomplice in his sinister designs — strange to say — the most generous, the most loyal of all the powers of old Europe.

The French, the soldiery of Re Bomba, the Spaniards, the Austrians, and the subtle arts of the priests — how could Rome have escaped utter ruin?

One man alone, Giuseppe Mazzini, foresaw, in that tragic moment, that from death itself — by virtue of heroism and of martyrdom — right might renew itself, life might spring forth. He endeavoured to transfuse his faith into all those around him, and the learned and the humble were left as though transfigured. The "brigands" who defended Rome wrote, in notes of blood, the purest, the most fruitful of the pages of our modern history. The iron determination that one day restored the glorious capital to our Motherland is owed, in great measure, to their defeats.


### Chapter Six

<!-- pages:34-39 -->



TO INSERT my own person among the great men who laboured in that titanic defence seems to me an act of irreverence. And I will say too that my spirit remains as though overwhelmed by the surging return of those powerful memories.

On the 30th of April, I too saw the backs of the invaders, and certainly, pressing our advantage, we should have driven the French into the sea, had Giuseppe Mazzini not thought it opportune, for his own diplomatic designs, to curb our impetus.

On the 9th of May, I was fighting with Garibaldi at Palestrina against the Bourbon forces of General Lanza. That easy victory sufficed to check the advance of Re Bomba, who evacuated the Alban Hills and hastily re-crossed the frontier a few days later, when Garibaldi, preferring to conquer rather than to appear disciplined, urged our vanguard to press the engagement at Velletri.

In that memorable day I had a part in an episode which I recall with pride.
