The lancers of Angelo Masina, overwhelmed by an entire enemy column, were falling back at a headlong gallop along the road to Valmontone. Giuseppe Garibaldi, catching sight of them at once, spurred his white horse and, drawing up a short distance from their front, planted himself squarely across the road to bar their way. Behind him, on a horse black as ebony, towered the giant Aguyar, his face as gleaming and dark. Heroism had forgotten that motion has its laws. The lancers made superhuman efforts to pull up short; but to no avail. In less time than it takes to tell, the road was blocked by a tangled mass of men and beasts — Garibaldi and Aguyar were buried beneath them. The enemy cavalry bore down upon the scene.

I was fighting along the road, to the right, among the vineyards. We were but a handful of beardless youths — yet we waited for no orders, and crying "Forward! Forward!" we charged resolutely to the rescue. The Bourbon troops, assailed from front and flank, took to precipitate flight, leaving a goodly number of prisoners in our hands.

Garibaldi recognized in our charge the instrument of his own salvation. In the evening, when by order of General Rosselli our offensive was brought to its ill-fated close, he came among us. That man, so terrible when the cannon thundered, gave my cheek a good-natured pinch. I smile now at this sort of codicil to the episode; but then — I felt all that I had supposed heroic within me simply dissolve. That gesture of paternal familiarity reduced me, with an ineffable forcefulness, to the humble proportions of a child.

At the end of May I returned to Rome, and we were already on the point of marching against the Austrians, when General Oudinot, adding treachery upon treachery, prematurely broke the armistice concluded after his defeat of the 30th of April, and seized by surprise the Villa Pamfili. There was no longer any need to march to Ancona to water the soil of Italy with our blood!

That day — the 3rd of June — witnessed such proofs of desperate valour as to astonish Europe and the world, long unaccustomed to descrying, among the hills of Rome, a Roman greatness. But the hydra of reaction exacted, on that day, an excessive holocaust of our finest. Would that it had been the last!

The French aimed to subdue Rome by seizing the Janiculum, which commands the entire city. To reach it, they first needed to take our advanced posts. Having seized the Villa Pamfili, they moved, under cover of darkness, against the Villa Corsini which, by virtue of its elevated and strategic position, could be regarded as the key to the Porta San Pancrazio. And by surprise they had it; but in a furious bayonet charge it was retaken by us.

Unfortunately, owing to the enormous disproportion in numbers and to various topographical reasons upon which I consider it needless to dwell, the assault on that villa proved more than arduous to those advancing from the city, whilst it remained easy for the French who had entrenched themselves beyond the Villa Pamfili. Our magnanimous contempt for our own lives could lead us, and repeatedly did lead us, to become masters of it once more; but to hold it was for us a superhuman and impossible undertaking.

None the less we displayed incredible aggressive tenacity from the earliest hours of morning until late evening. That splendid manifestation of the reawakened, virile Italian temper had, so far as I was concerned, an interval. During one of our first reconquests, as I was crossing, weapon in hand, a passage encumbered with the dead, I caught sight, through the crack of a door, of a reddish gleam — the trousers of a Frenchman — an officer who had persisted in the defence and had been unable to retreat with his men. To escape the first fury of the attackers, he had concealed himself in a *gabinetto di decenza* — a privy. Upon my demand that he surrender, he handed me his sword without a word.

I was charged with escorting him to the Chiesa Nuova, where the captured officers were being temporarily confined.

The Romans had treated the prisoners taken on the 30th of April not as enemies but as guests. Those were days when the rosy hope of fraternity between the two Peoples still trembled in the air, and the prospect of inducing the French to protect Rome from the Austrian hordes had been kept alive by the very assurances of the Triumvirs themselves. Carriage rides to the monuments, refreshments, songs, dancing — and then liberty. The pledge not to turn their arms again against the champions of our independence was neither given nor asked.

But by the 3rd of June the spirit of the Romans had been terribly transformed. The bad faith of Oudinot had roused to the pitch of paroxysm the indignation of a people so utterly foreign to all duplicity, so hostile to treachery. I perceived this the moment I passed through the Porta San Pancrazio, and the officer who walked at my side was made to feel it still more keenly, for it was not always in my power to shield him from violent insults.

Reaching the Ponte di San Sisto, I found my way barred by several militiamen of the National Guard who, refusing to hear reason, and acting on the impulse of the moment, demanded that I surrender the prisoner to them. They wished to kill him on the spot and throw his body into the river.

I cried out to them: "Whoever thirsts for French blood, let him run beyond the walls, and happy is he if his hands prove skilled and strong. But not here! Not while I have breath in my body will I permit the Italian name to be dishonoured by cowardly deeds."

The timely intervention of an officer calmed their tempers and averted every tragedy.

We arrived, without further incident, at the Chiesa Nuova, and the Frenchman, grateful, recounted what had passed to the other prisoners. While, not far off, two peoples made for mutual understanding and affection were hurling themselves upon one another, athirst for vengeance, in that comparatively tranquil corner I heard the very invaders extol our courage, and voice the wish that, once rancours were dispelled, French blood and Italian blood might flow mingled together — but in fraternal solidarity, but for a common, sacred vindication.

A prophetic wish! — though at that moment I considered it far more probable that I should be taken up alive into the glory of the heavens!

In climbing back up the narrow and steep road that leads to the Porta San Pancrazio, I was obliged to walk against the current — the current of the wounded being carried, or dragging themselves, to the church of San Pietro in Montorio, which had been converted into a field hospital. Soldiers and civilians applauded them as they passed. From atop the walls a band poured out, amid the shots, the strains of the Marseillaise, flinging in the face of the aggressors their own hymn to Liberty. Amid so much blood, in such an extremity, Rome found the means to make an irony of it all.

I had left the Villa Corsini in the hands of the Legionaries; I found it reoccupied by the French. Our Bersaglieri, led by Luciano Manara, were attempting its recapture. I watched them run up the flanks of the hill, leaving behind them ground strewn with the dead and the dying. The fire cutting them down was so sustained that, a short distance from the building, they were as though brought to a sudden halt by lead. Yet their breasts, swelling with love of country, had no room for weak sentiments. Exposed as they were, they dropped to their knees and continued to discharge their rifles, presenting an undaunted face to the enemy and to fate. Surely they expected to be reinforced by our men gathered at the Vascello and along the walls; surely they were offering themselves, with serene spirit, to death, in order to give those men time to arrive — to renew the attack at their side.

What moments those were for us, who watched their ranks dwindle, who ached with their wounds and were proud of their pride! The awaited order never came. They fell back when the officers commanded it; when discipline forbade the sacrifice to continue. But on issuing from the enclosure, which ended toward Rome in a sharp angle, they became a sure target for a converging and terrible fire, and then — My country, what a slaughter!

Thus the brave Lombard youth blotted out the stain of past civil discord, and saturating with their blood the soil of the Gracchi, traced upon it — invisible to the eye, but blazing in every heart — the fateful words: <change old='"Italia Italia una, Roma capitale d\'Italia!"' reason="Duplication of 'Italia' in translation; Italian source reads 'Italia una, Roma capitale d\'Italia!' with no repetition">"Italia una, Roma capitale d'Italia!"</change>

It was in that assault that Enrico Dandolo ceased to breathe and to fight for his — for our — Country. His death, exalting the courage of his brother Emilio, drove the latter to attempt the assault on the villa, leading alone twenty of his men. That slender band, thinned along the way by enemy fire, was all but lost among the rough terrain and the orange trees of the slope. Thirteen of them reached the portico, discharged their weapons, and drew back before the inevitable. When they took shelter at the Vascello they were reduced to six — not one of them without blood upon him.

Their deed is one which cold and calculating minds may well call madness — so be it! But it was the madness of natures almost more than human, who were not fighting to win. The defenders of Rome, it is worth making clear, had set themselves the task of disproving the belief that Italians do not fight; of placing between the Temporal Power and the longed-for third Italy the impossibility of any equivocation; of affirming, by a heroic death, our right to live. And we may now proclaim it aloud: they attained their arduous end.

The turn for heroism and sacrifice fell, after Emilio Dandolo, to Angelo Masina of Bologna. By now the upper floors of the villa, cannonaded from the bastions of the Porta San Pancrazio, are half in ruin and in flames; yet for all that, several hundreds of unseen Frenchmen, invulnerable unless dislodged by cold steel, are none the less formidable to the forty lancers of Masina.

They charge up the hill at a gallop, reaching the summit that nothing could hold them from. Here, to the great astonishment of friends and foes alike, Masina, suddenly assuming the dimensions of a figure conjured by the power of enchantment, launches himself on horseback up the steps, dragging after him, all the way to the terrace, that fistful of bold men.

And perhaps all of them would have left their bones there — had we soldiers, concentrated at the Vascello, exalted by that spectacle, not feared that our commanders, persisting in their error, might render even such heroism fruitless. It was as though an electric spark, struck from the heart of Angelo Masina, had instantly kindled a thousand and a thousand hearts with its own fire. We poured out from behind our defences and ran to the assault spontaneously, without waiting for any command from anyone.

A yet stranger circumstance: the populace, thronged upon the bastions as if in a theatre, divined our intent, and, seized by our fever, surges forward, pours like an impetuous flood between the walls that narrow the tight road, and spreads in great confusion, as though borne forward by its own momentum, up the flanks of the hill drenched in so much blood. They have no weapons, or weapons wholly inadequate; yet they seem convinced that their very presence puts strength into our arms — they would have shown, at the very least, that they knew how to face danger, how to die!

Such a breach of discipline, such an absence of all elementary prudence produced an effect contrary to what might logically have been expected. Not only was every Frenchman swept from the Villa Corsini; but, pressing the advantage, our bayonets also retook the Convent of San Pancrazio, where the invader had, since morning, barricaded his advance posts. And a band of daring men, veering to the right, succeeded in seizing the Villa Valentini, from whose windows the French had, a little earlier, been harassing us even within the Vascello.

Never, as in that hour, had the fortunes of the day appeared so propitious to the glory of Rome! When, from the Villa Pamfili, Oudinot drove forward, one after another, his seasoned and rested Regiments to retake the lost positions, numbers, discipline, and superior arms prevailed once more — but not without enormous sacrifice. Neither our men, exhausted by so many ordeals, nor the nearly unarmed People, took flight.

We fell back to the Casa Giacometti, to the Vascello, to the walls — but with our faces ever turned toward the enemy, and fighting still.

Giuseppe Garibaldi had in his heart the certainty that, with the loss of the Villa Corsini, the fate of the young Republic was sealed. The improvident General Rosselli had not troubled himself, during the armistice, to fortify it, to render it, if not invulnerable, at least more defensible. To crown the disaster, Garibaldi received from Rome only in dribs and drabs, and at long intervals, the fresh contingents necessary to sustain the battle. Seeing defeat inevitable, he refused to accept it without pressing his tenacity to the utmost extreme.

Night was drawing near when he, planting himself before the Reggimento Unione and what remnants of other corps the French lead had spared, personally led one last furious assault. The valour was the same — but the outcome was catastrophe.

I had just fired my last cartridges when I saw pass close by me, supported by his comrades in arms, pale but smiling, Goffredo Mameli. They told me he had been wounded in the knee — Italy was on the verge of losing her young Tyrtaeus!

Night fell. Despondency, recriminations? Not a shadow of them. Within us, after defeat, there persisted the same exaltation that had sustained us, that had multiplied our strength a hundredfold throughout that desperate and unequal struggle. And as for Garibaldi, we felt we loved him as though he had led us not to slaughter but to victory. Our spirit felt itself so little vanquished that, barely emerged from that bath of blood, we meditated and attempted to perform a deed which, in an army held together not by the overwhelming desire to act but by discipline, would have earned us the iron sentence of a court martial.

Masina, the heroic Bolognese who, for one moment, had seemed to friend and foe alike like a knight descended among the combatants by the virtue of enchantment, had proved himself all too mortal. Among us were those who swore they had seen him fall during our retreat, a short distance from the Villa Corsini. We looked one another in the face, and it was almost enough to say: "Let us go."

Shortly after, we slipped out of the Casa Giacometti under cover of darkness. We were twelve. We crept along the Via della Nocetta, hugging the wall that encircles the villa. The cannon had breached it in one small place — we entered through that gap into the enclosure, and still crawling, climbed the slope. Not a suspicious sound. We searched — and found. But the stalwart men who lay hands upon those glorious remains forgot their caution, and thinking to escape the danger more quickly, lifted their burden and began to descend toward the breach at a run. In the night, a volley of musket shots rang out with sinister resonance — three of them fell in a heap; the others scattered — I leapt behind a hedge and threw myself flat upon the ground. At my side, touching my elbow, lay the body of an enemy with his skull shattered.

I was the last to return to the Casa Giacometti — four of ours never returned at all. And as I was recounting to my comrades how I had managed to extricate myself, someone cried out: "Lord God — what a stench!" The sleeve of my uniform was smeared all over with French brains.

The 3rd of June had been a torrid day. I stood in silence for a moment, contemplating the sad transformation wrought, in so brief an hour, upon the sublime organ of human thought, beneath the kisses of the splendid Roman sun.


### Chapter Seven

<!-- pages:39-43 -->



I do not intend to narrate the detailed history of a siege that dragged on beyond all expectation. With his original plan, General Oudinot sought to seize the Janiculum by direct assault. But the fierce and unexpected resistance offered at Casa Giacometti, and above all that of the Vascello — which was reduced to a heap of ruins yet which French arms never succeeded in taking — had forced Oudinot to abandon that first design. He worked around the difficulty by extending his trenches southward and preparing a flanking attack. This prolonged the agony of the Republic by a month.

During that month I had, between one exchange of rifle-fire and the next, more than one occasion to hear the inspired words of Ugo Bassi and to hold vibrant conversations with Ciceruacchio. Poor martyrs of a cause more than holy, they return at times to enliven my solitude, and in those moments I hear their voices still and dare not stir an eyelid, for fear that reality will reassert itself and the vision vanish — precisely as a son who, after long years, has had the bier enclosing his father's body unsealed and found it intact, dreads that the lightest breath might scatter those dear features and reduce them to a shapeless handful of ash.

Among my memories of that time I also number the sad and ineffable smile of Anita Garibaldi, who, on the very eve of giving life to the hero's child, found the strength to share our toil and to receive into her frail breast the sorrows of a country that was not even her own.

And I had the opportunity to exchange a few words with the Princess Cristina Belgioioso, of whom I shall speak briefly. This cultivated noblewoman, whose very nature was exalted yet morbidly sensitive, and who felt the passions of the age with extraordinary depth, had, after Carlo Alberto's abandonment of Milan, lost for a time all her faith in the Savoyard Monarchy and had flung herself, with an impetus entirely her own, into the republican ranks. She had hastened to the defence of Rome and revealed a resourceful intelligence in the organisation of the hospitals, and an unwavering selflessness in the care of the wounded. And there her hand was sanctified by the touch of a great dying man: Goffredo Mameli.

I shall mention in passing that, when Rome fell, the Belgioioso undertook a journey to the East. But the slaughter of so many heroes was bound to rouse her noble indignation against the man whose treachery had provoked it. She drafted and published, in language at once lofty and scorching, her act of accusation, in which allusion was made — all too plainly — to the dealings that had taken place between the liberals and the prisoner of Ham; dealings which she promised to document.

Before she could, or resolved to do so, she was repeatedly stabbed by a Bergamasque servant in her employ. And there were those who perceived, in that attempted assassination, the influence of Louis Napoleon. True it is that the servant himself harboured a deep grudge against the Princess, who had dismissed him; yet it was observed that the agent of a powerful man would scarcely have exposed himself to carrying out the execrable crime in person. To inflame the servant's hatred and impel him to strike was an undertaking at once less compromising and more reliable.

The Bergamasque, while already imprisoned, contrived to evade punishment by flight, and of him nothing more was heard — a thing exceedingly difficult, not to say impossible, without the assistance of powerful allies. Nor should it pass unnoticed that certain publications by the Princess became mysteriously unobtainable.

I press on towards the end. The French seize, in the dead of night and almost by surprise, the papal walls... our defence is reduced to the inner line of the old Aurelian walls, where stands that Villa Spada which will shortly witness the fall of Luciano Manara, gasping in his last agony. The Genoese Medici, half-buried among the ruins of the Vascello, continues to triumph over the numbers and the arrogance of the foreigner; Giuseppe Garibaldi has moved his headquarters to the elevated Villa Savonarelli, not far from the Porta San Pancrazio. The villa crumbles under the artillery fire ranged from Villa Corsini; yet from it he can still command the field of battle... The night of San Pietro arrived.

Despite the straitened circumstances and the fate that now hung over us with terrible imminence, Michelangelo's dome was illuminated as though it were broad day. The Florentines, besieged by the army of Charles V, had three centuries before illuminated that of Santa Maria del Fiore... History was binding the new heroism to the old. Towards midnight, lightning flashes, thunder rolls, hail lashes down, water rushes in torrents through the streets, and no sooner does the fury of the elements abate than the French fury descends upon the city.

"Courage is the final proof!" We rush to the defence soaked to the bone, firing by the light of the lightning-flashes. Darkness, confusion, oaths, death-rattles!...

In the midst of that chaos, I stumble and fall headlong.

Before I could fully regain my feet, I found myself encircled by a ring bristling with bayonets — and it was well for me that the order to give no quarter was not sufficient to smother the generosity of the French.

Taken prisoner, I am marched back, from trench to trench, as far as San Paolo. I am in a courtyard among a throng of fellow-soldiers... some lie stretched out staring wide-eyed; others pace to and fro like beasts behind bars... here and there bandaged arms, faces streaming with blood.

Thunder still rumbles in the distance; a few paces away, rifle-fire and cannon; the air heavy with electricity and rage... my nerves transmit that impulse to my brain and heart. It was but an instant!... in the wide-open doorway the sentry stood black against the dark. He fell beneath my dagger like an empty sack, without uttering a groan, and I fled across the open country, crouching, creeping, leaping; passing, unobserved, through the trenches one by one. From the last I make out the breach and already flatter myself that I may once more plunge into the thick of the fray, when a lightning-flash betrays my red shirt. I am seized again, brought back to San Paolo, where I find the Judas who denounces me as the killer of the sentry. By the time day broke, I had been arraigned before a court-martial.

I have many times reproached myself, and continue to reproach myself, for having retained in my memory so many incidents of my life that are of no consequence whatever, and for having forgotten the name of the French officer who came to my aid in so desperate a strait.

He spoke for more than half an hour. By what quirk of procedure I was taken from the room, I cannot say. Yet from the adjoining chamber I caught the most forceful passages of his defence.

In substance he maintained the argument that, since I had not yet given my parole, I must be regarded as a lawful combatant, with every right and duty to kill. And pressing his case, he said to my judges:

"Does not the truth that acquits him more than shield you; does not truth itself compel you to silence every resentment and to receive into your breast, as into a temple, the sentiments of humanity and of justice? The prisoner has shed blood. But what thought, other than cowardice, could have stayed his hand? Was not the cannon thundering on every side? Was he not fighting for a cause which even his enemies must acknowledge to be noble and legitimate? Was he not risking his life in defence of his Fatherland and of Liberty?"

And he urged them to imagine the parts reversed: "Suppose Paris besieged by Roman arms; suppose that the beardless youth who awaits your sentence were of French blood and had killed, in the selfsame circumstances, an enemy sentry in order to hasten to the last defence of our beloved France... which of you would not admire his ardour and audacity? Which would not feel proud to call him a fellow-citizen? Which would not have wished to emulate him? Which would refuse to embrace him with fraternal warmth and to honour him as a true hero?"

I was acquitted, and on the 1st of July, together with some three hundred prisoners of every uniform, I was sent to Civitavecchia. Some twenty miles from Rome, I slackened my pace... no one took notice of me, and I took advantage of this to slip behind a thicket, ready and prepared to justify my halt with evidence all too plain. A few minutes later I was joined by another Garibaldino, a Venetian fisherman, who was in the act of furnishing, on his own account, the identical proof... Soldiers and prisoners marched on, they were far off... they disappeared. There we were, free once more, and we congratulated each other heartily, clasping hands with great effusion.

We clambered over hedges, crossed bare fields and pastures; took shelter for the remainder of the day in a shepherd's cottage, where he revived us with black bread and cheese. Late at night we set out again for Rome. But we found it occupied by the French. Garibaldi had departed the previous night from the Porta San Giovanni, with the nucleus of his men, to hasten to the defence of Venice... the last strip of Italy where the People, famished and bleeding, still stood upright, rallied about the banner of emancipation!...

The self-styled procurator of the good Lord, who had quitted the eternal city disguised as a countess's footman, was already preparing to re-enter it from Gaeta with theatrical pomp. With all traces of the titanic struggle effaced, he would find "his" Rome resplendent with a magnificence wrought by the centuries... so that, once his double throne was shored up again, he might splendidly conceal therein his own ignominy.

And there were those who, on the very eve of the catastrophe, suggested leaving of so many marvels not one stone upon another. With motives perhaps less practical, twenty years later, the Paris Commune was seized by a similar fever of desperation. But Giuseppe Mazzini held the manifestations of genius to be sacred, and his authority prevailed.

To the calm eloquence of the Master I had attempted, in my youthful presumption, to oppose the impetuous torrent of altogether different sentiments. "Genius is, in Italy, sown broadcast along every road and asks nothing to flower save a propitious occasion. That occasion would be offered by Liberty! The very hands calloused in pulling down temples would have rebuilt them equally splendid, and dedicated them, I held, no longer to falsehood, but to Truth. The multitude of the bigots, incapable of grasping the arduous abstractions of philosophy, must be educated in the school of deeds. Let the idols which that multitude regards as endowed with supernatural virtue be smashed in the public square. When, contrary to all expectation, they see the Sun continue on its course from East to West; when they are forced to acknowledge that no legion of angels descends from heaven to stay the arm of the alleged sacrilegists, they will also be forced to acknowledge that the demolishing hammer has shattered nothing but wood and marble. Let the Hydra return and find the hideous ruin that befits it. Catholicism, having survived so great a light of science, is by now emptied of every regenerative idea and, to sustain itself, has need of outward magnificence. The gaze of the common people halts at the surface, and its grandiose semblance suffices to make it venerable in their eyes. Strip away the gilding... lay bare the rot... reveal the emptiness! Rome had to belong to the Italians, or vanish from the stage of the world. Her ruins would attest to all peoples the desperation of a people that truly wills; they would be a monument more glorious than any basilica."

I attempted to rejoin Garibaldi on the road to Venice; but the stratagems that baffled the three armies pursuing him led me astray as well, and near Tivoli I very nearly fell into the fraternal arms of the Spanish soldiers. I too ended by taking refuge in San Marino, where, at brief intervals and in uncertain and gloomy outline, there reached me the horrible tales of legionaries disbanded, scattered, shot like wild wolves... Ugo Bassi shot, Ciceruacchio shot, and his twelve-year-old son who clung with both arms to him. Such was the penalty exacted, in my day, for the crime of loving one's Country. And amidst all that horror there passed, tender and sorrowful, the last breath of Anita.

It was at San Marino that I learned of the fall of the Lion of San Marco. With the surrender of Venice, the first part of the great Italian epic came to an end. The bloodied arms were laid away in their hiding-places; wrath was stifled in men's breasts. The picturesque peninsula continued to be the garden of Europe, and the pale races from the North continued to descend and bask in its sunlight. But around our souls there gathered an icy sadness that found a sympathetic echo among the honest, the wise, and the strong, wherever the work of civilisation kindled by the great and generous Latin mother burned bright across the wide earth.


### Chapter Eight

<!-- pages:43-48 -->



I drifted, almost rudderless, through various cities, until I found refuge in Genoa. The *Superba* had on her hands somewhere between ten and twelve thousand political refugees. She provided for them with generous largesse; but it was like pouring water onto sand.

The city, alive with political passions, was troubled by frequent tumults, and the exiles, rendered more restless by their sufferings, added no small quantity of fuel to the fire. A child of my times, far from considering myself above my surroundings, I did as the others did. Little by little I fell into dire straits. My father, learning of this, wrote a warm letter of recommendation to Alessandro Lamarmora, and unexpectedly I found myself offered the rank of second lieutenant in the Bersaglieri... I refused!

Why?

Sudden conversions were not yet in fashion in Italy, and I did not feel it was my place to inaugurate the practice. A Mazzinian to the marrow, I would sooner have served Satan than sworn allegiance to a Monarchy. Add to this that the Bersaglieri were, at that time, terribly hated in Genoa — the fatal train of the Piedmontese cannon-shots!

I continued to live by humble labour and by expedients, until an unforeseen event procured me the relief... of changing the nature of my embarrassments. A celebrated republican had died. His funeral proved memorable for the extraordinary concourse of the agitated multitude and for that, no less extraordinary, of the armed forces, intent on keeping it within prescribed limits. At the cemetery the passions broke forth. Bitter speeches were delivered, with open allusions to the monarchical abjection and to the hopes invested in the future. They roused a storm of approbation. All at once, the banner bearing the motto *"Dio e Popolo"* — "God and the People" — hidden against the heart of some Mazzinian, was unfurled aloft above the surging heads. It received from the Sun its vivid colours, its trembling from the sea breeze; from the generous and impatient, a long and thunderous salute.

That innocent banner precipitated matters. At the sight of it, the Bersaglieri launched themselves forward, resolute as if it were a question of avenging an affront. *Balilla* — the spirit of Genoese popular defiance, ever living in Genoa — met them with volleys of stones and there inflamed them. Then the veins of the rioters stained the enclosure of the dead with crimson.

Shortly after, arrests were made en masse and, as ever on such occasions, at random. I, who, rightly or wrongly, had been among the first to come to blows, did not wait for the pleasure of the gendarmes, and that very day, seizing on the wing one of those opportunities that seem to fall from heaven, I set sail for free America.

I did not reach it. A storm, which compelled us to put in at Cartagena, once again altered the course of my existence. Before us lay Spain with her manifold attractions; beyond Spain, France — political energy, life! I consulted with one Trolli, a Lombard patriot, exiled from Genoa under identical pressure and with the identical purpose as my own. We had but a few coins between us; yet we were endowed with nimble legs. We concluded, with unanimous cry: "To the Pyrenees!"

Trolli belonged to a most respectable family of Varese, which, proposing to make of him something in the nature of a Cardinal, had dispatched him to the priesthood. When the revolution broke out, he flung his cassock to the nettles and seized his rifle. An ardent republican, he invested his convictions with all the exuberance natural to a robust constitution and a flourishing youth — so much so as to declare that the most righteous shots he had fired were not against the Germans, but against that poor "execrated Carignano," whom he accused, among other things, of having betrayed the Milanese by abandoning them, without striking a blow, to Austrian vengeance. Which had not prevented him — he would add — from making off with the jewels donated by the Lombard women for the defence of their city.

After a few days of forced marches our coins had vanished, and the problem of appetite became for us as arduous as it was imperious. But my would-be Cardinal had an inspiration. He said to me, in substance:

"Garibaldian tactics: are provisions lacking? Wrest them from the enemy! We need bread? The enemy shall give us bread and something to go with it. I have studied as a priest; Latin is my forte. We come from Rome; we are two crows plucked bare by the revolution. I shall serve Mass; I shall preach in bad Spanish, but larded with excellent Latin. The curias and the monasteries will throw open their doors... to the refectory. A comedy with a laden table; players of mark on both sides, and... let our bellies rejoice!"

My disapproval was lukewarm. At the sight of the first bell-tower, Trolli put an abrupt end to it by acting.

By this artifice we made our way from Cartagena to Alicante, then to Valencia, then to Saragossa, then to Tarragona. Trolli gave proof of inexhaustible readiness of wit. The seeds deposited in his soul by the Seminary had not fallen on ungrateful ground. With a single glance he could distinguish the simple-minded and good-faith priest from the cunning one who, versed in the recondite ends of the Church, treated his "mission" as a comfortable trade. His transformations verged on the phenomenal. Humble, steeped in a seraphic candour with the former, he assumed with the latter an undercurrent of malice that asserted itself whilst scrupulously respecting the forms. If he encountered any suspicion, there he was, using and abusing his Latin. Those poor prelates, in their relative ignorance, felt themselves defeated, almost annihilated. Trolli would say to me afterwards, in private: "You see that the tongue of the dead works miracles in the mouths of the living!"

We did not always live in that fashion. In Barcelona, where Trolli was expecting a remittance from home which unfortunately did not arrive, we followed the counsel of a certain Piedmontese, a lame man who leaned upon crutches and made his living selling figurines of saints and madonnas. And in the Café del Liceo, a gathering place for political refugees, making good use of our brazen faces, we planted ourselves in the middle of the room and struck up, in thirds, the hymn of Mameli. For months we had not allowed our rebel souls any legitimate outlet, and that plunge into clean water had the power to impart to our singing an impetus which art alone could not have given it. There was applause. A gentleman with large grizzled moustaches asked us, in French, who we were. — Fugitives from Rome. That was enough. He turned to his companions and harangued them at the top of his voice. He told them that we had been driven from our Homeland for the crime of having loved and defended her. And having depicted, in vivid colours, the melancholy life of the exile, he added: "Who was the cause of these ills? Who has made the hearts of so many generous men bleed? We — we French, who, being strong, felt no shame in assailing the weak; we, who having risen in defence of a great principle, straightway disavowed it, turning our arms against a people who had risen as courageously as we, in vindication of the selfsame principle; we, who no sooner had we won our freedom than we abused it by strangling the freedom of others!" He concluded by recalling the inescapable obligation of every Frenchman to exert himself individually to alleviate the sufferings brought about by the Nation.

And, removing his hat, he made the round of the room, table by table, soliciting and collecting donations.

We left Barcelona with twenty-five *duros* in our pockets. Having crossed the Pyrenees on that provision, we arrived safely at Perpignan.

Here I was bound to acknowledge that, even outside the world of old-fashioned tales, a good deed may find a greater reward than that which derives from the mere satisfaction of having done it.

The remittance from Varese, which we handled, counted and recounted at Perpignan itself, had enabled us to transform ourselves from hat to boots and to enter a café for a little diversion.

The room was crowded with soldiers speaking loudly of affairs in Algeria. I was listening in silence, sipping a drink, when I felt myself fixed by invisible eyes. I turned sharply. At a nearby table sat several officers, one of whom was indeed scrutinising me.

— Pardon me. By your appearance... you are not French.

— Italian, and not of the worst.

— Were you in Rome?

— At the time of your siege.

— And... do you recall the bridge of San Siro? Oh, I am not mistaken! Your appearance, your voice...

And he embraced me.

In the room... a profound silence. I felt myself propelled, with an affectionate force, towards the group of officers.

— I wish to present you to my comrades-in-arms. The right hand of a French soldier always extends a hearty welcome to the brave — all the more so when, on a certain day, though bearing us no hatred, they found themselves arrayed against us.

It was my prisoner from the Villa Corsini, the officer to whom I had saved a mortal plunge into the Tiber. That evening we dined merrily in quarters. And as, in the course of conversation, we disclosed our intention of making our way into the territory of the Republic, it was insisted that, honoured as guests, we should accompany the Regiment — the 10th Light Infantry — as far as Marseilles, whither it was bound to embark for Africa.

It was a succession of dinners and receptions that stretched as far as Montpellier. The mysterious politics that already informed the affairs of France had sent thither an unexpected counter-order. The officer of the Villa Corsini gave me a last, warm embrace and continued with his men towards Paris; I went on with Trolli, on foot, to Marseilles — our paths and our souls met no more.

My sojourn in this last city is memorable to me, for it was there that I pronounced the inspired oath of Mazzini and affiliated myself with the Giovane Italia. At the same time I became a member of the Circolo del Paradiso, a French subversive association which, in many affairs, acted in concert with it. However, a month later — fresh troubles! Obedient to the reactionary wind blowing secretly from the capital, the Police surrounded the premises of the Circolo. All those found within did not sleep, that night, in their own beds. The foreign members were summarily expelled from France. To Trolli and myself, the Police issued a passport with a prescribed itinerary, which was to have conducted us, stage by stage, as far as England.

At Dijon, we slipped through the meshes of the net on the first train. The Authorities either took no notice of us, or were unable to recover our trail, and undisturbed we entered the great vortex of Paris.

It was June of 1851, and stormy days were being prepared for France; yet to the eye of the superficial observer nothing showed. Luigi Napoleone, having assassinated the Roman Republic, had contracted a taste for blood and was girding himself for a crime vaster, if not greater — the assassination of the French Republic. His Presidency expired on the 4th of March 1852. As the fatal date drew near, his hopes sharpened, his fears, his hatreds, his ambitions. But in dissembling his activity, in dissipating mistrust, he appears a master. His ideal — to aim at the heights of Napoleon I and to reach them by the arts of Julius Caesar. The careful study of the life of the great Roman had persuaded him that a new tyranny is not established when, having cast off the mask, one boldly proclaims it as such. The favour of the multitudes was indispensable to him. How to secure it? He had begun by giving credence to his own solicitude for the disinherited classes, publishing, on the subject of pauperism, pages that might not seem hostile to the doctrines of the socialists. Openly accused of ambitious designs, he replied with his famous comparison between Napoleon and Washington, concluding that, being unable to possess the genius of the former, his sole ambition was to imitate the virtue of the latter. And he drove the nail home by affirming, on every propitious occasion, that he would consider as an enemy of his country whosoever should attempt to make himself great by violating the Laws.

Such prattle lulled not only the gross multitude; it had its effect upon the National Assembly as well. This body was composed of the most disparate elements which, whilst extolling the excellence of the then prevailing regime, cherished altogether different ideals. And since, even in the political field, men readily believe what they desire, the honourable assembly was easily led, by those vanities, to the conviction that the President of the Republic, more than satisfied with his rapid career, entertained no thought of attempting any dangerous novelty. On the other hand, Legitimists, Orleanists, reactionaries, radicals exhausted their prudence and their energies in watching one another, and paid scant attention to Luigi Napoleone, who was reckoned, notwithstanding the warnings of his dark past, a man too feeble and insignificant to meditate enterprises virtuously great or greatly criminal.

Amid the general credulity, there were not lacking those shrewd enough to sense the danger; but conscious of their own weakness, they prepared themselves for the surprises of the future by working silently, in the shadows.

Unexpectedly, the National Assembly, disavowing by now even its own origins, abolished universal suffrage. Luigi Napoleone had found his opportunity!

He could now undermine the ground beneath his enemies' feet; embroil himself in a cowardly crime and find historians to extol its virtue; enslave the multitude and, assuming the posture of its champion, secure at one and the same time its neutrality — perhaps even its goodwill! He set in motion the sinister forces prepared long in advance, and carried out the Coup d'État!!


### Chapter Nine

<!-- pages:48-52 -->



THANKS to our connections with the Circolo del Paradiso, we were, from the very moment of our arrival in Paris, received as brothers by the Club de Batignolles. We were put to work making cartridges.

The rooms of the Club were permanently occupied by some forty revolutionaries — not a corner in which to lodge ourselves. But we ended by finding lodgings under the roof of a Turinese hatter, dwelling in the Faubourg Saint-Antoine, at the opposite extremity of the metropolis.

To rise before the Sun; to tramp along cheerfully, dwelling on past vicissitudes; to nourish ourselves with Spartan frugality; to submit ourselves to the life of the exploited, to comprehend their sorrows and their hopes, to share in them; to return honest love for love; to fraternally sharpen the capable yet benighted intellect; to lend a hand in preparing its vindication — such was our life until the second of December.

Hastening on the second of December and making our way to the usual meeting-place, we had occasion to be greatly astonished. The Place de la Bastille was occupied by troops: infantry and cannon. The artillerymen with their slow-matches lit, as though a battle were imminent. More soldiers we found drawn up along the boulevard. In the faubourg, beyond the barrier, suddenly a coming and going of bands advancing with gravity..., the silence that betokens a storm.

Quickening our pace and working at conjectures, we soon arrived at the Rue de la Paix. Here we stopped short.

Facing the seat of the Club was a hedge of Guards. We saw the workers employed in the making of cartridges issue from the gateway two by two, four by four, brutally thrust along by the gendarmes.

It was necessary to warn the allied Clubs. In retracing our way, we were obliged to persuade ourselves that the soldiers were not stationed about for mere display of force. Isolated, but with an ill-omened frequency, rifle-shots rang out. We were making for the Club de Saint-Denis. Before reaching it, we came near to stumbling over several corpses — workmen in smocks, unarmed, who had gone out unsuspecting, treacherously launched into eternity.

We found at the Club a number of citizens, nervous with uncertainty as to the situation, pale, incapable of resolve. I recounted what I had seen, and urged them not to let themselves be butchered like sheep, but to act. Forthwith the furniture is piled up in the street; the cobblestones are prised up, and men labour feverishly to raise a barricade. A red handkerchief, fastened to a stick, flutters over that improvised bulwark and is transformed into a banner of Liberty. But before even our work was complete, a battalion of chasseurs de Vincennes, turning the corner at the end of the street, advanced at a run to the assault.

We were sixteen Frenchmen and two Italians. No one moved a finger until the assailants were within sure range — then we fired simultaneously. No sooner had the smoke cleared than we began firing again, with a ragged, scattered fusillade.

The assault was attempted twice and twice repulsed. At the third attempt, the battalion, with ranks closed, flung itself forward with a fury manifestly inflamed and a resolution equal to the tension of their spirit. A third shower of lead was expected — which did not come — and they scaled the barricade with shouts, dealing blindly with the bayonet. They found no living creature before them.

The few had held their ground against the many because they were sustained by the hope that the people of Paris would come rushing to strengthen the resistance, with that admirable *slancio* — surging impetus — so characteristic of them. The shops, the windows, the doors had remained closed; the street desperately deserted. Our last cartridge spent, we took counsel together. To what end would our sacrifice have been? We decided to disperse. The red handkerchief, continuing to flutter, had mocked the assassins. The officer commanding them, believing himself scorned by it, avenged himself by dashing it down with a kick — not the least, perhaps, of the superhuman merits which afterwards earned him the ribbon of the Legion of Honour.

But what precisely had occurred; what was being prepared in the other quarters of the city? By what arts was Louis Napoleon consummating the Coup d'État?

It was in the dead of night that he had begun his dark work. The "suspect" generals — those he had not been able or had not dared to corrupt; those who, enjoying some degree of popularity, might eventually have placed themselves at the head of an insurrection — were arrested in their beds. At the same time, every printing-house in the metropolis was seized and placed under seal; a trusted guard was posted at the foot of every belfry. The people of Paris had not yet wakened, and already they had lost every means of issuing any protest whatsoever, of sounding a rallying-cry, of causing a resonant peal of alarm to ring out from on high. Nor was even this to suffice. Louis Napoleon set the compositors of the National Printing-House to work at pistol-point, and forced Paris, forced France, to speak for him, to lend a semblance of legality to his brigandage. From the earliest hours of the morning, the still half-drowsy population of the city was reading at every street corner three documents which have remained famous: the Proclamation to the People, the Proclamation to the Army, and the Decree by which the President of the Republic dissolved the National Assembly, re-established universal suffrage, convened the electoral assemblies, declared a state of siege, and dissolved the Council of State. This last document bore, coupled with the name of "Louis Napoleon Bonaparte," that of "de Morny," Minister of the Interior. Those two men, strangers in the eyes of the Law, bound by blood through the mysterious embraces of adultery, had strengthened the ties of blood by making themselves brothers in crime as well.

That very day the militia cleared the chamber of the National Assembly at bayonet-point, and the Police undertook to disperse the Supreme Court, which had assembled to protest, in deference to the Constitution, against the new act of parricide. The prisons of Mazas were packed with representatives of the People.

But at liberty remained the Deputies of the Left, who, through a misunderstanding, had attended the Assembly only in the smallest numbers. Upon them fell the arduous task of wiping out the outrage, of saving the Republic. Not a printing-press, not arms, not powder; a people stunned, paralysed by a fatal stupefaction; a forest of bayonets on every side, an entire Army Corps in command of the strategic points, a Police force sold to the transgressor. They were obliged, in order to escape being shot, to go in disguise and to change their meeting-place daily. They acted manfully; they knew how to face martyrdom with serenity — a blaze of heroism! But they could not, but they were not able to surmount the greatest obstacle: their own prejudices.

The nightmare of legality dominated them; the spectre of the Constitution shackled their arms, and to give it no jolt — at such a moment! — they accounted greatness.

They were seen to oppose with speeches the drunken soldiery, athirst for blood; to meet lead with dissertations upon the duties of the good citizen. They set forward, with profound conviction, the inviolability of their own persons — as though they were devotees expecting miracles from a relic. Language well suited to the lips of a despot; ridiculous on those of men who possessed not the force to sustain their own pretension.

In brief: indomitable courage, sublime self-abnegation — and a dotage of childishness.

The arid political questions left the masses cold; instinctively, if not with full awareness, they felt their hollowness. People who toiled from dawn to dusk for a meagre subsistence would have shown themselves sensible only to immediate improvements in their material existence. Certainly, universal suffrage interested, to a greater or lesser degree, even a good number of the unlettered, and the Assembly — or rather its remnants — hastened to cancel the error they had committed and to restore it. But Louis Napoleon had forestalled them; their act appeared a forced concession and was derided.

Victor Hugo, in whose vast mind the truth flashed for a single instant, proposed to his colleagues the immediate abolition of the *dazio murato* — the city toll — and of the tax on spirits. For some time the People had been clamouring against those fiscal measures. The great lyric poet obtained that a decree to that effect be drawn up. But it availed nothing. Once again the practical men showed themselves to have shorter sight than the poets. They chose to defer its publication until the morrow of a victory whose coming was problematical, thereby spitting, virtually, upon the most efficacious means of ensuring it. "If the People do not understand," they said, "that we are risking our heads in defence of their rights; if they do not fight, they are unworthy of any concession" — unworthy of "liberty." A folly of haughty and disdainful bearing, but a folly none the less. Perhaps those men who faced death serenely day by day, hour by hour, feared to give the appearance of conjuring up the spectre of the red republic.

One great intellect, having come out of prison by chance, on parole — for he languished therein for an offence against Louis Napoleon — found the means to cast among those perplexed men a note of cold, hard positiveness. I allude to Proudhon.

"You are the victims of your own illusions. The People have allowed themselves to be gulled. They will not stir, or will stir only to assist Bonaparte. A piece of foolishness, the restoration of universal suffrage, has caught on the hook the fools who are always in the majority. The usurper passes for a socialist. He means to be emperor and he will be. It is a crime of brazen effrontery, but brazenness triumphs when it is backed by bayonets. He has on his side the cannon, the popular prejudices, the errors and the unpopularity of the Assembly. Your attempts are destined to fail. You, Deputies of the Left, are honest men; he is a scoundrel — and that too is a great advantage in his favour. You have scruples; he has none. Desist from the struggle. Given the circumstances, much may perhaps be attempted; but nothing obtained. And you will do nothing but drag a thousand, two thousand, ten thousand generous souls into your ruin."

Prophetic words! Yet it was not in their nature to receive them.

The misfortune is that the most logical, the surest, the most expeditious, the least cruel means of paralysing the parricide occurred to no one, or at the least no one dared to speak of it. Louis Napoleon had contrived the Coup d'État; he was the pivot about which the foul conspiracy revolved. It was necessary to strike the pivot; it was necessary to suppress Louis Napoleon. By what strange aberration was the blood of one man who, by perjury, was enslaving the Fatherland to weigh heavier, in the minds of those representatives of the People, than the lives of thousands upon thousands of innocents?

Victor Hugo wrote, from exile, masterly pages upon the terrible power of the *pieuvre* — the octopus. He knew that the blade of a knife slides off the tentacles of the monster, which releases its prey and collapses like a rag the instant the steel turns about the one truly vulnerable point — the mouth that creates the suction and gives force to the suckers. But had a voice arisen in the Assembly bent on rousing the hero Gilliatt against the baleful *pieuvre* of the Empire — he, Victor Hugo, would have been the first to hurl the thunderbolts of his indignant eloquence to denounce "the shame of a vulgar assassination." A great and ingenuous dreamer, having roused the people of Paris to raise barricades, to fight and to kill, he carried his boldness to the point of facing unarmed the enemy's bullets; he carried his sentimentality to the point of rebuking the friend who offered him arms with which to defend himself and to strike. A puerile contradiction of a sublime intellect!

But the horror of blood was not to restrain "the man without scruples." The regiments, the batteries, stationed in the faubourgs, were, by his order, concentrated on the Boulevard Montmartre. To whet the military ardour, ten thousand francs' worth of brandy had been distributed to each brigade. The hordes of passive obedience do not attain the upper reaches of heroism without the fumes of alcohol. As for those in the higher ranks, broad assurances had been given of more considerable epaulettes and well-stuffed envelopes "to meet any eventuality." And for fear that even this might not suffice, the influence of certain high-class *courtisanes* was skilfully brought into play — the *courtisanes* who afterwards formed one of the most conspicuous features of the Second Empire. The soldiers remained for several hours, arms in hand, silent, forcibly motionless, like hounds straining at the leash. Suddenly, starting from no one knows where, fired by no one knows precisely whom, a rifle-shot rang out. It was a signal.

All simultaneously began to kill. It was not arms levelled against arms; it was the pitiless hunt of the innocent citizen who was quietly attending to his own affairs; it was the premeditated slaughter carried out in order to terrorise, out of a lust for massacre. The intoxication of alcohol being soon joined by that of blood, confusion and death spread from the boulevard across an entire district. Foot-soldiers, lancers, cuirassiers advance in close order, cutting down all who are guilty of being abroad. Nor is the absence from the streets a sufficient defence — they fire into the courtyards; through the gratings, down into the cellars; they send volleys of lead through the windows. By degrees, the random killing becomes a monotonous business. How to enliven it? The human beasts make a sport of it; they wager, laughing, on the accuracy of the shot — women and children the targets of the infernal contest. Soldiers were seen to rekindle the mania for slaughter by torturing the dying, and widening their wounds with cold steel.

After the killing by small arms, the cannon. The carnage lasted three hours — a holocaust of several thousand persons. But Louis Napoleon never dared to publish the revealing figures. A cowardly malefactor, having struck his victims to death, he turns livid at the sight of the enormous quantity of corpses and takes every care to spirit them away from his own eyes no less than from those of others.

Night fell. The heart of Paris became entangled with barricades; but the People are almost annihilated by terror and do not stir. Standing to defend them, unarmed and hopeless of victory, are a handful of virile consciences — an ephemeral obstacle to the tyrant's artillery.

The horrible tragedy is repeated for twelve consecutive nights, at the Champ de Mars, in the courtyard of the Prefecture, in the adjacent Rue de Jérusalem. The slaughter is carried out at the mouth of the sewer so that the human blood, flowing into it, may leave scant traces and not reveal the enormity of the hecatomb.

Then came the persecutions sanctioned by Law. What Law? Louis Napoleon creates the Commissions Militaires and the Commissions Mixtes. It will suffice to indicate the manner of functioning of the former. They sit behind closed doors. They receive from above a list of names, which they run through — the accused being absent — and against each name they affix, without further inquiry, a mark which, according to the case, has the power to acquit, or to send some wretch to Lambèse, or, worse, to the arid guillotine of Cayenne.

Three hundred and thirty-seven are thus taken all at once from the dungeons of the Tuileries, led between a triple file of soldiers to the Champ de Mars, struck down, and buried that same night in the cemetery of Montmartre, with their heads protruding from the earth — a grim expedient of identification.

The prisons overflow with the wretched. How to make room for the throngs that keep pouring in? The answer that came from above is brief and terrible: "Kill them." In the night they are brusquely roused and chosen at random. Neither their name is asked, nor whence they come, nor what position they occupy in civil society — what matters is to reach the fatally established number. Thus the Commissions Militaires and the Commissions Mixtes plunge forty thousand families into mourning! By such deeds does one acquire the sceptre "by divine right!"

And Louis Napoleon announced to Europe that he had — saved "the social order." Blessed, with craven gratitude, by Pius IX, he set forth, from so glorious a beginning, dragging France behind him, toward the dark abyss of Sedan.

---
*Chapter Ten*


### Chapter Ten

<!-- pages:53-58 -->

Reading through the translation carefully against the Italian and the Edgren dictionary entries, the rendering is generally accurate and period-appropriate throughout. One targeted revision is warranted:

---



Having abandoned the barricade to its fate, Trolli and I made our way toward the boulevard. But scarcely had we crossed the Pont Saint-Denis when we were compelled to agree that Martial Law was being rigorously applied, and that the surest way to invite death was to walk as a pair. We found corpses scattered here and there. I can still vividly recall one — a water-seller, lying face-down, his vessel upon his back, his arms outstretched, his head literally shattered to fragments. Blood, brains, and slices of lemon lay mingled in a horrible confusion.

We separated, having agreed not to lose sight of one another and to leave the city by the Barrière du Trône. To pass through it, guarded as it was by a good body of customs officers and soldiers, was a rather awkward undertaking. Before reaching it, I purchased a large loaf of bread and tucked it under my arm. Trolli saw the purpose at a glance and did likewise. As workmen returning from their labour we were scrutinised from head to foot, but suffered no molestation.

And when darkness fell inexorably over the bare countryside, we rejoined one another. Trolli asked me where we should go to lay our heads.

"We have a half — but we are free! Where shall we lay our heads? What does it matter to a wayfarer where, so long as it be not within the lunette of the guillotine? Switzerland is far. But we have known how to cross Spain — we shall cross France as well!"

We had been walking for some miles when a traveller overtook us in a small carriage. Catching sight of us, he drew up his reins, and once the horse had resumed its trot, the worthy fellow invited us to climb up. I cast an appraising glance at him. He was decently dressed and had the air of a good-natured, unassuming countryman. His nose and eyes were exceedingly red, and not solely from the cold — he could not have been a spy. And besides — he alone, and we two, possessed of nerve enough. We accepted.

Scarcely had the horse resumed its trot when the worthy fellow asked us where we were bound.

"To Troy."

"A long walk! I am not going so far myself; but I shall shorten your road."

And he proceeded to give an account of himself and his affairs. He had gone up to Paris to sell the vegetables — the produce of his own little garden and his own labours. And he showed us his calloused hands. The harvests had been rather lean; but with a little thrift and a sharp eye, his rabbit and his little wine had never failed him. And he ran on, reeling off the long litany of his amours with the goodwives of the neighbourhood. Of Paris, of the coup d'état, of the horrors of that day — not a phrase, not a word; as though he had left behind him a city perfectly at peace and silent. The wine he had swallowed made him talkative, but not enough to draw him from a prudent reserve.

At a certain point something slipped out regarding certain speculations of his, and he concluded that if they proceeded according to his hopes, he would have enough to live comfortably upon until the "Lady without a Nose" came along to deliver him the fatal scythe-blow. And with that allusion to Death he fell silent, as if absorbed in deep meditation. Ten minutes later, he was snoring.

I then took the reins, and laid on the whip — trot, horse, trot. We passed broad stretches of land, we passed thickets, we passed villages. Dusk came on, night fell — and the peasant was snoring still.

All at once, in the still air, a brazen bell-stroke rang out slowly. It was eleven o'clock. Shortly after, the wheels of the carriage were grinding over cobblestones. And then the horse stopped dead, and for all my flogging it would not move a step forward nor a step backward.

The strange thing was that this abrupt halt had the power to break the peasant's deep sleep. "Sacré cœur!" he exclaimed, after boring his eyes into the darkness. "Where have you brought me? Twelve miles from home!"

He takes out his anger — mark the justice of it! — on the horse, which ought surely to have remembered where its manger was. But in the end he consoles himself with the reflection that we were not in a wilderness. In the village he had good friends. "Do you see that strip of light? The inn is still open, and the innkeeper is an honest man. I shall stand you a drink!"

Half an hour later he went to bed so as to be able to set off betimes. Trolli and I stepped out into the open. Warmed within and without, we faced once more the darkness of the countryside, and that yet deeper darkness of the unknown.

Our subversive Society enmeshed the whole of France in a network whose threads came together again at Troy, so that we found no great difficulty in making contacts there and obtaining passage on the stage-coach as far as Langres — the place where, the meshes drawing together, they formed the next knot.

The horses were still champing under the gateway of the inn at Langres, and already I was making my way in haste to the dwelling of the postal official. A letter of introduction, a few conventional signs, and he would have furnished us the means to continue our journey. I pulled the bell, and immediately there appeared upon the threshold a lady of uncertain age, yet not without beauty.

"My husband is not at home, nor do I know when he will return."

"I am the bearer of a letter which I should like to deliver to him personally."

The lady's mouth contracted, as though between tongue and palate she were pressing a slice of lemon. She became inquisitive: "A letter? From whom? Give it here, give it here! I will undertake to deliver it to him — in his very own hands."

"I beg your pardon! I have my orders, and —"

Her face had gone pale. She insisted and extended her right hand — her fingers were trembling. All the more did I consider caution my duty. She went back inside, slamming the door.

That evening I was seated in the common room of the inn, before the fireplace, conversing cheerfully with the other guests, when — to delay the sweet process of digestion — there appeared, unannounced, the sharp-tempered creature with a face at once sour and melancholy. She had two gendarmes at her back.

The purpose of the visit was made plain at once. The lady demanded the surrender of the mysterious letter. The gendarmes had come to confiscate it.

I rose, drew out my pocket-book calmly and, removing the letter of introduction from it, drew the company's attention to the fact that it lay in an open envelope. Then, with a leap toward the fireplace, I cast it upon the flames.

A little confusion ensued. The gendarmes, considering their "dignity in uniform" offended, wished to proceed to my arrest — the more so as the other guests had been unable to restrain themselves from laughing uproariously. I was obliged to protest with all my energy. The very Laws of the Republic specified that only sealed letters were subject to confiscation; I had therefore, and quite rightfully, escaped an act of unlawful coercion.

In truth, the two worthies had placed themselves upon false ground: the Law was against its own defenders; moreover, their orders had been to confiscate the document, not to arrest its bearer. "We shall go for more precise instructions." They withdrew, followed by the lady, crestfallen and almost in tears; and I saw them no more.

Late in the evening, the postal official himself appeared, and I received the key to the enigma. His wife, an honest woman and possessed of many virtues, had the weakness of being jealous to the point of madness. This passion of hers had of late been inflamed by the mysterious coming and going attendant upon our political Society, of whose existence she was entirely ignorant. Having fixed it in her head that I was bringing her husband the secret missive of some rival, she had taken this rash step to intercept proof of offences that existed only in her imagination.

But once past the Swiss frontier, our secret network of influence was of no further use to us, and from that point began the truly arduous part of our pilgrimage. Trolli had written to his family to send money to Berna. We had to trudge all the way there — our pockets desperately empty, in the dead of winter, the snow lying deep, barely clad against the cold.

We arrived. But the chill of free Helvetia almost crystallised, like a fringe of ice, around our very hearts. As had happened at Barcelona, so now at Berna — the money was nowhere to be found.

Night fell inexorably. We lay down in the market square, beneath a vegetable-seller's stall. Despite hunger and bitter cold, we fell into a deep sleep. Blessed be youth!

But at a certain hour of the night I was startled awake by an imperious voice cursing in German. The thing was perfectly simple. Trolli was endowed with legs that put one in mind of a battering-ram; while sleeping, he had stretched them out as far as the pavement, and was, quite involuntarily, tripping a watchman who happened to be passing that way. He was dragged, like a sack of rags, out of his hiding-place and pelted with questions. But he did not understand a single word of German, and for all answer he yawned and stretched himself. The policeman finally lost patience, seized him by the collar and drove him forward with curses and shoves. I heard the heavy double footfall fade into the distance; then I drew my blouse tightly about my chest and fell asleep again. What good would it have done to intervene?

At daybreak I wandered around the solid walls of the prison, whistling a little *cabaletta* well known to Trolli. A brief whistle of recognition was returned to me, and immediately, framed between the bars of a small window, appeared the face of my Cardinal.

Farewell to circumspection! I waved my cap furiously and shouted my greeting with the vivacity of a schoolboy tumbling out of a stuffy classroom. Nor did Trolli answer with any less enthusiasm. And then and there he let me know that, in deference to the Law on vagabondage, the Police intended to have him escorted to the Italian frontier. "Do you promise to follow after me?"

I had barely time to stretch out my hand in solemn pledge.

A warder, hearing the commotion, had seized Trolli by his dangling long legs, and the cardinal face vanished.

That day I wandered long about the market-place, snatching a turnip here and there, and managed in this way to still the cramps in my stomach. Night having fallen again, I found shelter, like Diogenes, in a barrel. My sufferings were such that, at dawn, I very nearly resolved to give myself up to the Police.

But it was precisely from that extremity that chance held out to me, in the strangest fashion, the thread that gave me the opportunity to rise again. I had sunk down upon a bench in the Public Gardens and was chewing, by way of self-deception, the stem of my pipe — my stock of tobacco had long since been exhausted. The pathways were deserted; snow blanketed the green spaces, and upon it a flock of sparrows chirped and hopped. Despite the rigours of winter, they had breakfasted.

Nettled, I seized a small pebble, and through those happy creatures there flashed, like a thunderbolt, the characteristic protest of empty stomachs. A sparrow gave a little cry, turned its tiny feet up in the air, and lay stone dead.

This was no moment for hesitation. In such circumstances man eats man — I could well adapt myself to devouring a raw sparrow. I was plucking it when I felt a hand laid upon my shoulder.

"What are you doing, young man?"

"Having a snack."

"You must have the hunger of a hawk. In any case, praise divine Providence for having contrived to put an end to your fast."

Beside me sat a neat little old man of respectable bourgeois appearance — a Protestant minister, in all likelihood. I observed to him that the Lord God had provided for my needs by means of the slaughter of the innocent, and in a manner too derisory for me to feel my heart overflowing with gratitude.

He jangled some silver coins in his pocket. After a brief silence: "Would you sell me your pipe for six francs?"

I pocketed the money; the stranger pocketed the pipe. Then he began: "You see that our Heavenly Father —"

One can imagine whether I cared to waste my time in philosophising. I was off in a bound, four bounds; nor did I give myself any rest until I found myself seated where the sweet fragrance of roasting meat hung upon the air.

With what remained, I was able to make my way to Lucerna, where the twofold problem of feeding myself and continuing my journey was to be resolved, on my behalf, by a man whom I had fought against — Pope Mastai Ferretti.

Switzerland is a marvellous country. Drawn together by its beauties, upon those mountain-slopes, among those winding valleys, peoples different in origin, in language, in character live together in peace — a miracle of Liberty, a glorious foretaste of a holy Utopia.

And yet those peoples have not shown the greatness of soul necessary to embrace the full measure of their historic mission. The heart of Europe, in ages gone by, beat mightily among the Alps; but in vain! Just as from the snow-white crests the waters plunge down to furrow the fertile plains and water and fecundate so great a part of the Continent, so from those crags inaccessible to the conqueror should have descended the bright and terrible battalions, to perform the work of brotherhood, to rekindle the hopes of the oppressed, to extend the dominion of Liberty.

They did descend; but as a devastating scourge — the blind instrument of the wicked, flesh and souls alike put up for sale!

Selfishness is fatal to nations because, among other things, the corrupted air of thy neighbour sooner or later poisons thine own as well, undermines and diminishes thy glory. The despot, defended by his Swiss mercenaries, confined within the narrow circle of the mountains the very men who had nourished him; the Republic remained a minuscule entity, and in the universal estimation became synonymous with venality.

At the time when I was crossing it, this ill custom was not very intense, and already the Federal Government was branding as a criminal both the citizen who did not scruple to prostitute himself to a foreign Government, and the go-between who recruited him and made himself an accomplice. Nevertheless Pius IX, who mistrusted his own Catholics, maintained secret agents in the Lutheran Cantons, gathering by dint of *baiocchi* the handful of vermin destined, in his heart of hearts, to alter the course of the stars, to annihilate the hopes of the new Italy.

One such agent caught sight of me upon the lake-shore, absorbed in my twofold problem. Attracted perhaps by my soldierly bearing, he opened conversation in the holy name of God, and concluded it by formally proposing that I take up the musket in defence of the pontifical throne and the altar.

My first impulse was to hurl him headlong into the lake. But a sudden thought restrained me. The Garibaldian tactic! — when one has no ammunition, one takes it from the enemy. The money of the Holy See to repatriate an adversary of the sacred old order — my twofold problem more than solved! And, incidentally, I might also have done some good to the cause of my Country, by unmasking the intrigue and clipping the nails of the unctuous go-between.

I smiled and, passing myself off as a Swiss from the Canton of Ticino, I accepted.

I was conducted, through tortuous alleyways, to a house of wretched appearance; I impressed its number well upon my memory, mounted a dirty little staircase, and entered a squalid room of amphibious character — something between a study and a bedchamber. The contract was duly drawn up and signed, and a few hours later, from the stern of the steamer that was swiftly making for open water, I was waving with large gestures to my recruiter.

Arrived at Fluelen, I presented myself at the Commissioner's office and related, point by point, all that had occurred. I was taken back to Lucerna for the identification of the gentleman in question. I retraced the tortuous alleyways, found again the house of less than modest appearance, remounted the dirty little staircase. The blackbird was in his cage — indeed, in his bed; the proof was manifest, the Law precise. He was arrested.

While he was dressing, the Federal Delegate drew out a purse and attempted to count out for me I know not how much money. I felt a flush of colour rise to my face and burst out: "Italians are ever ready to face with serene spirit whatever sacrifice is demanded of them, including that of taking on the appearance of a scoundrel. For when it is a matter of serving the Fatherland and the lion's leap is not possible, well may the cunning of the fox be welcomed and blessed. But learn this and let it serve you as a rule: if you find one who, having made his sacrifice, holds out his hand and demands a reward for it, you may say with certainty that he was not born beyond your Alps — or, if he was born there, hold it for certain that he is of bastard stock, unworthy of the name he bears!"

I was taken back to Fluelen, and then, quite alone, I set out for Goeschenen, whence, braving ice and blizzard, I reached the summit of the San Gottardo. From up there, something passed over my face like a foretaste of southern warmth — an air laden with tremors and with sobs, with hatred and with love.

And I came down almost at a run, my heart full — I could not have said myself whether of joy or of grief.

At Varese I was received by Trolli as a dear departed one miraculously restored to life. And in the hospitality of that family I found ample recompense for my hardships. But in Lombardia the Austrian reaction was raging; and yet the sight of that hated uniform would rekindle my fever — and at that age, with my temperament, it is far too difficult to bridle one's impetuosity. Friends who were privy to the secret affairs of the cause discreetly urged a word of counsel. I must not compromise my hosts, and I had myself escorted to the frontier.

Having taken leave of my escort and remaining alone, I turned to gaze upon the beautiful and ill-starred Land which, for the second time, I was compelled to leave. In the majesty of the place and of the silence, I went over in my mind the events that had passed — the desperate onrush of the few, armed with their right, against the dense masses bristling with bayonets; the gaze of the innocent turned in vain toward heaven; the silent tears, the streams of blood. And in the exaltation of that moment, my thoughts sped from the feet of the Alps, onward and onward, to the uttermost Sicily. Everywhere hearts that were trembling, hands that clasped one another in the shadows, that in the shadows honed their blades. Then the great soul of that noble race — strong and unhappy — seemed to penetrate me and magnify my own. I stretched out my arms as though I expected to clasp within them, tenderly, all, all my companions in bondage. I renewed the inspired oath of Giuseppe Mazzini, and, with tears upon my face, took once more the road of exile.


### Chapter Eleven

<!-- pages:58-62 -->



IN APRIL of 1852 I was once again in Genoa, working for a certain Ferrari, a merchant tailor, when I was informed that Don Bastiano Barozzi, rector of San Pietro in Campo, was living as a refugee in Turin.

Barozzi, during the siege of Venice, had served as chaplain to a Legion composed almost exclusively of students from Padua.
