He was one of those priests whose cassock did not stifle his love of the Fatherland, and who, when his side was defeated, preferred to follow the impulses of his own conscience rather than submit passively. Italy counts many such men among her sons, and the Church of Rome might have drawn great prestige from them, had she not preferred — in order to please the powerful — to hear their confessions, and even lend a hand in their punishment.

Deeply versed in ancient history, Don Bastiano judged men through the prism of the ancient world, and would have wished to raise our entire race to the level of Atilius Regulus, the Scipios, the Gracchi. During my school holidays, as we walked along the Ardo, he would, without making a show of it, fill my mind with heroic episodes. I believe I owe him, in no small part, my love of Liberty.

Those priests of murky conscience, who dared not wage open war upon him for his patriotic principles, dragged in the Decretals and turned their attack upon his moustache and goatee, which smacked to them of modernism. He stubbornly refused to have them shaved off, and lost the right to say Mass as a consequence. In his straitened circumstances, he was assisted by my father, and that alone had been enough for Don Bastiano to preserve a gratitude towards him that bordered upon veneration.

It was to this man I resolved to turn, in the hope of re-entering, with his help, the vortex of action. Thinking that two words spoken face to face are worth more than four long letters, I set out for Turin — on foot, so as not to lose the habit.

My first care upon arriving was to make my way to the Caffè Nazionale, the favourite gathering place of political refugees. But before I reached it, I caught sight of a gentleman standing before a theatre bill, his face so close to the printed sheet that he seemed to be reading it with the tip of his nose. Don Bastiano Barozzi's extreme short-sightedness was well known to me, and that single detail was enough to reveal him.

His astonishment at finding me again was great; his joy was boundless. He could not contain it, and there and then, without the slightest regard for the passers-by, he planted two resounding kisses upon my cheeks: "There — that one is from me; and that one is from your father!" He then asked what good wind had brought me to Turin. And I replied:

"Don Bastiano, you know that the di Rudios are no idlers. What is being planned, what is being prepared? I have come so as not to grow rusty, and I am counting on you."

He lowered his eyes and did not answer. But after a brief silence, as though concluding some train of thought: "Good Calvi is here. Four steps and we're at his house. Come!"

Fortunato Calvi — the glorious organiser of the Cacciatori delle Alpi, my Colonel, the intimate friend of my family — received me with his arms folded, without moving a step, with an expression that was almost one of disdain. The flood of feeling that was about to burst forth remained painfully compressed within my breast. I did not even dare provoke an explanation. But the explanation came of its own accord.

Calvi had been informed by a person "worthy of trust" that I, having left Venice on his orders, had instead of proceeding to Chioggia gone over, arms and baggage, to the Austrians. He even cited the name of one who had sworn to have seen me, behind the trenches, firing upon my own countrymen.

Slander! — what man has not been, to some degree, its victim? Little by little, one's breast grows hardened to its blows, so that in time they no longer succeed in raising any tempest within me. I have known bold men who, by their very bearing, almost provoke it deliberately, for the bitter pleasure of showing themselves superior to it. But that was the first time I had come face to face with it, and its bite struck me as cruelly sharp.

Yet it proved easy enough to demonstrate the absurdity of the charge.

Fortunato Calvi, who had no less desire to find me innocent than I had to prove it to him, extended his right hand with a smile, which I grasped with feverish warmth. Meanwhile Don Bastiano, the silent witness of that scene — a man of exceptional sensibility — turned on his heel to conceal a tear.

The consequences of that meeting were, as far as I was concerned, of no small moment. Giuseppe Mazzini, Luigi Kossuth, and Ledru-Rollin had openly founded in London a Revolutionary European Committee, to which innumerable affiliated branches soon attached themselves. Every people that, having overcome the baleful apathy which spreads like a fungus through the spirit, understood that regeneration springs from action and sacrifice, organised its own branches. Italy was a hive of them, and by force of circumstances the Committee of Turin held sway over all the others of ours. To it fell above all the task of inflaming the spirit of rebellion that was spreading through the Peninsula, and of coordinating its movements. Its head in 1852 was Count Grillanzoni; but the command of eventual military operations fell to Fortunato Calvi.

I received from Calvi and from Count Grillanzoni the charge of travelling, as a special envoy, through the principal cities of Lombardy and Venetia — first to distribute subversive manifestos and subscription certificates for the Mazzinian national loan, and then, on a subsequent journey, to inspect the various garrisons and report precise data on their strength, their nationality, and their mood.

The system adopted was a simple one. It was arranged that I should be furnished with a passport in the name of Carlo Moretti, a merchant in silks. I had only to write: In such and such a square I sold two, three, four bales of white silk, or green, or yellow. The number of bales corresponded to that of the Regiments; the colour indicated their uniform and branch of service. A carefully worded allusion to the particular quality of the goods, or to the nature of the client, was sufficient to convey whatever other circumstances I judged it necessary to report.

I remember the admonition given me by Calvi on the very day of my departure. I was concealing in the bowl of my pipe the list of the various Sub-Committees, and I was smiling. Calvi, suspecting that the smile arose from levity, said sternly: "Do not deceive yourself, young man! A slight imprudence, a simple chance incident, and you would be hanged!" I never fell into the hangman's hands, but I passed more than once within close reach of them.

My first stop was at Stradella, where I shook the hand of Nino Bixio, who had survived the wound received at Villa Corsini; and of that Winkler who had been commander, in Venice, of a Hungarian legion, and who was, a short time afterwards, to close his heroic existence fighting on the banks of the Volturno. The company of men of such temper was a source of keen excitement to me. But once I had crossed the blue Ticino with the help of a smuggler and advanced but a little way into Austrian territory, it seemed as though an iron hand had seized me by the throat. The oppressor had, after 1849, sharpened his sluggish wits to transform those lands into a pit of hell.

As a general rule, Austria regarded the provinces subdued by her own arms, and those reconquered by the allied arms of Russia, as a den of brigands. To vent upon them a barbaric hatred, to indulge the most atrocious acts of vengeance — this seemed, in courtly circles, the height of political wisdom. Persuaded that he was fulfilling a mission entrusted to him by God, Francesco Giuseppe did not even consider himself bound by his own word or by the terms of surrender that had been agreed upon. General Klapka, the heroic defender of Komorn, had obtained excellent conditions of capitulation for his garrison. Every one of them was violated. A code of honour exists even among thieves; but under the pretext of *ragion di Stato* — reasons of State — none whatsoever existed for Francesco Giuseppe.

Of the thirty-four Hungarian generals — men of select intelligence and heroic fibre, who had repeatedly compelled the Imperial soldiers to show their backs — three were condemned to life imprisonment, twelve were duly shot or hanged. And the slaughter would have been greater still, had not the others saved themselves by flight and exile. For several months a swarm of executioners, clad, as if in greater mockery, in the colour of hope, busied themselves in Hungary heaping up the corpses of patriots. All this seems to have been erased by now from the memory of those men who cry their hurrahs in the streets for that old and contemptible wretch. And yet there is no end to the imprecations heaped upon the generous souls who, having taken into their own hearts the anguish of the victims, risk their lives to administer to despots a small dose of their own medicine.

In Italy, to tell the truth, Francesco Giuseppe had his claws blunted by Vittorio Emanuele II, who, ascending the throne amid the glacial distrust of his subjects, knew how to bend to the terrible necessities pressing upon him without compromising the future. He refused to sign the peace treaty with Austria unless she undertook the obligation of granting full amnesty to the hundred thousand Italians who, from Lombardy and Venetia, had taken refuge in Piedmont — whether to make war upon her, or to escape her cruelties.

Vittorio Emanuele II, though defeated, held a by no means negligible position of strength, and the Emperor kept his word. Yet his "pardon" was tacitly conditional upon complete submission to the Austrian yoke. Were pretexts lacking? Sequestrations, arbitrary impositions, chains and the gallows were not dispensed sparingly by the paternal solicitude of Francesco Giuseppe, and the rod was applied to the backs even of those — men or women — who, without openly rebelling, gave expression to sentiments of any virile character.

Poor young Italian women, your souls open to all that is beautiful and gentle; and you mothers, aristocratic or of the people, who passed with commanded slowness between two rows of ruffians bringing their rods down with all their force upon your naked flesh — lend me your voice, that it may suffice for a worthy *apoteosi* — a fitting glorification — of the apostolic beast!

And it is of Francesco Giuseppe that I speak deliberately, and not of Austria; for when the sum of power is concentrated in one man, upon his head, as a debt of justice, must be concentrated equally the sum of hatred and of infamy.

Mingled among the Italians, I found in my wanderings a number of Hungarian officers who had been degraded for having loved their country and defended it. Harassed with ferocious persistence, they had nonetheless the courage to rebel against the hateful commands issued to the detriment of a people made brothers by common misfortune. And there were among them those who, despairing of better times, put an end by suicide to a life that had become unbearable.

Fortunately — almost as if to disappoint the prophets of doom — Nature has so arranged her affairs that death itself is the great sower of life, and tyranny itself the great fertiliser of every liberty.

Notwithstanding the defeats and the blood drained from it and the persecutions, life was pulsing through our Fatherland — underground, but intensely. Italians who were organising themselves, who were conspiring, or who gave open sign of their feverish impatience to do so — these I found in abundance in every centre. The logical conclusion to be drawn from the terrible situation was that one must begin again from the beginning, and do better, and conquer at whatever cost.


### Chapter Twelve

<!-- pages:62-66 -->



Of my excursions, which extended, on two separate occasions, as far as remote Udine, I shall recount, in brief, some episodes.

Having eluded the police at Milan, at Como, at Bergamo, at Brescia, I was approaching Mantova with a great confidence of spirit — I was sustained by the certainty that not a living soul there could know me. But at the very moment I was passing through the toll-gate, violent as a blow to the chest, my full name and surname rang out.

In that instant, I believed myself discovered and lost. But the man who had called to me from above wore a smiling face, and his right hand reached out to welcome mine. It was the very chief of the guards. How he knew me I cannot now recall; I believe he was a patriot well versed in secret matters.

When he learned that I was not come to Mantova merely to take the air, his face clouded over. Only the day before, the police had carried out several arrests, and the reason for them remained unknown; he perceived a connection between those unexpected severities and my arrival. He counselled me to take refuge immediately in his house and to remain there shut in. He would endeavour to sound out the ground, and in the evening, over a bite to eat, we would deliberate on what was to be done.

Provoked by the conspiracy in which the gentle figure of the priest Tazzoli held a foremost place, there was beginning at that very time, in Mantova, the series of refined tortures that drove to an inhuman death the glorious little band of the martyrs of Belfiore. But on his return late that evening, my host could bring me nothing save vague tidings — along with them, however, came a breath of the terror that hung over the city. He had not encountered a single brother who felt sure of being able to pass the night in his own bed.

I lay down in the attic as best I could, without undressing — prepared, at the slightest alarm, to attempt escape across the rooftops.

Early the following morning, after a long conversation with my host, I learned that among the garrison there was an officer who had been trained at the Military Academy of Milan and had been my fellow pupil there: the bonds of early youth do not crumple away with the passing of the years. Half an hour later I was in the Castello, exchanging a brotherly embrace, or thereabouts. I was under the protection of the double-headed eagle.

To account for my presence, I gave it out that I was making for Belluno to attend my father, who was failing with age — I had made a detour as far as Mantova expressly to see my old friend again; to evoke, in his company, the years of happy carelessness. As if by tacit agreement, we were both careful to avoid any subject of a political nature; but amid the modest festivities organised in my honour, I never lost sight of the purpose of my journey, and gathered, in snatches and by degrees, all the information I was seeking. Two hours before sunset the officer himself courteously offered to accompany me by carriage beyond the city walls. And under his aegis I passed through the toll-gate, unmolested, saluted with a military greeting.

Having escaped one danger that was in all probability imaginary, however, I was compelled to face a danger all too real, at Verona.

I had taken lodging at the *Scudo di Francia*, and no sooner had I shut myself inside my room than I made, by instinct, a brief survey of it. It was situated on the third floor; the single window gave onto a courtyard. Directly opposite stood the rear wall of another hotel, that of the *Regina d'Inghilterra*. Craning my face downward, I made out a broad sloping roof that declined toward the centre of the yard. A smell of stables rose up to that height; I heard the stamping of a horse.

I sat down at the writing-table, beside the lit fireplace, and began to write my report on Mantova. All at once, measured footsteps rang out in the corridor. I blew out the candle and cast my letter into the fire; it carbonised and flew up through the flue.

There came a knock at the door, and an obsequious voice — that of a servant: "Signor Moretti, if you please!" Then, after a brief silence, another voice, gruff and imperious: "In the name of the Law, open!"

I had climbed over the parapet and was hanging there with arms fully extended, to diminish the distance that separated me from the roof below. The gendarmes began to batter the door with their rifle-butts. I released my grip and let myself drop, scraping the wall. The impact was rough, but the damage fell to the tiles. I slid along them, and reaching the edge, made use of a projecting beam and of what gymnastics my bruised limbs would allow, to put the gutter between myself and the constables. Crouching beneath it, I heard the door of my room give way with a crash — then a rush of hurried footsteps — then silence — then a ringing voice: "Sergeant, I'll wager he jumped from the window!" And the sergeant, who knew a thing or two: "Blockhead! One does not jump from a third floor!"

I believe they did not even go to the window-ledge. The courtyard was deserted. Through the half-open door of the stable, a strip of light fell across the ground. It revealed to me a heap of manure. I jumped into it.

A moment later, I walked boldly into the kitchen of the *Regina d'Inghilterra*. Scullions and servants, busy at their work, did not so much as lift their heads. A cook, his great belly swathed in a white apron, the white cap upon his head, was at that moment blowing on his ladle — he stared at me in astonishment; but before he had recovered from his surprise I was already passing through the door of the dining room. The waiters presumably took me for some scullion's acquaintance. Within seconds I found myself in the open air.

Where to pass the night? The police, rendered all the more suspicious by my disappearance, would not have allowed me to find rest in any hotel. I drifted aimlessly about until I found myself facing the Post. Several diligences stood there in a row with their doors thrown open — into one of them I flung myself at full length and lay there huddled until the dawn came. Several times, between sleep and waking, I heard the night-watch pass at my feet. Had I snored, in all probability I should not be here to recount that adventure of mine.

An even more devilish danger I brought upon myself, through excess of zeal, on my return to Milan. On the advice of a certain Valentini, a student of chemistry, I was lodging, during my stay in the Lombard capital, at the house of his sweetheart, a woman who made her living from a dairy shop she kept and from taking in lodgers. At her table there dined several customs guards and a Hungarian sergeant who was an obstinate tormentor of the pianoforte. I felt as though I were entrenched within the enemy's own camp.

The dairywoman, a fleshy creature with the face of a fresh *panettone*, was of the most Italian sympathies — so much so that, on my previous visit, I had entrusted to her, for destruction, a packet of revolutionary pamphlets that had become superfluous. Now she informed me that she had kept it, intact, at the bottom of a chest of drawers. I was glad of it: before returning to Piedmont, I might, by a thorough posting-up, give the finishing touch to my work.

I spoke of it to the Revolutionary Committee which, highly irritable in those days on account of the wretched news arriving from Mantova, approved my intention. And among those patriots I found one who, of his own accord, offered to assist me — a stocky workman with a broad scar upon his face. He did not tell me his name; he was an upholsterer, like the poor Antonio Sciesa.

A cold, rainy night — the streets deserted. We began at the locality known as the Carrobbio; I on one pavement, he on the other. We were pasting up our proclamations with those little wafers which the Milanese call *obiaditt*. We were making our way toward the Duomo, and already I could see the façade of that magnificent temple gleaming white, when from the other side of the piazza — precisely beneath the *Coperchio dei Figini* — there rang out in the silence that terrible warning: "*Scappa, scappa!*" — Run, run!

I saw the stocky figure of the upholsterer take to his heels toward the piazza Mercanti, with a police patrol that had emerged from some dark passageway in hot pursuit, shouting after him. Had it not been for his brotherly solicitude, I might perhaps have slipped away without drawing attention; but by hurling that cry at me he had also alerted the constables, a good number of whom advanced at a run in my direction.

There was no alternative. I flung down the pamphlets and fled headlong through a labyrinth of narrow streets, until, breathless, I found the line of the city walls rising sheer before me. I climbed the ramp on all fours; but with my pursuers at my heels and a toll-gate, with its respective guard-post, to right and to left, I could consider myself in a trap. I took temporary shelter behind one of the great horse-chestnuts, and for a moment I flattered myself that, uncertain of the direction I had taken, the wretches would turn back discomfited, or that, mounting the rampart, they would all bear to the right, leaving me free to slip away silently to the left. But they were not so simple.

Upon reaching the top they divided into two groups which, turning their backs on each other, advanced simultaneously from both sides, peering carefully behind every tree. As at Verona, I found myself faced with the terrible alternative: either to submit, at some later time, to that little leap which, by virtue of a halter, admits of no remedy — or to confront the immediate perils of a great leap that at least left me some percentage of escape. I resolved upon the great leap.

How long I lay stunned at the bottom I was never, in truth, able to say. Fortunately I had fallen on soft ground, and perhaps it was the rain — which fell as if being poured directly upon me — that helped me to my feet again. I set out, painfully and soaked to the bone, toward Pavia.

In what state I arrived there is not difficult to imagine. Three days under the bedclothes and I was in good order again. The brother of the *Giovine Italia* who had tended me with such affection would have wished me to stay longer; but the land of safety was too near for me not to feel a fever of longing to set foot on it once more. He accompanied me as far as the bridge that crosses the Ticino and joins the Lombard bank to the Piedmontese, and there we parted.

And then — two, three, a whole crowd of frantic figures came running noisily to the mouth of the bridge. From the phrases they exchanged in loud voices, I understood that an unknown man — some smuggler or hot-head — pursued by a patrol, had thrown himself from the parapet. They had hit him in flight with a gunshot in the back, and were now endeavouring to fish him out.

I gave the place a wide berth and, passing myself off as a deserter, found the means to get myself ferried across, arriving at Stradella by nightfall. Casa Winkler was still lit up. I entered and found before me a group of patriots who received me as though I had dropped from the clouds. A secret message arrived from Pavia had brought the news that I had been discovered, pursued, and mortally wounded while seeking escape by leaping into the Ticino. Fortunato Calvi had already been informed by telegraph.

I set out immediately and found Calvi still in bed. No sooner had he recovered from his astonishment than his first thought was for Don Bastiano Barozzi, with whom he had already made an appointment for nine o'clock at the Caffè del Pantheon. The good priest entered it pale and nearly beside himself. His short-sightedness prevented him from seeing me, and Calvi went to meet him. At the sight of him, the priest exclaimed: "Ah, what a calamity! What will my benefactor say when he learns that I repaid him by driving his son to ruin?"

— And what would you say, if Carlo were to walk in here, hale and smiling?

The face of Barozzi was convulsed. All at once the knot that choked him loosened, and he burst into tears.

Then the atheist flung his arms around the neck of the priest. Which goes to show that the hypothesis of God is not necessary for men to love one another as brothers.


### Chapter Thirteen

<!-- pages:66-74 -->



I KNOW of no Italian insurrectionary movement that brought Giuseppe Mazzini so many slanders, so much bitterness, and so many defections as that of 1853.

The so-called practical men — possessed of that short-sighted positivism which consists in letting the world run undisturbed, even to its own ruin — maintained that a people recently bled dry and broken could not respond with spirit to a call to arms, nor willingly submit to fresh sacrifices. And they pronounced, with perfect gravity, that revolutions come about when they ripen of their own accord, not when one wills them to.

A singular maxim which, carried to its conclusion, would amount to this: "For sons ill-disposed to win their own independence, to reclaim their own rights, nothing is better than to recline in an armchair, leaving to the Sun the care of ripening events, as it ripens gourds."

The movements organised by Giuseppe Mazzini were never true revolution, nor outright war; but they undoubtedly prepared the way for both, and rendered both necessary.

Moreover, the events of February 1853 were not set in motion by the great agitator himself, but by that very People who, if one were to credit the serious-minded men, would have had no thought beyond making themselves comfortable amid accomplished facts and their own misery.

And Mazzini said as much, and wrote it, and proved it. But even today there is no shortage of supermen, the lustre and ornament of our Italy, who, being unable to refute the declarations of that great man who never lied, leap nimbly over them with both feet and continue to extol the portents of the armchair, persisting in the old slanders.

The *Fratellanza* — a secret society bent on insurrection — had already been strongly organised in Milan, three years after every hope for Italy seemed to have withered. Composed of some thousands of workmen, it was divided into various sections distinguished by the letters of the alphabet. Its existence was entirely unknown to the Austrian Police, to Mazzini, to the intellectuals, and to the aristocratic class, which nevertheless shared with it a common hatred of the foreign yoke.

Its principal organiser, the dyer Assi, entered into communication with Mazzini when he judged that the *Fratellanza* had sufficient strength to act. Assi requested arms, money, and leaders capable of bringing a plan of attack to a successful conclusion.

Mazzini received the news as proof that the propagation of his ideas had sown good seed even among the popular multitude; but he did not place full faith in Assi's assurances regarding the numerical strength and seriousness of purpose of the new organisation, until Major Eugenio Brizzi of Jesi and other informants, whom he had secretly dispatched to Milan, confirmed them in every particular.

Nevertheless, he counselled delay, when in December of 1852 Austria, giving fresh vent to its own ferocity, crowned the ramparts of Belfiore with gibbets, and having chosen a victim from every class of citizens, hanged them there.

Confronted with this savage provocation, the workmen of the *Fratellanza* felt it a crime to deliberate further. They notified Mazzini that they had resolved to act, even without his assistance and without his consent. Mazzini's inescapable duty became that of supporting the inevitable, of striving to ensure that the generous initiative, should it proceed, did not remain isolated and barren.

In the last days of December 1852, acting on a commission received directly from Count Grillanzoni, I departed for Milan, and on the appointed day, after a brief wait beneath the arcade of the Piazza Mercanti, I delivered a packet of instructions to a man who, emerging from the shadows, had given me the agreed signal.

The final plan thus reached the hands of Major Brizzi himself, charged with overseeing its execution. Its principal lines had been drawn by Pietro Fortunato Calvi. Its fundamental premise was that arms must be seized by surprise from the enemy, first disorganised by the killing of its officers.

By a provision of military regulation, these officers slept, as a rule, not in barracks but in private lodgings. Their addresses had been noted down, and equally well known were the haunts where they were accustomed to gather.

The Austrian military establishment was, among other positions, quartered within the walls of Milan at the Palace of the General Command, at the Palace of the Grand Guard, and at the Castello.

The first was guarded — or rather watched over — by a picket of twenty-five soldiers. Their arms stood aligned in the rack beneath the entrance archway, before which a vigilant sentinel paced without cease. The remaining soldiers whiled away the hours stretching their limbs in the courtyard or smoking in the barracks rooms. At five in the afternoon, the Governor, the Generals, and the General Staff dined in that palace.

To render the sentinel powerless by a sudden stroke was a surprise attack of no great difficulty.

Something similar, but requiring more formidable means, was to be attempted at the Grand Guard, which housed some hundred men-at-arms; and especially at the Castello, which contained in its armoury twelve thousand rifles. The two armourers who inspected them daily were of our number. They undertook to leave the doors ajar, so that forcing them should not rob us of precious time.

Within the premises of the Grand Guard, the insurgents were to entrench themselves and establish their headquarters, so as to give the revolution a centre. And from that centre, clad in the uniforms stripped from the dead and the prisoners, small patrols were to fan out, beating the drum, inducing officers to emerge from their dwellings. On the threshold they would find those appointed to seal their lips — and for ever.

The sixth of February was chosen for the assault upon the enemy — a day of carnival revelry and of soldiers' pay. Their quarters would be left all but deserted. The surprise would thus stand a greater chance of success; and our men would have greater ease in rushing the cafés and taverns, to renew — for a more sublime purpose — the blood-baptism of the Vespers.

To smooth the path to victory, Giuseppe Mazzini had solicited and obtained from Kossuth, who was on the point of departing for the United States of America, a revolutionary proclamation addressed to his countrymen quartered chiefly in the barracks of San Francesco; and there were already assurances that at the first sign of serious insurrection, the ill-tamed sons of valiant Hungary would make common cause with their oppressed Italian brothers.

As the crowning work: the barricades! The contractor for the city's illumination had undertaken, in order to protect those who raised them, to plunge Milan into darkness. And to defend them, Giuseppe Mazzini had caused to be smuggled into the city certain projectiles of new invention, to be hurled upon the assailants in the manner of grenades.

It should be added that the students of Pavia, secretly consulted, had pledged themselves to hasten to the aid of the capital; the agreed signal: a sheet of flame upon the spire of the Duomo. They were to be commanded by Acerbi — and they asked nothing more than arms. Mazzini had provided them by way of the Po.

Nor did the preparation of simultaneous movements beyond the walls end there. In Piedmont, the throng of political refugees watched and waited, impatient for action. It was necessary to open for them a broad and easy road by which they might descend in force upon the enemy. Giuseppe Mazzini entrusted this charge to Fortunato Calvi, who conceived the bold plan of seizing Lake Maggiore — a plan I shall explain presently.

And Aurelio Saffi, having knocked at the doors of the wealthier patriots — on which occasion Agostino Depretis, the future President of the Council, donated twenty-five thousand lire — then went, defying death, to Bologna, where he fomented with good hopes of success the insurrection of that city and of the Romagna.

In brief, Giuseppe Mazzini had secured the cooperation of those centres which might have interposed a barrier between the garrison of Milan and the various Austrian nuclei scattered throughout the Peninsula. Fortunato Calvi confided to me later, in Zürich, that he flattered himself he might outflank, in a fortnight or so, with the revolutionary forces, the Austrian army of Italy, and cut it off at the Alps from its base of reinforcement. It was enough, to give Lombardy heart and time to rise, that Milan should hold firm and fight in its streets for even a few days. And Italy would have won her independence by her own strength, as Greece had done, as the United States had done, avoiding the fatal train of consequences left by hybrid alliances, murky intrigues, and the compromises that have dimmed her greatness, and whose effects, alas, will be felt for many years to come.

But here I do not argue — I narrate.

The workmen of the *Fratellanza*, excepting the leaders, were ignorant of the particulars of the insurrectionary plan; but they knew that on the sixth of February the action would begin. They had unearthed some hundred pistols and, on Mazzini's advice, set themselves to fashioning daggers and knives. Even the nails of their canal barges were wrenched out and fixed upon poles... All things serve the desperation of a People!

And I, seized by the general fever, was already weighing, so as to forestall them, all the sinister eventualities foreseeable in the assault upon the Grand Guard — to which Brizzi had appointed me — when I received from Count Grillanzoni the order to proceed immediately to Genoa and to await his instructions there.

These instructions were: to meet a certain affiliated member, to have counted out in my name, on behalf of Mazzini, four thousand lire in gold; to carry them to Sander and Radonitz, who were awaiting me at Locarno. To keep, on the journey, to the road along Lake Maggiore, and to watch whether anything suggested that Calvi's plans had been discovered, or that some circumstance requiring their modification had arisen. To meet, on my return from Locarno, with Calvi himself, who would be at Intra, and to report to him faithfully everything I had seen and heard.

Austria maintained upon the Verbano a flotilla of gunboats, which ordinarily lay at anchor off Luino. The surveillance of the shores and the passenger service were entrusted to the steamship *Radetzky*, which plied the lake daily and always carried aboard a company of soldiers. These, during the crossing, remained below decks, leaving their rifles aligned by the hatchway under the guard of a sentinel. With the sentinel disposed of, it seemed more than probable that one could seize the arms and lock the remaining soldiers in the hold. Sander and Radonitz, who had formerly served as officers in the Austrian navy before passing into the service of the Venetian Republic, would have taken command of the steamship and disposed of it as an Italian vessel.

Pietro Fortunato Calvi had arranged that the *Radetzky*, putting in at Luino late in the evening, should continue to fly the Austrian flag — I and a handful of our men on board, dressed in the hated uniform.

It would be necessary to approach as friends the gunboats unprepared for action; it would be necessary to seize them by surprise. Then, having towed them into open water, to turn their murderous mouths upon the small garrison. Pietro Fortunato Calvi, having organised a solid body of patriots, would have assaulted Luino from the landward side. With the Italian flag hoisted over that strategic point, and the lake in our hands, the munitions, the arms, and the armed men coming from Piedmont would have found a relatively easy road all the way beneath the walls of Milan.

When I arrived at Arona there remained some hours before the departure of the *Radetzky*. I spent them walking along the shore of the lake. I recall that the magnificence of the landscape, all green and bright despite the winter, made upon my spirit an impression of such sweetness that for a moment it nearly unmanned me. And I marvelled that men, being able to enjoy fraternally so much of paradise, should spoil it by grappling perpetually with one another, as though in their breasts nothing could take root but base and ferocious passions.

But no sooner had the bell of the *Radetzky* announced the imminent departure, than my rebellious spirit regained the upper hand. I thought: "Beautiful is our homeland. It is for that very reason they put the knife to our throats and attempt to make it their own. So much more holy, then, is the fury that drives us to defend it — and the fiercer that fury, the holier the cause. Let them recross the Alps and we shall be brothers again."

The steamship put out upon the lake. I lit my pipe, and walking with slow steps, now here, now there, endeavoured to assume the attitude of one who gazes in rapt contemplation at the landscape passing before him. But my mind was fixed upon my secret purpose. I saw the rifles aligned by the hatchway; I scrutinised — almost weighed — the sentinel who, with German rigidity, with the regularity of a pendulum, trod the planking back and forth; I heard the exotic voices rising from deep within the vessel and understood them... they spoke, with lascivious frankness, of hips and haunches, punctuating their talk with coarse laughter. Nor throughout the entire voyage did the pomaded whiskers of a single *kaiserlich* emerge from that dark den. Faithful to their orders, those geometric heads!

The *coup de main* seemed to me so certain of success that more than once I regretted being alone and unprepared to attempt it.

But at Locarno I found spirits less willing than my own. Sander and Radonitz received me, not without a certain stiffness, at the house of one Olivieri, a dealer in sundry goods and also in arms. They took and carefully counted the gold which I drew from my money-belt. But when it came to settling the preliminaries of the action, the ifs and buts grew as thick and solid as a wall.

A few days earlier — I was told — two Hungarians with whom secret negotiations had been under way had been thrown into prison; two of our men. "The Authorities held them in complete isolation and nothing had leaked out regarding the grounds of their arrest. Why such precautions, such mystery? There was cause for misgiving at far less."

Radonitz was firmly persuaded that the Police had some inkling of what was being plotted. To venture something new would be to plunge headlong into the net. He urged that courage should not be confused with rashness. The lives of so many patriots were at stake — lives which were sacred to him. What would be lost by waiting; by ascertaining, with due caution, the temper of an enemy so powerful and so cruel? Wisdom did not consist in haste, but in acting in such a manner that victory should be certain and as it were inevitable.

To all of this I replied that if the arrest of one brother were sufficient to turn Italians from their purpose, they might as well renounce action for ever. Since '49 there had not passed a day without the Austrian cudgel at work, without some chain being riveted anew. The Police could not suspect a movement as vast as ours without taking extraordinary precautions; and such precautions could not have escaped the notice of the brothers dispersed in every quarter. No alarm had come from any direction whatsoever. I myself had traversed the full length of the lake, aboard an Austrian vessel, with eyes alert, and had observed nothing — absolutely nothing — out of the ordinary. Moreover, the chances of discovery were in proportion to the time spent in organising the conspiracy and to the number of those taking part in it. All the greater, therefore, appeared the necessity for swift and almost lightning action. Fortunato Calvi must already have left Turin; he would be at Intra the following day. It was for him alone to decide upon the greater or lesser expediency of delay. Our inaction would have robbed him of the power of choice and would have amounted to a defection. In any case, I had come to Locarno not to debate, but to act. My orders were to report to Intra everything I had seen and heard. I would depart alone if need be, should it cost me my life.

Olivieri's wife was present while I spoke. She left the room without a word and returned shortly, carrying a packet of revolutionary manifestos and a bundle of daggers. With her own hands she helped me to conceal both upon my person, and with that all discussion was cut short. The following morning I embarked upon the *Arno*, a Piedmontese steamship, and descended the Verbano at speed. Sander and Radonitz had stayed ashore.

What a profound change in those few hours! The overcast sky heralded snow; the lake grey, almost ashen. From the Alps blew an icy wind that seemed to have swept away all festive life from the shores and the hillsides.

I found on the *Arno* muffled figures wearing the selfishly preoccupied air of men absorbed in their own affairs; common faces that betrayed the anxieties of commerce. My attention eventually came to settle upon the captain, a bony man with a phenomenally large head. He was deep in the reading of a newspaper and cast only the occasional glance at the course. Who was he? What sentiments animated him? Could the name and sufferings of the homeland move his heart?

I answered myself: yes — and committed a grave error. He had risen abruptly and, as though following the impulse of a passing thought, gone off with hurried steps elsewhere. He had tossed his newspaper onto a seat. I drew out a revolutionary proclamation, concealed it within the folds of that newspaper, and returned running to my place.

I had not long to wait. A face painfully startled, a knitting of brows, a waggling of the heavy head. He fixed me with a pair of eyes I have never forgotten. And perhaps they were set in the face of the most unblemished Aristide in all Piedmont; but to me they seemed steeped — almost drunk — with idiotic commiseration and bestial menace. I dissembled by whistling a little cabaletta of Verdi, against the wind roaring around me.

I am at Intra. Among the cluster of idlers gathered to watch the steamship arrive, I catch, as one might say in passing, a well-known signal. I followed at a distance the person who had made it and was led to a solitary villa. The unknown brother remained outside and I entered. I found myself face to face with Fortunato Calvi.

What a flood of feeling, and how many things I should have wished to say to him; how many others, I imagine, he would have wished to confide to me! But we had to content ourselves with clasping each other's right hand for a long moment. Just then, neither he nor I was able to speak a word. When I at last found my voice, I said: "Are the sentinels at their posts?"

Having received the assurance, I drew from my person the proclamations and every one of the daggers save one, and laid them on a table. I continued:

— All of them here and in the surrounding country. They await arms and the signal.

— I received word from Milan. (1) It was an imprudence; but his shrewdness served him well. He is now at Lugano. Let the arms do their duty — the brain is watchful and will do its own.

I then gave him an account of my journey, and did not conceal from him the strange hesitation of Sander and Radonitz, who had failed to appear at the rendezvous. I had not yet finished when a window opened and in the gap appeared the face of the same affiliated member who had guided me there. He said to me, in an agitated voice: "Run, run! The carabinieri are at the gate. They are looking for you!"

In a single bound I was in the garden, crossed it at a run, taking care that the view of the gentlemen at the gate should remain blocked by the house. I climbed a tree and scaled the boundary wall. Four strides and I was at the lakeside. On the shore, chained to a landing-stage, several small boats lay in a row. One only, propelled by two oarsmen, was gliding close inshore, as though seeking a place to ground. Before the oarsmen, stupefied, had time to open their mouths, I had taken the leap and was finding my balance in that little shell. "Twenty francs for you! Quickly, to Locarno!" They understood and bent to the oars with a will.

And there they were — followed by a swarm of children — two gendarmes arriving on the shore with wild gesticulations. One jumped onto the shingle and busied himself, but in vain, with the boats. They were, I believe, secured with padlocks. The other, flailing his arms like a Dutch windmill, shouted: "Stop, stop!" I judged it prudent not to entrust my safety to another's commiseration, and drawing my double-barrelled pistol: "Either Locarno or the next world!"

From the shore rang out laughter and applause. The two carabinieri scanned the surface of the lake — no hope of assistance from that quarter. They vented their vexation by shaking their fists at the horizon.

(1) — That Mazzini was in Milan in 1853 was never stated either by him or by anyone else, so far as I know. But the Count di Rudio insists that the assertion of Fortunato Calvi admits of no ambiguity. C. C.


### Chapter Fourteen

<!-- pages:74-76 -->

THE EXCITEMENT of the moment did not prevent me from reflecting upon the strangeness of my situation. I, a patriot — and no mean one, if the intensity of feeling counts for anything — was being driven out like an enemy from the only scrap of free Italian soil, and I burned feverishly to cross the midline of the lake in order to return, as they say, to the shadow of the black and yellow... the very object of our execration become all at once the symbol of my salvation. I reached it and breathed more freely. But it was a salvation of short duration, and fate had ordained that I should not reach the Swiss frontier without considerable hardship.

The weather, which had shown a threatening aspect since morning, turned for the worse, and from the Alps — toward which we had turned our prow — there came not merely wind but a full tempest. The waters, as if forewarning its approach, took on a formidable undulation, and our frail craft tossed about in wild abandon. I soon perceived that we were making no headway at all, and seizing the spare oars, I made myself a third in that desperate struggle between human will and the tyranny of the storm. But judging it imprudent to turn my back upon the two boatmen, I was obliged to resign myself to keeping my face directed toward the Alps. Then there came sweeping obliquely across a pelting of frozen sleet — or rather a whirlwind of tiny needles of ice that stung the skin, tormented my eyes, and took away my breath. And the waves, foaming at their crests, sheared off by the wind into thin curling sheets, threatened to capsize the little vessel. There could be no doubt of it — the elements had declared themselves for Austria, and more than once I thought that I had escaped so many perils only to end miserably in that pond of agoni.

Numbness of the limbs came upon me; night came upon me. It was necessity — and salvation — to abandon Locarno and to make instead for the nearest republican shore, Brissago. We arrived there with our moustaches crusted with ice and our bones half broken. Nor was I able to show my deliverers the full measure of my gratitude; for one of them, the very man to whom I counted out the agreed sum together with a comparatively generous gratuity, laid a rough hand upon my shoulder and said: "Whatever comes of it, we served you because that double-barrelled instrument of yours admitted of no argument. Go about your business; we shall see to ours. Good night and good fortune!"

Half an hour and I was between the sheets. Scarce had I lain down when Sander and Radonitz, the captain of the Arno and Fortunato Calvi, the lake and the whole of Italy vanished into darkness.

The following morning I found my anxieties again upon the pillow beside me. After long reflection I persuaded myself that my failure had been entirely personal. Pietro Fortunato Calvi, remaining at liberty, had surely known how to organise everything, even without my assistance. We were now at the 4th of February... therefore the useful time had not passed... an initiative delayed, not lost!

I resolved to make my way on foot as far as Locarno and, without more ado, set out. The wind had dropped, but snow was falling in broad flakes... mountain and valley were white with it. In that fresh snow my legs sank to the calf with every step, and beneath my shoes there formed an obstinate clog that stole away, treacherously, both the road and my energy. But when I came in sight of the Olivieri house and saw light filtering through the iron grilles, and inferred from it that men were keeping watch and were at work, my weariness vanished as though by enchantment. The agreed signal opened the door to me. I was led into a room where, seated at a small table with their faces lit by the lamp, were Sanders and Radonitz. Standing upright, with arms folded, I found a third person: Pietro Fortunato Calvi. Greetings and congratulations! I cut them short to demand news of the situation: "What have you arranged? What hopes remain to us?"

Sanders and Radonitz shrugged their shoulders. They were waiting for the Police to lift their ambuscades and fall back to sleep. My mishap, as recounted by Calvi, had not only confirmed them in their intention of *menare il cane per l'aria* — to spin out matters indefinitely — but had further persuaded them that the Austro-Hungarian authorities, grown suspicious, had incited those of the Republic. "We are no longer safe here!" said Radonitz. "By the way, how are you placed for funds?"

And without awaiting my reply, he stretched his hand toward certain rolls of money which, in my first preoccupation, I had not even noticed.

It was as though a veil fell from my eyes. I thought: "Late... but in time!" And turning to Radonitz, who had unrolled one of the bundles: "Since you regard our attempt as having failed, restore the sum that I brought you. It was destined for the purchase of arms and not for your own benefit. It was counted out to me in the name of Mazzini; to Mazzini I shall make delivery of it."

The expression assumed at that moment by Radonitz is among the most comical of my recollections. He became haughty once more: "I have issued you a proper receipt; your responsibility is covered. Do not concern yourself with what does not pertain to you."

With hardship and with sweat, men who groaned beneath the lash had scraped together that gold... bread taken from their own mouths, from the mouths of their children. I insisted. And Radonitz, pale as wax: "Young man, you are a subordinate here; take care you do not forget it!"

Sanders, who had remained seated with his elbows resting on the table and his face in his hands, thought it opportune to intervene: "How do you expect us to make our escape, to preserve our work for better times, if we lack the means of doing so?"

I replied: "I do not believe that the watch-chain and trinkets hanging at your breast, and your gold timepiece, are altogether indispensable to you. You have sworn to give to the Fatherland your time, your blood, your soul and everything... would you deny her now a few trinkets?"

I turned my gaze toward the man who had not yet spoken, who had remained standing in the half-shadow — toward Fortunato Calvi. He gave me a brief nod of assent. Then with a single bound I was at the door, I shut it, put the key in my pocket. Then drawing that double-barrelled instrument which at Intra had already rendered me such signal service, I levelled it and cried: "Enough of words! Either restitution, or no one leaves this room!"

I replaced the gold in my money-belt and shook Fortunato Calvi's hand. To the others I gave the military salute... not without a touch of irony... Then I departed. 69


### Chapter Fifteen

<!-- pages:76-79 -->

THUS it came to pass that on the fateful 6th of February I found myself neither at the assault on the Gran Guardia in Milan, nor aboard the Radetzky on Lake Maggiore.

Instead, I was climbing, in the early morning, a steep path that, branching off from the Ceresio, a few steps from Lugano, led up to a villa as white as the snow that surrounded it. Its green railings were the only note of contrast against that vast whiteness. Having reached the open ground before the villa, I paused to contemplate it, and through my mind passed the words of Fortunato Calvi: "Let the arms do their duty — the mind keeps watch and will do its own!"

But if the reaching of that height had been an easy undertaking, being admitted to the man within was to prove far less so. The surroundings, seemingly solitary and tranquil, concealed hearts in tumult and were as though saturated with a busy silence. I felt, beyond the walls, the suspicious eye that watched me, and in the robust servant who approached me, stooping and subdued, I glimpsed the conspirator ready to straighten himself and to attempt, if need be, even a stroke of violence. Rather than answering his questions, I pronounced the password and extended my hand to him as to a brother. I added that I was the bearer of a letter of introduction to the Maestro, on behalf of Colonel Calvi.

But the Maestro, being exceedingly occupied, had given strict orders not to be disturbed. And as I persisted and urged the urgency of the matter, the counterfeit servant snatched the letter from my right hand, assuring me that he would deliver it personally. It was a stroke of luck that he invited me to enter.

I found myself in a ground-floor room, spacious and cold — on the mantelpiece lay four pieces of wood neatly arranged, but not lit. A writing-desk, a few bamboo chairs — the walls bare. Here too the prevailing note was whiteness. In the recess of the wide window, between two white curtains, hung a cage of brass wire, wrapped in a rose-coloured gauze — within, a canary was hopping about. I planted myself before it; but my thoughts soon turned elsewhere — to the small great man, toward whom so much impetus of hatred, so much impetus of love, converged from every corner of Europe. I expected to see the sturdy "servant" reappear at any moment to announce that I was expected, that I would be welcome. I heard the door creak on its hinges and turned. The man who stood before me was slight, dressed in black, with a black kerchief at his throat. His brow was high and smooth; his cheeks pale, almost olive-hued. I felt my soul, as it were, enveloped by the warm and serene gaze of Giuseppe Mazzini.

He held between his fingers the letter that had informed him of our failure, and he had come at once to learn the particulars at greater length. He listened, with great composure, to my account, and when, by way of conclusion, I drew out the money I had salvaged from the grip of cowardly self-interest, he took it and placed it, without counting it, in the drawer of the writing-desk. Not a word, not a flutter of an eyelid — whether because he deemed honesty too proud for praise, or because grave thoughts prevented him from dwelling upon lesser matters.

He sat down and motioned me to do likewise. But I remained standing, to one side, and while he meditated, I observed with wonder that slight, spare frame, the pallor of the face, the intellectual profile, most delicate. The greying head seemed to me too heavy for the neck that bore it — an impression perhaps heightened by his attitude, for the small great man was supporting his broad brow with his lean fingers.

I felt perplexed. How could such a man have sustained so much toil, so many intellectual struggles, such a fury of battles? Whence came the force that had roused so many of the inert, formed so many consciences, kindled so many fires, and breathed new life into the Fatherland?

No sooner had he begun to speak than I was persuaded that a great heart has great impulses, even within a narrow chest. One is accustomed to hear the counsel: "Write as you speak." Giuseppe Mazzini, in grave moments, spoke instead as he wrote.

I summarise and piece together the various parts of what he said:

"Nothing has been attempted — therefore nothing is lost! One must set to work again — but not with such men as these. He who meditates an enterprise and yet trembles and quibbles works for his own enemy and unconsciously digs the abyss that will swallow himself and the hopes of the Fatherland.

"Today's sun is destined to illuminate new audacities. A people that was never sparing of its own blood will hurl itself, with the courage of desperation, unarmed against the mighty oppressor. The streets of their city, so full of gaiety, will be transformed into a pit of groans and slaughter. If heroism should avail nothing, if the brute force of lead and numbers should once more triumph over right, the youth of Milan will retrace alone the road of exile, will climb alone the steps of the scaffold. But when hatred of the present oppression, the manly impulse, the disregard of suffering, shall have power to ripen fate, we too shall gather the fruit of victory — we, the brothers of Italy. The Milanese rise up and sacrifice themselves for a cause that is ours. And shall we confine ourselves to the part of idle spectators? Shall we demonstrate, by our selfishness, to the enemy and to the world, that our chains sit well upon us — that the dignity of free men is unbecoming to our souls?

"Let Milan have the certainty of having aroused, beyond her walls, a sympathy of purpose and of passion — and not a barren sympathy! In arduous trials, fortunes hang upon a thread; nor are the supports limited to arms and munitions. Let our solidarity strengthen the arm of the bold; let our ardour feed the sacred flame that drives them to action. It is imperative — imperative!"

He remained a moment in thought, and his lean fingers tormented his smooth brow, as though to wrest an inspiration from it by force. "How to provide for it," he murmured, "in such a strait of time?" Then, suddenly, with great emphasis:

"Varese is the point! The way for reinforcements is easy there; the garrison small; the citizens patriotic and impetuous; the *Giovine Italia* well-rooted; the assurances I have received serious and almost tangible. The thing is to strike a spark —

"*Parva favilla gran fiamma!* A small spark, a great flame! A rough undertaking, but not a hopeless one. Let whoever departs from Lugano today be on the spot tomorrow; let him confer with the leaders, distribute manifestos, spread the news of the Milanese insurrection, revive men's spirits, call them to arms, take the enemy one by one, organise the barricades, sound the tocsin, rouse the countryside.

"To do this, a legion is not needed — one man is enough. But a man who has the agility and audacity of the feline, the sacred enthusiasm of heroes; a man who would prefer, if need be, to die with arms in hand rather than live as a slave. Where is such a man to be found?"

Such words, spoken by such a man and in such a moment, could not pass over my soul without taking hold. My youthful exuberance overflowed and, grown bold, I dared to presume upon myself. I said:

"Maestro, I began to serve my Country when the first down was barely showing on these lips. I have hazarded my life at Venice, at Rome. Exiled to Paris, I did my utmost to oppose the parricide arms of Napoleon. And Fortunato Calvi can attest that, even of late, in the Lombardo-Veneto, I more than once braved, in the good cause, the danger of dying by the noose. Certainly, I have accomplished no great things, but —"

"I understand, I understand! The spirit of sacrifice cannot be inexhaustible."

"But no, no, Maestro! Did you not teach us that right derives from a duty fulfilled? I have fulfilled mine as best I knew how. Now I claim the right to be deemed worthy of offering once more my arm and my blood for the benefit of my country."

He raised his head, and I felt myself once more, as it were, enveloped by the warm and serene gaze of the Maestro. He said simply: "The enterprise at Varese is yours."

I departed at dusk, escorted by some smugglers — the smugglers who gave Austria such endless trouble and whom resurgent Italy, prodigal as she is of marble and bronze to the heroes of the Five Days, appears to have entirely forgotten. We walked in silence, over crags and precipices; I marvelling at their agility, they, in all likelihood, at mine. By daybreak we were at the frontier.

There stood in those times, near the point at which we were to cross it, a small house. At the top of its door was fastened a branch of pine. This signified, in the figurative language of those parts: "Here one may drink" — even as the myrtle signified in ancient times: "Here one makes love!" The leader of the party directed his steps thither, and all of us, each at a discreet distance from the other, followed in his track.

That innkeeper, perched astride between two states, sold wine and *grappa* to give himself a social standing. His greater earnings came from the clandestine understandings he had woven with those bold spirits who defrauded the customs. The smugglers descending into Lombardy and those who, from the Republic, penetrated into the Kingdom, halted at his dwelling — to spy out the ground, to receive orders, to deposit there their goods and their subversive literature.

Around the innkeeper gathered those who had escorted me. They fell to conferring in low voices, with agitated manner; I, prudently, remained to one side, contemplating some images daubed upon the wall. Then toasts were drunk to one another's health, and drunk again. Growing impatient, I went out into the open air and cast a covetous eye away, away, across the plain below, in the direction of Varese.

But even as I was devising days of zealous endeavour, and a measure of heroism withal, a courier arrived at full gallop, one destined to thrust me back into grey existence, among the resigned, among the unknown. I was so absorbed that I was unaware of his coming until I heard my name called. "Ah, it is you! I had all but despaired of reaching you. I bring you a most urgent letter!" He searched in his greatcoat and handed it to me. As I broke the seal, my hand was trembling. I read:

*"Return immediately. Your sacrifice would be in vain. In Milan all is lost." — Mazzini.*

Brief lines — an immense calamity! They seemed to my eyes to have been traced by a pen dipped in the blood of martyrs. I retraced the road in haste, and before the setting of the sun I was once more in the presence of Giuseppe Mazzini.


### Chapter Sixteen

<!-- pages:79-83 -->

Here is the revised translation with minimal, targeted changes supported by the Edgren dictionary:

Here is the painful page: the secret of the vast conspiracy, jealously guarded; the Austrian Authorities utterly unprepared; the distribution of pay to the soldiers already carried out; the targeted positions stripped of their defenders; the garrison dispersed and in revel. Eugenio Brizzi himself, in the imminence of the planned assault, had penetrated into the inner courtyard of the Castle and had spoken with the two armourers. He found them steadfast to their given word; nor had any coup de main ever enjoyed more favourable auspices.

But at the moment of action, Assi and certain other leaders, instead of convoking the squads, vanished. The few brave men lying in wait, finding their numbers far too meagre, waited for a time, chafing; then, suspecting treachery, or supposing that everything had been postponed to another day, they dispersed.

Of Fortunato Calvi's plan, only so much succeeded as suffices to demonstrate that it was not built upon air, and that it would have met with a very different outcome had not certain individuals of great influence in Milan — men who desired the expulsion of the foreigner, yet who, under the colour of prudence, kept their eyes fixed upon the interests of their own caste — stood in the way.

The assault on the Gran Guardia, led by a common charcoal-seller, met with happy success. The insurrection would have had its stronghold and two howitzers with which to defend it. But the directing minds were conspicuous by their absence, and the popular impetus, intolerant of delay, cast aside the instructions it had received. Instead of barricading themselves within the palace, those few and valiant men poured out in tumult through the streets. They killed several officers, then fell back upon Porta Romana, where the innkeeper Giuseppe Monti organised a defence.

To suppress it, an entire battalion was dispatched. And while a handful of common people generously staked their lives, in the hope of holding out long enough for the middle class and the upper class to resolve themselves to descend, armed, into the revolutionary vortex — the wealthy, the *benpensanti* (respectable-minded), closed their street-doors, shuttered their windows, and lent to four-fifths of the capital the appearance of a deserted and silent city.

The insurrection failed because the intellectuals of Milan lacked the ardour, the heart, the spirit of sacrifice, and the faith of the People. Even in the preliminary negotiations conducted between them and Giuseppe Mazzini, Emilio Visconti Venosta — the future Minister of the monarchy — and those of his ilk had brought their acuteness of mind to bear upon the raising of objections. Compelled by argument to retreat from one position to another, they ended by declaring that, before resolving to lend a hand, they intended to seek the opinion of a competent military man. And they turned to Giacomo Medici, who was then residing in Genoa.

The hero of the Vascello, the future general of Vittorio Emanuele II, held precisely the same view that had governed the conduct of Giuseppe Mazzini: "To counsel against the rising; but to support it strenuously once it showed itself inevitable."

This did not please them, and they did not follow it. I believe that from the lips of Medici they had expected the phrase that would lend propriety to their timid egoism. The conspiracy gained substance and began its fatal course; but they gave neither a soldo, nor a man, nor a rifle. And they possessed them!.. But with an expression that foreshadowed the myopic and corrosive opportunism of times all too vivid in the memory of Italians, they declared "that they reserved the right to make good use of them on the second day!"

The flight of Assi and the others, however blameworthy, had no other motive than these underhand prevarications. The Milanese working class was not yet accustomed to the modern, subtle distinctions between proletariat and capitalism; it had not yet learned to stand upright upon its own spine. It had heard and read so many times that all Italians are brothers, and in its ingenuous good faith it had taken them for such. The first doubts are always the bitterest. Why did the *marsine* — the frock-coats — now refuse to make common cause with the *blouses*? Were not the sons of the People risking their lives for a holy common cause? Because they hurried all the risks fell upon them alone.

I knew Assi in exile. He was expiating, in muffled anguish, his own error and those which were unjustly attributed to him. And he defended himself, saying: "They wished to drive us alone to the slaughter, so that they might disown us if we were defeated. Had victory smiled upon us, without any risk to themselves, they would have stolen the fruit from our hands!"

The bourgeois and aristocratic inaction of 1853 had called to mind that in 1848 the direction of public affairs had been usurped by those who had not made the revolution. A deplorable logic, that of Assi — but logic nonetheless!

Giuseppe Monti and his men fought on, from house to house, until not a crumb of powder remained to them. And so effective was their resistance that fully one hundred and fifty of the enemy lay upon the cobblestones and ceased their torment of Italy.

Then came the martyrdom. A military tribunal, hastily constituted, hastily condemned to the gallows Giuseppe Monti and fifteen other brave souls. ¹

They faced their punishment with serene courage, teaching the faint-hearted how to die, after having taught them how to rise in insurrection.

Then came the arts of Tartufo. It was needful to diminish the heroism of the worthy in order to lend to one's own defection the character of an enlightened decision befitting supermen. There is no lack of *connoisseurs* who would dismiss the verses of Dante as insipid were they to come before them unsigned. The plans of Fortunato Calvi, attributed to Mazzini — an intellect held to be proof against all military discipline — were declared a foolishness more than puerile. And men were not ashamed, to prove the point, to borrow with a foolish air of triumph the Croatian phrases belched forth during the heroic hours spent at the tavern: "Had the Milanese killed our officers, we would have put the Milanese to the sword — men, women, and children!"

And the people of the *cinque giornate* — the Five Days — to oblige the Croatians and Visconti Venosta, were to have resigned themselves to being skewered on a spit like a dish of limp *gnocchi*!

Then came the infamy!.. To vilify the honest; to reduce, if possible, the stature of the giant, so that the People, deceived and deluded, might resolve to gravitate around faint and false flames. The tactic is an ancient one.

And it was insinuated and written and stated as certain fact that Giuseppe Mazzini was a spy in the pay of Austria. He provoked excesses, while prudently keeping himself well out of reach, in order to furnish the oppressor with the means to terrorise the timid by strangling the bold.

Then came the nauseating cynicism!..

To stupefy a conscience fitfully reawakened by the tormenting thought of one's own guilt; to seek to deceive oneself and others by disowning even one's own martyrs. To this they descended: they caused to circulate a protest in which, with all roles inverted, Giuseppe Monti and the other unfortunates were portrayed as assassins, and the assassins righteously slain in that desperate, glorious attempt to restore a Fatherland to us were portrayed as martyrs. Never had creatures born and raised beneath the fair sky of Italy prostrated themselves so low!

I insist: the failure of the risings of the 6th of February 1853 is due principally to the fact that a handful of wealthy, privileged, and influential young men recoiled in horror before the possibility of a popular Régime, and already preferred that Italy should not be made at all, rather than that she be made other than conservative and monarchical.

Yet those risings were not without fruit. They hardened Austria and, rendering her ever more blind to her own true interests, drove her back towards reckless sequestrations, towards fresh persecutions; towards remedying our errors with the momentum of her own, greater and irreparable, errors.

Giuseppe Monti and his companions in martyrdom thus indirectly rendered impossible any accord between the oppressed and their oppressors. The sacred work of redemption hastened its course and became inevitable!

¹ Giuseppe Monti, Siro Taddei, Alessandro Scannini, Bonaventura Broggini, Eligio Bigatti, Pietro Canevari, Cesare Faccioli, Luigi Piazza, Camillo Piazza, Antonio Cavallotti, Gerolamo Saporiti, Pietro Colla, Angelo Siasi, Benedetto Diotti, Alessandro Silva, Angelo Galimberti.


### Chapter Seventeen

<!-- pages:83-88 -->



AT LOCARNO, whither I had returned to convey to Calvi a missive from Mazzini, I was arrested and released a few hours later, at liberty, on my word to present myself to the Federal Delegate. I was subsequently tried for conspiracy against a friendly Power, expelled from Switzerland, and presented with the alternative of choosing between America and England.

England was nearer to the field of probable future enterprises, and I chose it. I settled in London, where I found not a few men compromised in the events of February, who had escaped Austrian vengeance by means of adventurous flights — among them, a Milanese Jean Valjean who had been carried out beyond the city walls in a coffin, with all the solemnity of a funeral.

Bound together by a common ideal and common sufferings, we lived for a time in community, in the quarter known as Baldwin's Garden, and precisely in the modest inn of the Milanese Tressoldi, a good-natured soul who possessed, among his other virtues, that of turning a blind eye when we could not settle our accounts.

Giuseppe Mazzini arrived in London in April. From him we learned that Pianciani, who was then dictating his *Storia dei Papi*, tempered the labours of the intellect by altering and re-altering the garden of his villa — a probable source of employment for several of us. We were introduced to his secretary, one Bardini, a Paduan law student, and he, in his turn, presented us to Pianciani, who there and then entrusted the manual work of those incessant modifications to young Varisco, granting him full authority to avail himself of the services of as many among us as he deemed necessary. I too was promptly engaged, and thus derived my sustenance for some months from the spade and the flowers.

That simple and laborious life was troubled by terrible images. Austria raged with ever greater fury, and every newspaper, every letter brought us the sorrowful certainty that the phalanx of the generous was being inexorably thinned.

I spoke of it to Giuseppe Mazzini during our long walks, and fixed my gaze upon that olive-hued, intellectual face, as if to say: *si decide — si decide?* — is it decided, will it be decided? Shall we let them be butchered like dogs? The Master quickened his step. His expression was one of ineffable melancholy; but of the thoughts that whirled within his mind — not a syllable!

One day in August he sent for me with urgency. Why, one might ask? Certainly not to bid me good morning or good evening. What, then?

I drove my spade into the middle of a flower-bed: "Stay there! You are not the only noble instrument!" And away I ran.

But when I stood before the Master, my arms fell to my sides, so sparse was his welcome in words. I was asked to carry a letter to Kossuth and to wait for the reply. Not one syllable more!

Kossuth held at that time, near Regent's Circus, a species of Court. At the door I found carriages and liveried servants; on the staircase a coming and going of measured Englishmen in evening dress; Hungarians in the national costume, elegant and martial; men bearing broad scars; uncertain figures, soliciting under the pretext of the Fatherland. Honest and dishonest alike, rich or poor, with difficulty obtained access to the Dictator who, even beaten and in exile, exercised, beyond the prestige of fame, that other prestige which comes from the habit of command.

While waiting in the antechamber for his leave, I endeavoured to beguile the time by observing the pictures that hung from the walls — Kossuth in the guise of the plain citizen; Kossuth in hunting dress; Kossuth in the uniform of the general — now in an armchair, now on horseback, now beside a shattered cannon; but always Kossuth, nothing but Kossuth!

At last, amid so many likenesses, the great man appeared in flesh and blood. I must confess that art, in its endeavour to idealise him, had slandered him. The figure of the illustrious Magyar recalled, more than conventional heroism and carnage, paternal affections and the mild emotions of the domestic hearth. It is the privilege of artists to alter, at their caprice, the tints of History — for this reason Napoleon I has been handed down to posterity crossing the Saint Bernard on a prancing colt, with manifest injustice to the poor donkey that lent its back to so great a weight.

The reception reserved for me was not such as the Count of Rudio, the Italian patriot, and the trusted emissary of Giuseppe Mazzini had any right to expect. Kossuth read the letter I proffered while standing erect upon the threshold, and, having tossed it aside, drew a memorandum from his pocket, wrote a few lines, and commanded me, curtly, to deliver them to their destination. Not a word of greeting! — and I, descending the stairs, asked myself whether the Cossacks of the Czar and the Croats of Jellačič, by lending their aid to Austria, had not, in substance, spared the brave Hungarians the inconveniences inherent in a change of dynasty.

The few lines consigned to me by Kossuth were addressed to a high-ranking officer whose name I am now searching for in the recesses of memory. I received from him several rolls of sterling — "The same," he told me, "that we received from Mazzini for our participation in the movements of 1853. May they serve your great, unfortunate country!"

Kossuth, on the morrow of that failure, had publicly denied all complicity in those very movements!

On my return, the Master opened his heart. He began by asking whether I felt disposed to hazard my life once more for the cause of the Fatherland, and having received the answer from my bearing alone, he pointed to a chair and we reasoned quietly, face to face, for upwards of an hour.

He explained to me that, on the advice of Fortunato Calvi, future insurrectionary attempts would be conducted in the Alpine regions. The reasons he advanced were the following: Our country is rich in latent forces, ready to burst forth; but he who undertakes an initiative must know how to give assurance of serious resistance. The mountain is the natural bulwark of the weak and the oppressed. There it is possible for the few to hold their ground against the many; possible to evade pursuit; to swoop with the swiftness of an eagle upon the plain; to surprise and to intercept. In place of pitched battle, which was denied to us, we must substitute guerrilla warfare, which is of necessity the daughter of the mountains. If the banner of redemption could be kept upright and flying for three, for two months, there would not be a patriot without the fever, not a corner of Italy where action did not blaze forth.

This last thought appeared to draw him into another train of reflection. A shadow spread itself, as it were, across his face; then he spoke, in words nearly these: "I know it! Cowardice, masked as prudence, accuses me of driving ardent youth to the scaffold, of provoking desperate attempts that offer Austrian tyranny, and our own native tyranny, the opportunity to redouble their cruelties. But no one reflects that the audacity and the sacrifice of the few always end by shaking the torpid and dragging the many into action. Nothing great without audacity! Napoleon III would in all probability never have smoothed his way to the Empire without the failures of Strasbourg and Boulogne. No King of Italy will show spirit great enough to champion the redemption of our country, if Italians show that they are content to bask, in peace, in their slavery. Would Carlo Alberto have crossed the Ticino, had it not been for the magnanimous self-abnegation of the Milanese? This keeping open, this pressing forward of the great Italian question is an imperative necessity. Europe cannot have peace so long as our agony endures. She will intervene, sooner or later, in our favour, and the sooner the more we show ourselves intolerant of the yoke and threatening. And what are good ideas, what are vigorous resolves, worth, when the want of initiative confines them to the realm of mere abstractions? Action cleanses our centuries of guilt. Between us and a free Italy lies this baptism of blood. Ought we to remain on this side of it, for the slanders of cowards?"

That same evening I procured a passport in an assumed name and set out for Switzerland. Fortunato Calvi was already awaiting me in Zurich.
