"The likelihood of the new movements rests with him," Mazzini had told me. "The execution falls to him to direct. From his own lips you will receive the necessary instructions."

At Brussels, where I made a brief halt, I delivered a letter from the Master to — the Fatherland is not redeemed by spontaneous process; nor is Liberty watered like the cabbages of a kitchen-garden! — Ungherrischio, the Venetian Catullus. "What is the Master doing?" he asked me, almost feverishly. And I, with his own verses:

*"Mazzini is in every place where men tremble*
*That the hour supreme may reach the traitor's ear;*
*Mazzini is in every place where men hope*
*To shed their blood for Italy entire!"*

Arrived at Zurich, before going to Calvi I wished to have my hands free, and hastened to call upon de Boni, to whom also I delivered a message; a third I carried to the Comasco Caronti. Giuseppe Mazzini had warned me to be very much on my guard with this man, of whom he was greatly suspicious. He did, however, contribute sums of considerable size to the good cause, and this held Mazzini back from dispensing with him.

The brazenly inquisitive questions that Caronti put to me came to me, already ill-disposed, like a sharp irritant, and when I set foot once more beyond the threshold of his villa, I felt a relief, as if I had suddenly freed myself from a nightmare.

At last I could restore myself in the embrace of an authentic patriot, a conscience without stain — Fortunato Calvi! What joy to see him again, still handsome, still proud, still exhaling that indefinable sense of serenity which breathes from honest souls!

The substance of our conversation had a beginning which I shall relate without false modesty.

"You are not," he said to me, "and must not be, a mere instrument to execute blindly orders you do not understand. I shall confide to you the plan I have devised, and which the European Revolutionary Committee has fully approved. You will thus know how to parry, with unity of purpose, the inevitable reverberations. If something concrete is achieved, you will be my aide-de-camp; if nothing is achieved — we shall in all likelihood be levelled by the same recompense — martyrdom!"

My particular preliminary mission was to make my way into the valley of the Trevis and to meet with certain smugglers, known by the sobriquet of the Burrasca brothers, in order to induce them to carry into the Agordino a large consignment of munitions. I was then to cross the border, enter Belluno clandestinely, and make contact with Don Bastiano Barozzi, who had returned there and was awaiting me. Barozzi had knowledge of certain weapons concealed in 1848, upon the return of the oppressors. In the Cadore there were abundant rebel spirits who had covered themselves in glory in its defence; the name and the exhortation of Fortunato Calvi, who had been the good genius of that defence, would have rekindled in their breasts the slumbering enthusiasm. It was necessary to distribute among them the old weapons. A band of exiles awaited the signal to descend from the Alps — Fortunato Calvi, leading them, would direct their fortunes.

The Austrian colossus was to be struck precisely at Belluno, and by a triple surprise: at the Revenue Office, at the Gendarmerie, and at the barracks of the regular troops.

The Revenue Office, situated near the old church of Santo Stefano, a solid building erected to serve as a convent, encloses a vast colonnaded courtyard. Between one column and the next stretches a parapet of such height that a man, walking close against it on all fours, cannot be seen by a sentinel who, however vigilantly, is patrolling other parts of the colonnade. The gate had no doors and was left unguarded. As the building's sole custody, a meagre body of militia, posted at the far end of the courtyard, to the right — three men in all and a corporal.

In the dead of night, a handful of our men lying in ambush by the gate; one bold figure creeps, barefoot, along the parapet, surprises the sentinel from behind — a muffled signal — men rush forward! — the three sleeping militiamen are rendered helpless. A bold enterprise, not a difficult one!

It was autumn, the season for the collection of taxes; the coffers of the Administration, by reliable reckoning, were expected to contain a million and a half florins. To seize them — the sinews of war!

Simultaneously, another band of patriots was to conceal itself near the Gendarmerie barracks. They would simulate a brawl and ring the bell of the Gendarmerie as if to seek assistance. The guard who opens the door must be cut down. They were to burst into the den, and the thirty men-of-arms sleeping within would pass, perforce, from the category of those who put on handcuffs to that of those on whom handcuffs are placed.

The garrison of Belluno at that time consisted of a hundred and fifty soldiers. Access to the barracks was gained, to the right and to the left, by a winding circular road — a *tourniquin*, as the engineers call it, flanked by trees and benches, a favourite resort of nursemaids and urchins. As a general rule, when the alarm sounded, the small garrison would pour out, in dribs and drabs, through the gate and form up on the esplanade awaiting orders. It was necessary to post a body of picked marksmen along the edges of the winding road, sound the alarm, and shoot down the militiamen as they came rushing out.

Once the triple surprise was accomplished, the insurgents would seize the civil and ecclesiastical authorities — the hostages! Then we would commandeer the means of transport, put on Austrian uniforms, and advance in military order as far as the fortress of Osoppo, in Friuli, taking prisoners and compelling all those we encountered along the way to follow us, so as to forestall any indiscretion.

At this point Fortunato Calvi, who had throughout been speaking in a low voice, as if meditating upon his own words, grew animated and said with passion: "We approach the fortress preceded by the Austrian flag and with drums beating, as friends. Once the bridge is crossed and a foot set under the archway, the scene changes, the Latin valour bursts forth. On the bastions, which will have become ours, we raise our own banner, and woe to whoever assails it! The hostages remain with us, with us the arms, the money, and the audacity. Osoppo becomes our base of operations and is an impregnable instrument of war. Through the winter we organise the guerrilla campaign on the Alps, we hold in check the troops that would seek to descend into the Peninsula. Come spring, Lombardy, Venetia, Hungary, France perhaps also, lend their support to the movement — the oppressed and the oppressors measure their forces one more time. If the errors of the past serve as our schooling, the secular vow is fulfilled, Italy rises, never to fall again."

I listened with head bowed, thinking what any other man would have thought: "To what extent could we count upon the ability of the European Revolutionary Committee to extend our initiative, to transform it into an international rising?"

Fortunato Calvi must have understood, and he said to me: "I suppose that the chances of success are twenty, and eighty those of dying — either with a bullet in the forehead, or at the hands of the executioner. The Fatherland is not redeemed by spontaneous process; nor is Liberty watered like the cabbages of a kitchen-garden. I take the risk! — either triumph, or death!"

Could I draw back when an entity like Fortunato Calvi was flinging himself wholly forward?

Three days, and I set out again on my journey.


### Chapter Eighteen

<!-- pages:88-92 -->



I TOUCHED Coira, where I received from Clementi — who was at that time under prosecution as a participant in the international disturbances — a letter for his younger brother. This brother would, in his turn, see to putting me in contact with the Burrasca brothers.

At San Maurizio, in the Engadina, at Muentzen — the point of contact between Switzerland, Württemberg and the Tyrol — at Salzburg, at Trento, not a single suspicious incident. At this last locality I presented myself to the Austrian Authorities and signed, under the assumed name, my *passaporto giardinetto* — my little pocket passport — and they did not cast upon me so much as a single searching glance.

And from Trento, deviating from the direct route, I ascend again the valley furrowed by the torrent Trevis, meet with young Clementi; and find myself that same evening face to face with the four smugglers.

Of them Clementi had told me that they were the despair of the manhunters and the pride of the valley. Jealous of their own name, they left the easy enterprises to lesser men and made it a point of honour to bring to completion the most hazardous. Spirits intolerant of every restraint, they lent themselves nobly even to the righting of wrongs and to the struggle for others' liberty. They had already rendered no inconsiderable services to the Italian cause.

No masterful phrases were needed to bend them to my purpose, and when the elder of the brothers — the captain of the small and courageous band — set down his glass from which he had been sipping with a vigorous thud, extended his right hand and pressed mine, I understood that I had concluded, with that simple act, a pact for life and for death, far more binding than any notarial document.

Early in the morning, having refused every guide, I set forth on foot along little-trodden paths which traced themselves, little by little, vividly in my memory before they unfolded themselves before my gaze. In the vast horizons, the sudden precipices, the solemn silences, the alpine fragrances, I found again my childhood, and was so filled with it that the phantoms of the future grew pale and almost dissolved.

When, from the heights, I caught sight of my Belluno, the rain was falling in great drops; but there was serenity in my heart. I stood under my broad umbrella, searching among the uncertain lines for my father's house, and having found it, it seemed to me that dear old father and mother and all my beloved ones must feel me near and trembling, as I felt them.

I reached the bank of the Ardo at dusk; but the autumn rains had swollen it so greatly that finding a ford proved impossible. It was a setback! Don Bastiano's house stood on the opposite side of the city. To reach it I now found myself forced to venture upon the main road, to cross the bridge, to traverse Belluno from end to end. I waited patiently for nightfall, hidden in a thicket.

Ten o'clock was striking when, my umbrella lowered over my face, I entered among the houses. The wayfarers were few and hurried. I scrutinised them from afar, in the uncertain light of the lanterns — not one *sbirro*, not one acquaintance, not a living soul that seemed to take any notice of me. I might have said that I was, to my fellow-citizens, an invisible spectre. But when the houses thinned out and I had left every lantern behind me, a sudden thought came to disturb me: "What if someone had laid a trap for me, to lure me into the solitary dwelling of Don Bastiano?"

I slipped into the little garden that surrounds it, with such precautions that not even the mastiffs took note of it. The ground floor — dark; all the shutters closed; those of the upper storey closed likewise — only one window yawned open a gleam of light that went to lose itself among the boughs of a double row of trees. I strained my ear — the voice of the Ardo, a distant rustling of leaves, nothing more! Then I gathered up a small stone and flung it against the window, while with an instinctive movement I gripped the butt of a pistol.

And there, darkening behind the glass, appeared a woman's figure — it was Salvi, Don Bastiano's servant, a handsome Cadorine girl with generous hips and impertinent bosom. She threw open the shutters and whispered into the night: "Is it you, Moretto?"

Don Bastiano received me with an embrace and with sobbing. His first words were: "Praised be Heaven! I had already given up hope of—" he meant to say more, but his voice failed him, and overcome by a surge of tenderness, I too sobbed.

It was my brother Giustiniano, who had remained to one side, almost unnoticed, who managed to utter a coherent syllable. He said: "Sad news. Our friends, the best of them — arrested; Don Bastiano — arrested; poor Father — arrested."

And he recounted to me the particulars of the catastrophe. Don Bastiano had calmly stretched out his wrists for the manacles, "happy to contribute, by his own sacrifice, to completing the series of sufferings demanded by Providence for the redemption of his Country." But Father had wished to break his bamboo cane across the shoulders of the *sgherri* — and can the cruelties of oppressors still be called news! For some days now the unexpected arrests have continued. My own house is under constant surveillance. How have you managed to arrive there without molestation? I have a vague suspicion that you have fallen from the sky!

I reflected, and concluded: "Now the important thing is to bring the family home. We shall think of the rest afterwards."

But poor Salvi, broken by the ordeal, rendered still more helpless by the darkness, stumbled at every step. I placed one of my pistols in her hand and said: "Go ahead and clear the way. Think of who might be lurking about. Sleeping in one's own bed has become an arduous problem in these times!"

At his request I recounted to him what I had seen in my rapid journey. And before Mother had time to notice and react, I had lifted her in my arms and proceeded, proud to bear the one who had borne me. And pressing her to me I printed upon her brow a long kiss. I did not foresee that it would be the last! "Until we meet again, Mother!" And I promised her that, however adverse fortune might show herself, I would always act in such a manner that, when we met again, thinking of her love, my conscience would have no cause to reproach me for having abused it.

"Listen, my son," said Don Bastiano. "You know how I love the Fatherland. For its liberty I would give — everything, save my soul. But it is one thing to sacrifice what belongs to ourselves, and another to imperil the safety of others, the lives of those whom we hold dear. It is a tremendous responsibility for which I should have to render account, sooner or later, to One who counts the drops of blood vainly shed and is not appeased by syllogisms. Heed me: these enterprises succeed — sometimes — if they burst forth suddenly, like lightning! But should the enemy, all-powerful as he is, divine them — Carletto! Carletto! of what avail are generous resolves, the daring of four, of thirty, of a hundred young men, against a colossus that holds all Europe in subjection? Would you offer your neck to the noose, thus light-heartedly, for the sterile glory that might derive from it? And has not our Italy already too many, far too many of her best to mourn and to commiserate? Reserve your energy for more propitious times. How that may come to pass I do not know and could not tell you; but I believe that the Police have some inkling of our plots. Arrests are not made at random. You are not safe here. O times, O times! Friend meets friend and must grieve to see him, and must beseech him to depart. Austria makes us worse than barbarians!"

At that moment, outside and only a few paces off, there resounded a sharp crack; the mastiffs howled — I blew out the candle.

Moments of terrible anxiety on Don Bastiano's behalf. I had gone, on tiptoe, to the door and having opened it a crack, had levelled the barrels of both my pistols through it. Then, thinking that to remain caged precluded every avenue of escape, I drew the door ajar and stepped out — a great darkness, a great silence. As a further precaution I made the circuit of the house — the wind, a shutter banging — that was all! But when I went back in and relit the candle, I was struck by Don Bastiano's mortal pallor.

And I thought again of the fine walks taken in his company, in the autumn, when he, to educate me in manly sentiments, would recount the deeds of our forebears; when his priest's cassock disappeared from before my eyes and I pictured him grave in corselet and helm — so fierce were his words, so intense his enthusiasm!

Don Bastiano, accustomed to reading my thoughts since my earliest years, said: "Do not number me among the cowards. My character as a priest and these grey hairs impose duties upon me. But the die is cast — upright and serene to the very end!"

Early in the morning, while I, wearied by the long journey, was still sleeping soundly, he went to inform Father of my arrival. I found the proud old man still vigorous, though on his noble face I could discern wrinkles that were unknown to me. But our meeting was different from what I had expected. It is probable that Don Bastiano had induced him to throw into the balance all the weight of paternal authority in order to dissuade me from so hazardous an enterprise. No embraces, no kisses; a powerful handshake, but a solitary one, and immediately, sharpened and rendered almost sarcastic, the same exhortations I had heard the evening before from the lips of the good priest. Our discussion grew so heated as to become deeply painful to me. I resolved to cut it short. "When the uniform and the bread of thieves grew hateful to me," I said, "I expected neither celebrations nor honours. Passive submission does not befit the spirit of a Rudio. And since you counsel it — very well then, I shall continue to beat my own path, alone. From this moment I have no longer a father!"

The poor old man remained for a moment with a gesture suspended, as though he had suddenly been turned to stone; then his eyelashes filled with tears. He flung himself forward with a sudden impulse, embraced me and held me fast with a strength of which I should not have thought him capable. When he could speak: "It is you, it is you, Carletto! I recognise my own blood. Go! So long as my strength holds, I shall be with you!"

It was decided that the arms should not be unearthed until the spirits of those bold men who were to handle them had been prepared and well disposed. Father and Don Bastiano undertook to approach them. In the meantime I would, by way of prudence, seek a refuge less carefully watched by the Police. In the environs of Belluno, towards the north, where the plain borders upon the heights, there stood an age-old chestnut tree, known and almost famous throughout the city and the surrounding country for its extraordinary dimensions. It was agreed that I should make my way, under cover of darkness, beneath its boughs. A trustworthy person would come, towards midnight, to indicate to me where I might lay my head. The recognition signal: three whistles, to which I would reply by whistling four times.

At nine o'clock I was already stretched out on the grass, musing as I contemplated the moon through the black foliage; then the moon set and the wind began again its unbridled course. I rose to stretch my limbs and posted myself behind the enormous trunk.

From the tower of Belluno twelve strokes rang out into the void, and my eye, now accustomed to the darkness, searched among thickets and vineyards. Another long hour passed — I was seized by dark forebodings.

Suddenly, softly, timidly, from beyond a group of bushes, there followed the three whistles — then a whisper — "On your guard, Moretto! There are several of them!" I looked intently and made out, through the twigs, something — like the overcoat of a *sbirro*. That I did not fire on the instant was only for fear of missing my mark and finding myself defenceless before the enemy. I answered the signal and waited with both weapons levelled —

Oh, what blood and what remorse were spared me by one moment of hesitation! A shadow advanced with arms outstretched — my mother!


### Chapter Nineteen

<!-- pages:92-98 -->



AMONG all things capable of stirring the noble rebel impulses that reach the heart of a son, nothing surpasses the sobs of his mother. Those of mine, in the silence of the night, resounded with terrible force.

I hastened to support her with one arm and placed my right hand upon her lips as if to stifle them. *Una ronda* — a patrol: that was what I meant to say, but no voice came, and, overcome by a surge of tenderness, I too began to sob.

The first to utter any coherent syllable was my brother Giustiniano, who had remained to one side, almost unnoticed. He said: word had come of new arrests. Our friends, the best of them, had been seized without impunity — Don Bastiano too had been arrested in the same fashion, and poor father had been left behind in chains.

And continuing, he recounted for me the particulars of the catastrophe. Don Bastiano had serenely extended his wrists for the manacles, "glad to contribute, through his own sacrifice, to completing the series of sufferings demanded by Providence for the redemption of his Country." But father had wanted to break his bamboo cane across the shoulders of the ruffians, and it was only with considerable effort that the caresses of his family had been able to calm him.

I reflected for a moment, and out of that reflection came light: the important thing was to bring mother home safely. We would think of the rest afterwards.

But the poor woman, shaken beyond measure by the terrible ordeal, rendered still more helpless by the darkness, stumbled at every step. I handed one of my pistols to Giustiniano and whispered in his ear: "Go ahead of us. Think of whom you are defending."

The di Rudios are not to have their toes trodden upon with impunity — he was satisfied with that.

I bent down, and before mother had time to realise what was happening or to resist, I had lifted her into my arms and walked on, proud to carry the one who had once carried me in her womb, and with how much greater a travail.

At the first uncertain light of a street lantern, I set the sainted woman down. I overcame my own resolve with difficulty, pressing upon her brow a long kiss. I sensed already that what lay ahead would bring grave misfortune — two women left to themselves, and such women! "For my own part, I do not intend to leave the vicinity of Belluno until I have looked well into the heart of these troubles." And I promised her that, however adverse fortune might prove, I would always conduct myself in such a manner that, when we met again, or when she thought of her love for me, her conscience would not reproach her as unworthy of it.

At the place where we had paused to rest, I spoke to Giustiniano in a sharp voice. "The situation admits of no delay. I choose as my refuge the house of Domenico. That man is made of the right stuff and will not betray us. The road offered me excellent cover. I told Giustiniano that no one expected me where I was going. If he discovered anything new, he was to inform me. My intentions? He would learn them upon his return."

I did not wait long. Mother and Giustiniano had reached home without encountering a living soul. My sister Luisa had prepared her a good cup of coffee; the poor woman showed herself, if not resigned, at least calm.

I left them below, in the oppressive darkness, and found once more that crisp and pure air, the glittering sky — and what became of all those anxieties that had troubled me! I had set down no burden along the ascent; yet the weariness had suddenly vanished. I climbed with great strides, and at every step the serenity of the night sank deep into my thoughts; my spirit came at last to mirror the picturesque majesty of the mountain.

And Giustiniano's voice beside me spoke again: "The di Rudios are not to have their toes trodden upon with impunity — that is your maxim; but it is mine as well! Let us reckon up the sum: poor Achille has left his bones at Venezia; you are undoubtedly sought by the Police, hunted like a malefactor through the countryside."

The quiet of the night penetrated deep into my thoughts; my spirit ended by reflecting the picturesque majesty of the mountain.

---

What I am about to narrate I have drawn from letters written to me by Giustiniano. I have them impressed upon my memory in indelible characters.

In seeking to reassure mother, Giustiniano sketched the situation for her, tempering it with a generous measure of optimism. Luisa would undergo some questioning, but would soon be set free. Father was marked as an old patriot, an irreconcilable man, and would not manage to extricate himself so easily — but how to condemn him without evidence? The judicial authorities were rigorous beyond all telling; yet they were anxious to appear irreproachable, and would not dare commit an open infamy. As for himself, he had personally accompanied me as far as the mines at Agordo. From that point, I had taken paths known only to me and to the chamois. By now I must already have crossed the frontier, and the Police still believed me to be in Belluno and were searching for me there. Before long, letters would be received from Switzerland, or from some other country over which the yellow and black did not fly. All that remained, then, was not to give way to despair, so as to preserve one's health — so necessary in that storm.

But the confidence he laboured to instil in his mother's heart was far from dwelling in his own. The newspapers, subject to rigorous censorship, seemed to concur in treating the latest conspiracy with a conspiracy of silence. Yet news finds its way a great distance even without the aid of newspapers. Now from the lips of trusted friends encountered along the road, now at the café, gathered around a table, between one remark about the fine weather and another about the price of agricultural produce, there slipped into his ear those words which could not be openly spoken without danger — and from them, as always in such cases, the hopeful reassurances appeared barely sketched in, whilst the sinister predictions were laid on in heavy strokes.

One evening, those predictions seemed to find in the facts a tragic fulfilment. "Among the gorges of the Alps, a patrol had barred the path of the fleeing patriot — the order to surrender disregarded — the noble heart broken by Austrian lead — the series of generous impulses severed for ever. That very night, the body would arrive in Belluno for the formalities required by Law."

Giustiniano hastened home to prevent the sad news from reaching his mother through incautious lips. He found her — he would have had time enough, and more, to weep. He walked the length and breadth of the room until very late; then, overcome by weariness, he sank into an armchair and fell asleep.

Through the half-opened shutters the first light of dawn barely peered, and already mother, impatient for news, was bringing him his usual cup of coffee. She looked at him, and guessing at the sleepless, anguished night, instead of waking him she went to the untouched bed, drew from it a coverlet and spread it over his knees and chest. At that very moment, the doorbell rang with urgent force.

Giustiniano started awake, looked about him, remembered, understood. He threw his arms about the poor woman's neck and said to her:

---

She was brought to a large, cold room. From the opposite wall there jutted a plank, one end of which rested upon a trestle; upon the plank lay a sheet; beneath the sheet, with sinister uncertainty, the outlines of a man in the rigidity of death could be made out. A soldier, his rifle at the order, stood stiffly beside it. Another, in a simple waistcoat, his sleeves rolled up, his left hand plunged into a large boot upon which his eyes were fixed intently, was working his brush with savage energy and splattering it. Four officers, arranged in a circle, were conferring in low voices. A burning brazier adulterated, with its fitful glow, the clear light of morning.

At the entrance of the mother, the officers broke off their conversation and turned their faces toward that austere apparition. She appeared not to hear; her whole spirit was concentrated in her eyes, which blazed beneath the black veil.

Then a single sob rang out in the sepulchral silence — one only — but it was enough to shake a heart.

And with a voice in which vibrated the muted threat of an entire people, she said: "No — this poor wretch is not my son."

I was obliged to exert myself to calm that youthful fever, and, insisting upon the impropriety of abandoning two women — and what women! — to themselves in moments of grave calamity, I made my case. I prevailed with difficulty. "Besides, I do not intend to leave the vicinity of Belluno until I have looked well into the heart of these troubles. And where find a more trustworthy person than yourself to serve me as informant and, should occasion require, as intermediary?"

In the bewilderment of his young triumph, he rose. She walked toward the door. The soldier in the waistcoat blew a warm breath on his boot and laid on the brush with strokes more furious than ever — he was wishing, perhaps, in that moment, that the barracks and the Empire were concentrated in that muddy toecap. The stiff soldier beside the corpse dared not draw breath. The situation, like a uniform awaiting a command, admitted of no delay. "I choose Domenico's house as my refuge. That peasant is made of the right stuff and will not betray us. I shall await you there tomorrow. Come by a wide circuit and make sure no one follows you. I shall judge by your shrewdness whether you are worthy of greater enterprises."

And having pressed his hand, I turned my back — and they had been borne in thought, perhaps, to some far country beyond the Alps, where other mothers suffered.

---

I left below the dark plain, the air thick and heavy; I found again that crisp and pure air, the glittering sky — and what became of all those anxieties that had troubled me! I had set down no burden along the ascent; yet the weariness had suddenly vanished. I climbed with great strides, and at every step the serenity of the night penetrated more deeply into my thoughts; my spirit ended by reflecting the picturesque majesty of the mountain.

I recognised Domenico's small cottage, backed against the cliff, the dark doorway beneath the pergola, now stripped of its clusters and leaves — the pergola that stretched as far as the road and divided the little kitchen garden's clearing into equal parts. From the foot of the old walnut tree, whose trembling boughs tickled the roof of the cottage, two hounds launched themselves barking; but they soon abated their impetuosity upon hearing themselves called by name. They followed in my footsteps, stretching out their muzzles and anxiously nudging my calves.

One, two, three knocks at measured intervals — sleep clings tenaciously to mountain labourers. A fourth, a fifth reverberated through the void. At last, through the cracks of the old door, a light filtered through; two wooden clogs advanced noisily.

"Open up, Domenico. It is Carletto."

The door creaked, and I was left as though dazzled by the lantern which, with a sudden movement, was raised from the ground and threw a beam of light into my face.

"Carletto! — Taller, fiercer, darker, with the goatee — but Carletto! And I had reckoned him at the ends of the earth! Our elders were right: only the mountains stay in their place. But what brings you here? At this hour! Have you lost your way on the path?"

I did not reply, but pointing to the right hand of the good old man, which gripped a billhook: "Were you meaning to cut off my head?"

In the kitchen with its smoke-blackened walls, beneath the wide chimney-hood, a fire crackled briskly — enough to roast a kid. We sat one facing the other, in the lateral recesses hollowed into the thick wall. And while I made clear to him the situation, he tormented the embers with the nozzle of the bellows-pipe. When he heard that father was in chains, he drove that iron implement with a formidable thrust into the log, which showered sparks. From the clenching of his jaws and from the fury in his eyes, I would have said that the blow was intentionally aimed at the head of some villain. He said to me:

"You did well to come up here. From our clearing, the valley can be commanded for more than five miles. They have barely set foot outside Belluno before we know already where they mean to cast their net. With five miles in hand, there is no candle-eating bureaucrat who can outrun an Italian's legs — not to mention that the mountain is thick with our people on every side. And should the devil help them and they came upon us unawares — well, there is a remedy for that too! Do you recall the old cave, where I used to drive you as punishment when you ruined my almond trees? A single leap from the house; the entrance narrow and so well hidden that you would not notice it even if you knocked your nose against it. In a desperate case, one man alone with an old musket, a rusty old thing, could ruin the stomach of half Bohemia there. There is no denying it — you showed sound sense in taking refuge up here."

He paused a moment as if suspended in thought, then added: "Here I am chattering, and you need rest. I shall go and rouse the old woman. Half an hour more or less — what does it matter? She must prepare the mash for the cow, which is near her time anyway. For tonight you must content yourself with sleeping in our own bed — we will see about making up something a little more fitting afterwards."

And as I protested that I was hardened to far greater fatigues, and that I preferred to keep watch beside the fire, he assumed the air of one who has been touched on the raw, and said by way of excuse: "In our own bed — but have no fear! — We shall put freshly laundered sheets on it!"

That same day, in the afternoon, Giustiniano arrived up there. He had left the city on the pretext of going after chaffinches and goldfinches; he carried with him the decoy owl, the perch and the bird-lime. Nothing suspicious in his long circuit; but the news he brought was very grave. A police raid, a full-scale search; mother in terrible agitation — the more so because Luisa had kept a highly compromising letter. Fortunato Calvi had a sincere admiration for Luisa, a friendship that led him to confide in her, as in a sister, his most intimate thoughts. In those days he had sent his instructions to father, addressing them, in order the better to deceive the Police, precisely to Luisa. They were written in veiled language, but not so veiled that they would not have aroused the suspicions of anyone who already had an inkling of our plot. Moreover, yielding to the impulses of his heart, Calvi, in a brief postscript, had expressed the hope that, "the Fatherland once freed, nothing more would stand between him and his union with Teresa Duodo," a gentle young lady from Padova who adored him and whom he adored.

Fortunately my sister, seizing her opportunity, had swiftly thrown the letter into father's guitar — a great plucker of strings was father! And the searchers, who had ransacked every corner of the house and every crack, had paid no heed to that humble instrument hanging on the wall.

They were already preparing to leave, the two women were already beginning to breathe freely, when the Inspector, who had undoubtedly received precise orders, declared Luisa under arrest. A long embrace — then mother had fallen back in her armchair, seized by a terrible nervous crisis.

On hearing that news, it seemed to me that the heavy air of the plain had risen to the mountain and was suffocating me. What purpose did it serve to linger any longer in the Cadore? I was held back by a chivalrous feeling which made me regard as cowardice the seeking of my own safety while my loved ones were persecuted and exposed to every danger; I was held back also by the hope, however vague and fleeting, that Fortunato Calvi and Giuseppe Mazzini, learning of the ill turn taken by their plans, would set about righting them and would have need of me. But events, tumbling forward, were soon to sweep away even those scruples.

One morning I was walking a short distance from the farmhouse and, by instinct, studying the terrain with a tactical eye, when, rounding a crag, I came face to face with an Austrian officer. I do not believe he noticed my agitation, for he greeted me in a manner of irreproachable courtesy and told me he had set out from Belluno with other officers before sunrise. He had strayed from his companions in pursuit of a covey of partridges. He asked me, in conclusion, whether I had seen his hunting companions pass through those parts.

Having received a negative reply, he offered me a cigar and then, with a certain wonder: "Do you live in these solitary places? One needs real spirit not to grow bored here!" Before I could reply, some rifle shots echoed from crag to crag. He turned his thumb over and said: "Those are my friends!" He saluted and moved off with long strides.

A few hours later Giustiniano arrived up there. I understood, from the mere sight of him, that some other unhappy news was pressing upon his heart. He told me that, early that morning, a clerk of the Police — in whom the trade had not paralysed every honestly sensitive fibre — had knocked at our door. He had confided to mother that positive word had reached his office that I was to be found in Belluno, or in its surroundings, for purposes that could not be welcome to the Royal Imperial authorities. These had issued a peremptory order to seize me, dead or alive. We should govern ourselves accordingly, and with promptness. He had done his duty as a good Italian — he trusted that, once he had left the premises, we should know how to do ours, maintaining, even under oath, that we had never so much as laid eyes on him.

In my mind, the chance encounter with the Austrian officer and what Giustiniano had told me linked themselves together of their own accord. If the Police want me dead or alive, they will have circulated my description to their direct and indirect agents. And if the officer, explaining his absence to his fellow soldiers, has spoken of me —

The thread was slender; but it would have sufficed to weave a noose — mine.

I turned to Giustiniano: "Assure mother that tonight I shall not sleep in Domenico's house. Tomorrow, at first light, I shall be far away."

The sun had barely set when I was already seeking refuge in the ancient cave, past which I had never walked as a child without a sense of profound apprehension. I entered it cautiously, without a light; then I replaced the ivy that concealed its mouth. From that hiding place, peering through the foliage, I found myself looking directly at Domenico's kitchen window — without bars and without shutters — which lay spread before my eyes as clearly as though I were inside. Domenico, at that moment, was slicing some cheese — he was preparing my provisions for the next day's journey. Caterina was sitting in a recess by the hearth, telling her rosary beads.

Suddenly the dogs barked. This was not unusual. At evening, foxes roam the mountain, and the hounds, scenting them from a distance, keep them at bay with incessant circling. But this time, as their barking grew gradually more furious, it gave way to yelping, and there was a circumstance extremely suspicious: instead of running through the fields, they were retreating toward the house. They had before them, therefore, an adversary capable of commanding respect.

I strained my ear and was not long in detecting a measured tread, a dry clash of metal; then — between my eyes and the lighted window, several shadows filed past on tiptoe. Plumes and carbines erased from my mind even the last traces of doubt — the ruffians of Austria had surrounded the house.

Inside, the two honest souls appeared entirely unaware of the storm that had gathered around the old homestead. All at once, the scoundrels brought their rifle butts down upon the frail door, which flew open with a tremendous crash. I saw Domenico spring back, and instinctively his right hand, armed with the large knife, drew back to gather the impetus needed to strike. But four levelled muskets dispelled, with the terror of reality, the courage that imagination had put into him.

A lanky fellow, his moustaches twirled to a point and a bureaucrat's snout upon his face, made a gesture, and two gendarmes hurled themselves upon that bewildered man, manacled him, and tied him to a leg of the table.

No one had spoken. The lanky one snatched the dark lantern from a soldier, cast its light about, and catching sight of Caterina who, having let her rosary fall at her feet, had remained as if paralysed, said to her: "Get up and come with me." She did not stir from her rigidity and appeared not to understand, until the lanky one, grasping her by a shoulder, shook her, forced her to her feet, and dealt her a powerful blow with his fist between the shoulder blades. I saw Domenico grind his teeth distinctly.

The lanky one, a sergeant, disappeared through the opposite door, preceded by Caterina and followed by four of his men. I heard him give the order to put four ounces of lead in the stomach of anyone who attempted to escape; then a sound of heavy footsteps on the wooden staircase; then a muffled shifting of furniture, a banging of drawers on the flagstone floor. They searched every room, went down even to the cellar.

When the sergeant reappeared at the head of his men, he was wiping his pointed moustaches with the back of his hand — he had taken the trouble to make his search less tedious by tapping some barrel or other. But evidently the wine had not made his heart generous. He seized a chair and, planting it beside the prisoner, sat himself firmly astride it.

He began to speak with a hypocritical calm, and so quietly that the words reached me only now and then. I caught, at intervals, in an Italian larded with Venetian dialect and encrusted with Croatian flavouring, these more than significant phrases: "We did not climb all the way up here for the pleasure of being made fools of. — The mouse is here and His Majesty Emperor and King — *el aaron o un falò de la tua casa e di to stressi* [Either they find him or they'll burn your house and you with it]. — The dungeon, the club on your... Dead irons and the gallows!"

After this last phrase, he rested his fist on the back of the chair, his chin in the hollow of his fist, and waited. Domenico did not move a muscle.

The sergeant shouted: "*Orsù! bisogna farla finna!*" [Come now, let us have done with it!] And he sliced the air with one of those gestures of his that had the power to infuse a burst of life into the stiff automatons standing behind him.

Domenico was untied and dragged, with his thumbs twisted behind his back, into the middle of the kitchen. In less time than it takes to tell, one ruffian slipped a running noose around his neck; another threw the other end of the rope over a beam. Meanwhile the sergeant, having become extremely calm once more, had busied himself with lighting a cigar, and, tormenting its tip between his fingers and blowing words and smoke together, said almost syllable by syllable: "Either you sing — or I hang you up like a sausage."

The mountain man turned pale. Yet his face, ordinary if not common, appeared transfigured by a noble fierceness. He said: "You are mistaken. There is no one here. But you are the stronger — do as you please." He had barely finished when two thugs, seizing the end of the rope, hauled it in slowly, slowly. The poor man's arms struggled with a supreme effort to free themselves, his chest swelled horribly, his face turned livid. When his tongue was already protruding from his twisted mouth, they suddenly let him drop. His wooden clogs struck the floor with violence and his knees buckled under him, as though he were a rag doll. The noose was at once slackened.

A brief silence followed, during which Domenico's face became of a cadaverous pallor.

"What do you think of my medicine? Has it brought you to your senses? Are you now prepared to reveal the truth to me?"

Domenico gave an affirmative nod of his head.

"Well and good!" continued the sergeant. "Give him a sip of water." And he blew a long, thin mouthful of smoke — it was a quick way of expressing his inward satisfaction.

But Domenico straightened himself and, stepping forward as far as the ruffians holding the rope allowed him, snarled through clenched teeth: "For you, and for all the carrion that has befouled my Country!" And he launched a broad spit full upon his tormentor's chest. The man gave a spasmodic start and burst into a stream of exotic oaths. But with a supreme effort he contained his intense fury and dissembled it with the slowness of his gesture and an icy tone. He said: "Hang that toad."

The end of the rope was hauled in slowly, slowly —

From my cave I watched and reflected. More than once I had tormented the butt of my pistol, but a whole, imperious voice had cried out to me: "Do not act rashly!" A corpse, two corpses, a spectacular revenge — to what end? I resolved to play the last desperate card only when the game was irretrievably lost. My anguish at Domenico's torture was free of apprehension on my own account. I knew the fibre of that fierce old man and I sensed that he would not betray me. But Caterina — the horrible spectacle, the certainty that a single word could end that atrocious agony — how would her woman's heart hold out, her companion's heart?

Caterina ran frantic from wall to wall; she invoked, waving her arms, the Virgin and the saints. And when, seized by a sudden frenzy, she made to throw herself with her nails upon the impassive tormentors and the iron arm of a gendarme had thrust her back, almost to the ground, she shrieked and tore her hair out in handfuls. But the secret kept deep in her breast — the more furiously the tempest of the spirit rages, the more tightly it clings to the flesh — and deep it remained.

Domenico was lowered for the second time. He was so far gone that the renewed questions reached his brain in all probability too indistinctly for him to be able to answer. The sergeant called his henchmen into consultation. They must have concluded that the mountain man knew nothing, that the Royal Imperial Police had caught a crab. In the still air, orders rang out; about the farmhouse the measured tread of the "armed force" disposing itself for departure could be heard. A few minutes, and all was silence —

Domenico turned his searching eyes about him, and his chest swelled with deep sighs. Then he rose, looked at his companion with a long, intense gaze, and stroked her head. A little while later, having taken up his knife again, he returned to slicing his cheese, as though nothing extraordinary had occurred. Caterina gathered up the rosary from beside the hearth and began to tell it once more — this time, on her knees.

I allowed as much time to pass as seemed sufficient to assure me that the accursed pestilence had truly gone back down the slope; then I pushed aside the ivy and appeared upon the threshold. Caterina did not so much as turn. Domenico contented himself with opening the front of his shirt and pointing to the weal scored into his neck by the noose. I took him in my arms and, pressing my lips to that livid furrow, I kissed it again and again, sobbing.


### Chapter Twenty

<!-- pages:98-100 -->

WHAT I am about to relate I gathered from the letters Giustiniano wrote me. They are impressed upon my memory in indelible characters. To hearten our mother, Giustiniano sketched the situation for her with a generous infusion of optimism. Luisa would undergo some interrogation, but would soon be set at liberty. Papa had the reputation of an old patriot, an irreconcilable man, and would not find it so easy to extricate himself. But how to condemn him without proof? The judicial authorities were rigorous beyond all telling; yet they were anxious to appear upright to the last, and would not dare commit so manifest a knavery. As for me, he himself had accompanied me as far as the mines of Agordo. From that point, I had chosen paths that only I and the chamois knew how to travel. By now I must already have crossed the border, while the Police still believed me in Belluno and were searching for me there. Before long, they would receive letters from me posted in Switzerland, or from some other country where the black and yellow did not fly. Everything, then, depended upon not losing heart, so as to preserve one's health, so necessary in that tempest.

But the confidence he laboured to instil in his mother's soul was far from dwelling in his own. The newspapers, subject to rigorous censorship, seemed agreed in treating the latest conspiracy to a conspiracy of silence. Yet news finds its way a very long distance even without the aid of the press. Now from the lips of trusted friends encountered along the road, now at the café, around a table, between one remark about fine weather and the next about the price of farm produce, there would slip into his ear those words which could not be spoken openly without peril — and from them, as ever in such cases, the hopeful assurances appeared barely sketched in, while the sinister predictions were laid on with a heavy brush.

One evening, it seemed as though these predictions had found, in the facts themselves, a tragic fulfilment. "Among the Alpine gorges, a patrol had barred the path of the fleeing patriot... the order to surrender disregarded... the noble heart shattered by Austrian lead... the long series of generous impulses cut short for ever. That very night, the corpse would arrive in Belluno for the formalities required by Law."

Giustiniano hastened home to prevent the sad news from reaching his mother through an unwary mouth. He found her already abed. Wake her? To what end? She would have more than enough time to weep. He paced the room up and down until very late; then, overcome by weariness, he let himself fall into an armchair and slept.

Through the half-closed shutters, the first light of dawn barely filtered in, when his mother, impatient for news, brought him his customary cup of coffee. She saw him, and divining the sleepless, anguished night, instead of waking him, went to the undisturbed bed, took from it a coverlet and spread it over his knees and chest. At that very moment, the doorbell rang furiously.

Giustiniano started awake, saw, remembered, understood. He threw his arms about the poor woman's neck and said to her:

"Mamma, my poor mamma, be strong and prepare yourself for the worst."

They departed in a carriage, a gendarme up on the box, an inspector at their side. No fits of hysteria, no sobbing, no silent tears. Giustiniano held tight to the right hand of the stricken woman, who paid him no heed, who remained motionless, her gaze fixed upon some distant point visible to her alone.

She was led into a large, cold room. From the opposite wall there jutted a table whose far end rested upon a trestle; upon the table a sheet was spread; beneath the sheet, with sinister uncertainty, the form of a man in the rigidity of death was outlined. A soldier, rifle at the order, stood stiffly erect beside it. Another, in his underjacket, sleeves rolled up, his left hand plunged into a great boot upon which he kept his eyes intent, was working the brush furiously, buffing it with savage strokes. Four officers, arranged in a circle, were conferring in low voices. A burning brazier tainted, with its lurid glow, the clear light of morning.

At the mother's approach, the officers broke off their conversation and turned their faces toward that stern apparition, which advanced to the foot of the corpse with a firm, almost resolute step.

Someone spoke. He alluded to the painful necessity imposed upon him by duty, and lamented the untimely end of those misguided souls who lay hands upon the sacred majesty of the Law... She seemed not to hear; her whole being was gathered into those eyes that blazed beneath the black veil.

Then one stepped forward and with a sudden gesture drew back the sheet. A livid face appeared. At that, a single sob rang out in the sepulchral silence — one alone! — but it sufficed to resolve the tempest of a heart.

Mamma lifted her veil, gazed steadily at the officers, and with a voice in which vibrated the muted menace of an entire People, said: "No, this poor wretch is not my son! Carletto lives!... he lives, and he will avenge the anguish I have suffered, and he will avenge his Country!"

In the general stupefaction, maternal majesty had its triumph. She moved toward the door with as firm a step as that with which she had entered. The soldier in his underjacket blew warm breath upon the boot and laid on more savage strokes than ever... perhaps wishing at that moment that the barracks and the Empire were concentrated in that muddy toecap. The soldier standing stiffly beside the corpse did not stir a lash... an automaton in uniform, he awaited a command that did not come.

The four officers, suddenly grown pale, had their thoughts drawn perforce to a distant land, beyond the Alps, where other mothers suffered.

Into the cold surroundings of the barracks, into hearts hardened by iron discipline, accustomed to the fierce emotions of brutality and bloodshed, the gentle memories of childhood evidently crept, and rekindled within them their deadened pity. For a moment, the despot's minions became once more men of the People. The stern apparition — unmolested, admired — disappeared!


### Chapter Twenty-One

<!-- pages:100-108 -->



From the heights overlooking the mines of Agordo, I cast a last glance at my brother, who was growing smaller in the plain below. Then I set my feet resolutely upon the mountain path, beginning a flight that was to prove full of emotion and truly singular in character.

Having reached the zone of perpetual snows, I slackened my pace, both from weariness and the increasing difficulties of the way, but also because I felt hidden and made safe by the vastness of space and solitude. It had been forty-eight hours since I had closed my eyes, and I felt myself terribly disposed to that morbid sleep which frost and altitude can render deadly. At a certain point, I was as though seized by the vertigo of the sublime; my legs would no longer support me and I fell prostrate upon the snow.

Now let one observe how far the dream — or rather, it is the mountain that comes rushing toward me — and the real are able to intertwine and confound one another! I slept and was incapable of moving a muscle... no doubt on that score. And yet the mind, vigilant within the inert body, retained consciousness of that boundless whiteness, of that fearful solitude; I felt the frail heart beating, the icy wind blowing around me and, amid the savage images of life pressing upon one another in the brain, the idea of my danger predominated, rigid and immovable as a boulder.

For I saw myself clearly — and I run, I run with the *sbirri* at my heels. I run, I run, I run: or rather, it is the mountain that comes rushing swiftly toward me. But the constables are equally swift — faster, even... already I hear the crunch of snow beneath their boots; already the foul mouths thunder their halt! and I run, run... Several shots rang out in my head!...

A single instant and I was on my feet, awake — perfectly awake! The shots still echoed from crag to crag. I peered down into the valley and made out a line of soldiers running along the edge of the forest, rifles in hand. They stopped and formed a circle around a prone, darkly clad human figure. I noted the agitated gestures; then I saw, or thought I saw, a soldier prod the fallen man's flesh with his bayonet, as though searching it for some last remnant of life.

A smuggler, I thought — mistaken, in these days of fearful suspicion, for game of greater importance. It was the very man who, stiff and senseless, was afterwards to cause such anguish to my poor mother.

I crossed the summit with all possible speed. The sky remained splendid; the snow, greatly hardened on the northern slope, no longer gave way beneath my feet. I received more than honest welcome, and bread and milk from a mountain peasant; then at Neumark I generously dissolved my cheese with an inward libation of excellent beer. At dusk I set foot in Bolzano.

If a crowd is good for concealment, I could not have arrived at a more opportune moment. For several days the life of the entire district had been flowing into Bolzano, drawn thither by the annual fair. In those times and in those places of difficult communication, fairs took on the clamorous character of village festivals. Brokers and dour farmers in pursuit of the florin; mountain women beautiful with that rustic beauty derived from open air and sun — having obtained their plenary indulgence, they departed in great throngs, singing in chorus. There was a reek of barrels issuing in gusts from the taverns; there were herds of heifers and oxen that opened a solemn passage for themselves through the human herds; mountebanks who drew the public with the grimaces of their painted faces, to the sound of the bass drum; sacks heaped in piles and ironware spread upon the flagstones, and baskets heaped with fruit and boards of sweetmeats sheltered beneath enormous umbrellas; and a press and crush of people and a buzzing of voices and a riot of colours... and above it all, conciliating every base human discord, a powerful wave of bells ringing out in celebration.

I arrived, not without making free use of my elbows, at the main square, entirely encumbered with stalls and vendors. At one corner of the square stood the inn of the "Queen of England"... along one of its sides it flanked the principal street, and it was upon this side that the main entrance opened.

I entered, went up to the first floor, and found myself face to face with a *chellerina* — a barmaid — with a rosy little face and a white apron... only to be told that all the rooms were taken, that I must content myself with sharing a bed with some other traveller — and good luck to me in finding an obliging companion for the bargain!

"Very well! In any case I can begin with a good supper."

I was shown into the dining room. By instinct I took the first table on the left, near the entrance. I was alone. The spacious room was dimly illuminated by a lamp. In its reflection I recognised upon one wall the effigy of Ferdinand, the emperor who remembered for the last time that he was a despot when he demanded, upon his deathbed, a dish of gnocchi with butter. I looked, but in vain, for that of Francesco Giuseppe... he had perhaps not yet found the means of making his way so far.

But I found something worse. Beside the door was pasted a large notice upon which the double-headed eagle appeared conspicuously. Amid the dark and crowded lines, standing out in larger characters, was a name well known to me — that of... Carlo Camillo di Rudio. The devil! What is this? I sprang to my feet and read. Austria was placing a bounty of six thousand florins upon my head. To take me dead or alive... to denounce me!... a trifling endeavour, the duty of an honest citizen — for any of those zealots of order and the Law. By how slender a thread my safety hung!...

I should be lying if I said I dined with a tranquil mind. If being in a crowded centre had seemed to me a short while before my salvation, now I perceived in it the greatest of dangers. How was I to escape unnoticed from that vortex? Was it possible that among so many people there was not a single soul who knew me? Who might guess the truth by some instinct?

Almost by way of answer, upon the stairs, mingling with the varied tread of footsteps, there sounded voices speaking the Bellunese dialect — voices which, I could have sworn, were not unknown to me. I hid my face in my right hand and peered anxiously through my fingers. The door flew open; several strangers entered, and behind them, one after another, four sturdy figures... the smugglers of the Trevis valley — the Burrasca brothers.

They looked at me, recognised me; but gave no sign. They sat down at the neighbouring table, and the eldest, who was at my side, touched my foot with his foot; then, without turning his face, pretending to speak to his brothers: "I am pleased to see you. Given the rumours going round..."

At that moment the *chellerina* with the rosy face and white apron entered. She lit another lamp... the letters of the notice with the double-headed eagle darkened energetically, almost doubling their eloquence.

I said: "Saved!... only to leap from the frying pan into the fire." And pointing to the notice: "What a fine prize the lot of you might claim!"

Four faces darkened. The eldest of the Burrasca, striking his fist through the air: "Morsels seasoned with shame do not pass down this throat!"

And another of the brothers, in a deep bass voice: "In the veins of the Burrasca runs no traitor's blood!"

They had occasion to prove it to me. A few minutes more and through the wide room spread the reek of gendarmerie. One brute remained upon the threshold and, his back against the doorpost, blocked the exit. Another brute stepped forward and asked me, in a dry tone, for my passport. I drew it from my wallet and was unfolding it when he, losing patience, snatched it from my hand. He scanned it, stepped closer to examine it better by the lamplight, and ended by saying: "I do not understand Italian. You must come with me to the gendarmerie."

"Hold firm!" cried one of the Burrasca to me — he who had clearly understood the perils of the fine business being proposed to me.

One instant... one stroke! I snatch the passport back from the importunate fellow and, before he can react, the smuggler's fist strikes him like a mallet beneath the chin. He fell, his head struck the brick floor, and he moved no more.

Meanwhile, another smuggler who had drawn near to the threshold, on the pretext of reading the terms of the bounty, deals his blow upon the teeth of the brute who was blocking it... blood spurted from his mouth, from his nose; he swayed and went down in a heap.

"Wings to your feet!" was the cry. But I needed no urging... already I was taking the stairs four at a time, pistol in hand — a providential inspiration! A third gendarme was on guard at the foot of the stairs. He turned, saw me, saw the weapon, understood its silent threat reinforced by an energetic oath. He let me pass without lifting a finger, and I made my way off, retreating with the weapon aimed.

But in the compliance of that constable lay no small measure of strategy... he had conjured away his own danger and had driven me into a greater trap. A fourth gendarme, having heard the tumult on the floor above, had run to summon the intervention of the patrol. Both were advancing at the double, while I turned the corner onto the square, and it was nothing short of a miracle that I did not fall straight into their arms. In front of me the patrol, behind me the gendarme on guard who had followed me and was gesticulating furiously, pointing me out.

Night had fallen. The square, encumbered with stalls and people, was lit by lanterns of many colours, in the Venetian manner. With a sidewise movement, I plunged into that maze. "Stop him, stop him! Catch him, catch him!" But in the confusion, no one thought to act. As for the soldiers, held compact by the virtue of discipline and hampered by their arms, they contented themselves with pursuing me at a run along the sides of the square... I slipped through and crossed it.

Then, either put on my trail or having been told I had turned down a certain alleyway, they divided into two detachments; one rushed at my heels, the other went round the block and ran to the far end of the same alley, to bar my way and catch me between two fires.

They ended by finding themselves face to face with one another.

In the entryway of a house, my eye to the keyhole, I watched their movements, noted the faces, heard the words. "Der Teufel! Has the earth swallowed him up?" They discussed the matter for a moment and concluded that I must be — heaven be thanked for it! — a cutpurse. The police had spread good nets throughout the whole township and, sooner or later, I would be caught in them. They reformed their ranks and moved away.

Without a special warrant, the constables could not, at that hour, violate a private dwelling. I felt myself temporarily safe.

I climbed the stairs to the third landing — the last. There were four doors: two facing each other, one to the right, one to the left. I listened... absolute silence. I therefore sat down upon the steps and, my elbows on my knees, my face in my palms, began to reflect. Imperceptibly, my thoughts darkened and I fell asleep.

When I returned to the reality of things, the first light of dawn was revealing itself through the skylight, and already beyond the door on the left one could hear voices alternating in brief, indistinct exchanges, and the clinking of plates and crockery.

I rose, brushed down my trousers, and was about to descend when the door opened and a woman of uncertain age, in a short dress, saw me and gave a cry.

"What is the matter?" demanded a gruff masculine voice from within. Before I could open my mouth, I found before me a stocky man in his shirtsleeves, an apron at his sides, an awl in his fist.

I urged them not to be alarmed. I was a cattle dealer arrived from Switzerland the previous evening at a late hour. For all the inns I had gone the round of, I had been unable to find a bed, even offering to pay through the nose for one. And rather than spend the night in the open air...

"For the love of Christ!" said the man. "From the pavement to a landing... what difference does it make? Come in, you shall warm yourself with a cup of coffee in our company."

I offered to add a drop of *acquavite* to it and drew out my purse, which to the eye appeared well stocked.

A contest in three ensued... the cobbler affecting to take offence at my thinking him capable of demanding payment for an act of kindness; the woman drawing back with gestures of mock modesty, unwilling to let my gold coin touch her forearm and hand; I rebuffing the man's protests with a succession of "But really! come now! come now!" and pressing the woman, doing her a courteous violence. I ended by triumphing, and then added: "Indeed, to speak truly, the cold has given me an appetite, such an appetite!... and, in the town, in these days, one eats... one eats wretchedly! If you would be so kind as to fetch a few morsels... whatever you like. A little bite does no harm to friendship!"

When the woman had gone down a flight or two of stairs, I raised my voice and called her back to commend to her the portentous virtue of holding one's tongue. In Bolzano I was not without friends, and if word reached their ears that I had preferred sleeping on a landing to disturbing them...

It was an imprudence — ninety-nine times in a hundred, the most effective means of making a woman gossip is to bid her hold her tongue. But my good fortune was to ensure that it was precisely from that crack that my salvation would spring.

No sooner had my hostess reached the street and the door was heard to slam, than the cobbler took me by the sleeve and, dragging me inside into the kitchen, fixed me with a long gaze without saying a word. Then, all at once: "You have been telling me stories. You, a cattle dealer! You are a deserter!"

— Hush! How did you come to know it?

By way of all reply, he twisted his lips and pointed his forefinger at his own right eye. Which, among all peoples known to man, signifies: "I have too much salt in my pumpkin to let myself be bamboozled."

With the conversation set upon that ground, I found myself, as they say, at home, nor was I short of arguments and precise details with which to entangle my host ever more deeply in his own mistaken assumption. And he, as he listened to me, showed me his growing confidence by winking and giving me certain little claps on the shoulder that were meant to carry the full air of a demonstration of brotherly affection; for the deserter usurped, in my time, among those populations, the reputation that properly belongs to heroes, and to refuse him a helping hand tasted to them of cowardice.

Had my spirit not been terribly burdened, that would have been a day of rest. My host showed a cordiality that was rough but thoroughly genuine. However, too much spoils everything, and when, solicitous for what should come after, he began to devise plans for my escape, he was, all unwittingly, the cause of no small anxiety to me. In his enthusiasm, he never ceased from constructing schemes, each one more fantastical than the last, and on the pretext of knowing the district better and being more experienced in judging the temper of men, he insisted on forcing his hand upon me — I who, in that game, was staking my skin.

I ended by persuading him to hire a carriage and drive me out of the township in time to coincide with the change of the coach bound for Marano, which took place several miles away, in open country and well into the night.

"The police have spread good nets throughout the whole township," the corporal of the patrol had said on the previous evening. But no sooner had Bolzano and its fair subsided into slumber than I passed through them, mesh by mesh, almost in triumph, reclining upon the cushions of my carriage.

An incident not worth the telling caused me some little apprehension at Marano, and no more. But at Salzburg, in the German Tyrol, I ran a danger so great that my escape from it, without excessive effort, seems to me almost like a dream.

The postilion had pulled up at the Albergo della Posta, thus indicating to me the place where I was to spend the night, which was already well advanced. I went up at once to wash my hands and face in the room assigned to me and, when I was called for supper, I took the key from the lock but, carelessly, did not shut the door. Upon reaching the foot of the stairs — to recount these trifling details is necessary — I set my bag upon a small table and hung the key upon its hook.

In the dining room I found myself alone; but it was not long before there arrived the inevitable two carabinieri — unarmed this time. They ordered a jug of wine, a pack of cards, and struck up a game of tarocco. Supper being finished, I drew near to them as though interested in the game, and, to be brief, we ended by starting a three-handed game ourselves. We passed an hour in this manner, drinking to one another's health and trading jokes, until those tender exchanges between the devil and holy water were cut short by the landlady, who came to remind us that the Law is the Law and that the prescribed hour of closing had struck some minutes since.

I asked her: "At what hour does the coach depart?"

— At seven precisely!

— In that case, be so good as to wake me at four. I must go up the mountain to see about some heifers and I should like to be back in time to continue my journey by coach. May I sleep soundly?

— Between two pillows!

I took my leave of the gendarmes with great handshakes; but, scarcely into the corridor, my dark thoughts returned to me, and I went up the stairs without retrieving the key. Go back down? The door was ajar; I could bolt it from within with the latch. I entered, threw myself upon the bed and snuffed the candle.

It had been many nights since I had lain at full length, stretched out upon a soft mattress, with a good blanket over my legs. When I awoke, the sun, already well above the horizon, was beating full upon my face.

A curse upon the landlady! I descended with a troubled spirit and ears alert... the inn seemed deserted. Whereupon I, concentrating my fury in the knuckle of my fingers, knocked upon the small table so energetically that the candlesticks upon it shook.

And out came the old landlady from the kitchen, her face framed in her cap. She was neat, dressed in her Sunday best. At the sight of me she stopped short, open-mouthed. She suffered herself to be assailed by a tempest of reproaches; then, with perfect composure: "Good Lord! And whose fault is it? Your room key was there on its hook, and from this I concluded that you had already set out for the mountain. If you do not believe me, ask your two friends from last night. They came here at just about four o'clock. They wished to speak with you. I am sorry for it. I told them you were already well on your way."

— They came to speak with me? Prepare a good breakfast for three. I shall go and find them...

And I departed with all the appearance of a man at peace with the world. But the landlady was left with her trouble and her expense for nothing.

It was a Sunday in September. The population of the village had poured into the little church, which must have been packed to the walls, for a human swarm was crowding outside upon the small churchyard. In crossing the square, I saw the plume of a gendarme rising above those dark heads, and I was glad of it. "Here is one who will not arrest me!" And I added: "So true is it that even the Mass serves some purpose!"

Once clear of the inhabited place, I left the main road and struck out among the fields, along hedged lanes; then I took to the pine wood, which served me as a screen to perfection.

The nearest settlement was Muntzen, a cluster of houses straddling the border. It had, like Janus of ancient memory, two faces: one overcast, the other smiling — Austrian the former, Swiss and republican the latter. This I knew, and I knew moreover that the border line divided, politically speaking, the square and the village inn, which thus found itself under two jurisdictions. Hanging upon a wall of the common room was a clock with a cuckoo. The gendarmes of the Empire could not pass beyond that clock without provoking an international incident. On this side: the regime of the bludgeon, the manacles, the gallows; on the other: the regime of free men, the breath of full lungs, salvation!...

I crossed the frontier at an unfrequented spot; I entered the village and the inn from the Swiss side. Having set foot in the common room, I was searching with my eyes for the cuckoo clock when my attention was drawn to a group of *Jaeger* — the militiamen whom Austria employed for the guarding of the borders. Among them, sweet sight! the uniform and well-known face of one of the guardian angels who had come early that morning with the purpose of impressing the holy stigmata upon my wrists. I could not restrain myself from laughing heartily.

"Why, my friend! Poor sport this morning. And to think that the blackbird was in the cage. Had you climbed the stairs, the six thousand florins of the bounty would have found their way into your pocket sooner or later!"

He admitted that I had had the best of it and carried his amiability so far as to offer his congratulations. "Indeed, to show you my sincerity," he said, "come and let me stand you a bottle!"

— And I a barrel, if you like. But you must be pleased to come over to this side! And I rubbed my hands.

But that gay effervescence, so natural in one who leaves the open sea behind him, was for long months the last I knew; for long months, whenever I thought back upon it, I felt something like remorse.

The failure — already manifest to me — grew more pronounced with each piece of news I gathered as I advanced further into free territory, until at last it assumed the proportions of a true disaster, and my spirit was left as though overwhelmed by it.


### Chapter Twenty-Two

<!-- pages:108-112 -->



HISTORIANS affirm that the spy Felicità Buanvecchiato, having caught some fragment of a conversation held by Pietro Fortunato Calvi, hastened to reveal our attempt to the Authorities, who thus had full knowledge of it before we had even taken our first steps toward putting it into action.

How far that denunciation extended I cannot say; but I know that in bringing about our ruin it was very nearly superfluous.

How I made my way from London to Belluno without molestation or mishap, I have already told. Others, setting out from different points but with the selfsame objective, were not so fortunate.

The foremost of all the qualities required of a conspirator is the ability to judge at a glance which papers must be kept and which destroyed — a simple art, yet so exceedingly difficult. Ignorance of it has brought down countless enterprises that might have altered the course of History; it has furnished the executioner with innumerable victims.

Francesco Chinaglia and a certain Cerruti, both from Lonato, both of our number, crossed the Alps in perfect safety and arrived at a small village on the border of the Tyrol and the Cadore. Here they committed the fatal imprudence of raising their elbows excessively and picking a quarrel with the innkeeper. They were arrested, taken to the gendarmerie, and searched. What was this? An itinerary marked with initials and annotations that aroused some suspicion. The Police became alert and detained them, and then arrested, at brief intervals, Luigi Moratti of Castiglione delle Stiviere, one Roberto Morin, and a Fontana. Either they carried in their pockets the same compromising itinerary, or they were unable to give any satisfactory account of themselves. The official report states that these men were arrested simultaneously with Calvi, who had passed through those parts on the 17th of September. When the latter was interrogated, he passed himself off as a certain Meyer, a subject of the Duchy of Baden, and produced a corresponding passport; but it availed him nothing. He was taken to Police headquarters and searched, like the others — and from the lining of his overcoat was drawn, signed by Mazzini, by Kossuth, and by Ledru-Rollin, his appointment as commander-in-chief of the Italian revolution. It was Calvi himself who, from an excessively modest estimate of his own influence, had insisted upon obtaining that document. A formality as vain as it was fatal. Who would have contested the first place, denied obedience to the modern Leonidas, in that Cadore which was to have been his Thermopylae?

He was taken to Mantua. The grim walls of that fortress had already swallowed Don Bastiano Barozzi, and my father, and his sister Luisa. Poor Luisa! — her indomitable instinct for openness constrained within a living tomb; her delicate constitution tortured by an iron, inhuman discipline; her sensitive heart torn by the thought of her father's sufferings and by the unheard-of miseries that surrounded her; the modesty of her girlhood violated at every hour by the coarse searches of the turnkeys, insolent and brutal — not one word of comfort, no love, no light!

Was she compensated? Margherita di Savoia, having become Queen of Italy, was pleased to pay a visit to the women who had, for political reasons, savoured the delights of Mantua. She haughtily forgot Luisa of the Counts of Rudio. From her point of view, she was right. Our family had fought and suffered, in those dark times, that Italy might be; but it had not fought and suffered to round out the domains of the House of Savoy.

The Castello di San Giorgio at Mantua had by now become what the Spielberg had been in the days of Ferdinand. Other fortresses compressed within their dungeons the beating of hearts that yearned for the redemption of the Fatherland; but in none of them, as in Mantua, did the ferocity of the oppressor manifest itself with tragedies so vast, so sinister, so relentless. And yet, fatally, from that torment of bodies the Italian spirit blazed forth.
