In their fearful caution, the Culoz, the Kraus, the Benedek did their utmost to conceal from the civilised world the identity of those imprisoned and the terrible barbarity of their methods of procedure. But there are virtues that cannot be hidden, that shed their light beyond the walls of any Bastille, revealing by their very excellence the full wretchedness of the ephemeral superiority that has sought to crush them.

Italy saw, trembling, within the narrow compass of those secret cells, pass by without ceremonial pomp, in rags almost, yet no less untarnished than of old, the iron temper of her Attilio Regolo, of her Thrasea Paetus; no less serene than of old, the soul of more than one Socrates — and it was no small conspiracy toward finding herself again. An age of history drenched in martyrdom, it has found diligent scholars who coordinated its pages and documented with meticulous care its every phase — but not its own Tacitus to immortalise it in granite prose, to draw from it an austere monument worthy of so much greatness. Let him arise! — on that day, the thousands upon thousands of statues which Italy lavishes upon her crossroads will seem like toys for pygmies.

When Calvi was admitted to the Castello di San Giorgio, Don Giovanni Grioli, Pietro Frattini, Angelo Scarsellini, Count Carlo Montanari, Carlo Poma, Giovanni Zambelli, Don Enrico Tazzoli, Tito Speri — the hero of the Ten Days of Brescia — giants of duty, a chosen contingent from every class of society, had already been subjected, by the application of the noose, to the ultimate insult of despotism.

A designated victim, Felice Orsini was instead buried there, after some months, and precisely on the 28th of March 1855 — he who was afterward to regain his freedom by sawing through the double bars of a window and lowering himself on knotted sheets, injuring his foot in the descent, and who was to have so great an influence upon my own destiny.

Felice Orsini was for a certain period locked in Cell No. 9, where my father was confined with several other patriots. He told me, in 1857 in London, that the good old man, breaking the prison rules with the benevolent connivance of the warders, would help his fellow captives to endure the bitterness of their long imprisonment by gathering them about him and humming the romances then most in fashion.

Felice Orsini also had the opportunity of corresponding, by knocking upon the wall, with Pietro Fortunato Calvi. They encouraged one another, wishing each other an early release, free among the free. But the heroic defender of the Cadore harboured no illusions whatsoever. He knew the systems of government dear to Austria far too thoroughly, and from the same wretched end of the ten martyrs who had preceded him in that place, he divined his own inexorable condemnation.

Throughout the long, tedious trial, he displayed the imperturbable calm of a man accustomed, on the fields of battle, to look death in the face, and he kindled with noble indignation only when his judges, confronting him with an abject informer, were not ashamed to prefer the purchased word of that creature to his own — the word of a loyal enemy, a man of honour.

His participation in the movements aimed at overthrowing by force a tyranny that had imposed itself by force, he admitted without reservation; the companions who were arrested with him he defended to the last, affirming that they had joined him to take part in an alpine excursion, knowing nothing of his subversive designs; the secret of the other conspirators' complicity remained jealously guarded within his adamantine breast, and my father, and Luisa, and Don Bastiano Barozzi, held for a long time on mere suspicion, were afterward released by virtue of a decree of amnesty.

Arrived at the foot of the gallows, he took the cigar from his mouth and gave it, still lit, to one of his guards. He brusquely thrust away the arm of the man who wished to help him mount the board placed beneath the column from which he was to hang. From up there he looked, smiling, at the Austrian officers, several of whom had removed their hats in a sign of respect. And having kissed Monsignor Martini repeatedly — a priest held in low esteem, for his honesty, in those places where prebends are distributed — he said in a firm voice: "I am ready." Then the executioner set about his work.

As a crowning misfortune, the gallows was not proportioned to Calvi's great height, and his feet brushed the ground. His agony was long and exceedingly painful. Edoardo Winkler, commanding the military escort, was seized with fierce indignation and could not contain himself. He cursed the executioner and, when the execution was over, walked away, almost fleeing, his hands thrust through his hair.

Pietro Fortunato Calvi was the last of the great men slain for the Italian cause. He remained hanging for several hours; taken down at evening, he was thrown into a pit dug at the foot of the gallows.

But his greatness moved even a hyena — the infamous custodian Francesco Casati — who composed his remains in a coffin and preserved his relics for the veneration of a reborn Italy.

And the virtue which the Mantuan Bastille had not known how to conceal seemed to blaze forth from that humble pit dug at the foot of a gallows. His moral influence was so great, so intense the treasury of latent energies that it awakened, as to alarm the oppressors. Marshal Radetzky, already decrepit, already on the point of quitting forever the hateful contests of the world, was not ashamed to urge the Special Tribunal that had condemned Calvi to compose a libel, in order to paralyse the "deleterious" influence of that shade.

He was to find in President Visentin the base soul ready to second him. How many official lucubrations — which yet serve as guides for exalting or vilifying the figures belonging to history — repeat a similar origin and have no greater value than that of Visentin's!

The sycophancy that blossomed in the hour of triumph, busily and jealously attributing all the credit of Italy's redemption to the great and the small who knew how to exploit it, glides with the lightness of a bird over the beneficent influence exercised, beyond the tomb, by the martyrs who sacrificed their existence to rouse the indolent. It is an influence that may be felt by intuition, but that eludes all calculation — which makes it all the easier to adulterate the truth.

Nevertheless Calvi was fortunate in this respect: the rancour of the cliques and factions, which spared no adversary however great and unblemished, seemed to blunt itself before this figure so disinterested, so calm in its audacity. And it was said that Italy "would have had in the hero of the Cadore a second Garibaldi." But the opportunity of becoming one was criminally taken from him.


### Chapter Twenty-Three

<!-- pages:112-120 -->

I have reviewed the full translation against the Italian source and the Edgren dictionary. The translation is generally faithful and well-rendered. Two targeted revisions are warranted on the basis of the dictionary evidence.

**Change 1:** "a merenda" is rendered "at the midday break." Edgren defines *merenda* as "afternoon luncheon" — a specific meal later in the day, not a midday break.

**Change 2:** "qualcosa di eterogeneo" is rendered "something discordant." Edgren defines *eterogeneo* directly as "heterogeneous," which is both more accurate and period-appropriate.

All other paragraphs are reproduced exactly as in the current translation.

---



I pushed on as far as Zurich, where I found a nucleus of compatriots who had escaped, through exile, from the reactionary storm that had followed the upheavals of February. Among them was Radonitz, who received me with great courtesy. He made not the slightest allusion to the incident at Locarno. I also went to pay a visit to Caronti. His words could not have been more flattering; yet at the first sight of me, his face had assumed an expression of wonder not entirely free from a tinge of unease. Perhaps, I thought, it is merely my imagination. Giuseppe Mazzini had so thoroughly predisposed me against him!

That dealer in trinkets, who had gone bankrupt at Como, was living in Zurich in great luxury, at the expense of a wealthy lady who manufactured chocolate. He had, however, contrived to set up a modest felt-hat factory, and several Italians found therein the means to earn their keep. I became one of their number, and it seemed to me a ray of good fortune. But scenting the air, it was not long before I discovered something heterogeneous. There were handshakes that resolved themselves into a mere contact of skin; evasive replies; monosyllables, innocuous in themselves, but which acquired a shifty flavour from the grimace of the face that accompanied them. In sum, a coldness, a coldness! — even at the afternoon luncheon, when lips exchanged somewhat more spontaneous phrases, the mysterious threads that connect souls were wanting.

What was I being reproached for? Perhaps for having come to hover about their bread and to take a small portion of it for myself?

Things suddenly changed, and so radically, as to make me suspect an understanding. But what understanding, and why? The sharp edges vanished; they addressed me with the familiar *tu*; they tapped me confidentially on the shoulder, they offered me tobacco — it seemed they had finally resolved to regard me, in every respect, as one of their own. So much so that one Saturday they proposed a nocturnal pleasure-excursion on the lake. Four strokes of the oar, four tankards, or eight even, a patriotic chorus under the starry sky. It would be as if living again beyond the Alps, in the fair country where our sweethearts languished.

So as not to appear discourteous, I accepted. It so chanced that, in order to remedy some forgotten matter I no longer recall, I returned to the factory that very same evening. To my great surprise I found it lit up. Drawing nearer, I heard distinctly the agitated voice of the exile Grioli saying: "By God, I'll have no part of it! I should never again have the courage to think of my poor old mother!" I entered, and it seemed as though an iron hand clamped a gag upon every mouth. *(1)*

From that day forward no one spoke to me again of the lake, or of the tankards, or of the starlit sky. The reason I never knew, nor did I trouble to investigate it. Nevertheless I grew certain that the sudden cordiality of my companions had been feigned; that I found myself, like an intruder, alone and despised among them. I ended by feeling thoroughly disgusted and resolved to depart for Paris.

Without taking my leave? Not entirely. Caronti, honest or dishonest as he might be, had procured me work; I felt it my duty to pay him a farewell visit. This procured me yet another surprise. Upon my arrival in Zurich, he had received me with cheerful and courteous words, but with a face that betrayed his disillusionment; now, no sooner had he learned the purpose of my visit than he poured forth a deluge of phrases expressing regret; but his inward sensation would not stay suppressed, and stamped itself upon his face with signs that I deemed unmistakable. My departure caused him an intense pleasure.

I arrived in Paris in December 1853 with two francs in my pocket and a letter of recommendation for a certain Zappa, a Turinese hatter, which had been given me by my factory foreman — the only person in Zurich who had shown me any sympathy. The two francs did not carry me far; but the letter served me well. Zappa, a man of liberal opinions and a generous heart, offered me hospitality at once. One had to make do with sleeping on a mattress in the kitchen; but when one is penniless and in that season, the offer was far from contemptible. All the more so as I found means to repay the debt by giving Italian lessons to the lady of the house.

Signora Zappa was French. She held ideas that were more than liberal — they were revolutionary. They were enough to establish between us the most cordial of relations. She was not young, she was not beautiful; but she retained a grace whose charm it was not easy to resist. When she spoke of the 2nd of December, in which she had taken an active part, and of the fierce repressions from which, by good fortune, she managed to escape, she became eloquent and, in the heat of her recollections, forgot even the corsets she manufactured — to which, truth be told, she owed her popularity rather more than to her heroism.

So as not to live at the expense of those good people, I bestirred myself and at last found employment near the Pont de la Bastille. It brought me two francs a day. What employment? For fourteen consecutive hours I turned, by main force of arms, the wheel of a merry-go-round.

Having my hands on the wheel did not prevent me from having my eyes on politics. And at that period, even for those who were not in France, to occupy oneself with politics was to occupy oneself with Luigi Napoleone.

The coup d'état, regarded with execration by upright consciences, blessed by the Pope, was received by the other Sovereigns as a great feat of skill, combined with enviable perspicacity. The blood shed was not of the kind held to be blue, and the stupor that seized liberal France was, in their eyes, the restoration of peace. And since Luigi Napoleone delivered the reactionary Governments from the spectre of the Republic — too often confused, and artfully confused, with that of the Terror — it was entirely natural that the reactionary Governments should hasten to turn their benevolent gaze upon Luigi Napoleone, to recognise his advent, and to expect great things of him.

After the coup d'état, the Dictatorship! Napoleon was too shrewd to abuse it without setting against his odious persecutions a series of measures calculated to beguile the multitude. France was overflowing with hands ready to man the barricades. Unable to put them in chains, he provided, with shrewd haste, to win them over to his side. Prompted by an impulse emanating from Paris, every centre in France, great and small, was seized by the fever of public loans and public improvements. And the wave of well-being that spread through the working classes received a sensible increase from the sanitary laws designed to improve their housing; from the reshaping of taxation, which, lifted from articles of primary necessity, was made to bear upon those of luxury. To this was added the liberal extension of agricultural loans.

Throughout all France, the cannon-shots had thus been succeeded by the hammer-blows, and the moral effect that naturally flowed from the improved economic conditions was reinforced by splendid promises that kept hope fresh and green, that led men to suppose this unexpected period of extraordinary prosperity would endure. Luigi Napoleone, if one took him at his word, abhorred war. He proposed to make many conquests, but they were to be the conquests of Peace — lands to be reclaimed, vast tracts to be opened to cultivation, harbours to be deepened, canals to be dug, rivers to be made navigable. In these useful and glorious undertakings, the sword would yield its primacy to the tools of labour, and every peasant, every workman, animated by good purpose, would become his faithful warrior.

Ideas worthy of praise! But in order to lend them credence, it was necessary to erase from one's mind every bitter memory; to lose sight of the fact that this man of fate spent half his day in disguising, with flattering phrases, the wicked actions he reserved for the other half.

Unfortunately, from the multitudes, constrained almost perpetually to devote every energy to satisfying, with difficulty, the needs of the stomach, it is not reasonable to expect a cult of the ideal that is far-sighted and steadfast. The popular soul does not advance to its sacred claims save under the impulse of exceptional circumstances, and then by lurches, necessarily as tumultuous as they are fleeting. And it is much when it does not attempt to make up for lost time by degrading its magnanimous wrath with ferocious manifestations. Then the ineluctable need drives the herd back to graze the grass — the soul defers to better times its strivings toward the good and falls back to sleep.

The French People did not understand, or would not understand, that every new prince, risen by tasting crime and inclined to perpetuate it, does not venture upon it again until, by astutely practising virtue, he has given the ill seed time to root itself firmly. The laborious digestion dulled thought; the rosy hues of the present prevented men from discerning the menacing phantoms that would have massed on the horizon. And when the servile Senate restored, in the person of Luigi Napoleone and his male heirs, the imperial dignity, the multitude hastened to ratify. Nearly eight million *Sì* — a slender minority of two hundred and fifty-three thousand *No*!

On the 1st of December 1852, a mere year after having stained his hands and his soul with the blood of his own fellow-citizens, Luigi Napoleone placed upon his head, at Saint Cloud, the crown of France; and in assuming it, he engraved upon History the name of Napoleon III.

With the Empire re-established and the Constitution retouched, the various branches of Government assumed, in appearance, their regular functions; in reality, France continued to be governed by Dictatorship — a veiled one, if one wished, but no less absolute.

The Prince Consort, who had come from London in the spring of 1854 to cement the friendship then taking shape between England and the new Empire, had occasion to express his profound astonishment at the matter. Napoleon himself candidly confided to him that he never permitted his Ministers to assemble to discuss public affairs. Each individual Minister dealt directly with him on any matter of importance, and what he had resolved with one was rarely communicated to another.

This wholly personal form of government, deemed pernicious even by the old sovereigns, led us liberals to frame the most sombre prognostications regarding France's future. The fixed idea upon which the policy of Napoleon III was to pivot became manifest at once — to emulate the great intellect that had drawn his own house from nothing; that had stamped deep traces upon so great and so illustrious a part of the world. He set about this arduous enterprise armed with unbridled ambition, with an immoderate lust for intrigue, an utter want of faith in pledged word and in treaties, a capacity for corruption well able to undermine far sturdier constitutions; the absence of every regenerative principle; a mediocre intelligence, barely that; his presumption, and his great name.

How could the immense disproportion between the means and the end have failed to alarm us? The thought and the momentum of a great State, concentrated in that dark and ambiguous nature, were in our eyes the power consecrated to the doing of evil, to driving, with precipitate steps, a noble Nation toward dishonour and ruin. After half a century, I am surely permitted to invoke History in order to establish, with the indisputable wisdom of hindsight, how well-founded our predictions were.

Napoleon III had one flash of practical intelligence in allying himself with England. But in placing the crown upon his head, he had not blown from his soul his natural duplicity. He could not show himself a sincere ally, and his crude manœuvres so deeply irritated the subtle British diplomacy that he found it hostile to him in the moments of supreme need. England would indeed have declared open war upon him, had the Germanic victories not forestalled her.

Napoleon III could not transform the war with Russia into a glorious uprising of peoples yearning to repress the tyranny of the barbarians. It was reduced to a long, bloody siege; and when Sebastopol fell, it was because General Pélissier manfully refused, and not without stinging phrases, to submit to the orders that came from Paris.

The war in Italy? It is insisted upon that he alone championed it, with the forgetfulness of the eagerness with which the French People subscribed to the loan raised to defray its costs. There are two pitched battles in that war: Magenta and Solferino. The first was won almost in despite of the Emperor, through the disobedience of General Mac Mahon. In the second, the Napoleonic fibre had something of a posthumous flash, and it was the only victory of the Second Empire owed to the personal merits of Napoleon III. But immediately afterwards, his vulgarity reasserts itself. He tore up, without warning, the articles of the alliance; he insulted the King who had fought heroically at his side. And the substantial aim of the war — that of substituting his own influence in the Peninsula for that of Austria — was destined, thanks to the virile patriotism of the Italian party of action, to fail miserably. Napoleon III, even as victor, emerged from the bloody struggle irremediably defeated.

The expedition to Mexico, born of financial speculations that could not be confessed, is an adventure worthy of a filibuster. It began with a betrayal analogous to that which cleared the way to Rome for his Oudinot, and ended, as the war in Italy had ended, with the shameful breaking of solemnly undertaken pledges. The fact that Maximilian, and not the Emperor, fell victim to Mexican lead serves to demonstrate how little human, or divine, Justice governs the affairs of the world.

In the secret negotiations that preceded and followed the defeat of Denmark and the resounding victories of Prussia over Austria, Napoleon — playing a double game, simultaneously offering his alliance to both contending parties — aimed at obtaining a gratuity from the victor; at reclaiming for France the line of the Rhine. But he was deceived and mocked and covered with ridicule by the sagacity of Bismarck.

What is one to say of the Franco-Prussian War, willed by him, undertaken by him without the most elementary knowledge either of the enemy's forces or of his own?

I do not measure the greatness of men by their success, for I know of defeats infinitely more glorious than any triumph. But for that to be so, the ruins must appear to us as irradiated and hallowed by the greatness of thought, by the nobility of sacrifice, by the sublimity of purpose. The life of the Third Napoleon seems to me, from this point of view, as arid as a desert.

To be sure! To be sure! No one could outrun his time and discern in the uncertain future the positive catastrophe. Yet already in the spring of 1854, tangible reasons abounded to justify the distrust — nay, the profound hatred — of liberals. Luigi Napoleone, trampling upon the principles he had sworn to defend on pain of death, had, through his crimes, enfeebled France and robbed her of every possibility of those democratic initiatives that stand among the greatest of her glories. He had shown himself most cowardly and ferocious precisely against the factions that had contributed most to drawing him from the near-obscurity in which he had so long lived. Gratitude weighs upon vulgar souls. He would yet have to render a stricter account of all this than can be balanced on a page of History — History, too often venal and mendacious, like the conscience of the men who write it.

To Italian liberals, the power of that man was in a special degree tormenting — the man who had repaid their benefits and their expectations by championing the interests of the age-old enemy of the Fatherland; who had sacrificed the flower of Italian youth to vilify resurgent Rome; to thrust her back into the arms of the papacy; to prostitute her by brute force to that which was most monstrous in what had survived the barbarism of bygone centuries. In the spring of 1854 it was already considered more than probable, indeed imminent, that Napoleon III would form an alliance with the House of Savoy. And since it was well known that the Empire did not desire Italian unity, and that Vittorio Emanuele II and his Ministers did not consider it possible, it was inferred that from such a *connubio* there would issue, at most, a territorial enlargement of Piedmont. Some French prince substituted for the petty tyrants who infested Italy; but the Pope in Rome, sustained by the imperial arms and bound, by force of instinct and circumstance, more to the interests of the Empire than to the cause of Italian independence. History would have renewed its ancient phases; we should have had upon our necks, alongside the old masters, new ones.

This train of ideas drew strength from the marriage of Napoleon III to Eugenia Montijo, Countess of Teba, a Spanish woman of incomparable beauty, restless, intelligent, crafty, tenacious, Catholic not by calculation but by deep-rooted conviction. Thanks to her influence, upon the throne of France there sat, virtually, alongside Napoleon III, the priest.

The cause of Liberty could not have resumed its inexorable course, either in France or in Italy, so long as Napoleon III — the grape-shotter of the People, the usurper — stood between it and the future. Therefore it was necessary to think of suppressing him — to hasten the return of things to their natural order; to spare, at slight cost, a long succession of tumults and hatreds and rivers of blood.

Such ideas take hold like a vice upon natures disposed to action, and it was from that very time that I cast, in my soul, the seeds of an action that was to burst forth some years later. Even then I understood that an event of such political importance would not have produced the fruits I anticipated of it, unless thought were given to preparing a vast organised force ready to profit by it. I meditated at length, took my notes and wrote — and to whom could I write if not to the most steadfast conscience I knew; to the man who had known how to surprise the heartbeat of the Fatherland and to proclaim her living and to foretell her greatness, when all believed her inexorably laid in the tomb?

He was then in London. I communicated to him in full detail a plan of mine (of which I shall have occasion to speak further on) asking him, in order to carry it out, for his aid; prepared to go to the very end, or to draw back, according to his counsel. Felice Orsini, who at that period was still on the most intimate terms with Mazzini, saw my letter, and it is to that letter that I owe my future relations with the patriot of Meldola.

But to that first communication of mine, nor to two others that followed, did Giuseppe Mazzini reply. I bethought myself of Pianciani, and in a fourth letter addressed to him, I poured out all my bitterness at that silence — undoubtedly deliberate — which smacked to me of undeserved contempt.

The reply was not long in coming, and it was brief and terrible. "In London I was accused of having been the deliberate cause of the arrest of Fortunato Calvi and of the failure of the attempts at the Calore. The accusation came from Zurich."

So Austria, powerless to throttle me, had avenged herself for my escape! — to isolate me, to annihilate me through calumny. And it was as though a veil fell suddenly from my eyes. Now I understood the conduct of my companions in Zurich; nor did it require great penetration to interpret the secret motive behind Caronti's strange behaviour. The Austrian poison had been spread by his lips.

I replied to Pianciani without losing a moment and, in my resentment, did not even weigh the phrases that alluded to Mazzini — so quick, I observed, to lend his ear to the slanders of a man, upon whose honesty he himself, only a few months before, had expressed to me the gravest suspicions. I concluded by asking for the means to go and confront my accuser.

Pianciani sent me one hundred and fifty francs; but accompanied by what amounted almost to an injunction to keep quiet and to await from time my vindication. Having pocketed the money, I embraced the Zappa household with great warmth, and turned my back on Paris. It was a splendid morning in May 1854 when, suddenly, from the heights, I saw spread at my feet the blue lake, and gleaming in the first rays of the sun, the damp rooftops of old Zurich.

I had myself announced to Caronti, without revealing my name, as an exile who wished to communicate an important message to him. Long was the wait in the antechamber; but brief was our interview. He was still in his dressing-gown and received me standing, both thumbs hooked into his waistband. The moment he recognised me, he turned pale and muttered through his teeth: "What has brought you here?"

— I shall tell you in a moment! — I closed the door and stepped so close to him that our very breath almost mingled, and I satisfied his curiosity to the full, reinforcing my words with a demand for satisfaction. "And mark this well! I require it, and I shall have it before leaving this room!"

He peered, with a furtive glance, out through the open window, and attempted to gain time by protesting his innocence. Indeed, he had always held me in unconditional esteem, with an affection almost fraternal. Speak ill of anyone? He would sooner his tongue dropped from his mouth in shreds!

— Very well! But words are carried off by the wind, and what is written remains. Attest in black and white your unconditional esteem; furnish me with a formal retraction. That, and nothing else, will satisfy me!

As I spoke, his lips were trembling. I believe he understood, from my countenance, that this was no time for equivocation. He went to the writing-desk, wiped the honest sweat from his brow, and wrote. Then, without turning around, he stretched out his arm and handed me the sheet.

I read it, and contented myself with saying: Very well!

Thereupon, as though we were old friends competing in civilities, he extended himself in offering me his services — in whatever part of the world, at whatever time. I had only to express the wish. If moreover I were bound for Italy and thought it opportune to make a brief stop at Lugano, he would furnish me with a letter of introduction for Count Grillanzoni, who had settled there.

He was evidently trying to reconnoitre the ground in order to prepare some new mischief for me. I cut short his words. What I intended to do I did not know; in any case it concerned me alone. I turned the key and left.

In the corridor I felt a hand placed on my shoulder. And a voice murmured in my ear: "How goes it, Moretto?"

It was Angelo Fumagalli, pale and smiling. He placed a finger across his lips and added: "Come, come to the stables. We shall talk at our ease!" He took me by the right hand and, with gentle force, dragged me along as though I were a child.

"I know everything, I heard everything! I saw you cross the garden and recognised you at once. I said to myself: there is something afoot here. What is afoot? I had dark suspicions and — why hide it from you? — I committed a shabby act, I put my ear to the door. But better so! There will be no more misunderstandings. Do you know that that scoundrel even accused you of having stolen a watch from his coachman? That one, I never believed for a moment. As for the rest — you must forgive us. What would you have? We fell into the trap like a pack of idiots. And you had a narrow escape, I tell you! Do you remember that plan for a nocturnal trip on the lake? They had plotted to stun you with a blow of a cudgel on the head and to throw you, bound hand and foot, as fodder for the fish! — Then Caronti would have denounced you all, and at a single stroke, delivered Austria of an entire wasps' nest. — The Devil take him! Fortunately Grioli had sense enough for all of them, and he saved you. God bless him! We believed we were avenging the Fatherland!"

He embraced me and was sobbing. Then, precisely as the other had done, but with a different aim: "And now, what do you intend to do?"

— My dear friend, I have tried everything in my power to do my duty, and I have found myself suspected by those very men who should have defended me. One road alone remains to me: to persist, to answer the accusations with silence and with action.

I left Zurich with the intention of making for Piedmont, and made my stop at Lugano and paid my visit to Count Grillanzoni. He received me coldly — I understood, and presented him with the Caronti retraction. Thereupon he showed me in his turn a letter received a few days before. In it, that same worthy exhorted him to do everything in his power to prevent my returning to Zurich. My irascible temperament, he wrote, was the cause of continual vexations and of scandals which, for the good name of our emigration and in the interest of the cause "so dear to our hearts," it was imperative to avoid.

I renounced Piedmont and returned at once to Zurich. In the meantime, Angelo Fumagalli had taken his leave of Caronti, with indignant words. From him I learned that Felice Orsini had been making inquiries to obtain my address.

A few days later I was engaged in the attempt at Maloggia.

---

*(1) This Grioli was the brother of the canon martyred at Mantova. When, after the Risorgimento, Francesco Giuseppe came to Venice, a Regiment was assigned as "his escort of honour." Grioli, who belonged to it, found his soul revolted by the bonds of blood, and preferred to be brought before a Council of Discipline rather than render homage to the torturer of our Fatherland. And there were those who, for that act of noble pride, called him a fool!*


### Chapter Twenty-Four

<!-- pages:120-127 -->



THE plan, this time, was to seize the steamers that ply the Lario and to attempt the capture of Como. At the first sign of insurrection in that city, several armed columns were to descend from the Grisons to raise the Valtellina and give force and breadth to the movement.

The proposal to take part was made to me directly by Pompei of Pavia, of the Giovine Italia, Stradella section — yet he spoke to me in the name of Felice Orsini. I note this because Orsini, in his *Memorie*, names Giuseppe Mazzini as the effective and manifest chief of this attempt, using as pretext the meagre number of persons who answered the call, to demonstrate "the utter want of influence" of that honest man among the ranks of the party that was more properly his own.

In all truthfulness, both Fumagalli and I learned of Mazzini's direct participation and of his presence in the Grisons only some months later, after the enterprise had already failed.

Great is my veneration for the memory of Felice Orsini; yet the motive behind his virulent denunciation of this and other Mazzinian ventures has never been entirely clear to me. Had he not, by taking part in them and at times accepting a directing rôle, implicitly approved of them? And in approving, had he not assumed full responsibility? Every word of blame that Orsini addressed to the Maestro seems to me blame that falls back upon his own head. Never did I have cause to suspect, as when I read his autobiography, that the flashes of that ardent spirit were not always worthy of the hero. And I shall say, in passing, that those very phrases of recognition — recalled and held up to ridicule by the fierce Romagnol — are a variant of those we actually pronounced, in accordance with the instructions we had received, whether he allowed himself to be carried away by the heat of polemic, or whether his memory played him false.

The man posted on the road did not say to us — who wore a flower in our hats — "Halloa, good fellows, where are you bound?", but asked us whether we were Italians. And our answer was not, as Orsini asserts, "We are going to see Signor Francesco, or Signor Giuseppe"; but simply: "Conti." And when the other pressed our hands, I added, still in accordance with the instructions received: "Dov'è Tito Celsi?" (1)

In what condition Fumagalli and I arrived at San Maurizio, Orsini has noted: "weary, famished, in rags, dressed like beggars." He might have added that, for all that, we were no less eager to set our hands to work for our Country.

Be that as it may, the man who questioned us led us to an inn and left us alone. Several days of fruitless waiting followed. Though I had somewhat improved my appearance, I could not assume the air of a prosperous burgher come to those parts to enjoy the fruits of honest industry. This circumstance, which might attract attention, was a source of some unease to me. Nor did the abandonment in which we were left tend to reassure me.

Enterprises such as ours are secretly attempted even by Governments with an interest in fomenting some complication. And it is enough, for those of us who live in California, to recall General Frémont. But Frémont could afford to keep his mind comparatively at ease, for he knew that behind him stood a great force, vigilant and ready to act. And besides, the happy outcome of the attempt matters little when little is at stake. The important thing was to furnish the United States with any pretext for intervention — a trifle suffices the wolf that drinks from the same stream as the lamb.

Matters stood very differently for the Italian patriots, who were compelled to build all their plans upon the fortunate outcome of a first, uncertain surprise. The painful experience of recent years inclined me to suspect, in our abandonment, the cruel tricks of the unforeseeable — a storm that had escaped all calculation, a ford temporarily impassable, a letter gone astray, a misinterpreted order, a conspirator's brief hesitation, an imprudent word, the untimely curiosity of an idler, the barking of a dog — a thousand minute causes, sprung as if from nothing — and nothing behind one to fall back upon!

And the consequences of failure? The reproach of the timid, the arrogant contempt of serious men, the venom of cowards. Any rascal may stone you, any holy man of the Law spit in your face, any miser sell your head, any ruffian put a bullet in your back out of pure lust for slaughter. And the hatred of the modern inquisitors, which multiplies the hardships of your flight, which contrives to have every door slammed in your face, to snatch the bread from your mouth and the pillow from beneath your head; which would seize you in its pincers and wring your neck on the pretext of restoring the age of easy digestions; which, when it cannot rid itself of your body, murders your soul with calumny.

With what intensity must the forerunners of the third Italy have kept their eyes fixed upon their ideal, to prefer, over a life of ease, the wretched *odissea* — the wanderer's odyssey — of the rebel!

One evening Fumagalli appeared before me waving a letter in the air. It was from Tito Celsi, announcing that his arrival was imminent — and he fell upon us just as we were reading his few lines. In our conversation, Felice Orsini named no one; he confined himself, so far as his plans were concerned, to assuring us that he could dispose of two thousand rifles. He handed us a note of introduction for an innkeeper at Maloggia and, assuring us that we should soon receive his instructions, took his leave.

The innkeeper at Maloggia was not privy to the secret affairs, but had some inkling of them and favoured them. He informed us that he was expecting a crate of "ironmongery" from Saint-Étienne — it arrived, and with it the instructions from Tito Celsi. These were the arms that were to have served the redemption of the Fatherland; Orsini indicated to us a locality, not far from the Muretto glacier, where we were to place them in readiness. The innkeeper quietly provided the means of transport.

The chosen location was favourable. A cliff falling sheer on three sides; an abandoned shepherd's hut, hidden among bushes and chestnut trees. From up there we could observe every twist and turn of the path that led to it. No danger of surprise.

It was the middle of August 1854, and we made the most of the long days to press our work with diligence. We rose from our beds of grass at first light and, having contemplated the dawn, set ourselves to the task. Once a rifle was assembled, it was levelled; its action was tested with a silent manoeuver; then it was ranged along the wall overgrown with foliage. We leaned it there with a delicacy, as though it were some precious trinket.

In those days I committed an imprudence which, though it had no bearing whatever on our enterprise, had a very considerable bearing on my little barque and caused it to change its course. I had just finished setting in order my fiftieth rifle, and as I examined it and ran my hands over its various parts, it seemed to me so exceptionally fine that I should have thought myself a fool not to claim it as my own. I used the screwdriver as a bodkin and engraved my initials on the stock. Fumagalli, who was watching me, said: "What are you doing?" And I, paraphrasing an expression of old Domenico's: "A tool like this, and I'll ruin the livers of half Bohemia."

The calm of the mountains reclaimed us. But all too soon came agitation, and collapse.

I had gone out to keep watch, and had barely raised my hand to shade my eyes when, in the distance, through the thickets, I caught sight of a cloud of dust. The breeze, which blew towards the mountain, drove it along in the wake of the wayfarers who raised it; yet it could not hide from me the glinting of arms. They were soldiers, and in good number; nor were they surely climbing the slope in search of hares.

I had great difficulty in persuading Fumagalli that we must seek safety up the heights. He wished to stand his ground and "sell his skin dear." We crawled through the undergrowth and gained the mountain.

They arrived, surrounded the hut and demanded our surrender at rifle-point. Half an hour later they were departing in small detachments, carrying away our rifles and our hopes.

We came down to Maloggia after nightfall and the innkeeper, on seeing us, merely shrugged his shoulders. He counselled us to remain quietly withdrawn in his inn until he had had time to take the wind's measure.

Two days passed. On the third he appeared before us with a downcast air and announced that Tito Celsi had been arrested. He added: "As far as I am concerned, no fear! You are free to make use of my inn as you please — as long as there is polenta and milk for me, there will be for you too. But if I were in your shoes, I should prefer to pitch my tent a mile and a half further down the valley, precisely where the coach horses are changed. News reaches you sooner there, and if matters grow cloudy you will have the means of slipping away close to hand. Besides, a brother of mine lives thereabouts, to whom I shall recommend you — it will be as though you were still among family."

We followed his counsel and on the very evening of our arrival, having with difficulty swallowed a forkful of food, we heard the jingling of the harness bells and the crack of the postilion's whip. Going out with the other onlookers, I noticed a heavily cloaked individual who had just descended from the coach and was scrutinising me from head to foot. I returned his scrutiny in kind, and he stepped forward — the signal! Drawing me aside, he handed me a roll and a note. The roll contained two hundred francs in gold; the note a single word: "Save yourselves!"

With the help of the innkeeper's brother from Maloggia, we hired two saddle horses. We left word to knock at our door at three in the morning; but I was roused abruptly much earlier. At the foot of the bed, drawn up in a semicircle, stood several men in uniform — the barrels of their rifles converging upon my chest.

We were conveyed to Chur and brought before a Federal Commissioner who had come expressly from Bern. The interrogation was brief. We declared ourselves deserters. We had no knowledge of political conspiracies or clandestine arms. The Commissioner allowed us to have our say, then made a sign. They brought in a rifle, and he, smiling, drew my attention to the stock. I had no need to strain my eyes. In characters that, by some strange illusion, seemed suddenly to loom larger, my three initials — *C. C. R.* — stood out plainly. Up there, near the Muretto, without knowing it, I had engraved my own condemnation and another's.

And we were locked away *in domo petri*. My first thought was for my father and for Luisa, who were in the prisons of Mantova; nor did I ever have a clearer vision of them, or feel for them a deeper compassion.

I will say at once that at Chur, Austria's delightful methods were unknown. Imprisonment, but with respect for misfortune; observance of regulations, but humane regulations; some privation, but not Galeazzo's Lenten fare.

The greatest torment for me was the forced inaction — though, to one degree or another, I knew well enough where patience lived. Not so Fumagalli who, as the gaoler told me, paced ceaselessly in his cell like a caged beast.

He was confined on the first floor, I on the third. It was impossible for us to see one another; but taking advantage of the brief moments when the sentry was at a distance, we would exchange a greeting. If the sentry reproved us; if the warder struck the door, I would step back and sink onto the bench with a calm that surprised even me; Fumagalli, on the contrary, would resume his pacing furiously. On his feet he wore heavy mountain boots, reinforced with hobnails no larger and no more lacerating than those displayed by the Christ nailed to the cross in our country churches. The floor, which was of brick, ended by bearing grooves deep enough to resemble cart-ruts, and the warder, pointing them out to him, remarked, half in earnest and half in jest: "You cost the Republic too dear!"

Our fate depended upon the decision that the gentlemen of Bern would take. But before any word could arrive from that capital, whatever it might be, the first signs of winter were already making themselves felt.

Along the prison wall there was a small garden, green and pleasant. A large pear tree spread its broad crown almost level with my little window. When it rustled, the cell filled with a murmur that my mind transformed into indefinite and melancholy melodies. I often asked myself: "Who can tell me whether I shall still be here when the man who tends that garden comes to gather the pears?"

One November morning, pressing my face to the bars, I saw among the branches a man with a thick beard, plucking the fruit and filling a basket that hung by its hook from a bough. We looked at one another — the man swung his arm and tossed me one, two, three, half a dozen of them. There was a mist; the leaves had turned yellow. And I said to myself: "Who can say whether I shall still be here when the tree puts forth its blossoms once more?"

At that very moment the warder threw open the door and, jingling his bunch of keys and smiling, invited me to follow him.

I found Angelo Fumagalli in the corridor, stamping his iron-shod feet like an impatient colt.

We were taken to Bern, and for the second time I heard from the Federal Authorities the sermon upon the sacred duties that rest upon those who receive generous hospitality. As a recidivist, I was banished in perpetuity from the borders of the Republic. And they warned me not to set foot within them again, on pain of severe punishment — extending to me the courtesy of reading aloud the articles of the Code that specified those punishments.

Upon being asked to name the part of Europe or America where I wished to be conveyed, I replied, as on another occasion: "To England."

And so ended, for me, for Fumagalli, and, I believe, for a couple of other participants, that attempt which Felice Orsini calls "more comedy than tragedy." That same Orsini was likewise arrested, as I have already noted; but he slipped from the hands of the gendarmes and, after having sought momentary refuge at Chur and at Zurich and attempting thereafter to foment some new venture at Milan, departed for Vienna, where he entered into negotiations — first to join, for purposes I cannot fathom, the Russian army; then to spread revolutionary ideas within the Austrian one. Both attempts having failed, he passed into Hungary and was arrested at Hermanstadt in Transylvania, whence he was conveyed by painful stages to Vienna, and from Vienna to the castle of Mantova, which swallowed him up, as I have already had occasion to recall.

Giuseppe Mazzini, Maurizio Quadrio, Nicola Ferrari and a few others, who had assembled in the Grisons to prepare the armed bands, had succeeded, after various vicissitudes, in making their escape.

Of the arms gathered here and there at the various passes, I believe only those entrusted to Fumagalli and myself fell into the hands of the Federal Authorities, who, already on the alert since the time of the Clementi trial, had redoubled their vigilance in response to warnings received from the Police of the Lombardo-Veneto kingdom.

And since there were no casualties to lament, we might even accept, without rancour, the definition of "comedy" that issued from the wrathful mind of Felice Orsini.

But to one who reflects that in those very days, through the strangest of alliances, the map of Europe was being pondered and redrawn, that affirmation of the persistent restlessness of the Italians, that renewed protest against the tyranny which forbade us to constitute ourselves a Nation, will seem neither ridiculous, nor inopportune, nor vain!

(1) This is the name assumed by Felice Orsini to deceive the Swiss Police.

END OF THE FIRST PART


### Chapter One

<!-- pages:127-134 -->

AS an introduction to this second part, a page essentially of love claims its rightful place. What I here relate I had, in part, from the Count di Rudio himself, and in part from his wife. Nor was I without the aid of certain intimate letters, yellowed by time, yet still exhaling, fresh as ever, the fragrance of the past.

The little politics that peeps in through the doorway carries the soul of Giuseppe Mazzini. For what, after all, is di Rudio, if not a reflection of that great conspirator? Setting aside the disproportion of their genius, the only substantial difference between the two seems to me this: the one held that without faith in God neither a spirit of sacrifice nor an enduring honesty is possible; the other managed to sacrifice himself and to live honestly, having set aside that mighty hypothesis altogether.

In London, di Rudio made his way to the innkeeper already known to us, at whose establishment Fumagalli had preceded him. Tressoldi introduced him to the chorus-master of Drury Lane, a Milanese who engaged him as a chorister in the Italian Opera company. Thus, as in other days from flowers, di Rudio drew his sustenance from music — that is, music sung by others; for, on the whole, he was obliged to content himself with opening his mouth wide and waving his arms. The important thing was to pocket five shillings each evening, and in this he succeeded marvellously well, as any seasoned chorister could have done.

Let it be added that he was paid to listen to Madame Glassier and to the tenor Armandi; whilst, to be transported into ecstasy by the high notes of this Bolognese cobbler, the wealthy paid relatively fabulous sums.

When the season ended, the Opera Company drifted back, one by one, beneath the fair skies of Italy; but di Rudio remained beneath the leaden skies of London. He persisted in reading the newspaper advertisements and at last found work at the Wapping Docks, a locality situated at the eastern extremity of the metropolis. The work consisted of handling rushes and fashioning coverings for bottles, making use, for this purpose, of a newly invented machine, the contrivance of a Frenchman.

The excessive distance of the workshop obliged him to forgo the cordial hospitality of the good Tressoldi. The necessity of bread had, in any case, already thinned out that pleasant company. To follow the manifold threads of their lives, to trace worthily the stubborn, exhausting struggle sustained by our exiles in order to keep afloat, would require a volume unto itself.

We should encounter feet peering out between sole and upper, bearing witness to the long and fruitless hunt for employment; bellies pinched by the belt, betraying the enormous disproportion between appetite and the means of satisfying it. I should have to tell of nocturnal hunts across the rooftops and the mewing of innocent cats destined for the pot; to describe the cramped lairs shared between half a dozen human creatures, each instinctively reclaiming, in sleep, their portion of the blanket — a blanket sometimes pieced together with the art of a mosaicist, compounded of newspapers gathered from the benches in the public gardens.

The drama would not fail to chill us with its cold breath... virile constitutions corroded by hunger; sensitive natures ruined beyond all hope of recovery; poems vanished silently amidst the clamour of a thieving, pleasure-seeking world; death rattles; corpses laid out in pieces upon the cold marble of the mortuary slab... acts of heroism without witnesses, unrecorded, yet no less worthy of history than those which unfolded in the light of great occasions.

Against this backdrop, rendered now in strokes of lamentation, now of comedy, now of the sublime, stands out the pensive figure of Giuseppe Mazzini who, to feed the generative flame of divine conceptions, of genius and of virtue, goes knocking at the moneylender's door and pledges... his own boots.

Nor should we want for hearty laughter hurled, like defiance, in the face of misery... frequent dreams of glory; sentimental love affairs piercing to the heart through threadbare elbows; long fasts forgotten in the reading of Byron... and likewise those occupations dripping with grease that know nothing of poverty. Angelo Fumagalli, to cite one example, made an excellent living manufacturing sausages.

The Count di Rudio had found lodging at Baldwin's Garden, in the house of one Mancherini, a figurine-maker from Lucca. Fumagalli and a certain Leoni were the only exiles with whom he maintained, in those days, relations of close friendship.

He had come to know Leoni at Tressoldi's inn, and they had grown intimate through a characteristic incident which I am pleased to recall.

Austria and the petty tyrants of Italy, who had been given so many headaches by the exiles, devised a new means of defence. They granted to the birds of prey chained in the prisons of their homelands full freedom of flight, on the condition that they should pass themselves off as political refugees and insinuate themselves amongst the honest company.

The calculation was that a change of sky would not change their ways, and that the public, implicating the good in the blame provoked by the wicked, would cease to pity the exiles and to lend moral support to their noble aspirations.

A veritable flock of such gentlemen descended upon Tressoldi's inn. They were led by one Bonacina, a Milanese, a swaggering instigator who, backed by his confederates and emboldened by the innkeeper's soft character, soon turned that congenial establishment into a sinister den of ruffians.

Tressoldi's comical despair, and the fact that he had caught the scent of the troublemakers' special mission, induced di Rudio to resort to an extreme expedient. He found willing souls in Leoni, a kind of athlete, and in other well-built young men; then, having wrested from Tressoldi the promise of the strictest neutrality, he awaited a favourable occasion.

One evening Bonacina and four or five fellows of the same stamp were demanding to drink at no cost and threatening to turn everything upside down if they were not served instanter. Di Rudio stepped forward and ordered them to clear the room... and never to set foot in it again.

"Methinks the young pup smells of good health!" replied Bonacina, making as if to deal him a backhanded blow. It was the signal for a furious brawl... Fumagalli had his head broken; but the ruffians, overwhelmed and badly mauled, ended by beating a retreat, leaving their leader to make the best of things as he could. Alone, with one eye closed and the other barely open, his mouth bloody, Bonacina backed away to the window and, swearing he would have his revenge, leaped out and vanished into the darkness of the street.

Not a single grim face, that evening, at the sign of Tressoldi's! But the affair had, for di Rudio, an unexpected sequel. One foggy night, returning from the Opera, with his head full of trills and high notes, turning a corner into a deserted lane, he found himself face to face with Bonacina, heavily muffled, fist raised and in that fist a large knife.

"Ah, ah!" said the scoundrel mockingly. "Now we shall see whether your liver is sound!" But instead of delivering the blow, he drew back... he had caught the gleam of a pistol barrel.

Then di Rudio was seized by a singular whim: to add, to the silent eloquence of the weapon, that of a brief homily. He recalled to Bonacina the heroism and greatness of his fellow Milanese, in such striking contrast to his own conduct. And probing that heart, to rekindle beneath the ashes the gentle sentiments of childhood, he asked him, among other things, whether he did not believe that his mother was ashamed to have brought into the world, nursed and raised a son who was surely betraying her finest hopes; who mocked the sufferings of his fellow citizens; who spat upon her grey hairs; who renounced the Patria.

Bonacina, hard and contemptuous at first, ended by absorbing, head bowed, his dose of medicine. Then, flinging away the knife with a sudden movement, he fled headlong, sobbing like a child. The love of the Patria had known how to kindle some glimmer even in that base and debased soul!

The Count di Rudio, from his lodging at Baldwin's Garden, went every evening to exchange a few words with Fumagalli and Leoni. After their customary long walk, they pooled what few coins they had and allowed themselves a bottle... of concentrated sunshine. Such was the name they gave to the product of the Lombard vineyards; the only available means by which they could warm themselves at the sun of Italy.

The first to appear rarely at the accustomed gathering was di Rudio.

Apropos of these absences, Leoni asked him one evening, with an expression that revealed the unspoken meaning, whether he was lodging in a house of young ladies. "Without a doubt!" replied the Count. "At the Mancherinis' there are two. One is called Sara and is the daughter of the household; the other goes by the name of Elisa and is their niece. Her parents live in some corner of the County of Surrey and have sent her to London, if I was correctly informed, to train in the art of artificial flowers."

Leoni pressed to know more, and the Count di Rudio, who had picked up the musical jargon at Drury Lane, replied:

"Miss Sara has a soul tuned in the minor key, soft and caressing; Miss Elisa, on the other hand, has vibrations in the major, impetuous, conquering."

And Leoni, looking fixedly at Fumagalli and winking at him: "Let us recite the *de profundis*. The *Moretto* is ill and... we shall soon lose him!"

The following evening and the next and many more besides, di Rudio was nowhere to be seen. His two friends grew anxious and resolved to go to Baldwin's Garden to investigate. They found the door ajar. To ring the bell, or to step boldly forward without asking leave? Whilst they stood in two minds, they heard a sweet, gentle voice saying: "In my garden there grows a pear..."

A masculine voice, that of di Rudio, interrupted: "I have already told you that the names of trees are masculine, and the names of fruits feminine..."

— Except *dattero*, *limone*, *ananassa* and *fico*! — observed another woman's voice, more vibrant than the first.

Leoni drew Fumagalli away. Then he said: "I foresaw it. The fellow is done for!"

Signora Mancherini, a Booth by birth, a distant relation of the founder of the Salvation Army, was English through and through; but her husband's occupation had dusted her, not to say plastered her, with art more or less classical. She spoke with evident ostentation of Venuses and Dianas. And with the Gods of Olympus she had overburdened every piece of furniture, every shelf, every projecting surface of her little apartment. She was also accustomed to dressing her hair in a fashion that recalled times long since vanished.

Her daughter Sara was a fair flower of eighteen years. In the judgement of the Count di Rudio, she took a little from her father and a little from her mother... a blend of English and Italian. But the northern disposition, active and assertive, had, by an apparent contradiction of nature, ended by remaining as if atrophied, whilst the gentle and submissive disposition of the Italian woman, developing and all but bursting forth, stamped the whole of her character in a very marked degree. She did not seek to shine; she did not imperiously demand love; she preferred to love without being loved in return to being loved without loving in return. Not yet having reached the age at which feeling, fully developed, knows what the tempests of passion are, she was content with the tepid breath of friendship, and lavished upon her cousin Elisa the tender devotion which she would later reserve for a husband; in her cousin she rejoiced, suffered at her vexations, and exulted in her joys. Happy in her own half-light, she asked for nothing more, provided that Elisa should triumph.

This latter, meanwhile, as if to make it so deliberately, was endowed with a temperament that tended to accentuate Sara's disposition. Of the English people it is generally said that they have strong teeth and that once they succeed in seizing their prey they never let it go. Beneath Miss Elisa's fair hair, beneath the rosy skin, behind the blue eyes that even today call forth a sense of sweetness, there nestled a tenacity of purpose and a courage by no means common. These qualities, finding occasion to manifest themselves, appeared as a paradox and astonished the superficial observer; but not one who, accustomed to analysis and deduction, had noted the significance of that broad, firmly drawn chin and the energy of her gesture, however rare and restrained as befitted every young English woman.

The Count di Rudio told me that, as a girl, she possessed a certain suppleness of character and intellect; but she could never have been brought, even had she wished it, to the point of self-negation. To love strong souls; to open to them, as a corolla to the kisses of the sun, the most hidden gradations of her heart; but in order to be stronger, greater together as two. To conclude a treaty of alliance — that yes, with all her ardour, and to remain faithful to it unto the last; to sign even an honourable surrender, never!

Di Rudio had resolved to teach Italian to the two young women at the urging of Signora Mancherini. Why refuse? To study our language becomes fatally synonymous with loving our Patria, and our friends are always something of enemies to our enemies. The sympathy of the English was greatly prized by the Italians, and even the arts of peace may serve to win a battle. Besides, every small stone has its value in great undertakings!

And so it came about that the Count di Rudio, every evening, after having laboured prosaically among rushes, applied himself to instilling the sweetest and the strongest of modern tongues into the charming heads of two young women born and raised upon the foggy banks of the Thames; and perhaps the Gods of Olympus, from the heights of their shelves, smiled down upon the scene.

Accustomed to the blunt directness of military life, the Count di Rudio proved a rather brusque master, and the hour of the lesson assumed, so to speak, the rigidity of a geometrical problem. But no sooner had that hour passed than Signora Mancherini set her steaming tea service upon the table and the atmosphere changed as if by enchantment... books and notebooks vanished, and animated conversation flowed without interruption, punctuated and, as it were, annotated by sips of the warm beverage. Signora Mancherini, who was stout, filled the large armchair entirely with her own person. Sara sat, by a habit consonant with her inclinations, upon a low footstool, resting her forearm upon Miss Elisa's lap. The Count di Rudio, lean, nervous, dark, amidst so much triumph of fair complexions and rosy hues — like a grain of pepper — sat at first calm and composed; then, little by little, he grew all gestures and starts, and his countenance, most mobile, flashed and sparkled.

Before long, through his doing, the titanic figures who had striven for our *Risorgimento* were embroidered upon the fibres of those souls, all the more clearly for the fact that the fabric receiving them was sensitive and unversed in powerful emotions. And they were transformed by it: they felt, suffered, hoped and willed in the Italian manner.

What was bound to happen, happened. The heroes were dead or far away; the Count di Rudio was close at hand, young, attractive. He too had suffered for the sacred cause. In the eyes of the two young women he ended by growing to heroic stature, and they blended about that head his own prestige and that of others.

Angelo Fumagalli had a singular expression to characterise that situation: "Venice," he would say, "had the Moor; Belluno, being smaller, has only the *Moretto*. But the Moor was loved by a single Desdemona; that rascal di Rudio has two. Perhaps... and why not?... with not a shadow of malice, mind you!... perhaps he has three!"

The only one who seemed unaware of this was the Count di Rudio himself. Love knows no tropics; hearts burn at the Pole as furiously as at the Equator. It is the manner of expressing affection that varies with latitude. The Italian impetuosity is unknown to the young English woman, who perhaps senses instinctively how discordant it would sound in that world of subdued tones. Free and mistress of herself, she adorns herself, when she loves, with a composed dignity... the distinguishing mark of the free. Hence her unjust reputation for coldness. That charm glided over the spirit of the Count, absorbed and all but saturated with a wholly different passion.

True it is that in the long hours spent at the workshop, whilst his practised hands carried out the habitual labour mechanically, the mind of di Rudio strayed toward the two young women. Often the daydream absorbed him so completely that the noise of the tools and the faint odour of the rushes were, as it were, suppressed. In those moments — so he affirmed to me — a mysterious fragrance of youth and health penetrated his lungs and gave him a subtle intoxication. Sometimes he had caught himself yearning for the evening... But was it not in the evening that, having scraped away the scales of vulgarity, he conversed with his great ones, upon great things? The explanation seemed more than sufficient to him, and he did not trouble to enquire any further.

Unexpectedly, his employer, a Frenchman, withdrew from the business. A new proprietor, new methods: the foreign labour was dismissed, and the Count di Rudio found himself once more without immediate resources, very nearly destitute. For a time he went forward on the small savings he had put aside; then he found himself reduced to utter penury. The day he turned out his pockets and searched his every pocket within, he reflected, not without dismay, that he already owed a month's rent to Signora Mancherini. For several evenings past, at the lesson, he had appeared pale and listless. Miss Elisa asked him whether he was unwell. "Not precisely," he replied, "but I must say something that weighs upon me. I have most pressing obligations. Tomorrow evening I shall be engaged elsewhere; and I do not know when we shall be able to resume our lessons."

The festival of rosy and azure hues was over... the *via crucis* of the hero reduced to the level of the pariah began, the one to whom society denies salt and fire. He went out early in the morning, and returning at a very late hour, he managed to slip along the corridor, avoiding unpleasant encounters — he had come to call them such. He was of such a temper that he would have preferred to face the mouth of a cannon to the face of a creditor.

Unfortunately, one cannot leave hunger on the threshold of one's own house, nor hurl it into the street like an awkward burden. He hurled instead to the moneylender his few superfluous possessions, one by one. He had left behind his old friends, and to approach them now, when he was in extreme need, seemed to him a deliberate courting of rebuff. He would have been glad of any work; but these were times of crisis and the unemployed formed an army. In the newspapers the "Help Wanted" columns were conspicuous by their absence; those of "Situation Wanted" were measured by the yard. He went about soliciting here and there; but to compound his misfortune, he had the appearance of a gentleman. The more humane contented themselves with answering with some pleasantry. Others, having barely glanced at him, grunted the laconic "Go on!" which to Italian ears seems saturated with so much contempt. He walked as long as his legs would support him; then he resorted to the recourse of those without recourse... sitting upon a bench in some park and waiting for the late night... a painful repose.

Relief came to him unexpectedly, one night by mysterious ways, when he had not tasted a morsel in forty-eight hours. He had returned home with his customary precautions. He did not light the lamp, and before undressing, from the force of habit, turned down the bedclothes. Something heavy rolled upon the bed. Shortly afterwards he was unwrapping a parcel tied with great care, and a delicious fragrance tickled his nostrils. Before him, entirely at his disposal, was a marvel of roasted viands. And he bit into them with canine voracity, without reflection, without thought. Only when he had wiped his mouth did he, wondering that he had waited so long, ask himself: "Whence has all this abundance rained down upon me? Who can have taken care of me?"

His first suspicion fell upon Signora Mancherini and her two gentle creatures whom she, in the manner of Providence, sheltered beneath her ample wings. But he reflected, not without logic, that Signora Mancherini would have laid out a proper table, and that a parcel tied with care must have come from a distance. None of his old friends knew of his critical circumstances.

What mystery, then, was this?

To put it briefly, he had already stretched himself beneath the bedclothes and the outer world was already receding, and even the consciousness of his own being had grown dim; but a small part of his brain still worked on mechanically... Silence; the limitless and tenebrous void. Now and then, within it, the two questions flashed unresolved: "Whence this abundance? Who is taking care of me?"

And since life and the universe, in practical terms, exist only insofar as they are reflected in our minds, we may gauge to what minimal terms they may be reduced even within the skull of a hero!


### Chapter Two

<!-- pages:134-136 -->



THE GRATIFYING nocturnal surprise continued with mathematical regularity — a neatly tied and well-stocked parcel every twenty-four hours. The Count di Rudio resolved to get to the bottom of the matter, and one fine morning, having tidied his room as usual, he curled himself into the recess of the window behind the curtaining, determined to remain in ambush and in silence for the whole day if need be. From up there he commanded a good stretch of the pavement to the right and to the left, and he reasoned, not without justification, that the parcels could not be reaching him through the air by flight. He would unmask his mysterious benefactor.

A little before noon, whilst leaning against the sill he was photographing with his eye the dark to-and-fro that stirred confusedly at his feet, a sudden awakening of vital vibrations, a gentle warmth, a sense of penetrating light, shook him. He turned with a start — he was alone no longer!

Wrapped in a white mantle, her hair partly gathered up and partly flowing free, Miss Elisa stood erect just within the threshold. The fingers of her right hand held, by its little cord, the restorative parcel, and her blue eyes wandered in an ineffable agitation.

"Lo Carlo!..." she murmured. "I did not know you were here!" She recovered herself at once and added composedly: "I have brought this parcel for you."

"When? No living soul has entered by the door."

"Early this morning. Had I known you were at home, I should have delivered it to you straightaway."

"This morning, you say?"

"Early. A well-dressed gentleman, Italian in appearance and accent. I asked him his name; but he answered that, for reasons entirely his own, he did not wish to make himself known. It did not seem to me right to insist. Besides, he spoke English so poorly and I know so little Italian!..."

She added nothing more, and remained with her eyes cast down, waiting for some phrase, for a word — alluding, were it nothing more, to the fine weather. And as the word absolutely refused to come, she arched her brows with a nervous little movement and raised her eyes. The mild inquiry that shone within them soon lost itself in an expression of pained wonder. The Count di Rudio seemed absorbed in the thought of distant things — his mind was running after the well-dressed Italian who, without being a sorcerer, knew the secrets of his stomach so very well. And he was irritated by the mystery which, despite the tedious ambush, he had not succeeded in resolving. When he became aware that he was being watched, he silently, with dignity, brushed with his right hand the jacket whitened by its long contact with the sill.

The situation was growing awkward when, in the corridor, there sounded the tread of precipitate footsteps, and Miss Sara came hurrying in. She was bearing, clasped to her bosom, a great vase of roses.

She set it down beside the window, and after a few seconds of silent contemplation: "It fits perfectly!" She turned to Elisa and added: "The idea is yours, and it falls to you to give an explanation!" But she gave her no time for it, and fixing her gaze upon the Count: "One evening you said that the rose is the emblem of your family, and that for this reason, among others, it is your favourite flower above all others. Elisa had an inspiration. 'Let us procure a bush — perhaps he will be glad of it.' See what abundance! So as not to trouble you, we shall continue to water it ourselves. Elisa was pointing out to me that it will remind you of your mother and your Homeland — for Italy is the country of roses. Is it not so?"

Here she seemed, or pretended, that someone was calling her, and she ran away. Miss Elisa bade farewell with a nod of her head and, swift and light as a shadow, vanished.

The Count di Rudio stood for a long while motionless, his gaze fixed upon the void, as though something of the two young women had remained within it. From that day they occupied the greater part of his fanciful hours. They insinuated themselves, unbidden, into his thoughts, and no sooner did they appear there than they were received with an affection more than brotherly. The sad eyes, that had never witnessed anything save the torment of the innocent and the triumph of villainy, found renewal in that inward contemplation. And for the first time the Count felt profoundly that, above the spiteful world, above men who gnaw at one another, above insatiable cupidity, pitiless arrogance, and the varied and nauseating slime in which our civil life is mired and entangled, there hangs suspended in the blue sky — of which it is the very substance — a serene force that transforms, that redeems, that creates, that can make us good and happy: Love!


### Chapter Three

<!-- pages:136-141 -->



LOVE!... for Miss Sara or for Miss Elisa? The problem, simple as it was, stirred in his breast a singular alarm, succeeded by a no less singular irritation. "Why torment oneself to resolve it," he concluded, "when marriage is forbidden me by other and higher ideals?"

Di Rudio was acting unconsciously, and in a different sphere, much as a man acts when prejudice has taken root in his brain and he is confronted by new ideas. He senses the reasonableness of those ideas, and yet — he shuts his eyes obstinately to the truth, and the very certainty that his idol is crumbling makes him a more strenuous champion of the old fables and the old lies.

And now, here is how the long drought of di Rudio's purse came to an end.

From the day on which the Coup d'État had triumphed in definitive fashion, Victor Hugo, taking the road of exile, had settled on the island of Jersey. There he published "L'Homme," a periodical whose pages blazed forth, with all the intensity that genius lends it, a condemnation of the baleful imperial politics. The hero of the Second of December, as sensitive to the stings of criticism as any criminal who aspires to glory, complained of it adroitly to the Cabinet of London, which with equal adroitness deflected the complaint. But when the complications of European politics indicated to England that Napoleon III was the sovereign best placed to lend her a strong hand in the meditated enterprise of breaking the pride of the Czar, it became an act of wise diplomacy to humour that man's caprices. And the freedom of thought and the traditionally generous hospitality of the English people were, at least in part, curtailed and sacrificed to the real or supposed interests of the Nation.

Victor Hugo was surrounded by various brilliant minds, among whom Pianciani had joined himself. That nest of intellectuals was suddenly thrown into disarray. And so great a sacrilege did it seem that even the French reactionaries dared not applaud.

Pianciani resolved to take refuge in London once again. Before leaving the island, he dispatched, together with his other papers and insured for ten thousand pounds, the manuscript of his *Storia dei Papi* — his "History of the Popes." The other parcels arrived at their destination; the manuscript disappeared. And rumour had it that the Jesuits had taken possession of it, most probably to consign it to the flames. A certain colour of truth was lent to this rumour by the fact that the Insurance Company paid out the considerable indemnity immediately, without initiating the smallest inquiry, without uttering a word.

Pianciani and the Count di Rudio met, and the Roman gentleman — who, with his expansive cordiality, his generous impulses, made people forgive the deformity of his face, which the smallpox had robbed of its nose — received the gentleman from Belluno as an equal among equals. At parting, and as if in passing, the conversation fell upon the latter's financial straits.

"You could not have come at a more opportune moment!" said Pianciani. "I have set myself to attempt the *Storia dei Papi* anew. Will you be my amanuensis?"

The man accustomed to the bold ventures, the fatigues, the dangers of action was thus confined to an armchair; and he made a virtue of it, though not without the occasional dark brow and muttered grumble. In compensation, he was brought into contact with those men illustrious for genius, for character, for sufferings manfully borne, who frequented Pianciani's house — rebels persecuted, calumniated, without roof or bread, yet unvanquished; great in their loves, their hatreds, their utopias. A noble and proud company!

Most of them nourished a fixed idea. In those days the Crimean War was limping forward.
