From the very first stirrings of the troubles that led to that war, Giuseppe Mazzini had proposed making appeal to the sense of unity in Germany; seconding the national aspirations of Italy and Hungary; sending a breath of new life through the Balkans; forming, in short, around the Muscovite Empire a great bulwark of free States that would have compressed and paralysed Russia. This conception was not unwelcome to the Cabinet of Turin. But Napoleon III and England aimed at not provoking Austria's hostility; at inducing her, rather, to take active part in their alliance. And it was precisely because Austria would never have resolved to take the field so long as the terrible hornet's nest of Italy hung about her neck, that the cooperation of the House of Savoy was accepted with good grace — indeed, actively solicited. The Piedmontese army was the only strongly organised nucleus; the only one deemed capable of precipitating events in our Peninsula. Austria remained unmoved, and thus spared the Italians the humiliation of seeing the heroes of our independence fight side by side with the Croatian hordes, in order to maintain upon the European continent "a melancholy projection of Asia."

And the great meditated insurrection of peoples was reduced, in practical terms, to a simple, long, and tedious siege. England, shaken in her military prestige and short of soldiers, resolved to open enlistment in order to organise a few foreign legions.

Scarcely had the appropriate decree been promulgated than Fumagalli and Leoni presented themselves before di Rudio. Leoni had decided to depart for the Crimea and had come to pay a farewell visit. "And if it should be for the last time," he said, gripping the Count's hand, "well then — for the last time — farewell!"

They went to pass the few remaining hours before departure at the public house of a certain Mrs Shaw, wife of one of our countrymen — the very meeting-place of the recruits. The establishment, fitted out with a show of elegance, was already exceptionally animated — a confused animation, full of sudden bursts of laughter. Soldiers and civilians, gathered in knots, clapped one another on the shoulder and exchanged nicknames and hearty, broad epithets. In the secluded corners, a few gentle figures in bonnets, with pale and tearful faces. Flowers and sprigs of green on every breast; bluish spirals of cigarette smoke from almost every mouth.

But the noise and confusion ill suited di Rudio's temper, the more so as the sight of so many Italians on the point of shedding their blood for a cause he held foreign to the Fatherland brought to mind the sorry days of the *soldati di ventura* — the mercenary soldiers. And when Leoni, half in earnest and half in jest, said to him, "Come, Moretto! Make up your mind and sail with us!" he asked with an excess of bluntness: "What harm have the Russians done you, that you should offer up your youth as a holocaust, with the aim of swelling the number of widows and orphans amongst them?"

And Leoni replied: "Italian colours are flying in the Crimea!"

— "Any flag is a mockery when it does not stand as the symbol of truth and justice!"

The conversation was taking a bad turn. Fortunately a Milanese conscript, picking up the thread of a previous discussion, gave voice to his regret at not having thought to bring with him the *Sepolcri* of Ugo Foscolo, which would have contributed so greatly to shortening for him the hours of the crossing.

"I have a copy at home," di Rudio told him. "Will you accept it? It is a cheap little edition, the margins covered with scribbles — but they do not spoil the substance."

In four strides he was back at Baldwin's Garden. He mounted the stairs and entered his room, leaving the door ajar. He was already turning the chest of drawers upside down when from the other side of the corridor there reached him distinctly the sound of sobbing and sighs. What could this be? Stepping onto the threshold, he found himself face to face with Miss Sara, who, colouring fleetingly and without waiting to be questioned, said: "Elisa is weeping because she is convinced you are leaving tonight for the Crimea. Love is consuming her. And she has also compromised herself in Mama's eyes. It was that business of the receipts — you remember? Mama has drawn everything from her heart, and the least she can expect now is a thorough scolding. The mysterious stranger — it is there that she is in despair. It was Elisa who provided from her own savings — for everything, everything!"

The Count di Rudio picked up the great poet, turned him this way and that, and finally thrust him with an angry motion to the bottom of his pocket: "Tell Miss Elisa that the di Rudios are no mercenary soldiers. For Liberty — always! Mercenaries of foreign despots — never!"

And he descended the stairs at a headlong pace. But the storm that Miss Sara's words had raised followed him into the street; it entered through the crack of the door into Mrs Shaw's public house, and eddied about his head the whole evening long — so that the conscripts and the literature and the politics and the uproar and the tumultuous scene of the embarkation and the farewells reached his brain not as a vivid and well-defined reality, but as the pale evocation of images emerging confusedly from the mists of times long past.

A second surprise awaited him. Coming home towards one o'clock in the morning, he made out, seated on the landing, a female figure in a white gown — in the darkness it seemed to him phosphorescent. A moment of stupefaction seized him; and she rose, and retreating, seemed to dissolve away. He would have taken her for a phantom, had not a door creaked on its hinges. Miss Elisa had passed so great a part of the night keeping vigil — in tears, perhaps — to assure herself that the sea and the war had not taken him from her.

When he presented himself, rigid and master of himself, to the two young women, Elisa received him with the words: "Are you unwell? You are pale this morning!"

— "It seems to me that you are both pale!" observed the cousin. And she offered to prepare a cup of warm milk — Mama too, on her return, would have been glad of one. When she reached the threshold she turned, and smiling: "Do not commit any foolishness. Every moment lost is stolen from your happiness, and by dint of concealing yourselves you might end by not finding each other at all. You love each other; you were born for one another. Is it really so very difficult?" And she was gone.

The decisive moment had arrived. Elisa was nervously twisting a ribbon of hers, and di Rudio observed her, remaining at a respectful distance. He began by thanking her for all she had done for him. Denial was useless — Sara had confided everything to him. The misfortune was that he could demonstrate his gratitude only in words. And he went on to outline the sad vicissitudes to which one who champions a holy and persecuted cause must submit. With what heart could he have dared offer her his hand — to drag her into his tumultuous life, into his poverty? Where could he find her a nest that would not be, sooner or later, contaminated and destroyed by the fury of men? Would it be wise, humane, honourable to reserve for children born to perpetuate the gentle maternal virtues an atmosphere haunted by the terror of conspiracies, the menace of weapons, the clang of chains, the dread of blood? Childhood yearns for the kisses of smiling lips; it wishes to mirror in its candid soul the serenity of quiet eyes. And what else could he say to her; what happiness could he promise her, without resorting to mendacious phrases? He had chosen, between two evils, the lesser; he had resolved to take himself away — for ever. He entreated her to interpret his resolution with the very rectitude of feeling that had suggested it; to preserve a kindly memory of him.

Here — and here only — Miss Elisa raised her face and fixed her eyes upon his. With the keen discernment of simple souls, she knew how to sound another soul. She expressed herself as a creature conscious of the power to dispose of her own destiny, with the calm and chaste eloquence which to some of my readers, accustomed to extolling "the opulence of the hip and the tremors of the flesh," might seem exhumed from some museum of archaeology — other times, other men, other loves!

"Towards a soul such as his she could advance without suspicion and without artifice — she would have preferred to be definitively rejected rather than to become his through simulated virtue. There were Italian women, she was not ignorant of it, who had suppressed their serene aspirations and imposed upon their hearts solitude and silence, in order not to become wives and mothers in calamitous times. She admired their austerity, but could not bring herself to approve it. The wicked bring up the wicked freely, and should the good withdraw from the duty of rearing those who will perpetuate honourable traditions? The very environment that seemed unfavourable to the cultivation of the gentle virtues he attributed to her in tender hearts would have fostered the development of the virile paternal virtues. The tempering of manly characters is a mission that refines a mother's pride. Or did he perhaps think her, like his ancient Roman women, fit only to oversee the good order of the kitchen and to spin wool? Was the anguish of hearts that sweat blood, the terrible straining of muscles that swell to break their chains, the tormenting labour of minds that seek everywhere equity and light — were these to have left her indifferent? She had a profound sympathy for the country he had painted for her so beautiful, so great, so unfortunate. The love she bore the Fatherland did not make her jealous of his — to draw him away from his purposes, to blunt his generous impulses!? Precisely the contrary! They would have worked together, and with redoubled energy, for the triumph of Liberty. He had kindled a flame that made her greater than herself — let him try to extinguish it, if he had the heart for it, if he had the strength!"

The Count was thinking: "Blessed be the rosy lips that conquer the eloquence of so many martyrs, living and dead!" And yet he insisted:

"No, Elisa! The conspirator is solitary even in the bosom of his family. He must at times forget that he has a mother and a wife and children. If his predestined hour arrives, he closes his ears to their prayers, does not see their tears, and goes — goes without confiding his secrets even to the air he breathes. If that hour were to come—"

"— One last embrace, a wrench of the heart, perhaps even that — but no indiscreet questions. Am I not certain that you can do nothing of which I should have cause to be ashamed?"

When Miss Sara returned with the cups and the warm milk, the lover who but a short while before had wished to flee far, far away, had one knee upon the ground. Holding Elisa's hands in his, he kissed them — kissed them again and again.

On the ninth of December, 1855, a flame was made lawful — upon which misfortune hastened to blow, but in vain. The stern vicissitudes of more than half a century were destined not to extinguish it, but to make it burn ever brighter.


### Chapter Four

<!-- pages:142-143 -->

THE word to the Count di Rudio: The very year I settled into married life I was drawn into an ill-fated affair that caused a great deal of noise. The exiles who were not well furnished with *baiocchi* — pennies — frequented a certain eating-house in Rupert Street, where for a modest sum they could savour a dish or two cooked in our own fashion. I had found my way there more than once myself, and had there struck up an acquaintance with one Rossi, a young man from Mantua, who quickly earned my sympathy through his open and cheerful character. Rossi was excessively loose in matters of the heart, but I could discover no other vice in him; and when the Romagnol Foschini warned me, in the strictest confidence, to keep well clear of that "ugly customer" because he was a spy in the pay of the French Government, I observed that to lend easy credence to calumnies most probably insinuated, for dark purposes, by enemies of our party, was to play into their hands in the most childish manner imaginable.

Foschini, sullen, turned his back on me, and I — drew closer to Rossi still — partly, also, from that imperious instinct within me to see clearly to the very bottom of things. I came to be persuaded that Foschini was genuinely mistaken. That conviction, truth to tell, is not so firmly rooted in me today. A certain Rossi, as I learned several years later, had been darkly implicated in the conspiracies of Mantua. Yet Rossi is a very common surname in Italy, and I have never since had the means of ascertaining whether my London acquaintance and the spy of the Austrian Government were one and the same person. For anyone who cares to pursue these minutiae, I will add that the Rossi of London had a brother who was a priest — a circumstance which might serve as a guide toward establishing the truth.

One evening, at a late hour, I found myself in the tavern in Rupert Street in the company of the "ugly customer." Foschini was there as well, seated quite alone, apart from the rest; but from the black looks he had cast at us, and from certain syllables muttered through clenched teeth, I had been able to gather that there was some risk of sailing into troubled waters.

The other patrons thinned away, and we were left nearly alone. At a certain point Foschini rose to his feet and, passing stiffly beside Rossi, jostled him at the elbow and said: "Spy!"

Rossi answered with a compliment worse still, and Foschini, stung across the face, wheeled round in a fury. I thought it my duty to intervene, and was urging them to make no scandal, so as not to compromise our emigrant community, when the Romagnol drew a dagger and sprang forward. One second more and Rossi would have been done for. To prevent the crime, I seized a chair and did my best to bring it down upon the attacker. Unfortunately I struck the lamp hanging from the ceiling; it shattered, and we were left in darkness. I remember the sensation of a cold blade entering my breast — then nothing more.

When a glimmer of consciousness returned to me, I understood that I was in a hospital. I could not keep my eyes open, but I heard a voice — that of the physician — pronounce that I should not see tomorrow's sun. They made me swallow some preparation or other, and that brief effort was enough for me to lose my senses once again.

But my youth reasserted itself, and in spite of the grim prophecy, my first impression was precisely that of the sun's rays beating upon my face. They roused in me a firm will to cling to life.

I learned that the doctor, considering the case hopeless, had resorted, in order to arrest the internal haemorrhage, to a heroic remedy of his own devising. He regarded the outcome as a marvel, and for a long succession of days he brought to my bedside now one colleague, now another, engaging in discussions of probably great scientific value — which to me, a layman and a man of irritable nerves, arrived at the most singularly inopportune moments.

Before I received the regulation visit from a Police Inspector, the doctor informed me that Foschini had made two other victims. But Rossi — the one man Foschini had beyond all doubt wished to dispose of — had come away without a scratch.

Upon Foschini, who had gone into hiding, the Authorities placed a reward of two hundred pounds. But he was a good patriot, and although his hiding-place was known to many, no one breathed a word. On the contrary, in the praiseworthy effort to shield him, the political motive that had impelled him to act was greatly exaggerated, with little scruple as to whether, in saving the reputation of the guilty man, the reputations of the innocent were diminished.

Now it reasserted itself, and in spite of the strangeness of a fate which in Europe first — upon my face. They roused in me in America afterwards — a will to face the gravest perils; and although I was more than once a hair's breadth from leaving my bones there, I always came away with my skin intact. It was reserved for my companions in faith to repay my disinterestedness with thirty-six stab wounds, two of which had penetrated vital cavities and were judged mortal.

Foschini closed his days splendidly, fighting at the side of Carlo Pisacane in the ill-fated, glorious expedition of Sapri.

Sapri was the first grave manifestation of the new change in tactics adopted by Giuseppe Mazzini. The Crimean War being ended, the great Cavour, notwithstanding the splendid showing of our soldiers and their victory at the Cernaia, had obtained nothing for his King — not even the hoped-for annexation of Parma and Modena, whose petty princes it was wished to foist upon Moldavia and Wallachia. But Napoleon III had promised that one day he would do something for Piedmont as well, and Mazzini held it as certain, from those very days, that an alliance between the Empire and Vittorio Emanuele II would come. He was, however, firmly convinced that from such an alliance nothing would result save an enlargement of the Sardinian States, or at best, in the most favourable hypothesis, a federation of Italian States governed, in large part, by French princes. To forestall so great a calamity, he abandoned his old field of action and laboured, together with his most trusted followers, to raise up the Kingdom of the Two Sicilies. To set against the monarchical agitation of the North the insurrection of the southernmost extremity of Italy; to impose from Palermo and Naples the unity of the Nation. This inspired conception earned Giuseppe Mazzini and Garibaldi a sentence of "ignominious death," yet it was destined to figure among the greatest factors in our future greatness.

Left entirely to one side — most probably because it was assumed I had grown soft under the influence of new affections — I was already resigning myself to long inaction, when suddenly the call reached me from the quarter I least expected.


### Chapter Five

<!-- pages:143-148 -->



IN September of 1857, whilst I was making shift as best I could by giving language lessons in Nottingham, an Italian exile, passing through that city, came to call upon me. He found himself in circumstances far more dire than my own. I assisted him with what I could, and that same day he continued on his way. Arriving in Birmingham, he applied to Felice Orsini for aid — Orsini being, if I am not mistaken, his fellow townsman. In recounting his tribulations, he made mention of the visit he had paid me. "By God!" exclaimed Felice Orsini. "The very man I am looking for!" And he wrote to me, appointing a meeting at the Great Northern station in London.

I had not seen Felice Orsini for a long while. But no sooner had the train come puffing under the station roof at Charing Cross than I spotted him standing among the crowd, with his cases and his coal-black moustache. Within seconds I stood before him. "Where the devil have you been all this time?" he said to me. And I told him. When I alluded to my marriage, his face clouded over. "Then farewell to patriotic intentions!" I disabused him of that notion. On the contrary, I was now under an obligation all the greater to conspire and to fight — my son was not to have a servile and unhappy fatherland.

He asked whether I knew the reason for which he had given me an appointment, and upon my replying in the negative, he invited me to step outside the station. Once in the open air he reminded me, without preamble, of the letter I had written to Giuseppe Mazzini and of my proposal to rid France of Napoleon by suppressing him. He had it in mind to translate that old idea of mine into action. The times seemed to him propitious. Was I still of the same opinion?

— What does Mazzini think of it?

At this, he made a gesture as if to say: Why do you speak to me of archaeology? And he pressed his point by affirming that he no longer spoke with Mazzini. Moreover, Massarenti, the *alter ego* of the "prophet," was a false specimen of the Bolognese breed and would, the moment he caught wind of the matter, most certainly denounce them. For this reason too, it was necessary to leave Mazzini to one side.

I ended by telling him that I was not averse to taking part in the enterprise. But I wished to see clearly. With Napoleon killed, what would happen? What were his plans for the aftermath?

I repeat, in summary, his explanations. They reveal, if not the plan itself, the ends of a conspiracy about which so much has been written, yet without ever laying bare, in its entirety, the truth of the matter. They constitute, at the same time, a posthumous refutation of the charges which certain historians, lacking the necessary faculties of judgment, saw fit to bring against Orsini.

Felice Orsini had reached a complete understanding with the French Republicans. The coup d'état had surprised and paralysed them, but had not subdued them. They were conspiring to prepare a revolution. A successful stroke in Paris meant the liberation of France, and France restored to liberty meant all of Europe in ferment. Ledru-Rollin — Orsini assured me — despaired of success so long as he faced the sinister figure of Napoleon. There is more force than one would suppose in a name. Were France to stir, Kossuth would answer for Hungary. As for our Italy — Napoleon suppressed, the barricades would be organised in Paris. The enemy, caught unawares, without an authoritative leader, with the cohesion of the army already undermined by subtle intrigue, would fall ignominiously. The Republic would be proclaimed, and I would immediately be appointed, with full powers, Commissioner to the French army stationed in Rome. These are thirty thousand seasoned and well-equipped men whom I gain, at a single stroke, for the Italian cause. What do I do? In a single day I cancel out the work and the shame of Napoleon's action of 1849. The Pope and the Cardinals become my prisoners and I lock them away in Castel Sant'Angelo — their fate will depend on the attitude of Europe. In the meantime I sever the temporal power from the spiritual, and from the Capitol I proclaim the Roman Republic restored. The French garrison leaves Rome and I drive it swiftly to the aid of the Italian revolution. Do we win? So much the better! Do we suffer a reverse? Republican France, stung to the quick, rises up like a fury and lays down her arms no more until the prestige of her own name is fully vindicated. Austria, attacked from every side, without friends in Europe, with rebellion blazing within her own borders, is swept away and never again sets foot in Italy.

True, the definitive political complexion of things would remain to be determined. But all of this depends on audacity! The first spark must be struck. Without it there is no conflagration, no independence is possible, no Republic is possible. You know that I am opposed to the shedding of blood. Nature has endowed me with a humane temperament: I aim at the heights, and the means imposed by these dark times are repugnant to me. But I say that the blood of Louis Bonaparte — of the man who knowingly broke his solemnly sworn pledges, who betrayed us, who put our Fatherland back in chains, who makes a desert of every noble aspiration of our Peninsula — that blood ought not to weigh in the balance more than the liberty of seventy million human creatures. To shed it averts the sacrifice of thousands upon thousands of honest souls, and the cruel act becomes a work of mercy. Decide!

And I decided. The integrity of the man was known to me; the French revolutionary undercurrent was known to me, more or less; the conditions of Italy were known. To probe further I deemed superfluous, and I contented myself with asking the names of those who were to carry out the material execution of the plan. But Orsini was evasive on this point. The great danger in great conspiracies — he pointed out to me — lies precisely in their very vastness. The more numerous those who carry the secret in their hearts, the greater becomes the likelihood that some lip will let it escape. To mitigate, in part, this unavoidable danger, he had decided that the participants in the attempt should not know one another.

To shelter from the cold, which that day was rather biting, we entered a public house. The conversation continued in fits and starts. Orsini had to be in Liverpool by the following day, and he begged me to return to Nottingham. "Stay quietly at home and get on with your work, until I make myself heard." If he could not come himself, he would send a trusted person, bearing a letter in his hand and carrying his instructions. And, as a further precaution, we established a password. I asked him whether, with regard to the attempt, he already had some well-defined plan. He knitted his brows and seemed put out. Orsini could not dissemble his own emotions. After a brief silence, he replied that he could communicate nothing precise to me. "My intention," he added, "would be to have us invited to Court. There are those who can procure us as many invitations as we desire. To approach the usurper during a ball is a matter of small moment, and one might, with cold steel, achieve the aim without spilling a single drop of blood more than necessary. But there is a fear that those in interest would close the doors of the palace and prevent the news of what had occurred from spreading, until all the orders necessary to forestall the consequences had been given; until they had proclaimed the Regency. Perhaps it will be necessary to resort to a noisier means. Be that as it may — your word is given. May I count upon it with full assurance?"

I replied that Carlo di Rudio had never broken his pledges; and since, by simple intuition, I believed I had grasped, more or less, what the "noisier means" might consist in, I observed to him that the usurper's entourage must necessarily be composed of supporters of the Empire and of soldiers who had been accomplices of the 2nd of December. The more of them one killed, the fewer we should find arrayed against us at the barricades of the morrow.

We parted that same evening. The day after, I was on my way back to Nottingham, and as I turned over in my mind, to the rhythm of the train, what had been confided to me and the likelihood of drawing from it a broad movement that might prove salutary to the popular cause throughout all of Europe, I understood immediately what an immense advantage the knowledge of Mazzini would prove to be. I knew of no influence more profound and more efficacious than his. To leave such a man to one side was to risk losing the fruit of our daring. The figure of the "Bolognese" crossed my mind, but seemed to me of no great consequence. I knew full well that it was enough to speak with discretion and to ask for a promise of absolute silence, for the Maestro to face and to endure any torture whatsoever without letting slip a single compromising syllable. The "coward" of the Savoy expedition, as certain moderate newspapers called him, was indisputably among the most humanly courageous individuals of the century.

The disagreements between the Maestro and Orsini? I felt certain they would vanish in the face of the supreme interests of the fatherland. And from that moment, I resolved to open my heart to Mazzini, to confide in him so much as would suffice to ensure that events should not catch him unawares.

My wife was pleasantly surprised by my return. She ventured not a single indiscreet question, and for several days our life passed in its customary calm, as though nothing were in the air. Towards the middle of November, a letter was delivered to me bearing the Birmingham postmark. It contained these simple words: "Go to London and send me your address."

I understood that the knot was drawing tighter, and reflecting that in all probability, once I had left English soil, I should be given no further opportunity to see again either my wife or my little child, I said drily to Elisa: "Prepare your trunk; I intend to take up residence in London." She set about complying at once. I observed her. In her every movement there shone through the loving conviction that she must seek no further; that whatever I demanded of her was for good purpose and for a just cause. She brushed and folded and put away — gentle and silent.

We departed and took up lodgings in Bedman Buildings, on the square at Soho. Two days of waiting. On the morning of the third, I was tickling the little one under the chin — Elisa had just set him in a basin of warm water — when a ring at the bell made me start. On the threshold I found myself face to face with a well-dressed gentleman, evidently a foreigner, who, having asked whether I was such-and-such without adding anything further, held out an open letter to me. It was from Felice Orsini, requesting me to place "full trust in Simon Bernard, a French physician from Carcassonne." As an added safeguard, I asked for and obtained the password. I then invited my visitor to enter.

The child was kicking his legs in the basin and beating the water with his little hands, spattering the table. At that sight, Simon Bernard fixed his eyes upon my face, as though addressing me a silent question; then he looked again at the child and finally cast upon my wife a troubled and perplexed glance.

I thought I divined the cause of his embarrassment and pointed out to him that he might freely speak in French; my wife would not understand a syllable of it.

"Oh, it is not precisely that!" And he became thoughtful again. Suddenly he seemed to make up his mind, and, recovering all the gallantry natural to his race, he lavished compliments upon the mother for the flourishing health of her little one, and upon the husband for his excellent taste in the choice of a wife. He spoke of London and its accursed climate; of France and the matchless gaiety of Paris; he touched upon a hundred subjects without delving into any of them, with an elegance of phrase and a volatility of thought more appropriate to a drawing-room of idle gentlemen than to the humble chamber of a conspirator and among conspirators.

When he took his leave, I accompanied him to the door, and he, touching my arm, said to me in an undertone: "Let us take a few turns about the square; we shall have more freedom to talk."

And out on the square, at last giving free rein to the thought that had been preoccupying him: "*Parbleu!* I did not know that you were a family man!"

— Felice Orsini knows it.

— But the Secret Committee here in London does not. In any case, this visit was necessary so that we might make one another's acquaintance. This evening the Committee assembles and I shall report. We shall meet again tomorrow.

And on the following day he came. From the moment I opened the door to him, I noted that his expression was that of a man in good spirits. This time he ventured to speak in Elisa's presence — in French, naturally. He had nothing new to impart concerning the conspiracy. But he was pleased to announce to me that the Secret Committee had decided to pay my wife, during my absence, fourteen shillings a week. In case of misfortune, the allowance would be continued until my child had reached the age of fourteen years.

Rightly or wrongly, the paramount preoccupation of being able to serve my country had almost entirely crowded from my mind the other preoccupation of the future. Elisa's mother belonged to that order of women whose name cannot be separated from the idea of veneration. The certainty that she would not leave either her daughter or the little one destitute had been sufficient to keep me, in this respect, perfectly tranquil. And to tell the truth, the news communicated to me by Simon Bernard at first nettled me. But had I the right to indulge the impulse of pride; to lay upon frail and unknowing creatures the full weight of my actions? Would it have been honest to refuse?

I gave my thanks, and Simon Bernard, pleading pressing business as his excuse — on account of which, moreover, he would not be able to show himself for some days — shook my hand and departed. My astonishment was therefore considerable when, the following morning, I found him once again face to face with me upon the threshold. Without even crossing it, he set forth the reason for that unexpected visit. The Secret Committee had reconsidered its decision and had concluded that it could not hazard the life of a man who had a wife and was a father. I was accordingly relieved, with a thousand thanks, from taking any part in the affair. I contented myself with replying: "Very well. Let us say no more about it."


### Chapter Six

<!-- pages:148-152 -->



FOR several days I forced myself to suppress the thoughts that swarmed tumultuously through my head, whilst renewing old acquaintances. At the same time I sought some foothold that might put an end to my extremely precarious financial situation and secure me work and bread for the future.

One evening, shortly before sunset, I was making my way home, unusually weary, almost dispirited, from my customary excursion, when, turning the corner into the square of Shoah, I literally collided with Simon Bernard.

"Bravo! I was just coming from your lodgings. Signor Orsini has written. Do you know what he has written? That he knows you by proof. That he wants a man in whom he places his fullest trust. Either you, or the whole affair goes to ruin. What do you intend to do?"

This time I answered: "I have given my word to Felice Orsini; I shall keep it."

Instead of returning home directly, we walked round the square three or four times. Orsini's letter had dissolved the coldness of Simon Bernard and loosened his tongue. I learned that Felice Orsini was already in Paris, gathering the threads of his conspiracy; that the means definitively chosen for the attempt was that of bombs. That the Baron Egassy di Torocfalda had conceived a wholly novel type, fashioned in the form of a hedgehog, each point armed with a percussion cap, so that they would explode however they might be thrown. The Taylor foundry at Birmingham had cast them piece by piece, as though they were gas fittings. The compound intended to detonate them was mercury fulminate. The same Baron di Torrocfalda had taught Orsini how to prepare it, and the latter had profited so well from the lesson that he came through the business having barely scorched a few fingers.

On taking his leave, Simon Bernard urged me to hold myself in readiness, as the order to depart might reach me from one day to the next. And I concluded at once that the time had come to solicit the cooperation of Giuseppe Mazzini.

To obtain a meeting with him was not so simple a matter as it might appear; whether because the Maestro, watched as he was by every police force in the world, had grown wary; or whether, as many maintained, certain persons by whom he had surrounded himself were at pains to keep at a distance all those who might effectively assist him in some bold undertaking of his. In conversation among friends one would say, sometimes with a smile but more often with no little vexation, that it was harder to obtain an audience with Giuseppe Mazzini than with the Queen of England.

In those days, to tell the truth, there had gathered about me, along with Massarenti, several other individuals whom I knew to be in close relations with the Maestro. But my legitimate mistrust had led me to play, in their company, the part of the man who listens and does not speak.

Unexpectedly, chance appeared to favour me. I was walking in Cheapside with a certain Gori, formerly a band-leader in the Zambeccari Legion, when I caught sight, a short distance ahead, of Mazzini walking arm in arm with a stranger. I quickened my pace and drew level with him. Naturally I could not speak of so weighty a matter in the presence of strangers, and after the necessary greetings I was obliged to confine myself to telling Mazzini that I wished to have a meeting with him, and to beg him to indicate the hour and place. Giuseppe Mazzini gazed into the air, as though seeking inspiration there; then, having asked for my address, he said that Massarenti would be able to tell me something on the matter. Massarenti — of all people, precisely the fish that repelled us! We parted ways.

Events rushed headlong and drew me into their vortex before Mazzini made any sign. What would have come of it had I been able to speak with him? Would he have dissuaded me from the enterprise, or would he have lent to it the contribution of his influence and his ardent soul? These are questions that were put to me repeatedly. Certainly, to answer them signifies nothing. Yet I observe: the man who wrote that certain exceptional actions, aimed at the vindication of human rights, must not be judged by the measure of common morality; the man who pointed out to Gallenga the dagger that should have killed Carlo Alberto; who had a part — not clearly defined, yet certain — in the Tebaldi conspiracy against Luigi Bonaparte, could not logically oppose that of Felice Orsini. And the Maestro knew full well where logic has its dwelling place!

The Tebaldi conspiracy was in fact organised in that very year, 1857. Napoleon III frequented the house of the Countess Sommariva for reasons which, it was said, were better known to him than to the Countess's own family. Tebaldi and his two accomplices were to enter that house with a false key, and seize the person of the Emperor. It appears they wished to compel him to abdicate. But the capture of a man so closely guarded could not be effected without bloodshed, and in the eyes of the Maestro the life of a simple police constable was worth no less than that of an Emperor. As it was, Tebaldi's two accomplices were unable to keep the secret, and all three were arrested. Tebaldi was consigned to the Île du Diable; his two accomplices to Algeria.

The reaction provoked by the misdeeds of Luigi Napoleon was too intense not to manifest itself in a violent and personal form. Regicide was in the air, and Napoleon knew it so well that as early as 1852, after the coup d'état and before the proclamation of the Empire, he had the Romagnols Galli and Rossini deported to Cayenne, guilty of nothing but having fallen foul of the infamous Law of Suspects.

The attempt of that other bold Romagnol, Giovanni Pianori, failed because Napoleon, unable to shelter himself

*"Sotto l'usbergo del sentirsi puro"* — beneath the shield of a clear conscience — secretly wore a coat of mail. Pianori was struck down before he could fire his second shot. He swelled the ranks of those martyrs who died beneath the guillotine.

His brother, already embittered by political hatred, upon that tragic death felt his furies redouble within his breast. He left Italy, nursing thoughts of a resounding vengeance. But scarcely had he touched French soil when he was arrested and deported to the hell of the tropical islands, like Galli and Rossini, without the shadow of a trial.

Fierce tempers, those of the Pianori! They acknowledged no duty save the vindication of their own rights and those of others, and towards that end they pressed forward, resolute, without troubling themselves about the Laws, which they regarded as a scarecrow devised by the cunning for purposes of domination and plunder; without regard for their own lives, which they called well spent if spent in cancelling an injustice.

Giovanni Pianori, the man guillotined, stands out in all his grim entirety from yet another tragic incident. He loved his wife to distraction. Compelled by the turbulent vicissitudes of his existence to leave her, he had entrusted her to the care of a close friend who, having insinuated himself into the heart of the charming woman placed in his keeping, showed himself too tender a lover to remain a faithful friend. Some zealous acquaintance whispered the painful truth into the ear of the betrayed husband, who, braving a thousand dangers, made a sudden return. It proved easy to wrest from his terrified wife the ill-advised confession. He became calm once more and did not lay a finger upon her; but he imposed absolute silence with that resoluteness which brooks no reply.

The intimate friend arrived. Pianori, dissembling, invited him, after a brief conversation, outside. They would be able to discuss certain secret political matters with greater freedom in the open air. Then suddenly, in the darkness, the friend felt himself confronted with this question: "What would you do to a friend to whom you had entrusted your honour, and all that is most dear to you in the world, if he were to dishonour you, if he were to betray you?"

— You have pronounced your own sentence!

And without another word he left him gasping in his own blood.

A crime? I do not say otherwise. I say only that iron times make iron men, and that it is folly to expect mild manners and sheepish sentiments from people accustomed to the daily spectacle of virtue dangling from a gallows. And I say also that abstract ideas are fixed in practical life, incarnate in History, not by timid bleating, but by the impetuous action of tempers such as these.

The next letter sent me by Felice Orsini had been written in Paris, but its envelope bore no postmark other than that of London. Evidently a trustworthy third party had received it and re-posted it from that city. Orsini begged me to call upon a Miss Elisa Cheney, residing in Cambdentown, who would deliver to me a valise and a pair of gold spectacles. What was really meant by "gold spectacles" I learnt shortly afterwards from Simon Bernard. In all likelihood, so as not to attract the attention of the customs officers with an excessively bulky parcel, Felice Orsini had carried with him, on his journey to Paris, only a portion of the mercury fulminate he required. He was now entrusting me with the conveyance of the remainder.

I went to Cambdentown that very evening. Miss Elisa Cheney, a woman apparently in her thirties, slender, slight, dark-haired, not beautiful in the classical sense but very attractive, received me with exquisite graciousness and a smile upon her lips. The valise she handed me was, more precisely, one of those bags the English call a carpet bag — its sides of fabric, its base of leather. Upon the sides a tiger was embroidered. The receptacle for the mercury fulminate was of mahogany and had every appearance of a toilet-case. It sat upon the mantelpiece among some trinkets and a clock. Before reaching out to take it, the young lady tossed Orsini's letter onto the marble surround. She was perfectly composed; so much so that her unvarying smile did not falter even when she held the box in her fingers. Either she was endowed with nerves of proof, or she was ignorant of the terrible power of the elegant object she was handing me. She stood watching me in silence while I stowed it back in the bag; then, all of a sudden, still smiling: "You are going to Paris? Bon voyage and my compliments to Signor Orsini!"

When I returned home and Elisa came to offer me her cheek for the customary kiss, the child in her arms, I had to make an effort to carry the valise with an easy air. "Have you been shopping?" she asked, and stretched out her arm. But I said lightly: "Take care! The tiger is alive and might scratch you!" Then, becoming serious again: "It is only papers that cannot interest you." And I locked the tiger away in a cupboard — the tiger capable of rending apart, concealed beside the other embroidered and harmless one.

The following day, the 8th of January, 1858, Simon Bernard appeared once more upon the threshold. His expression was grave, almost solemn, and I, who was now expecting some definitive communication, understood at once that four turns about the square were most advisable. Nor was I mistaken.

"You must leave," Bernard said to me. "Orsini reckons, indeed, that you should set out tomorrow morning. In that case, tomorrow evening, between six and eight, you would arrive in Paris. He will be at the station — the rest will take care of itself. But should you be unable to depart in time, or should Orsini, through some unforeseen mishap, fail to keep the appointment, take note of his address!" And he dictated it to me.

He took his leave with a hearty handshake and the assurance: "Au revoir, in a few days, in Paris."

That evening, on returning home, I did not give Elisa the customary kiss, as though to prepare her for the parting. While she laid the table for supper, I sat apart, nervously tormenting my beard. She caught me with my eyes fixed upon her and, unable to restrain herself, threw her arms about my neck, murmuring softly in my ear: "Carlo, what is the matter?"

Then I rose, stroked her hair, kissed her brow, and spoke words which, on another occasion, in a very different circumstance, I had impressed upon her tender heart, almost as the seal of a solemn covenant: "The conspirator must be solitary even in the bosom of his family. He must at times forget that he has a mother, a wife, and children. If his appointed hour arrives, he closes his ears to prayers, he does not see the tears, and he departs — departs without confiding his secrets even to the air he breathes. Elisa — that hour has come!"

She turned pale and, at first, found not a word, breathed not a sigh. But it was as though a certain key had been struck; it must necessarily sound a certain note. She smiled sadly and, with a thread of voice that yet revealed the sovereign mastery of her spirit: "What can I say, Carlo? I know that you fight for a just cause; that you can do nothing of which I need be ashamed. Go, and come back soon — as honest as you are, more glorious!"

I knelt and covered her hands with kisses, as on the day of our betrothal.

So as not to prolong the painful hours, I had resolved to leave the house that very evening, if not London; I would be more resolute and more composed in the morning, at the embarkation. We supped in silence; the fullness of our feelings we exchanged in glances, in long caresses. At a certain moment I drew out my watch. She understood, and rose and ran into the other little room of ours. She returned carrying in her arms our first-born, plump and rosy amid white linen.

He slept and smiled — perhaps, in his dream, at his mother's full breast. I embraced Elisa's head with deep feeling, murmured some word of counsel to her — one more kiss with trembling lips — then I fled!


### Chapter Seven

<!-- pages:154-156 -->



THE next morning, I was pressing forward at full steam towards my destiny. To face it, I had my valise... between the leather and the wooden panel of the base, it concealed the mercury fulminate and a blade that had already, during the insurrectionary attempts of 1853, known the beating of my heart. I had, moreover, a passport in the name of Giovanni da Sylva, Portuguese, commercial traveller for a brewery, and about ten shillings. The money and passport had been entrusted to me by Dr. Bernard during our last colloquy. Above all, I had my youth and my courage. What would come of all this? The spark capable of altering the course of history, or nothing more than desolation and death?

Leaving the mouths of the Thames behind us, we encountered a most turbulent sea — a true tempest, indeed. The upheaval of stomachs and the terror spread great confusion aboard. But the infinite, roaring on every side, stirred in me an excitement that redoubled my consciousness of life. For some time a certain quietism had been attempting, as it were, to impose itself upon my nature, and that manner of being plunged into force and motion seemed to regenerate me.

Yet the tempest itself, which I almost blessed, was — unbeknown to me — an answer from things themselves to the question I had put to myself, a question that pressed upon my mind with great insistence. The steamship, slowed by wind and heavy seas, put in at Calais at five o'clock in the morning of the ninth of January... twelve hours late! And when, in Paris, I stepped down from the train, I searched in vain among the crowded throng for the noble figure of Felice Orsini. This setback made imperative a visit to his lodgings in the rue Monthabor... a circumstance which was to have consequences far graver than its apparent insignificance might have led me to suppose.

Here, in brief, is what occurred during that first visit of mine. Orsini's apartment was on the ground floor. The concierge accompanied me to the door, knocked, and when the door opened, discreetly withdrew. I entered with a bound and was on the point of embracing Orsini as a brother — he stood upright and expectant — when I noticed the presence of a third person... a young man stretched out with easy familiarity in an armchair. He was fair-haired, with cheeks and lips completely clean-shaven, and the bearing of a good-natured priest. I then assumed the deferential air of a commercial traveller and, giving the name on the passport, offered to submit to "Tomaso Allsop" some samples of beer.

I was rummaging through the valise, already turned upside-down by the Customs officials, when the false Allsop broke into a loud laugh. "We are among friends," he said. And he introduced me by my true name to Antonio. He then asked me to account for my delay and, when I had done so, said: "How did Miss Cheney receive you? Did she hand over the matter?"

I tipped my few belongings onto the table and set about removing the panel that concealed the mercury fulminate. A moment later, it lay spread out upon a newspaper — *Le Droit*, if memory serves me. Felice Orsini was running his fingertips over it, as though to test the degree of its moisture. His face, so mobile and open by nature, had assumed an expression rigid with deep melancholy. He ended by saying: "The stock is now complete, and not for nothing shall we have cast twelve apostles." And he sighed.

Struck by that melancholic air and by that sigh, I recalled his first plan, inspired by his horror of bloodshed. Evidently, he still held to entrusting the liberation of France to cold steel.

"This devilry is very damp," he resumed, "we shall have to make use of the fireplace and the thermometer. But this does not concern you. I owe it to your wife and your son not to expose you beyond what is strictly necessary."

At that moment there came a knock at the door... assuredly a prearranged signal, for Orsini did not stir and left the fulminate on the table, in plain sight.

There entered a man of my own height, with a grizzled beard and an intelligent eye; the pallor of his face lent extraordinary intensity to his dark pupil. He was taken aback at the sight of me and contented himself with uttering a simple, commonplace greeting. But his words resounded in the room in an extraordinary manner. That voice recalled the monotonous drone of a *bag pipe*. It was the first time I had heard it, yet it would have sufficed to make me recognise it among a thousand, even many years hence. And, there and then, it seemed to me the most salient feature of the man. Beyond doubt, the beard concealed a full throat.

Felice Orsini made the introduction. Thus I came to know personally Giuseppe Pieri, with whom I had already had, by post, a brief correspondence. He launched at once into conversation with great familiarity, as though for long years we had shared good fortune and ill together. And so, in practice, that prudent resolution of Orsini's — to reserve for himself alone all knowledge of the participants in the attempt — was coming to an end.

I was intending to speak to him on the matter at the first opportunity, when he, as though to accentuate the ill turn things were taking, interrupted Pieri's voluble chatter: "By the way," he said to me, "where have you taken lodgings?"

I observed that I had arrived but a short while before... just long enough to snatch a mouthful at the nearest eating-house and to make my way here. I had been left with very little money in my pocket. Since the understanding had been that, for greater caution, we should lodge in a first-class hotel, I was awaiting his instructions.

"You know what?" he resumed, after scratching his head. "Our friend Pieri is staying at the Hôtel France et Champagne, rue Montmartre. He has a room with two beds. Go with him, and things will remain simplified."

He gave me no time to raise objections. He had leaned his body back and, letting his gaze pass in succession over each of us, he said: "A Venetian, a Tuscan, a Neapolitan, a Romagnol. And other, yet other fragments of Italian soil shall be represented in this proud protest. It is not this man or that, not this region or that, but all Italy together that is awakening, that is striving to clear its own path to redemption. The significance is lofty, and Europe shall understand it. And this union of hands that seek one another, today perforce in secret, pledges us that other union which shall be accomplished in the sight of the Peoples and in the light of the Sun... which shall be accomplished... shall be accomplished!"

He fell silent, and I bowed my head, and a powerful shudder ran through my bones, as though the great soul of a prophet had passed suddenly through the air around us.


### Chapter Eight

<!-- pages:156-160 -->





THAT VERY EVENING, I had taken quiet refuge at the Hôtel France et Champagne, in Pieri's room.

Giuseppe Pieri has found no biographers until now. The writers who have mentioned him in passing, relying on the documents of the trial, so magnified his faults as to eclipse his fine and uncommon virtues. I myself, for a time, held him to be the chief, and half-willing, cause of our ruin, and did not always treat him with the serene impartiality to which he was equally entitled. I shall not write his apotheosis here; but I say that, contrary to the majority of civilised men, who have polished their surface and rotted their core, he had nothing of the common about him save the surface alone.

In the brief days we spent in one another's company, following his open, trusting, impulsive nature, he revealed to me his every thought, every secret of his intimate life, and seemed happy to have found a propitious soil in which to pour out the torrent of his speech — less Tuscan than purely Lucchese in character. For all that strange, monotonous voice of his, what he said was anything but tedious; often, indeed, it proved most interesting. He knew how to make one forgive his somewhat unpolished manner of expression, interspersing it with flowers of practical philosophy that betrayed a singular mind and a life tumultuously lived.

His philosophical ideas, freely expressed, and too frequently with excessive emphasis, did not tend to multiply the friends around him. Even today, notwithstanding the fact that certain doctrines have become so democratised, what he said would generally be met with a hostile face. And yet that phenomenal, open-book sincerity of his, and that habit of acting in full harmony with his own convictions, ought to be enough to raise him above a legion of small men — the ballast of History — whose names have none the less filled pages of record, and whose nullity was honoured with a pedestal.

I will adduce one example of his manner of being. Pieri's wife lived in Paris; he had separated from her. But the complete cessation of all conjugal relations did not prevent him from bearing her deep esteem, or from visiting her as a tried and faithful friend. I myself, a man newly married and steeped in altogether different ideals on that score, looked at him with wondering eyes one day when he spoke of it; whereupon he clapped me on the shoulder and said, more or less: "Young man, a love that seeks happiness in the gratification of the senses at the cost of another's happiness is not love — it is the egotism of savages. You labour that a People may become the master of its own destiny, and you are worthy of praise. But would you have it, you little rogue, that the finest, the noblest half of that People should not be master even of its own heart and its own person! The tyranny of the male degrades the woman, who takes her revenge by repaying it with false kisses and betrayal; it leads to sorrows all the more terrible for being the better concealed; to sorrows that dissolve the integrity of the soul and make themselves felt in the souls of the children; to domestic tragedies that end with the pistol-shot. My theory? It reconciles freedom with human dignity, and, in place of a love that is fading, sets the gratitude founded upon the sweet memories of the past, the friendship founded upon mutual esteem."

In matters of religion, Pieri revealed to me a mind free of all prejudice — not through convictions acquired from reading, but by natural inclination. He had, I believe, settled into serenity of necessity, as a light body detached from the dark depths of a marsh must of necessity rise to the luminous surface. This atheism of his brought him at times into collision with Orsini, who, from the education received in childhood, had drawn — as had Mazzini — something nebulous, which persisted in both of them for the whole of their lives.

In the brief days that preceded the undertaking, both I and Pieri endeavoured to live as though nothing of gravity were impending. We usually dined at a trattoria in the Galerie Montmartre, and the frugal meal was preceded and followed by long, aimless walks. We paid a few visits, but he showed himself pensive and taciturn, and the calls were brief.

The afternoon of Saturday, the twelfth of January, was to prove an exception. We found Orsini and Gomez fully dressed, with their overcoats on and hats upon their heads. The moment he caught sight of us, Orsini let slip an exclamation that seemed to carry off at a stroke the impatience accumulated by a long wait. He asked us to accompany him. We went out, and as we walked, he said he wished to take us to the little square before the Opéra to study, on the very ground, a plan of action he had formed. We set off slowly along the Rue Le Peletier, and, having arrived in front of the theatre, we halted, as though to admire it.

There was a long silence. Felice Orsini broke it with these words: "My plan fits perfectly to this locality." And, without making any gesture with his hands, which he kept thrust constantly into the pockets of his overcoat: "Gomez, you will post yourself on that side of the street, to the right, as close to the peristyle as you can manage. Pieri will take the precisely opposite position. The Moretto and I will plant ourselves here, more or less where we stand at this moment. I know that the Emperor will shortly be attending a performance featuring the Ristori. The imperial carriage must be allowed to proceed, undisturbed, as far as the theatre. The moment Napoleon descends, Gomez will hurl his bomb. If it takes effect, each man slips away by his own road as best he can, and we then reassemble on the Place de la Concorde, where I shall give instructions as to what is to be done. If it does not take effect, one of two things: either the Emperor attempts to save himself by retreating into the theatre, in which case Pieri hurls the second bomb at his feet and finishes him; or he falls back into the carriage and it makes off at a gallop, in which case Pieri strikes it from behind, and I and di Rudio strike it from the side. That should suffice to shatter a small fort. But even allowing that the Emperor might proceed — whether to the right or to the left along the Rue Le Peletier — before he reaches the Boulevard Montmartre or the Rue Rossini, he will encounter patriots who will know how to send him the greeting he deserves. In any event, the moment the attempt is accomplished, all of us, by separate routes, to the Place de la Concorde!"

The following day, Sunday, Pieri proposed an excursion to Passy. The wide, open air of the countryside seemed to intensify in that Tuscan patriot the need for expansiveness. In his journey he had stopped at Brussels to bid farewell to a mistress. Under the impulse of emotions that incline any man to easy abandon, and making an exception to his usual self-command, he had become even more than ordinarily carefree in his expansiveness. To the mistress who had begged him to remain longer, he had replied that his immediate departure for Paris was irrevocable.

— What! You are going to France? And you do not fear falling into some ugly snare, you who were expelled from it?

And Pieri had replied in turn that this time he was going there with the hope of repaying in full measure those who had persecuted him.

Throughout that day, he opposed to all restraint an exceptional fervour of phrase, of image, of evocation. In that interminable succession of words, the man of fierce resolve thirsting for liberty, the practical philosopher, now imposed himself upon the sensual man, serene in his facile loves, now appeared subdued by him and as it were eclipsed. Never before had I found myself confronted by so complex an amalgam of solidity and vanity.

But of all his revelations, the one that made the deepest impression upon me was perhaps, in itself, the most insignificant. Pieri had come to know Orsini in Birmingham, and it was from there that he had set out to come to Paris. In the course of his journey he had stopped at Brussels to take leave of a mistress. Under the impulse of emotions that render any man prone to easy confidence, he had become, even more than usual, heedlessly expansive. To the mistress who besought him to remain longer, he had replied that his immediate departure for Paris was irrevocable.

That evening I knocked at Felice Orsini's door. One glance sufficed to tell me that he was more preoccupied than I. None the less, I pressed for an immediate conversation. He looked at me with searching eyes, but said nothing. And I, leaping straight into the matter, reminded him of his own first, prudent resolution to keep the conspirators entirely unknown to one another. That resolution, I added, had been mocked and almost swept away by circumstances — our security had become precarious and fleeting. I did not speak particularly of Pieri's imprudence, but insisted on the probability that some imprudence had been committed. Account had to be taken of unforeseen eventualities — doubtless many — which might imperil not only our lives but the destiny of two Peoples; and I concluded by affirming that, in my opinion, we were wrong to attempt the execution of the enterprise with a superfluous number of participants. Two resolute men, or even one alone, could suffice.

I reminded him too that the design of striking at the heart of the French reaction by suppressing Napoleon was not new in me — he knew it well, having full knowledge of the letter I had written as far back as 1854 to Giuseppe Mazzini. Why not leave me to accomplish alone an enterprise upon which I could claim a certain right of precedence? If I succeeded, our cause would have nothing to lose; if I failed, other unknown, intact forces, reanimated by the desire for vindication, would renew the attempt. The certainty of success lay in persistence.

— The imminence of the action, replied Orsini, not only persuades me that your fears are groundless, but renders all withdrawal impossible. How, in any case, do you propose to let slip an enemy who is destined to ride into our ambush at a trot, and as it were at a gallop towards his own defeat? What awaits us? Simply that the Emperor, in fixing the evening himself, places in our own hands the signal to strike. That is all.

I persisted, and pointed out to him that my plan fitted admirably upon his own, reducing the risks by its very simplicity. If he could procure for me — and it could not prove difficult for him — a uniform of a *sergent de ville*, I should mingle, thus disguised, among the guards assigned by their districts to protect the Emperor. Every section furnished a contingent, and those from one part of the city did not personally know those who had come from other parts. When the Emperor is about to step down from his carriage, one of these guards, by a custom that has become a right, hastens to open the door. Consider: Luigi Bonaparte advances, surrounded by his *sbirri*, as far as the peristyle of the theatre. I dart forward to the carriage door. I am a non-commissioned officer and—

Felice Orsini paced the room back and forth. He was agitated, and upon his most mobile countenance thoughts chased one another like flashes of lightning. At last he came and stood before me, seized several papers and flung them back upon the table, then burst out: "It is too late, it is too late! And besides — equal the glory, equal the risk. Let us say no more of it!"

With that refusal, he irrevocably sealed his own fate.


### Chapter Nine

<!-- pages:160-162 -->



ON the evening of the following day, the 14th of January, Pieri and I dined, as was our custom, at the trattoria in the Galerie Montmartre. We repaired immediately afterwards to the hotel, and the porter, handing Pieri his room key, informed him that half an hour earlier a gentleman had come asking for him. He had not disclosed his name, but had assured the porter that he would return shortly.

There we were in the room, frantically searching for a match to light the gas. We had left the door open so as to make temporary use of the light coming from the corridor. Turning instinctively, I made out, drawn in black against that light, the figure of Felice Orsini.

His words were brief: "Compatriots, the Emperor has given the signal. Come to my lodgings to collect the implements. I shall be there within the hour. Everything is arranged for the best — success cannot fail us. But given that the unforeseen may occur... here is the means to facilitate your escape and your safety."

He drew out a purse and counted two hundred francs for each of us. Then he continued: "I would have wished to provide better for your needs, but it proves impossible for me. To the triumph of the great unacknowledged principles one contributes more by integrity of spirit than by the force of gold. Gold is wanting to the persecuted and is more properly the weapon of despotism. Bear in mind that upon your punctuality, upon your steadfastness, depends the redemption of the Fatherland. I say no more!"

He bade us farewell with a powerful handshake and disappeared. But something remained.

A tormenting spirit that seemed to saturate the air, and for a moment my heart was very nearly seized with dread. The moments that precede action always arrive more painful than those in which the action itself is accomplished. Doing engenders a fever that mounts with the danger and leaves no room for reflection or repentance. However that may be, from the tumult of thoughts that assailed me, the sense of duty emerged triumphant, and the spirit was left, as it were, armoured by it.

I turned my gaze upon Pieri, and at that very instant, to my astonishment, I saw him fling upon the bed the money left him by Orsini, as though he resented it. A little later he drew from a case a revolver — that marvellous American invention which had recently been introduced into Europe — and holding it out to me said: "Take it! It holds six shots... six friends, true friends! They might serve you well!" He tossed the case into the drawer of the commode, locked it, took up his money again, and we went out.

We went out to walk, in silence and for the last time, the road that led to the well-known lodging in the Rue de Monthabor. Having arrived before Felice Orsini's apartment, and as we were about to knock at the door, it opened and two men crossed the threshold. They had the collars of their overcoats turned up, their faces turned toward the darkness of the courtyard. I did not recognise them.

Once more we found ourselves assembled together — I, Pieri, Gomez, and Orsini — as on the day when I had wished to submit my samples of beer to Tomaso Allsop. But Gomez, no sooner had he seen us, begged leave to absent himself in order to make, he said, a small purchase. We awaited him in silence, Orsini leaning, almost seated, against the table. Those long moments of waiting made him nervous. He rose and said: "It passes through my mind that that man has gone to sell the mice to the cat."

But Gomez returned, and Orsini smiled. He then proceeded to distribute the bombs. He said simply: "For you... for you!..." So that they should not slip from our hands, he had wrapped them in a black handkerchief which, held by the knotted corners, would acquire the force of a sling. Gomez and I received one each of large dimensions; Pieri a small one; Orsini cautiously thrust two small ones upon himself, one into the right pocket of his overcoat, the other into the left. We set out, I at Orsini's side; Gomez and Pieri went ahead of us. At this point I could not forbear to note that Pieri had kept his lips constantly pressed together, as though the imminence of the action, or some secret thought, had rendered him suddenly mute. I drew conclusions that were none too favourable. Soon we lost sight of him, and Orsini observed to me, without my having opened my mouth, that "the loquacious Tuscan had every appearance of a man who intended to desert."

I must now relate an incident that was reported to me later by Orsini himself and which Pieri confirmed. At a certain point, Pieri turned back upon his path and passed close by us; but he was not alone. He was accompanied by a stranger, neatly dressed, with a contented, almost cheerful countenance. This man walked on without heeding us; Pieri, however, exchanged a glance with Orsini. Absorbed in my own thoughts, or distracted, I could not rightly say which, I noticed nothing, and Orsini did not think it fitting to mention the matter to me. He imagined that a wholly harmless intruder had attached himself to the heels of "the loquacious Tuscan," who would undoubtedly have known how to rid himself of the fellow, perhaps in some wine-shop, so as to be found afterwards at his station at the appointed hour. That silence exerted a decisive influence upon my conduct immediately after the attempt.

We are now on the point of setting foot in the Rue Le Pelletier. Felice Orsini stops and looks about him as though impatient to see some expected person. A few seconds pass, and a man with a hurried step, with long moustaches projecting beyond his turned-up collar, emerges from the crowd and advances toward him. Out of courtesy I withdrew a few paces; but the ear of the conspirator acquires habits that are not precisely those of the well-bred man. For this reason, perhaps, and not by deliberate intent, I caught the following brief exchange:

— How does the business stand?

— All is well!... Tonight!

And they touched hands. Immediately, the man with the long moustaches moved off, in haste, as he had come. We resumed our way, and Orsini said to me: "Do you know that man?" — "Certainly! It is Francesco Crispi!" Whereupon he, with a slight shade of vexation: "The devil! I believed you did not know him!"

Ought I, or ought I not, to add that with that final "handshake" Orsini had completed the distribution of the bombs; fulfilled his part as organiser? I noticed that only one pocket of his overcoat showed any sign of being weighted...


### Chapter Ten

<!-- pages:162-165 -->



A FEW minutes later we were in position opposite the Opera. The light cast from the premises of a restaurant — the Broggi, if I am not mistaken — struck me from behind; the extraordinary illumination of the theatre fell full upon our faces. The entrance to the restaurant was flanked by columns. Against one of these Orsini leaned his back; I placed myself upright to his right. We were among a great crowd, for the most part stationary, awaiting the imperial cortège. Opposite us, just beyond the pavement, a cordon of municipal guards had drawn up, arms at rest... beyond the rue Le Peletier, along the flanks of the little square, a dark throng stirred and pressed... among them, at his appointed station, I saw Antonio Gomez — tall, erect, motionless as a statue; but search as I might along the other side, I could not see Giuseppe Pieri.

And already one could hear, in the distance, the confused clamour of the multitude exalting the Emperor... a few seconds more, and the voices grew full and distinct. To raise to the heavens the man who had mown them down with grapeshot — what deplorable degradation of Peoples, who prefer the quiet life to the arduous struggle for Freedom!

The imperial cortège arrived. It was preceded by a carriage containing several Court officials. The carriage in which Napoleon III sat, as it were concealed, behind iron cuirasses cunningly disguised, came second and was attended before and behind by half a squadron of mounted guides... the very same who had so ably seconded the heroic enterprise of the 2nd of December. After the intoxication of blood and the proclamation of the Empire, they had been rechristened with the designation of Lancers of the Imperial Guard.

A simple and strange thing occurred. My gaze had flown to the Emperor's carriage and was straining to pierce the opaque veil which the cold had condensed thickly upon the glass of the door. At that moment a white handkerchief — betraying the restless nervousness of the hand that clutched it — wiped away that veil, as though some sensitive soul, having received my full gaze and the hatred with which it was saturated, were searching the street with an unwary yet watchful eye.

I did not see, but I felt, the presence of Eugenia Montijo, and the magical power of the eternal feminine, which knows how to work its way through armour; which, by caressing and softening, bends force and triumphs over it.

But to overwhelm the sentiment of pity, I had presently the intuition of another presence — a doleful figure, with a nose like a vulture's beak, of vibrations in the extreme repulsive. And I felt — oh, let it be believed as truth, even though it wear the face of falsehood! — I felt the warm odour of republican blood made to drain into the sewers of Paris; blood of generous men, slaughtered under cover of night, their hands bound behind their backs; slaughtered like herds of cattle. And the Roman epic, glorious in its deeds and sacrifices, passed like lightning through my mind... not as a pale memory drawn from the remoteness of time and space, but almost as a present tumult, full of tangible shapes and maledictions. It gave me the fever that gunpowder gives on the field of battle. *"È giusto, è giusto"* — it is right, it is right, that the liberty of peoples should spring from the death of him who has betrayed; it is right that this man should be cast down in the very moment he enjoys the triumph procured for him by his crimes!

*"L'Italiano non paga il Sabato, ma paga!"* — The Italian does not pay on Saturday, but he pays!

The carriage slowed. I sought out Antonio Gomez with my eye and saw him again. He had bowed his head — he does not pay on Saturday. But overcome perhaps by a generous impatience, he could not wait. His hand, wrapped in the fatal black handkerchief, rose in menace before the carriage had reached the peristyle, and those few seconds of anticipation placed the trajectory of the bomb in a line perpendicular to the Broggi restaurant. I had barely time to throw myself to the ground and to give a jerk at Orsini's overcoat so that, imitating me, he might save himself. Orsini remained rigid as the column against which he had planted himself.

A sinister flash, accompanied by a deafening roar... then a darkness that seemed to me like night itself. I sprang to my feet, while the theatre, so lately ringing with cheers, shuddered with shattered glass and cries. *"A te, a te!"* Felice Orsini whispered to me — in a strangled voice. Blood was spurting from a wide wound on his forehead, and his face was all smeared with it. He stooped, and laid upon the flagstones the terrible black handkerchief...

I launched myself, bent low, over the cobblestones, teeth clenched, passing over the municipal guards who lay in convulsions, already dead. The Lancers of the Imperial Guard were at that moment taking to precipitate flight, abandoning to his fate the man they had sworn to defend. A few steps... the throw!... the second flash, the second roar! The diffused light reveals to me the Emperor's carriage lurching toward the theatre, one wheel in pieces, the *cocher* rolling on the cobblestones among the horses, and the ground ripped open. Every lantern is extinguished and a black night descends.

I had thrown myself face down among the dead and the half-living, waiting anxiously for the discharge of the remaining bombs. How long did I remain there? Ten seconds, it is affirmed. I was prepared to die, but I should lie if I did not say that they seemed to me a century. And a third bomb came. Who threw it? Felice Orsini said it was the Italian to whom he had entrusted it, in the rue Le Peletier, a few minutes before the attempt. He was not believed, and the question still awaits an explicit answer... for others, not for me, who saw and knew that Italian. Be that as it may, whoever threw the third bomb had a stout spirit and a steady hand. It went straight to its mark and burst directly beneath the shattered carriage. Its detonation seemed to strike my very brain... but an instant, and I recovered full awareness of the situation. I heard the ground thunder with the hoofbeats of approaching horses. The Lancers of the Guard came bursting in at the gallop and reacted against the impulse that had swept them into flight, firing point-blank upon those who fled. The little flames of their weapons flickered in the darkness and revealed to me, in fitful glimpses, the horror and confusion. And the victims — undoubtedly numerous — of that heroism which so closely resembled fear, were afterwards attributed entirely to the "blind and ferocious rage" of the conspirators.

From that horror, from that confusion, I tore myself free — now crawling on all fours, now lurching forward in bounds — escaping, I could not say how, from the horses that seemed to pour in droves out of the night, from the bullets that whistled about my head.

Repeatedly, in the course of my brief but tumultuous existence, my spirit had been saddened by the spectacle of innocent victims sacrificed to the security and placid digestions of despotism. But this availed nothing to render me insensible to a profound feeling of commiseration for those caused by our attempt. Nor, to suppress that sentiment, was it sufficient to reflect that by inexorable law, nothing is granted to a People without grave sacrifice of tears and blood.

Nevertheless, I must also say that the depressing effect of pity was, in large part, neutralised by the conviction that France, that Italy, had now cleared the way toward reaching their glorious destiny; that Napoleon III was no more; that my own work had helped to lift that nightmare from the world.


### Chapter Eleven

<!-- pages:165-168 -->



SCARCELY had I found myself out of danger, when my first thought turned to Elisa. I had made my way toward the Boulevard des Italiens… and entered a café. I must relate things that will seem trifling; yet they carried their own weight. The café was empty. The proprietress, behind the counter, was speaking animatedly with a customer who stood facing her. She did not see me enter, nor notice my presence, until I asked her, if she would be so kind, for paper and an inkwell. I wrote briefly: "Elisa, all is well. I shall depart tomorrow for Oporto."
