Having sealed the letter, I returned the inkwell to the counter and asked for an eight-sou stamp. The lady rummaged in a drawer and asked, handing them to me, whether four two-sou stamps would not serve as well.

I made my way to the Place de la Concorde. It was deserted. I stood my watch upon the bridge for a long while. The thousand sounds of the city reached me in a confused murmur, the humming of an immense beehive. The Seine, grown darker by the restless reflection of the lanterns, flowed toward the sea with an air of mystery, as befitted the hour that hung over us. Not one of the conspirators appeared. When to wait any longer would have been madness, I rid myself of the revolver by casting it into the river, and set off toward the Boulevard, resolved to reconnoitre the ground.

During that journey, every faint breath of conversation caught in passing struck within my breast like a gust of tempest. The noble audacities, the boundless hopes, the stubborn insistence on glimpsing a thread of blue in the darkest of situations; the instinctive faith in the slow yet certain justice of things — all of it remained as if uprooted, reduced to dust, and scattered into nothingness. Napoleon III was alive!…

Sheltered behind the iron plates of his carriage, he had remained there as though paralysed until the explosion of the third bomb… Then, all at once, he appeared at the theatre, erect in the imperial box, ashen as a dead man. At his side stood Eugenia Montijo, exceedingly pale but beautiful still, with a few drops of blood tracing crimson lines across her brow. At the frantic applause that broke out on every side, she had let herself fall back into her chair, her breast heaving as though she had, at that very instant, recovered the only atmosphere capable of breathing life into her. But Napoleon had remained standing. The sense of being once more Emperor had restored colour to that pulsing cadaver, who now bowed with studied grace to right and left.

Outside, meanwhile, the Police and the most trusted battalions were securing his road back to the Palace.

Thus fell the intended revolution; thus the Empire remained, in all its integrity, stronger perhaps than before. Napoleon III clinging to France more than ever, like a vampire; France interposed more than ever, in my understanding, between Italy and her independence and her greatness. And I asked myself, trembling, whether it were an inexorable law of Nature that the vulgarity and the treachery and the corruption and the greed of the few must hold eternal dominion in Europe; whether noble aspirations derided, persecution, misery, and the hangman's noose must be, for ever and ever, the only inheritance of the honest.

And yet — yet not as one who, paralysed by fate, does no more than bite his own hands. Not a single fibre was there within me, in those moments, that was not swollen with turbulent spirit, that was not all one impulse of rebellion. Had occasion offered, I feel I could have killed even without the highest of aims; simply as a human protest; to die, perhaps, but in vindicating the humble and the oppressed…

I resolved to return to the Hôtel de France et Champagne. On the way there I had occasion to convince myself that the situation was growing murky… *Pardon*, public conscience, as it manifested itself in the clusters of people gathered here and there, was already attributing the attempt to foreigners. It was no longer merely a question of resuming the unequal struggle; one had also to think of saving one's neck. And perhaps from that very moment I might have seen to my own safety, had it not seemed to me an act of cowardice to depart without ascertaining at least the fate of my room-mate.

Nothing out of the ordinary at the hotel. Pieri had not returned… the room perfectly in the order in which we had left it. I prepared my valise, the one quilted with the tiger pattern; went down to the office and asked for my bill. From that moment events precipitated.

While the proprietress was verifying the date of my arrival in the register, I settled myself at a small table and began to leaf through an illustrated newspaper. Just then, several individuals entered noisily — plainclothes policemen; the uniform cut of their hair was enough to reveal them to me. They requested leave to inspect the register. One of them, the apparent chief, bending toward the lady, murmured something. The lady indicated with the tip of her finger a point in the register. A dozen eyes fixed themselves upon me with such intensity that I felt their gaze driven into my skin, and the *Maître d'Hôtel* came forward all deference and asked for my passport. I drew it out and unfolded it… the policemen took possession of it, glanced it over, exchanged looks. "*Pardon, monsieur!*" And they departed.

They departed… but from my position, from the corner of my eye, I saw two hounds remain on guard at the entrance. Was I to consider myself a prisoner within that hotel? The proprietress came with the bill and I was compelled to sustain the conversation in a gallant vein which she was pleased to strike up. All at once I sighed; though not for the reason perhaps imagined by my charming temptress. Glancing beyond the window, I had seen the policemen moving away. The way was clear!

And I took advantage of it. Slowly at first, so as not to arouse suspicion; then, once I was quite certain I was not being watched, away at nearly a forced march.

By now I was persuaded that Pieri had made arrangements to quit Paris. I come upon a public cab and engage it: "Station of Orléans… a handsome tip if you arrive in time!" The coachman whips up with such zeal that it seems he means to flay the hide from his nag. We arrive… it is eleven o'clock… four minutes late!… no train for Bordeaux until four in the morning!

What was to be done? Doze at the station? Seek hospitality at the Zappa household, or with some other trusted friend? I decided to return to the hotel. The Police had already poked their nose in there… a circumstance which seemed to me sufficient, in itself, to render it an inviolable sanctuary.

And so I set off on foot, my valise hanging from my arm. I had covered some five hundred paces, more or less, when there came at a full gallop a squadron of municipal guards. It swept past me like a whirlwind, slowed at the station, surrounded it, and enclosed the passengers within a ring of iron. I thought: "If the bird of the wood was slow in taking flight, the hunters are equally slow in springing their trap." At midnight I was in my room. I had left orders to be woken in good time; I had turned off the gas; I had stretched out, as was my custom in such circumstances, without undressing in the least. And already I was promising myself to draw sound counsel from the night, when I heard the bell at the entrance ring. The window of my room gave on to the courtyard. I leap to it, and down below, deep in the shadows, I made out a dark knot of figures about which I could harbour no illusions whatsoever. In the silence of the night, there reached my ear the words: "We wish to see the foreigner in number 53!" The number of my room.

In haste I undressed and burrowed beneath the bedclothes. The better to display a tranquil spirit, I had left the door ajar. The guards arrived accompanied by the *Maître d'Hôtel*; they entered by the thin light of a candle, lit the gas… I felt a gentle touch upon one shoulder. At that I ceased to snore. "*Excusez-nous! — nous devons faire notre devoir.* Your passport, if you please?" They examined it minutely, then looked at the *Maître d'Hôtel*: "But is this not the gentleman with whom we spoke an hour ago, in your office?" A night for misunderstandings, truly! "*Pardon, monsieur!*" I heard their footsteps recede along the corridor and fade away down the stairs.

But it was fated that I should not rest in my bed. Half an hour later they were back, and this time they stood upon no ceremony. "A certain Monsieur Pierey," they said, "who lodged in this very room, declares himself to be a friend of yours. He is at the Prefecture, in safe keeping. You must resign yourself to keeping him company. We have orders to arrest you!"

While I dressed, two policemen stood at my sides, at attention; others busied themselves forcing open our wardrobe. From it they drew the revolver case, which Pieri had tossed there, and some bullets. "What is this?"

— I don't know! The wardrobe is for the exclusive use of the gentleman who lodged in this room. Of my own I have nothing but this bag.

They felt it, rummaged through it, drew out, item by item, the few things it contained; but the dagger concealed between the inner lath and the false bottom escaped their scrutiny.

On arriving at the Prefecture, yet another blow awaited me. I had been conveyed there in a cab, with two guardian angels inside with me in white, a third seated on the box beside the coachman, and a fourth behind the carriage. I was about to alight and had not yet set foot on the step, when another heavily loaded carriage entered the courtyard at a trot. I made for the staircase and, seized by a grim presentiment, turned my head. A few paces away, preceded and followed by a guard, came a man who at that moment surely carried within his breast all the sorrows of his Italy. He was pale of face, with dark side-whiskers, his head swathed in bandages… Felice Orsini!


### Chapter Twelve

<!-- pages:168-172 -->



They led me through three rooms which were occupied, despite the hour, by clerks intent upon their desks. Arriving at a fourth, I sat for a long while before a fireplace in which two brands burned crosswise. And since, in order to better collect my thoughts, I had closed my eyes, I heard a guard say in an undertone: "False alarm! If he were an accomplice, he would not be sleeping so soundly!"

At last they brought me into a great hall, whose brilliant light dazzled me for a moment. The first impression I distinctly perceived was that of the door closing at my back; then I saw take shape before me — I must be forgiven for dwelling on these particulars — a writing-desk strewn with papers, standing perfectly isolated in the middle of the hall. An old man apparently in his sixties, still vigorous in appearance, with smooth skin full of lustrous reflections, sat to one side of it and was frowning over a document. At my appearance he spread it across his knees and raised his face. It was the Prefect of Police.

The desk stood four paces from the entrance, along the right-hand wall. The stirrups were so high as to allow me to discern of the man seated there only the crown of his skull and nothing more — the skull of the Examining Magistrate. Beside the desk, some seated and some standing, were various high-ranking officers of the army, their chests laden with medals, and among those gaudy uniforms there moved, with a sombre air, certain black beetles in civilian dress. Among these last I recognised a personage of the imperial family. The Examining Magistrate rose, and his physiognomy was not unfamiliar to me. He had a history. In the days when Louis Napoleon was in low estate, he had trampled underfoot the very Laws of which he was now the salaried champion. He owed his position to this. Beside the desk, in various attitudes, some seated and some standing, were those high officers; and as my eyes travelled here and there over those showy uniforms with the intensity of one who peers into the abyss by which he might be swallowed, I saw leap out from the red-panelled walls, and as though emerging from them at that very instant, several marble busts which fixed me with their pupil-less eyes. And the men too fixed me without the flutter of an eyelid, as though they partook of the marmoreal nature of those busts!

Then the magistrate asked me my particulars, and I replied in accordance with the passport they had confiscated. But when he asked whether I knew my room-companion and I answered in the negative, his face became animated.

"How can that be?" he asked. "You sleep with a man and do not even know who he is? Where did you first encounter him?"

"During my journey from Calais to Paris. He understands a little of my language. He was the first person I met on arriving in this city, and I asked him the address of a hotel. 'Come to mine,' he answered; 'I have a room with two beds. You will spend less.' I accepted. He told me his name, I told him mine. That is all! Why should I have required an account of his past?"

"But you — what was your purpose in coming to Paris?"

"To earn my bread, selling Burton Ale."

"How did you pass yesterday?"

I had already committed my answer to memory, one which, moreover, differed but little from the truth. Monsieur Pierey, being well acquainted with Paris, had taken me in the morning to the Louvre Gallery. Later Pierey himself had gone to call upon some acquaintance of his, and I had waited for him in a tavern. I then recounted the dinner taken at the restaurant in the rue Montmartre.

"At what hour did you dine?"

"Towards seven. We returned to our hotel. Scarce had we arrived when the porter told Monsieur Pierey that a gentleman had come asking for him — he should wait, the visitor would return shortly. And so it happened. That is all!"

"Who was this gentleman?"

"I do not know. He had come for the other man and not for me. Besides, I noticed that he wished to speak on business, and out of discretion I withdrew."

"Where did you go?"

"To the cabinet at the end of the corridor. When I returned, the gentleman had departed."

"But do you not recall his appearance?"

"The room was dark. I remember, however, that he wore a full beard."

"Would you recognise him if you saw him?"

"Perhaps yes, perhaps no."

At this point the judge turned to a guard and ordered him to bring in "the other prisoner." When the door opened again, Felice Orsini appeared upon the threshold. The bandage bound about his head was steeped in blood. He had the air of a man weak and weary. His first glance was for me. He seemed to address me a mute question, and upon his face I saw pass a fleeting unease.

"Is this the man?" the Magistrate asked me.

"The man who came to the hotel wore a full beard. I believe I said as much."

The judge then left his desk and, approaching Orsini, ran his fingertips beneath his chin. He wished to assure himself that the razor had not passed there recently. Fortunately, the prisoner's skin must have seemed, to the touch, rough with bristles.

It was to Orsini that he directed his next question. He spoke in English.

"From what part of England do you come?"

"From Kent."

"How far is Kent from London?"

"Thirty leagues."

Those two words were enough. In England one speaks of miles, not leagues. Moreover, Orsini had pronounced his "thirty" by converting the sound of the *th* into a *t* and rolling the *r* vigorously — which is not in the habit of English mouths.

The Magistrate said: "You are as English as I am Turkish. Indeed, to tell you the plain truth, you are not even French."

The pale face of Felice Orsini flashed with sudden fire. He drew himself up, disdainful as an ancient Roman. He said — nay, he cried: "Ah no, by God! I am Italian, and I am proud of it. My name is Felice Orsini. Ask me nothing further; I would not answer you."

He had poured his whole soul into his words — the soul of the noble and resolute man who commands the pygmies of character. They were subdued by it and dared not oppose him. He was led away, without more ado. But the Prefect of Police, who throughout the interrogation had remained upright and motionless beside his desk, now set himself to rummage feverishly through the drawers. "Ah, ah!" he said at last, advancing towards the personages in uniform and out of it. "Here is a secret Austrian circular. Felice Orsini — the dangerous revolutionary who escaped from the castle of Mantova. We have made a fine catch!"

Then he returned to his desk and, turning to me: "Monsieur da Sylva, do you know these implements?"

I looked, and it was as though a ray of light illuminated the cells of my brain. Now I understood why Napoleon lived and triumphed! Two of the "implements" which, by their explosion, were to have sounded the signal of awakening to two peoples, lay idle as paperweights upon the Prefect's desk. By what means had they come there? They were there — that was enough for me. Their dimensions were equal; upon both, at the tips of the lower half, the deadly detonating caps still gleamed. Inexplicable imprudence!

That sight sufficed to rouse within my breast the furies which the overriding thought of my own safety had momentarily lulled. And my every fibre swelled again with a turbulent spirit; my every fibre recovered an impulse of rebellion.

I thought: "Perhaps what was not achieved by the direct path will be achieved indirectly, by carrying new confusion into the enemy's camp, already doubtless thrown into disorder — and why not? But in any case — to kill as a human protest, avenging the betrayed and the oppressed!"

I do not say now that my thought was that of the prudent man; I say that such was my thought. And I say that even today I hold supremely human the cry: "Let Samson perish, and with him the Philistines!"

I rose and leaned forward, half-closing my eyes as though short-sighted. Two steps — only two steps without arousing suspicion — and I would have sprung forward — one swift movement, the thunderbolt! — guards, Prefect, Magistrate, generals, myself — ambitions and hopes, servility and the lust for command, crimes and virtues — a few shreds of flesh — nothingness! —

A hand heavy as fate seized me by the shoulder, and I fell back upon my chair. My heart was beating convulsively, but I made an effort and said in a calm, almost bewildered tone: "I do not understand — of what does this concern?"

From that moment my interrogation proceeded swiftly and according to my wishes. In putting the final touches to my account of my movements, I took care to transpose, so to speak, a few minutes, and made my entry into the café coincide precisely with the hour of the attempt. I also alluded to the letter I had written there. The game was dangerous; but the proprietress had not seen me enter, and on that I relied. The Magistrate whispered a few words in a guard's ear, and the guard departed immediately. I gathered that a verification was to be made. It was not long before the same guard returned and fully confirmed my deposition, recounting even the incident of the four two-centime stamps which I had forgotten. The Magistrate then turned to the Prefect and said: "For my part, I have no further question to put."

"Very well!" observed the latter — "But Monsieur da Sylva might be a valuable witness. I would ask him to remain at his hotel at the disposal and at the expense of the State. We will return his passport later. No objection?"

"None whatsoever! Especially if the State undertakes the expenses. My means are extremely limited!"

And for the third time that night I heard the French Police lavishing apologies. The high personages, in uniform and out of it, fell into conversation among themselves, glancing at me from time to time as though exchanging their impressions of me. And the Magistrate, bidding me good night, turned to the guards at my sides. His last words were: "Escort Monsieur da Sylva out of the Prefecture!"

As I passed through the various rooms my thoughts retraced my steps, and I pondered the extraordinary perspicacity of a Magistrate who had let the wolf out of the trap in such a fashion. It would have sufficed for him to interrogate me as to my place of origin; to have asked me which clients I had dealt with in my capacity as seller of Burton Ale; to have addressed to me, or had addressed to me, a single word in Portuguese, and my true nature would have been laid bare. I concluded that the Magistrate and the Police were in a state of over-excitement — perhaps even of vague apprehension — which clouded their vision and halved their dynamic power.

Yet he who dozes tonight, I thought, may be wide awake and watchful tomorrow. Tomorrow? Let me but set foot in the open air once more and clever is he who finds me again! The Orléans station is under close watch; the same must be true of the other stations — but many roads lead out of Paris, and Paris is vast, nor do I lack friends and hiding-places there.

I had reached the last room, and there I found the unexpected. Through the very door which had been opened to grant freedom to the wolf, there entered at that very moment, under close escort, Antonio Gomez. I looked away at once, the only means within my power to make him understand that he should feign ignorance of my presence. But he too had his wits clouded, and passing by my side he murmured in a low voice, in French: "*Siamo perduti!*" — We are lost!

Fate willed that among his escort there should be an intelligent man. "Ah, ah! Do you know one another?" And Gomez, driving home the nail that was piercing me: "Of course we know one another!"

I had hastened to set foot upon the threshold. "*Ferma!*" — Stop! — thundered a voice at my back. I was invited to sit down and the door closed. Gomez continued on his way to the great hall; I remained in the trap in great anxiety, biting my lips. Ten minutes passed. A clock creaked and struck four. Other guards came. They took me by the arm without a word and led me away. We traversed an underground passage.

I passed the rest of the night in a cell of the Conciergerie!


### Chapter Thirteen

<!-- pages:172-177 -->



And so it was that events came rushing headlong to their conclusion. First of all, the rash confidences that Giuseppe Pieri had made to Rosina Hartmann in Brussels had produced, as I had feared, their unhappy effect. Scarcely had Pieri departed than that wretched woman hastened to denounce him to the French Minister, who telegraphed to Paris information only too explicit concerning a probable attempt on the Emperor's life. When Pieri descended from the train, without the slightest suspicion, several secret agents were already posted at the station with orders to arrest him. Unfortunately for the final outcome of our enterprise, he had allowed his beard to grow and passed through the midst of those bloodhounds without being recognised. But the Police would not resign themselves to this affront, and charged the agent Hébert, who knew Pieri personally, with seeking him out. And perhaps even his patient efforts would have come to nothing, had not Pieri himself, meeting him a few minutes before the attempt, stopped him then and there with the most consummate carelessness. He greeted him with the words: "Comment ça va, mon vieux?" And the other recognised him at once, less by his appearance than by his voice, which, as I have said, had a peculiar timbre, almost like a bagpipe. "Ah, ah! I find you at last! You are under arrest!"

When Pieri saw him coming towards him and making signs, he did not trouble himself greatly about it, most likely because his own demeanour at that moment unconsciously reflected the stoic indifference of the Tuscan. The latter had the bomb in his pocket and found himself in a terrible pass, perhaps, but he had not the slightest doubt of success.

A little of this, and a little of his boastful nature — which was far from feigning, in his character, a simple manner while they searched him, or dissembling in any way — led him to abandon himself to a deplorable act of braggadocio. "By now," said he, "your Emperor is not worth the sole of my slippers!"

Later, as we have seen, he revealed his lodging. Perhaps he believed me to be safe, as indeed I had believed myself to be. But he had the fault of worsening his own situation by admitting afterwards that the foreigner found in his room was precisely the man they sought to trace.

I recount these things as they occurred, but I am far from setting myself up as a stern judge. If it is exceeding difficult to perceive true correctness of judgment in a man acting in ordinary circumstances, with perfect calm in both mind and heart, how can one demand it of one who is thrown into confusion by the extraordinary gravity of events, of one who is led astray by passion? Having been witness to the last days and last moments of Giuseppe Pieri, and to the heights he was capable of reaching, I do not hesitate for an instant to affirm that he was heedless, nothing more than heedless. If I myself, as I have already had occasion to admit, believed for a moment otherwise, I was driven to it not only by the bitterness inherent in my own downfall, which I rightly attributed to him, but also because my temporary pessimism had been aggravated by an unpleasant incident that occurred in my presence between Pieri and Orsini — an incident which, at no great distance from the attempt, had raised as it were a barrier bristling with mistrust even between those two generous men. I shall not relate the particulars, for it had its root, in substance, in a misunderstanding and would perhaps interest no one.

One of the bombs which I had occasion to examine on the desk of the Prefect of Police was therefore the one confiscated from Giuseppe Pieri. The other was the very bomb that Felice Orsini had laid upon the pavement opposite the inn of the Broggi, in order to have a moment to wipe away the blood that, trickling down over his eyes, was blinding him. A passer-by, a certain Villaume, noticing on the ground a black handkerchief wrapped about something bulky, had first pushed it aside with his foot, then picked it up, and then handed it over to the Authorities. A little more force in that foot, and Villaume would have been hurled, blasted, into eternal darkness!

To what is the arrest of Gomez to be attributed? To that southern nature so susceptible to excessive exaltation, so susceptible to profound depression. Having thrown his bomb, instead of making his escape he had crossed the Rue Le Pelletier and taken refuge, together with a crowd in a state of utter terror, in the trattoria of the Broggi. It is said that he wept — he himself denied it to me. Nor, for that matter, would such a circumstance have had any further consequence than that of drawing upon him the innocent, passing attention of those around him. Unfortunately, he was carrying a revolver, and the thought of the probable complications attendant upon this circumstance, exaggerated as they were by his state of mind, led him to attempt a stratagem that amounted to the complete abandonment of all circumspection. He stealthily drew the weapon from his pocket and, furtively, concealed it beneath a shelf. He was seen, and the rash movement was interpreted as an explicit act of self-incrimination. From that moment, Gomez became a prisoner of the People.

Who delivered him, shortly afterwards, to the guards of the under-sergeant Michat, who, posted on duty upon the steps of the peristyle, having seen — or rather having imagined he had seen — the bombs thrown from the house opposite the theatre, was conducting a prompt search of the premises.

From the moment of his arrest, Gomez considered himself absolutely lost, and without reflecting, in his extraordinary agitation, that every moment gained might mean the salvation of his companions, he declared, then and there, that his name was Swiney and that he was in the service of an English gentleman. At the same time he named as his own lodging the hotel Saxe-Coburg in the Rue St. Honoré. True, he did not pronounce the name of Thomas Allsop; but the Police were now placed upon the right scent. And when, following it, they brought Gomez to the indicated address, the hall-porter of the hotel revealed that the supposed Swiney had been escorted there by the concierge of Number 10, Rue Monthabor — the address of his no less supposed employer.

Without delay, the Police proceeded thither and, upon the indications of the concierge, broke into the lodging of Thomas Allsop. He was in bed, his head bandaged — proof enough that he had, at the very least, been in the vicinity of the attempt at the moment it was carried out. And so the arrest of the head of the conspiracy was effected.

In this manner the Police, without displaying any extraordinary acuteness and without great exertion, succeeded in laying hands upon four of its principal authors within barely a few hours of the tragic event.

It remained to establish the individual part respectively played by each. But this was a simple question of time. What defence has a prisoner, intent upon concealing the truth, save his own denial? Which denial ends by becoming absurd and ridiculous in the face of the evidence that, sooner or later, overwhelms him. Man has arrived at the capacity to verify and even to minutely reconstruct events that unfolded upon this Earth before he himself appeared upon it; events that no ear recorded, that no eye perceived, that no creature could denounce. Let one imagine, then, whether he should not be capable of reweaving the history of exceptional events, of recent date, which unfolded beneath the thousand and thousand eyes of a curious and gossiping society! The hard truth is this: Woe to the suspect who does not know how to remain *uccel di bosco* — a bird of the forest, free and untraceable!

There was one man who remained such, though the snare was so close about him that all who observed the situation and were acquainted with the facts believed he had been caught in it. The French Police, shaken by the unexpected tragedy and by the responsibility of having failed to prevent it, had at first somewhat lost their heads. Nor did the mysterious rumours floating in the air do anything to restore them. For it was well understood that an Empire without deep roots, born of crime, has an undercurrent of terribly hostile forces ready to break forth at the slightest extraordinary event, however unforeseen. There was fear of the Parisian multitude, so ready to raise barricades; fear of certain Regiments that had shown themselves, unexpectedly, restless. And more than one highly placed personage, implicated in the grim events of the 2nd December, dreading popular vengeance, had secretly made ready to seek his own safety in flight. This explains the excessively courteous manner of the Police... For the persecuted of today might become the masters of tomorrow.

Nevertheless, something had to be attempted, and no sooner had it been established with certainty that Italians had a hand in the conspiracy than a veritable mass arrest of them was set in motion. In those dragnets, carried out moreover at random, Francesco Crispi was also caught.

The future Minister of the Kingdom of Italy was able to prove an alibi. He had remained — he declared — the whole of that evening shut within his own room, and his household confirmed his statement. He was set at liberty, and upon leaving he did not encounter his Gomez — that good, simple, and high-strung soul — who might involuntarily have plunged him back into trouble.

Nor was that the only grave error of the Police. They had occupied all the railway stations and given orders that no foreigner might leave Paris — with the exception of the English. I would not now wish to name names; but setting aside the fact that the conspiracy had been prepared in England, with the involvement of Englishmen, I know for a positive certainty that several of the latter had been present under arms at the scene of the attempt. The way of escape stood open, and doubtless they needed no urging to avail themselves of it.

And, speaking of insufficient penetration — look here! This dagger is the very same that was hidden beneath my cloak by Signora Olivieri of Locarno, while in Italy men were trembling with passion and facing the gallows. From that day, save for brief intervals, it has followed me on every one of my wanderings. I have said that upon departing from London I had concealed it in the tiger-embroidered valise. Well, the Police rummaged through it with their heavy hands more than once — during my interrogation they took it up, examined it, and made of it a piece of evidence. When it was handed over to Elisa as my private property, the dagger was still between the lining and the board where I had hidden it. A Damascus blade — a terrible blade!

It seemed that upon the face of the writer there appeared, as he examined it, a slight expression of repugnance, for the Count smiled bitterly and said: "Honest are the Governments and fortunate the countries where such weapons inspire horror, or serve as mere curiosities in a Museum. But in my time and under the Austrian Government the matter was altogether different. In his struggle against his oppressors, the People has no choice of arms; it defends itself and attacks, not as it would wish, but as it can. The fault lies with those who compel it to do so. And besides, motion, force, wisdom, morality — all is relative. The nails driven into the flesh of Christ were infamous. But in 1853 the common people of Milan, yearning for liberty, gathered together to strike with the very nails of their *barcacce* — their river-barges. Every piece of iron became sacred in their hands. Let us not disavow methods that tended, with desperate resolve, towards a desperate end. They form part of our glory. I maintain that the dagger cleared by two thirds the path that led to the wars which won independence for our Fatherland."

He fell silent for a few seconds, and his expression grew calm once more. Then he resumed:

Here then, mounted step by step, is the staircase that led to the abyss destined to swallow up both us and every hope we had: the childish trust of Pieri in Hartmann; the storm at sea that prevented my meeting with Orsini at the Paris station; the contact struck up between the executors of the attempt, contrary to what had been agreed; the failure of Pieri to play his part and his too easy loquacity immediately after his arrest; the nerves of Gomez. Add to these — of which I shall speak later — the character of Felice Orsini, little or not at all suited to the life of a conspirator, and the good faith of an English young lady, blinded by love for him. If other causes have been put forward, they were invented either by the malice of men or by the imagination of novelists.

I had forgotten one thing! There was yet another cause, secondary perhaps, but one which must needs be taken into account: the defection of Simon Bernard. He did not come to Paris. While others, for having attempted to serve his France, were being driven at great strides towards the scaffold, he gave a fine proof of his own courage — strolling tranquilly about the streets of London, prattling of his own heroism!

On the very morning of my arrest, after a few hours of tormented sleep and as many more of wakefulness spent in brooding over the revelations that Gomez might have made, I was conducted to the Palais de Justice.

I had scarcely set foot in the antechamber when I was already obliged to convince myself of the ill wind that was blowing. Seated in a row along the wall, almost as though displayed in a shop window, were four persons of my acquaintance: the proprietress of the hotel "France et Champagne"; the Maître d'Hôtel; and Monsieur and Madame Morand, the concierges of Felice Orsini's house. All of them summoned, beyond any doubt, for the purpose of identifying me. And so far, nothing amiss. As da Sylva I had passed myself off, and as da Sylva they would denounce me. But I had denied all knowledge of Felice Orsini. The Pipelet couple would give the lie to that — my deposition was becoming untenable!

But the harshest blow came when I was brought before the examining magistrate. It was the same personage who had interrogated me a few hours before. He received me with a smile, as though seeing me again afforded him immense pleasure. "Ah, ah! And how do you do this morning, Signor Conte? No longer da Sylva this morning; but Carlo of the Counts di Rudio!"

No doubt remained: Antonio Gomez had let slip my name. I must say at once, in his exculpation, that both Pieri and Orsini had always addressed me, in his presence, either as Moretto or as di Rudio. He, in all probability, was unaware that I carried a Portuguese passport.

And so I told over again, point by point, the same story I had narrated at the first interrogation. But, reflecting upon the Pipelet couple waiting in the antechamber, I admitted to having paid a brief visit to Orsini. With no mysterious purpose, moreover. We had conspired together in Switzerland and I had wished to see him again in order to revive old and cherished memories.

— But why did you deny it last night?

— Simply so as not to compromise a man who, from what I could gather, was already in difficulties. I do not believe that any of your Laws required me to fail in this duty of honour.

— And how does it come about that you carry a false passport?

— As a most natural precaution for a political exile who might have the spies of Austria at his heels. And how can that be held against me by one who did the same when he landed at Boulogne with Luigi Napoleone?

The thrust struck full in the chest; but the magistrate contented himself with a grimace. He resumed: "Now tell me everything you know concerning this attempt."

— Only what the guards who arrested me have told me. I know that there was a wish to kill the Emperor, and nothing more.

— That may be. In any case I must inform you that your friends have denounced you as their accomplice, and that you will be tried along with them!

And he gave the order that I be taken to Mazas.


### Chapter Fourteen

<!-- pages:177-183 -->

FROM that moment I harboured no further doubt as to the fate that awaited me, and if I resolved to struggle to the end, it was solely, as one says, for honour's sake; that I might be able to cry out, upon reaching the bottom of the abyss: "I have done everything humanly possible to avoid being cast into it!"

That very same day, I was compelled to satisfy myself that a masterful will was at work in the contrary direction, reinforcing the means honourably at its disposal with fraud and perjury.

I was confined in a ground-floor cell. A companion was assigned to me — an agent of the secret Police, dressed in plain clothes, who announced that he would spend both day and night in my company. Certainly not to keep my spirits up, I thought, but to snatch some word that might escape me, in the travail of my soul, during sleep.

He appeared to perceive my thought and begged me not to judge by appearances. If he could not do me good, he would at least do me no harm.

Half an hour later, the bolts grated and the heavy door swung on its hinges. Two persons entered: the Examining Magistrate and a man dressed as a municipal street-sweeper, with one empty eye-socket, hideously scarred. Two guards remained standing at the threshold.

"Well, then! Kimf —" said the Magistrate after a brief silence.

"It is he!"

"Are you quite certain?"

"As certain as I am that I am baptised."

"Repeat your account."

"I was on duty at the Opéra, spreading sand at the entrance reserved for His Majesty. It would have been seven o'clock, or a quarter past, at the most. I saw two gentlemen chatting in low voices, gesticulating. They had the air of persons studying the locality. I warned them that this was no place for the public and asked them to move along. They told me to mind my own business. The gentleman who is here said: 'Hold your tongue, you scoundrel!' I made as if to strike him, and the two of them cleared off."

"When did this take place?"

"Yesterday evening, the fourteenth of January."

"An hour, or a little less, before the attempt upon His Majesty the Emperor?"

"An hour, or a little more."

I had understood, perfectly understood! They wished to establish, in a manner that should appear incontrovertible, our presence at the scene of the attempt. For Orsini there was his wound; for Gomez the arrest effected in front of the Opéra. To fry me and Pieri, they had put into the mouth of that wretched creature a tale invented out of whole cloth.

Yet I felt none of that anger which impels a man to fury, and was content to ask my strange accuser how much they had promised him in payment for his lies. He turned away the single greenish eye that remained to him and made no answer.

Late in the evening, a second agent was locked into my cell. Scarcely entered, he greeted me and, offering his hand, said that he likewise would leave me neither by day nor by night. They were shut in with me — so I could only surmise — solely to prevent any communication between myself and the accomplices they suspected I had outside. He hoped that all three of us would endeavour, as good comrades, not to embitter our imprisonment with needless friction.

Several days of relative quiet passed. No contact with the outer world, of which but little air penetrated my cell, and still less sunlight, while the warder appeared only at rare intervals. He had orders to speak neither with me nor with my guards, who thus became the only human beings with whom I was permitted to exchange my thoughts. I knew nothing save that my companions were, like myself, confined at Mazas: subject to the same snares, sliding inexorably toward the selfsame fate. In such moments I would sit apart on the plank bed, motionless, eyes fixed upon the ground. And here I owe my guards a word of thanks — somewhat belated, it is true — who, so as not to disturb my meditations, moved about on tiptoe.

I attempted to occupy myself, to put myself, as it were, in the place of the Authorities and thereafter to prepare my defence. But my thoughts defied all such endeavour; the subtle conjectures and the intricate reconstructions ended always in the same thing: nervous irritation, exhaustion. Then nothing remained but to have recourse to the great remedy of youth — I slept.

The first clear proof of the inextricable net that was closing about me came when, conducted under close escort before the Clerk of the Court, I saw placed before me first a certified copy, and then the original itself, of a letter written by me from London to Felice Orsini. In it I informed him how a certain Carlotti, a native of Brescia, had, to my great surprise, spoken to me of our conspiracy. I had good reason to suspect this man of being a spy in the service of the French Government. He must be left alone. If he already knew too much... either one must rid oneself of him by whatever means, or renounce the enterprise altogether.

Felice Orsini, a warmer patriot than a prudent conspirator, had preserved this most compromising document among his papers, and the English Police, at the instigation of Paris, had carried out a search of his premises and taken possession of it.

My intimate connexion with the conspirators, and my knowledge of their designs, appeared only too firmly established. Another long stride down the fatal slope! And when I attempted, *pro forma*, to deny the undeniable, the Examining Magistrate answered only with a mocking smile and dismissed me back to my darkness, casting after me, by way of *viaticum*, the shattering phrase: "Oh, we have far other proofs! And your accomplices have fully corroborated them!"

The following day I was transferred from Mazas, in a closed carriage, to the Conciergerie. I was assigned cell No. 1, and soon learnt that Pieri was confined in No. 2, Gomez in No. 3, and Orsini in No. 4.

My new place of sojourn measured, roughly, ten feet by fifteen. Light entered timidly through a small window set with heavy iron bars, situated near the ceiling. In the door, at the height of a man, was a circular aperture — a five-franc piece would have passed through it only with difficulty. It permitted the warders to observe my every movement; it permitted me to observe, up to a certain point, whatever passed in the corridor. On the walls, the hands of the prisoners who had preceded me had undoubtedly carved verses and maxims, afterwards scraped away without mercy by the warders' knives.

What fragments of hearts and what reflections of souls refined by suffering, stolen from my meditation by the narrow observance of duty! The history of one of those souls I knew, in every particular, from my childhood. It was indeed from my cell that Marshal Ney had gone forth, to have his patriot breast shattered by the Bourbons' lead!

At the Conciergerie too I had two guards. Of them I must say something. The more congenial was a former non-commissioned officer of the navy. The State, having wrung from him the best years of his life, had rescued him from destitution by entrusting him with tasks of dubious stamp that evidently went against the grain of his nature. A large family, poor and without connections, kept him bound to his lot; but his resignation was only apparent, and I was not long in perceiving that his secret rancour, finding no outlet, was accumulating drop by drop in his heart. Nature had denied him the resolution and the impulse that drive men to virile acts; unless a foolish discipline, too long endured, had cut away his every force of will. Yet he still admired in others the virtues that were not his own, and his instincts revealed themselves, in the substratum — and perhaps without his own knowledge — as rebellious. The years have effaced his surname from my memory. His Christian name was, if I am not mistaken, one very common among the Parisian people — a mythological name: Saturn!

The other guard was a former soldier of the marine infantry — a rather stout man of few words, who would sit for hours on end with his hands upon his stomach, fingers interlaced, placidly revolving his two thumbs one about the other, now in one direction, now in the reverse. He was fond of dozing.

At the Conciergerie, I received my first intimation of what was passing in the world. The two guards maintained, in this regard, a sealed silence. But other persons attached to the service of the prison supplied my thirst by a most simple expedient: planting themselves before the cell and conversing in loud voices one with the other.

By this means I came to learn that public opinion, which had at first shown itself hostile to us, had rapidly changed in our favour. The substance of entire newspaper articles threaded its way through the narrow circular aperture to my ear. Among them, those translated from the English press seemed to me especially explicit, and almost laudatory.

But why did the staff of the Conciergerie go to such trouble? I believed I divined the reason on the day I heard a warder affirm that "in this affair (our affair) there were mysterious and powerful influences at work." He had added: "It is not difficult to foresee that none of the four prisoners will be executed." I deduced from this that he who had spoken thus was not altogether unconnected with the direct inspiration of the "mysterious influences" to which he had permitted himself to allude.

And now we are at the precipice! From the far end of the corridor a voice cries: "Prisoner of cell No. 4!" It was Felice Orsini's cell. I press my eye to the circular aperture. Let it be seen, then, how capable of gnawing away a man's existence — even an undaunted one — is the anguish of a great enterprise failed and the consciousness of one's own responsibility! Felice Orsini passed swiftly across the narrow arc of my vision; but even that sufficed for me to discern, upon that sculptured head, the traces of the tempest that must have raged within. It was almost entirely white!

Half an hour of sorrowful reflection passes, and I hear Orsini's step returning. Immediately, from the far end of the corridor, the selfsame voice: "Prisoner of cell No. 1!" I was ushered into a long, narrow room. The single window that lit it stood before me. At a writing-desk beside the window sat the familiar Examining Magistrate, and with him another individual clad in black — the Imperial Prosecutor. But my attention was violently seized by a third figure, seated a little apart from the desk, to the right of the Magistrate. Her profile stood out sharply against the light of the window. I knew that profile — it was that of a small, slender, dark woman, not beautiful in the classical sense, but very attractive; and at the sight of her there stirred within me the recollection of a persistent smile of hers. It was Miss Elisa Cheney, the friend of Felice Orsini. I dissembled the agitation that seized me so intensely as to prevent me from distinguishing the other persons who stood about me, and barely noticed a second woman who, seated at a table near the entrance, leaned her arm upon it and hid her face upon it.

The interrogation proceeded with a rapidity that seemed to me like lightning. The Magistrate addressed himself first to the English young lady, speaking to her in her own tongue.

"Is this the man who brought the letter found upon your mantelpiece, and to whom you delivered a valise belonging to Signor Felice Orsini?"

"It is he."

"Signor Conte, what have you to say?"

Persons of chivalrous disposition, seated at table in a warm room with their feet upon soft carpets, will perhaps not be able to forgive my discourtesy. But it was a question of keeping my head from the guillotine and preserving it for better use. I replied: "How can that woman possibly claim to know me? She will have seen me one night, fleetingly, as she plied her trade along the pavements of Haymarket. The testimony of a woman of equivocal character signifies nothing."

The young lady turned pale, and with a voice broken by anger: "Let the valise be examined. On the leather, at the bottom — in indelible characters..."

She could not continue. And the Magistrate: "Compose yourself, compose yourself, Miss!"

"— in indelible characters, the name of Felice Orsini is written!" At a sign from the Magistrate, the Clerk flashed before my eyes — produced from I know not where — the tiger-skin valise. Time and dust had blackened its base. But it was at once washed, and the indelible characters appeared. Mere participation in a conspiracy that had led to the shedding of blood was enough — those characters were my death sentence!

But I did not admit defeat, and attempted one last desperate move. I had understood clearly, there and then, that Miss Cheney was acting neither from venality nor from hatred. The spirit that animated her could only be love. The Police, master of the art of turning passions to account, I thought, must have promised her the salvation of her adored one in exchange for a complete disclosure. I learnt later that I had intuited the truth. To the detective Sanders, who was urging her to reveal all she knew, she had replied: "Everything, everything, to save that man!" And she had allowed herself to be brought to Paris.

Be that as it may, impelled by this thought, I raised my voice as I had never done before my judge, and turning to my accuser: "You are attempting to save Felice Orsini, and to succeed in this, you are betraying the innocent!"

What followed I had not expected. My voice, ringing through the room, had penetrated a heart, had shaken a creature, had lifted a head bowed upon a hand in painful languor.

Two trembling arms clasped me, and my muscles were forced to make an effort to support the weight of a being who had given herself over entirely to me. And I, who still did not understand, felt as if a wave of rose and blue were flooding the depths of me and stilling my tempest; while a cloud of hair, golden and light, caressed my face and enclosed it, as though to hide it from profane eyes. A resounding kiss, long —

For a moment, the Magistrate, the Imperial Prosecutor, the room, the conspiracy, France, the entire world ceased to exist. I was in the arms of my bride!

They separated us. The Magistrate was the first to break the silence. How shrill his voice seemed to me! He said: "Is the Conte di Rudio your husband?" And she, with honest pride: "I should not be here, were he not!"

Then they allowed us ten minutes for a private meeting. But when we drew apart, Sanders, the head of Scotland Yard's detectives, who had accompanied my poor Elisa to Paris, followed and planted himself at our heels. I foiled him by beginning our conversation in Italian.

"The child?"

"Adorable! Mama is taking care of him. Carlo, Carlo, what can I do for you?"

And Sanders, curtly: "Speak in English!"

"Go to the devil!"

He lodged a protest with the Magistrate, who, perhaps thinking that speaking the language of Shakespeare might prove difficult for me, gave orders that a Corsican interpreter should attend our conversation. Sanders withdrew. And I then resumed my discourse, but in English.

"Elisa, this is no time for illusions. All is lost and I shall be brought to the scaffold. What can you do? Nothing but bear your fate with fortitude. Unless —"

Here, loud protests from the Corsican, who insisted that we speak Italian. And the Magistrate ordered that both interpreters remain by our side. But before Sanders had returned, I had already expressed my thought — "unless you succeed in engaging on behalf of my cause, your own, some English personage of great influence. Then, perhaps —"

At this point, the arrival of the second intruder exasperated me, and my powerful emotions lost all restraint. Which of them drove me most strongly? Whether it was the horror of prolonged dissimulation and the need to vindicate, in that supreme moment, my own part; or whether it was the presence of Elisa that rekindled my pride — the fact remains that, raising my voice once more, I flung these words into the astonished face of the Magistrate: "Yes, I too attempted to destroy the murderer of my Country and of the French People. One regret alone: that I did not know how to strike home. The second bomb was hurled by me!"

The Magistrate, pale and grave, made as if to speak. But I forestalled him: "Do not trouble me with useless questions. What I have said is sufficient for your Justice!"

I saw him cut the air with the gesture that dismissed me back to prison, and I turned to my bride to embrace her once more. Alas! The rose colour had vanished; the blue eyes had lost their vivacity. Elisa, who had fainted, had fallen back in her chair, arms hanging limp, head thrown back against the rest. My kiss — the last, perhaps — I laid upon lips made pale and senseless, yet still trembling.

Then I ran out, so swiftly that the guards, hurrying after me, seemed almost to be giving chase.


### Chapter Eleven

<!-- pages:183-186 -->

WHEN the door of the cell closed behind me, it was as though the turbid passions that had agitated me remained on the other side of the threshold. Saturno and the other questioned me with their expressions. "It is all over! Every aspiration toward liberty has been cut off for me from this day forward. One must prepare to die." I did not say this aloud, but within myself I felt that I should be sustained by the pride of having sacrificed myself for my Country.

No sooner had I finished my account than they endeavoured, as though by tacit agreement, to drag me back from my convictions. They put forward, among other things, the sympathies of the population and the favourable dispositions which were said to animate the imperial Court. To this I replied by wagging my head and smiling. A curious dispute arose from this, and we ended by laying a wager. If the Tribunal pronounced a sentence of death, they would undertake to pay me a bottle of champagne — and of the finest. If not, I would pay for it myself. We sealed the pact with a vigorous handshake.

Mathieu de la Drôme paid me his first visit that very day. He was a dignified man, already somewhat advanced in years, grey at the temples, placid in bearing and in speech. For several days he had spontaneously offered to act on my behalf, but it had not been permitted until the matter was sufficiently certain. He drew near, so to speak, to my soul with the delicacy of a physician who knows he must touch limbs that are sensitive and sore. And I told him everything at once, without reserve — all that was my past.

When I had finished, he lowered his eyes and withdrew into his reflections for a moment. Then, with a paternal affability: "The damaging admissions might be retracted. It is within your prerogative. But to what extent it would benefit you, I cannot say. You are accustomed to facing danger, and I prefer to speak plainly. The direct evidence gathered by the Police renders an acquittal unattainable. Were it only a matter of your own person and of your bearing before your compatriots, I should say to you: go to meet death majestically, all of a piece, blessing the Fatherland, cursing its oppressors. The People will accord you, in their great heart, the place that is your due. But the wise man—"

Here he drew away and explained at length what, in his view, a wise man would have done in my position. To rush headlong toward the scaffold was not to serve the Fatherland; nor would my sacrifice improve the situation of my fellow conspirators. And how would my aged parents, who had already suffered so greatly, endure a blow past all remedy? A young wife trembled for me, and I had made her a mother. Why not attempt every honourable means to live, when, by cheating the executioner, I should spare untold grief to those unhappy souls?

He went on at length, explaining what a wise man would have done in my position. To rush headlong toward the scaffold was not to serve the Fatherland; nor would my sacrifice improve the situation of my accomplices. I asked him for an explicit clarification of what he required of me. "You are free," he replied, "to attempt the means that might save you, and I ask of you only a formal promise to moderate your impulses, whatever the part I should judge it fitting to have you assume."

And this explains why, in the records of the proceedings, I emerge colourless, almost without a will of my own, easily swayed, driven to the terrible act by the iron mind of the leaders and by the morbid preoccupation of not appearing pusillanimous, of not being confused with the Rossi from Mantua, of not being taken for a spy.

I have never had, nor do I have, toward Mathieu de la Drôme anything but sentiments of filial gratitude. Unhappily, his noble efforts to "cheat the executioner" subsequently served as a convenient instrument of denigration for those ill-disposed toward me, blinded as they were by personal rancour or partisan rancour — not the least among the reasons which, for fifty years, kept me locked in a disdainful silence.

The evidence gathered by the Police was of itself sufficient to secure a conviction under the letter of the Law. To reinforce it with the admissions of the accused would have lent greater emphasis, greater theatrical effect, to the cleverness of the Police.

The better to achieve its end, it had begun its subtle work upon me, as I was the most jovial; then came the turn of Antonio Gomez, who was some years my senior. Before him, the examining magistrate triumphantly laid a letter of introduction obtained from Pieri, addressed to Orsini. Worse still, a letter written in his own hand to Orsini himself. In it he laboured to dispel certain suspicions that had insinuated themselves into the mistrustful mind of the leader, and, pressing for confirmation of the mission that had been promised him, he declared that he wished to take part in the conspiracy, moved solely by the love he had always borne and still bore to the common Fatherland.

Upon Italians, risen again to nationhood, falls the moral obligation of counting such an appeal and the sentiments that inspired it to the credit of Gomez — but before the French Police, could there have been a more compromising document?

As though this were not enough, Gomez was seen to set out, step by step, his every movement from the 6th of January, when he departed with Pieri from Birmingham, pausing first in London at the house of Miss Cheney, then in Lille, awaiting Pieri who made a foray to Brussels — and so on, up to the moment when he set foot for the first time in Orsini's furnished apartment at No. 10, rue Monthabor.

And here, as the final stroke, came the artful insinuation: "his accomplices had confessed their own part and his. What purpose was there in persisting with denial?"

After various reticences and various moves too desperate to pass for sound tactics, upon being told plainly that I, di Rudio, having admitted to throwing the second bomb, had revealed everything else, he ended by confessing to having thrown the first. But even in such extremity he was not abandoned by an instinct widespread among the population of southern Italy: that which leads a man to draw from his pocket the bag of *dabbenaggine* — simple-mindedness — and to smear it upon his face. He, who had been a soldier, tried to make it believed that he had thrown his projectile as a faithful servant, without having formed any precise notion of its deadly effects. It was upon this supposed simple-mindedness that his defence was founded.

A more arduous struggle it proved to be, in like straits, for Giuseppe Pieri. The police had placed before him several revealing letters; they had recounted, with indisputable precision, step by step, his movements before the attempt; his relations with Orsini, with Gomez, with Simon Bernard, with myself; his complicity in importing the unfilled projectiles into France, in the purchase of certain revolvers found near the scene of the attempt. Elisa Cheney pointing him out, and Hartmann denouncing him, had with their personal testimonies corroborated the written evidence. The arrest carried out within minutes and within steps of the attempt, the charged bomb in the pocket of his overcoat, the boasts that had escaped him after his arrest — could there have been a more encompassing net, a more complete story?

He added nothing to it, for there was nothing left to add. He attempted instead to embellish the facts with outlandish inventions and to draw from them deductions tending to distort their import.

"He had come to Paris solely to settle some family business and intended to go to Italy, where a revolution was imminent. He had discussed bombs with Orsini, but solely in order to use them in the Fatherland, on the barricades. And since he had expressed some doubt as to their efficacy, Orsini had handed him one; but with the understanding that they would set it off, by way of experiment, at the Barrière des Martyrs. Only Pieri had kept the appointment; and so the bomb had remained in his pocket and been found there upon his person. If on the evening of the attempt he had gone to the rue Le Pelletier, it was simply because he had been drawn there by the extraordinary illumination."

A shield assembled with sufficient cunning — but wasted effort! A few of his own written phrases and a little analysis were enough to breach it. He had the merit of perceiving this in time. Thereupon, citing as his pretext "the inquisitor's methods of the examining magistrate," he declared that he would answer no further questions; that he would refute the charges only during the actual trial, before those he regarded as his natural judges.

By this means he conjured away the danger of compromising himself still further. But was what awaited him truly the worst possible?

Nugent de Saint Laurent, who subsequently gave his full approval to Pieri's tactics, attempted to lend them greater weight by laying great emphasis on the animosity existing between Pieri himself and Orsini, as though to conclude from this that a conspiracy, and the fraternal relations it entails, were impossible between two men who cordially hated each other. Unhappily, the reason for the ill-feeling was not made clear; what was made quite clear, however, was that it was of too recent a date to have had any dissolving force upon the conspiracy.


### Chapter Sixteen

<!-- pages:186-190 -->

AND so we come to our chief. I shall call up old impressions; I shall express judgements which fifty years of meditation have not availed to alter.

We find ourselves before a character capable, in the highest degree, of generous impulses. Yet, through the mysterious reactions of his soul, the frequent exaltations of feeling were followed, always or almost always, by profound depressions. A nature excessively nervous and sensitive, he oscillated fatally from one extreme to the other, laying bare strange deficiencies which I regarded, even in those days, as maladies — of what precise nature I could not then define, nor can I now.

The conspiracy against Napoleon III reveals in him a genius adroit in conceiving a design in broad outline. But the success of such undertakings, when analysed, proves to consist in the sum of trifles patiently worked with a chisel. Felice Orsini, in the practical act, showed so little regard for the most elementary precautions that I do not hesitate to affirm that the essential gifts of the conspirator were conspicuous in him by their complete absence.

Giuseppe Mazzini, censured by Orsini to the point of denigration — to the point of allowing him no genius save that of the spoken word — would certainly never have committed the folly of leaving England, of plunging into an enterprise of such magnitude, while forgetting behind him, intact, recoverable, all but in plain sight, the correspondence that revealed every preliminary of the attempt.

This imprudence will appear all the more egregious when it is known that he himself, in writing to me, frequently urged me, urged us, to destroy his letters and to leave not the slightest trace of them.

It weighs upon me to say it, but I must say it in the name of truth: the trouble with Felice Orsini's mind lay in an overflowing imagination that prevented him from discerning the exact contours of things, despite his pretension to being a practical man.

Having shouldered a difficult enterprise, he would construct in imagination the events which ought, through a long chain of consequence, to lead to triumph; and dwelling upon that triumph, he would exalt himself so thoroughly as to regard it as inevitable, savouring it before it had come to pass.

With an irony that seemed cruel, it was observed how Felice Orsini, on the eve of one of his desperate attempts in the Lunigiana, had carried his faith in success to the point of procuring for himself a general's uniform. One recoils, before the majesty of martyrdom, from being compelled to recall how, setting out for Paris to attempt the life of an Emperor, to initiate a revolution, he — who stood in such need of obscurity and silence — purchased a saddle-horse and rode frequently through the streets of the metropolis, as though anticipating therein the emotions of the epic moments to come.

This explains so many other things. The excessive development of one mental tendency is accomplished at the expense of other faculties, which remain all the more deficient as that tendency manifests itself more turgid and overbearing. An immoderate faith in success, which rests in substance upon an exaggerated estimate of one's own powers, is answered, too often, by a corresponding failure to perceive the true strength and character of others.

What reason led Felice Orsini to break so wrathfully with the Maestro? Let historians seek it in this or that incident, and from their point of view they are right to do so. But the primary cause is entirely psychological. Historical incidents are, in reality, nothing other than the expression assumed, in a given environment, by the deplorable flaw of a soul otherwise noble and great.

In a different field and in different measure, yet from the selfsame source, Felice Orsini was destined to treat us also — his companions in danger and in misfortune — in like fashion. The bitter words uttered during the brief absence of the Gomez, in the house in the rue Monthabor, on the evening of the 14th of January; the no less stinging phrase launched, shortly afterwards, at Pieri's address as we were making our way to the Opéra, demonstrate that a dark reaction was already fermenting in his soul even before the catastrophe.

An ill-starred incident provoked by Gomez served to lend some substance to the phantoms that troubled him. Before resolving upon a full confession, Gomez had attempted to persuade the examining magistrate that Orsini, exasperated by his hesitation, had at the moment of the imperial carriage's approach brusquely snatched the bomb from his hands, and that Orsini himself had hurled it against the Emperor. When confronted, he immediately retracted, and the matter had no legal consequence whatsoever. But it certainly did not help to restore Orsini to a just perception of reality.

He was undoubtedly in such a state when he was ensnared and beguiled by the arts of the police. The insinuation contrived against the integrity of his companions made a wide breach in a soul already so open to suspicion. And whereas before that moment, following his generous impulses, he had shown a wish to assume every responsibility for what had occurred, he now repented of this and dictated a detailed account in which, while assigning to each his share in acts already known to the Police, he let fall towards us words full of bitterness.

He then repented of his repentance and attempted, with a vigorous counter-declaration, to take every responsibility back upon himself; but at bottom the doubt remained, and the bitterness found, from time to time, the means to burst forth impetuously — always overcome thereafter, and with equal impetuosity, by feelings more consonant with his greatness.

A pendulum that bewildered us most grievously!

Even today, I could not interpret with any certainty certain of his denials, certain of his disavowals. These might have been inspired as readily by a transient determination to sever himself from us and stand alone, as by an ardent desire to open for us a path to safety, to offer us the means of softening the rigour of the fate about to strike us.

Here, to avoid misunderstanding, I must insist upon one point: the insinuations of the Police were undoubtedly intended to irritate Orsini, in order to wring from him a full confession. Orsini had his faults, but he was not a coward. He did indeed acknowledge everything that the Police showed they knew with certainty; but he took good care not to disclose particulars that were unknown to them, unless those particulars concerned himself alone.

I will say this further: no human soul could be polished and perfect on every facet of its prism. To constitute greatness, one great side sometimes suffices. Felice Orsini had more than one.

It is easy, for example, to display courage in the light of day, upon a field of battle. So easy, that I have known men transformed into heroes by a single spent bullet, who were incapable of standing without trembling before a snarling cur. The disciplined multitude carries along even the most timid and feeble individual — to keep his legs from giving way beneath him, he has, or imagines he has, the eyes of his fellow citizens fixed upon him in their thousands. But Felice Orsini, alone in his room, spreading mercury fulminate before the fireplace and drying it over the flame; with a watch in one hand and a thermometer in the other, watching for the spark that might reduce him to fragments, without leaving of his work one atom to earn him the applause of men — nothing, not even the memory of generous intentions — Felice Orsini, I say, gave proof of a courage whose temper eclipses any noisy and belligerent heroism.

There is furthermore a trait which has escaped, so far as I am aware, all who have written of him hitherto, and which reveals a courage yet more refined and sublime. The seizure of his correspondence had, among other things, revealed him as the centre from which radiated the entire clandestine operation; the point towards which, in turn, converged the concealed hatreds, the subterranean forces working to undermine the Empire. To suppress Napoleon for the mere pleasure of suppressing him would have been the work of madmen. The French political police had well understood that the blow, by its diligent preparation, must be intended to impart an impulse to broad forces, ready to reap its fruit. To lay hold of secrets which it sensed to be of vital importance was a matter to which it attached careers, wealth, power, glory — everything.

Felice Orsini found himself, therefore, in a position not of submission but of power. A single thread of the vast web, secretly surrendered, would have led him to safety, to opulence perhaps. He would have looked again upon the white crests of the mountains, the green fragrant plains, the incomparable sky of Italy; he would have conspired and fought for her still; he might have cherished the hope of witnessing her third rebirth. Nor was his heart deaf to more modest affections, no less intense — he was a father; on the banks of the Thames, a woman hoped and trembled for him. But between that tragic present hour and that rejoicing lay the blood of the generous-hearted, lay the tears of orphans. He chose instead to set forth, pale and silent, towards the scaffold. An austere greatness, worthy of an ancient Roman!

The defence of Felice Orsini arose spontaneously from the circumstances, and Giulio Favre was not the manner of intellect to ruin, through pusillanimous scruple, the splendid material those circumstances offered him. He laid upon it the powerful hand of the artist who knows the mastery of effects; of the man of heart who makes his own the trembling of a glorious and unhappy People.

His masterstroke was the letter addressed by Felice Orsini to Napoleon III. Without revealing anything, without compromising anyone, it permitted him to flash before the Judges, the jurors, and the civilised world the loftiness of our purpose. It flatters the lion who is exercising his claws in our flesh; but amid the blandishments, it insinuates, unperceived, a muffled threat. A single phrase honestly expressed suffices to pierce a wicked heart. In the silent hours, in the hours of remorse, it will reproach the broken oaths; it will exude the undying wrath of a race righteously vengeful, which was his own and which he has betrayed. The impetus of noble passions raised Giulio Favre above petty legal manoeuvres and mean things. Moving among great matters, he was able to accomplish a great defence. He did not snatch a man from the executioner, but he did something better — he restored his character. He transmitted to posterity a spirit, already so virile in itself, transfigured, as it were, by strokes of captivating beauty.


### Chapter Seventeen

<!-- pages:190-195 -->

THE twenty-fifth of February arrived, the day appointed for the conduct of the trial. I was brought to the Palais de Justice and, for the first time since the evening of the attempt, I saw my companions in misfortune fully before me.

The courtroom was thronged with men and ladies. There was a splendour of attire... people had come to the trial as to the theatre. Yet, through the perception of some mysterious and indefinable sense, I understood at once that the manifold spirit of that audience was not hostile to us. Hostile, on the contrary — deliberately hostile — were the individuals seated upon the bench and the handful of hireling attendants that surrounded them. These men perhaps imagined themselves to be the Empire and its power, and regarded our attempt as one regards a personal offence.

The trial was, at its core, a repetition of the preliminary inquiry — revised and corrected. The right of defence exists for the virtuous and exists for the wicked, and I should be a fool were I to reproach the French judicial authorities for not having pronounced a eulogy upon the accused; but equally foolish would be he who, blinded by the prestige inherent in social position and possessed of the instincts of a fetish-worshipper, were to take the opinions expressed by those same authorities as the unit of measure by which to judge us.

From the first unfolding of History down to our own days, privilege has been compelled, in order to subsist, to reinforce tyranny with artifice. Tyranny is, by its very nature, intermittent and does not reach everywhere. It makes good its deficiency through a discipline refined across the centuries, which manifests itself in various ways according to time and custom; but which aims constantly to portray the rulers of a State or a religion — at least so long as they live — as arks of genius, arks of sanctity; which aims, at the same time, to portray the refractory who refuse to enter the orbit of those great ones, those saints, as fanatics, as degenerate and wicked creatures.

This discipline creates the insubstantial larvae, rosy and phosphorescent, that draw the multitudes along tortuous paths; it creates the terrifying phantoms that drive them back, with senseless fears, from the straight roads. It was this discipline that rendered the Grand Lama inaccessible, lest the eye that sees, analyses, and judges should discern, in his flesh and in his spirit, the defects inseparable from human nature. It induced the Sultans to cause themselves to be preceded and followed by a pack of assassins whirling their scimitars. The bold ones who dared raise inquiring eyes to the demigod provoked their own instantaneous death.

It has taught, on the other hand, that the rancour against rebellious souls must continue even after execution. To sever the heads that refused to bow is not enough; they must be exposed, bleeding, to the mockery of the unthinking. To burn the thinker at the stake is not enough; a fierce malediction must be pronounced over his ashes, and those ashes must be scattered to the winds.

Austria would have been naïve had she contented herself with hanging the Italian patriots. She buried them without funeral rites and without coffin, in out-of-the-way pits, as was the practice with the carcasses of four-footed beasts.

It is that baleful discipline, modified by the times, which brands and persecutes as a crime the honest expression of an honest thought; and where certain extremes are no longer possible, the livid war against reputation and memory is still very much in vogue. Which war, if it be useful to privilege more or less legally constituted, becomes an indispensable necessity for privilege that has attained its station through violence and crime.

At my age one records facts; one does not vent rancours. In the trial mounted against the authors of the attempt of the fourteenth of January, the denigration aimed at our detriment was all the more necessary inasmuch as the very origin of the Empire was, more or less openly, under discussion. We sat in the dock facing Napoleon; but Napoleon sat in the dock facing France and the civilised world. Every nobility of spirit on his part, every loftiness of purpose, became an aggravation of our guilt; every loftiness of purpose on our part tended to diminish him — it was a condemnation of him.

Nor must it go unsaid that violent action sometimes gives rise to incidents which demonstrate it more efficacious than any other form of propaganda. To blacken us, to make us pass for malefactors of the lowest order, to diminish the likelihood of finding imitators — this, therefore, had to be the undeclared yet essential purpose of the trial. I acknowledge that, from their own point of view, the French judicial authorities acted wisely in attempting to decapitate, along with our bodies, our reputations. I acknowledge, at the same time, that they flung themselves into the enterprise with all the sinister dexterity of the worms of the Second Empire.
