The bill of indictment, formulated and read by the Imperial Procurator Chaix-d'Est-Ange, slid deftly over the initial uncertainties and the police machinations; it portrays the examining authorities with the bearing of genius that surveys, dissects, and conquers. It suppresses, on the other hand, every impulse of ours that might reveal a character above the common, and, by insisting upon the most readily censurable aspects of our conduct and giving emphasis to our weaknesses, it burdens us with commentary — often wholly fantastical — that savours of cowardice.

The President of the Tribunal, Monsieur Delangle, for his part, laboured with viperous persistence to bestow upon the Procurator's semi-literary creation its proper embellishments, and concealed its improbabilities by introducing incidents which, even had they been true, would have had no connection whatsoever with our conspiracy. Worse still: he cast "charitably" over the threadbare garment of disinterestedness the broad mantle of the hired assassin; he stamped the brand of the charlatan upon a dying virtue.

An easy task, after all! We lived in times of intense passions; times in which the attractions and repulsions of souls succeeded one another with the violence of the tempest. What man who took any active part in those times, however great or small, has not found himself treated — to his own amazement — now worse than the devil, now better than a saint; the object, often simultaneously, of profound veneration and immeasurable hatred? It is readily understood that the Police could not have been wanting in mud to rake through.

Thus upon Giuseppe Pieri the President Delangle poured, in Paris in the year 1858, the hatred of the Tuscan reactionaries which had pursued him since 1830; the hatred which, by painting him as a beast of liberalism, had succeeded in having him stripped of his colonelcy; the hatred which had seized upon a prank among *scapigliati* — free-spirited rogues who had relieved another such rogue of a watch worth a few *baiocchi* — as a pretext for his infamy.

The generosity of spirit, the pride, the indignant impulses of Felice Orsini were branded as "ridiculous ostentation." And when Orsini persisted in maintaining that the third bomb had not been thrown by him, but by the Italian to whom he had entrusted it a few minutes before the attempt, Delangle demonstrated his serene impartiality in the search for truth by breaking forth and portraying him as a liar who was anxious to make it believed that his hands were free of the blood of the children, the women, the old men wounded and killed; "because the smell of that blood rose to him and went to his head."

To complete his meritorious work in the service of lofty and moral principles, President Delangle then lent a complaisant ear to the whispers of the sacristy, and Felice Orsini — Commissioner of the Roman Republic in a distant province, who, surrounded by foreign enemies and weakened by enemies within, maintained his phalanx through requisitioning — was portrayed to the jurors as a common thief who had subsequently escaped, by flight, the delights of the pontifical *ergastolo* — life imprisonment.

As concerns myself personally, Delangle had not the shadow of a pretext for reproaching me with either disreputable family connections or misappropriations real or apparent; but this was not to render me immune to his venom. I had revealed how and why, on the evening of the fourteenth of January, Felice Orsini had handed us the sum of two hundred francs. My disclosure was intended to make our judges understand that venality was not in our nature. But I was to discover that I had not reckoned with the refined malice of my judges. That slender drop, in the midst of such a crisis, sufficed for the incorruptible Magistrate — assuming an air of indignant surprise — to accuse me of having, I who belonged to an honourable family, abandoned school, abhorred honest labour, and so degraded myself by degrees as to become a mercenary assassin for two hundred francs.

A word on the records of the proceedings. The tactic of denigration could not arrest itself at the margin of the written documents, as our fabled progenitors halted at the threshold of paradise lost. The records were retouched and mutilated and distorted, like everything else, at the pleasure of His Excellency.

An example: when President Delangle asked whether we wished to adduce anything further before the imminent application of the sentence, Gomez and Orsini replied: "Nothing!" Pieri observed that he had shed no blood. I pointed out Kim, the municipal street-sweeper who, filing past with the other witnesses, had delivered his false testimony. And I denounced him aloud, protesting that in pronouncing sentence, justice required that no weight be given to a perjured deposition.

The official record states that, contrary to my three companions — unanimous in pronouncing their "nothing" — I had implored "the clemency of my judges." The meeting of Francesco Crispi with Orsini? I should be lying were I to say that I did not deny it. But one must remember that Orsini himself had, a few seconds before, declared that the Italian of the third bomb was not known to any of his accomplices, not even to Pieri; that he had revealed his existence to no one; that none of us had so much as laid eyes upon him.

Was this not almost openly suggesting to me my line of conduct? And was not, moreover, that denial of mine the most expeditious means of avoiding insidious questions and stratagems that might have compromised a man who remained unsuspected and at liberty? Had the charlatans of the Empire not felt in their souls the terror of the truth, they would not have imposed upon the newspapers the strict requirement of adhering, in their reports, solely to what was published in *Le Droit* and the *Gazette des Tribunaux*. A halved truth is no longer truth at all, and a raison d'État that does not scruple to adulterate truth by paring it down, will not hold itself back from scruple when it serves to deck it out like a strumpet, or to line its skirts with a few tendentious little falsehoods. Let the man who swears by the infallible sanctity of the powerful believe blindly in official records — with that simplicity of soul which leads others to believe in the infallibility of the hypocrite into whose ear a dove would coo the truth. Infinite and manifold is human folly!

But the prudent man who does not allow any broth to flow into his intellect without first filtering it will certainly not fail to reflect that we find ourselves, at the very least, in the unfavourable light of those whose history has been written by their own enemies, at a time and in circumstances that led those enemies to believe our every refutation impossible.

The sentence was pronounced on the night of the twenty-sixth, and almost as though to heighten the theatrical quality of that denouement, as the Court — its deliberations concluded — re-entered the great hall of the audience chamber, the clock of the Palais de Justice struck its chimes. To Gomez, life at the galleys; to Pieri, to myself, to Felice Orsini, the penalty reserved for parricides. Luigi Napoleon had become, after having been cannonaded by him, the father of his people... History is full of these sanguinary and yet clumsy ironies! And lest any doubt remain as to the nature of that penalty, its description was there and then read aloud: "led to the scaffold, in a shirt, barefoot, the head covered by a black veil; then the reading of the sentence... then decapitation."

I looked into the faces of my companions in faith and in misfortune. Gomez, deathly pale, maintained a marble immobility... the grief of the condemnation and the natural satisfaction of having escaped the worst seemed to cancel one another out in his heart. Pieri swayed his head and kept his eye fixed upon the President with an indefinable mingling of contempt and commiseration. I noticed that his lips were moving, and I had the impression that he was rehearsing, in that moment, a certain phrase he had addressed to Delangle himself during the interrogation: "I knew well enough that nothing good would ever come of dealing with that obstinate fellow!"

Felice Orsini turned his eyes slowly from one judge to another. He seemed to say, without the slightest trace of bravado: "Your meanness does not touch me!"

As for my own state of mind... what am I to say? I had long foreseen the condemnation; now that the foresight had been fulfilled, I struggled to possess its full consciousness, to grasp its full import. It was to assure myself that I was indeed awake that I had looked into the faces of my companions. All at once there rose vividly before my mind the image of Elisa — not fresh and smiling, but as I had kissed her for the last time — slumped in her chair, eyes vacant, bereft of her senses, as though the tribunal of the Seine had pronounced her condemnation and not mine.

That image fixed itself in my mind so relentlessly that it left me no room to see or think of anything else. As we passed through the underground passage that led us back to the Conciergerie, I roused myself and, turning to Orsini, who followed a few paces behind me, I said: "You who have means — remember them!" He understood, and murmured: "I promise you." The prison door had scarcely closed behind me when I experienced a strange sensation, as though I were being plunged into an almost familiar quietude. Saturno and the other guard already knew everything... they had lost their wager, and they had prepared champagne. But they wished for the chance of a return match. If the sentence were not quashed by the Supreme Court, or by a decree of clemency, they would pay another bottle; otherwise I should pay. Never did a wagerer so ardently desire to lose.

And so, by a caprice of fate, conversing gaily and raising our glasses, I ended the night on which I had been condemned to the guillotine. How often have I wished to know how my Judges, Napoleon III, the street-sweeper Kim, and the others who had contributed to saving the social order and the usurped Empire all ended their days... but my desire has always remained unsatisfied.


### Chapter Eighteen

<!-- pages:195-200 -->



The following day, at La Roquette. They made us put on the grey uniform of the prisoners; they put us in straitjackets — not from any lust for torment, but to forestall attempts at suicide.

We had been conveyed to the new prison separately and separately confined; yet our cells were situated in such a way that we could see and speak to one another. A rectangular room, rather spacious, bare — four white walls, four windows, four doors. Whoever enters by the door communicating with the courtyard sees, directly opposite, the door and window of Felice Orsini's cell; Pieri's cell lay beyond the left-hand wall; beyond the right-hand wall, mine.

This was, for us, a great advantage over the Conciergerie; death was in prospect, but it saw fit to be preceded by some small relief.

Another circumstance contributed to making the present melancholy hour more supportable. My guards had followed me to La Roquette. They were to sleep in turns so as to keep me under continuous watch; but this did not trouble me in the least, for I had no intention whatsoever of anticipating my end by taking my own life.

On the very day of our transfer, our lawyers came to counsel us to lodge an appeal. Nothing concrete was hoped for; nevertheless, both Pieri and I consented without raising any objection. But Orsini would hear nothing of it. He declared that death would by now relieve him of a great burden, and that it would be all the more welcome the sooner it came. Giulio Favre, to obtain his assent, was obliged very nearly to force his hand.

The days of agony began and passed swiftly. In a state of mind only too understandable, the memories of those days flash through my mind in fits and starts, standing out like disconnected images against the sombre background of the hours in which, lost in meditation, I was preparing myself to die.

The chaplain of La Roquette, Monsieur Hughon, paid us a visit at least every twenty-four hours. He had been a former captain of dragoons and retained something of that character — thickset and robust despite his years, liberal despite his position. From his very first appearance I told him plainly that he would always be welcome on one condition: that he should never speak to me on religious subjects. He gave his word and kept it.

Giuseppe Pieri, on the other hand, willingly acknowledged his right to exercise his ministry, but not with the intention of submitting to it passively. From the very first evening he drew the chaplain into argument with a frankly defiant air, and in the days that followed allowed himself to be carried away by a destructive spirit, by a manner of arguing *ad hominem* that was exceedingly sharp and almost offensive. Constrained by the sorrowful circumstances, the former captain was obliged to put a good face on more than one lance-thrust to the ribs.

Pieri's voice was, moreover, the predominant note in that confined and severe atmosphere. And how could he — already so loquacious in even the most colourless circumstances of ordinary life — have brought himself to be silent, now that powerful emotions exalted him?

He talked and argued about everything, obstinately — at times even with a generous vein of comedy, especially when he chanced to refute the ravings of our Judges. But however he turned and twisted, he returned by preference to his political and philosophical ideas, as though a chasm were dragging him inexorably into the neck of a funnel.

A strange contrast with the demeanour of our leader, from whose cell there issued, for hours on end, not the least token of life — so much so that more than once I had asked myself whether it was not the tomb of Felice Orsini, and whether Felice Orsini had not become the tomb of his own soul.

For one hour each day we were permitted to walk in the great courtyard, though not simultaneously. Pieri was taken out for his breath of air alone; upon his return, Orsini and I went out together. We wore our straitjackets even while walking, and were watched not merely at close quarters but at the point of the musket.

In that brief hour, Orsini filled his lungs like a man reborn; yet the gaze he turned towards the blue sky was full of melancholy. He walked at a brisk pace, and I, bred to military discipline, followed him instinctively, keeping a little to one side. It was this way: if I were out of humour and said nothing, he would turn his head and question me with a look as if to say, "A storm?" But if I spoke, he would press on as though absorbed in the vision of other worlds, or if he answered, the words were drawn from deep within him, as with a corkscrew.

Those brief words sufficed to reveal to me a man rapidly undergoing a radical transformation.

Felice Orsini had brought two resentments into prison with him: one against Pieri, and one against Giuseppe Mazzini. It is well known that in his *Memorie* the irascible Romagnol had portrayed Mazzini as a modern Mahomet presuming to mould the destinies of Europe according to the whims of his odalisques — a circumstance of which the monarchist Cavour made admirable use. He had circulated those *Memorie* by the thousand, thus craftily throwing a bridge that offered the means of crossing the Rubicon, of furling the banner with its motto "God and the People," for too many, far too many Mazzinians — whether wavering from weakness of fibre, or eager to lend a semblance of respectability to base and venal calculations under any pretext whatsoever.

But the old resentment towards the Master dissolved on the day that the guards and Giulio Favre informed Orsini of the noble defence of him made by the "Prophet" on the morrow of the attempt, at a time when other patriots, scrupulous as to the means of redeeming their country, had all but branded him a common assassin.

To this transformation, which I might call regenerating, another and far wider one of quite a different nature made correspondence. It is better to broach the matter at once. Felice Orsini was the only one of the three men condemned to die who listened to the exhortations of the dragoon-chaplain with more than passive, indeed with complaisant attention. And our daily walks had instilled in me a presentiment of what afterward came to pass. The convictions slowly acquired through a combative existence were, in Orsini, slowly disintegrating, and were giving way — indeed, yielding the ascendancy — to the underlying strata saturated with the fables absorbed in an unknowing childhood. In the imminence of his end, he returned to those religious practices to which only the blind multitude should still attribute essential virtue.

He therefore errs and lies — unconsciously — against truth, who, admiring the stoic fortitude with which Orsini mounted the scaffold, sought to hold him up as a model of consistency to be set against the figure of Silvio Pellico and others softened, as it were, by their sufferings. But he would be equally mistaken who should presume to protect Orsini's greatness by passing over his conversion in silence. Silence attenuates nothing and cancels nothing.

It may well be said in his defence that he succumbed to a cause far from ignoble. Capable of leonine boldness under a fleeting impulse, he was powerless — owing to his supremely sensitive nature — against a thought that was tormenting, persistent, obstinate, fixed: not that of the death overhanging him, but the other, deriving from the consciousness of his own responsibility in the ruin of high ideals, from the vision of the tragic deaths of others. His hair had rapidly turned white under it; more than one delicate fibre of his brain must have been left shaken and as it were scored by it.

Felice Orsini never, in any case, presented himself to his contemporaries as a discoverer of philosophical and moral truths. He called himself a patriot, rather, and was one from earliest youth; a patriot he was and proclaimed himself to be until the blade of the guillotine hung over his neck. He had pledged to Italy his every energy and the last drop of his own blood. Italy has had greater sons — none more unconditionally faithful. I say there is no need to glean in other fields to establish the glory of Felice Orsini.

Besides the dragoon-chaplain, there appeared almost daily at La Roquette a very powerful personage, the Prefect of Police, Pietri. He treated us in a most affable manner, and never failed to exert himself to instil some ray of hope into our minds — now asserting that the Court of Appeal would in all probability quash our trial; now assuring us that the Empress and a powerful court faction were disposed towards clemency so openly that the mind of Napoleon must in the end be influenced and won over by them.

But neither his manner nor his intelligent countenance could avail to dispel the instinctive distrust, to bridge the abyss that separates the conspirator from a man who must, by the very force of circumstances, hunt him down and derive his own advancement from the conspirator's ruin.

This distrust was in me sharpened by other circumstances. On more than one occasion I had sent to Elisa letters of the most passionate character. When I wished to write, the guard on duty would free my right hand more or less, and then, having bound it again, would carry the letter to the prison Director for him to affix the *nulla osta*. But my ardent appeals received no answer — absolute silence, as though Elisa had utterly abandoned me, or were dead. It is easy to imagine my torment. Then, all at once, there entered into my heart the suspicion that it was a refined cruelty on the part of the Police, devised for ends I could not clearly comprehend, yet whose existence I divined. From that moment my torment ceased, giving place to a smouldering rancour against those who had inflicted it upon me.

Moreover, Pietri had the fault of bringing to the prison, almost without interruption, one distinguished personage after another. A morbid curiosity seemed to impel the upper classes to verify — what shall I say — whether perhaps those who had dared to provoke such disorder were truly made of flesh and bone. This being put on display, like a wild beast in a cage, weighed upon me terribly.

One day I found myself in my cell with Pietri and a high-ranking military officer whose head was bandaged. I cannot quite recall whether it was General Magnan or General Roguet. Pietri introduced him to me simply as "one of your victims"; which caused my ill humour to break out. I retorted sharply that I had sought to strike no one but the man who had been the ruin of my country — and as for being a victim, I should very willingly have changed places with that wounded man. At this the officer stepped forward and, extending his right hand, affirmed that he bore not the least rancour, neither towards me nor towards any other. And I clasped that hand as best I was able, but made no reply, and, turning to the Prefect, asked him what interest the Police could have in intercepting my wife's letters. He appeared surprised, but contented himself with a shrug of the shoulders. The following day he returned alone, and before entering the cell stood for a few seconds erect on the threshold, filling the doorway with his tall, fine person. It seemed to me that I detected in that countenance something unusual, something inquisitive. He entered and, after repeating in a manner more affable and, I was almost about to say, more paternal than usual, the customary phrases, he rubbed his forehead and, lowering his voice somewhat — the guard had, moreover, withdrawn at his appearance — spoke to me at length of his deep sympathy for my youth. And, commiserating the sad fate reserved for me, in part through force of circumstance, in part also — he said — through the supreme recklessness of my companions, he assured me he had given much thought to finding a means of saving me — and not me alone! I already knew, he said, that at court there was no aversion to adopting measures tempered by clemency. But what I did not know — he added — was that His Majesty the Emperor was animated by a vivid sympathy for Italy, whose greatness he admired and whose misfortunes deeply moved him. Once a man such as this was set upon such a course, who could predict where he would stop? Certainly no sovereign in Europe was better placed to advance the redemption of Italy than Napoleon. So that if I considered matters with the eye of an enlightened patriot, I should not be long in understanding that I could better serve both myself and my country by aiding — allying myself, so to speak — with the man I had attempted to kill.

I believe my face expressed my profound astonishment, for Pietri laid a paternal hand upon my shoulder and, lowering his voice still further, said:

"I know that I am dealing with an intelligent young man and I expect, therefore, not to be considered a boor. Between His Majesty and his great designs stand the internal enemies of the Empire. Too weak to act openly, they have allied themselves in secret with its external enemies and have conspired. I have not come to propose anything dishonourable to you. The safety and well-being of States have their ineluctable demands. Assist the exalted personage upon whom the triumph of the Italian cause may depend; protect him against his internal enemies — and I give you my word of honour that you will not be long in rejoining your young wife. For what strange aberration would you leave her poor, abandoned, and alone to struggle in the world and against the world?"

Now the true reason for Elisa's pretended silence became not merely hinted at but plain to me; now my indignation had double cause to burst forth. "I know nothing," I replied, "and I have nothing to reveal. But even were it otherwise — you yourself, even you, if I were to accept your proposals, would be the first to feel horror for me. Nor from you, a Corsican, would I have expected such proposals — Italians abhor traitors!"

My natural vivacity urged me to gesture, and, being unable to do so, I trembled. But without intending it, I had touched a sensitive chord in a heart not wholly stripped of noble feeling.

I saw that man — hardened to so many emotions — cover his face with his hands. A few seconds, and he was looking me in the face; his eyes were red with tears. He departed with a promise that he would do everything within his power to save our lives. If he failed in that endeavour, he would tender his resignation.

I have no reason whatsoever to doubt the sincerity of that man; but what he may actually have done, or attempted to do, I cannot say. This much is certain: on the eleventh of March he came to La Roquette and communicated to Felice Orsini, if nothing else, his hopes. This was a little before the hour appointed for our walk. The conversation between the two was still in full warmth when that hour struck and the guards led me out into the courtyard alone. On my return, Felice Orsini — always so taciturn and almost sombre — appeared to me all at once transformed, and I was about to say buoyant. The moment he saw me, he said aloud: "Di Rudio, be of good heart. We shall not be executed!"

This took place, I repeat, on the morning of the eleventh of March. That very same evening, the Court of Cassation rejected our appeal. If Orsini's assertion was destined to find confirmation in reality, the events of the twelfth of March would have demonstrated it. That day passed, and our expectations were disappointed. That same night of the twelfth, by the light of torches, men were at work in the square of La Roquette erecting the scaffold.

The explanation of the enigma? I received it later from the lips of Mathieu de la Drôme. Public opinion was exerting powerful pressure in our favour. In spite of the Police, in the popular gathering-places a rousing song was being sung celebrating "the heroes of the fourteenth of January." The Empress and a powerful court faction had declared themselves for clemency. Napoleon III called their demonstrations indecent; but he had not the courage to oppose them openly. To eliminate the enemies who had fallen into his power, to cast terror among those who had escaped him — such was the secret yearning of the enemy of liberty; but it had to be accomplished while appearing to have his hand forced, as though through a scrupulous respect for the Law, and by shifting the responsibility onto others.

The Council of State was convened to decide upon the greater or lesser advisability of a sovereign pardon, and the Emperor made a brief appearance before it. "The Councillors of the Crown," he said, "were free to vote according to their own conscience; but he could not refrain from expressing one conviction of his. Within that Council there lurked traitors."

It was the dark threat. The Councillors had been convened not to yield to the tide of compassion, but to render service to their master. Could the dignitaries of an Empire — whose very essence was profiteering and corruption, and which, in that vortex, would have dragged all France down with it — hesitate between their ample sinecures and the lives of a handful of outcasts, rendered incapable of resistance or of doing harm? And had not the reason of State concealed still other and very different acts of cowardice?

As though this were not enough, the Archbishop of Paris, having scented the situation, dampened the mild influence of Eugenia Montijo by interposing between her and the Emperor the measured, subtle exhortations of the Church. Never had the hatreds and interests of clericalism and of the State shown themselves so closely akin: the conspirators smelt of Freemasonry; they had fought body and soul to put an end to that temporal power which is the ill-concealed supreme aim of every religious intrigue; they, had they been able, would have renewed the struggle. It was well worth suppressing them, even if, in doing so, one repudiated Christ once more.

The Council of State and the clergy — Napoleon III had more than he had hoped for, and his mind, which could plumb characters behind their masks, must have exulted darkly, and perhaps, like the despot of tragedy, exclaimed within himself: "Oh, how many are the consciences for sale!"


### Chapter Nineteen

<!-- pages:200-206 -->



ON the evening of the twelfth, the chaplain Hughon came to pay me his customary visit. He was grave, grave, grave. I spoke to him of our rejected appeal, and he grew flustered. Then the suspicion of the grim truth flashed upon me, and I resorted to several subterfuges to confirm it. But Hughon promptly assumed the air of the ingenuous, and sheltering behind that simple artifice, became unassailable. Perhaps every suspicion of mine might have dissolved, had my visitor not extended his hand as he took his leave. Those fingers seemed to me to trace mysterious signs in the air, so visibly did they tremble.

I awaited the return of Saturno who, of the two guards, seemed to me the more candid, and without even giving him time to remove his cap: "Saturno," I said, "I know that tomorrow morning I shall be led to punishment. Listen well, and do, you who are good, exactly as I bid in my last wishes. At five o'clock you will wake me..."

Here he began to protest. Why had I put such nonsense into my head? The appeal being rejected, there remained still, thanks to our connections, a great deal of rope to twist, and before anything... eh, eh!... it would take much more than that!

"My friend, Monsieur Hughon intended to hear my confession and instead... I have confessed him. He has revealed everything to me!"

At this, Saturno, who was still gesticulating to give emphasis to that *eh, eh!* of his, let his arm fall as though I had snapped it off. "He revealed everything to you? That is against the Regulations and he ought not to have done it." He stood for a moment with eyes downcast, then murmured: "Since you know everything... well, then — yes! there is nothing left for you now but to die like a man, as you have lived."

Then, all at once, a great silence fell within my soul, and with it a kind of great darkness; but in that emptiness, life still manifested itself powerfully through an instinct that drove me to crave something yet darker, yet more silent. *"Tanto meglio quanto più presto!"* — "The sooner the better!" — is Orsini's expression; only then did I understand its full meaning.

"At five precisely!... I have a great deal, a very great deal to write. My last thoughts, from the prison, to my kinsmen and to Elisa; my last thoughts, from the scaffold, to the Fatherland... And you?... Take this! (I kissed him.) Tomorrow, at this hour, think of me. Say to yourself: earthly things touch him no longer!"

Saturno was punctual. My first sensation on that memorable day was caused by the extreme pallor of his face. I wrote my last thoughts to my father; I wrote to my wife's kinsmen, commending that unhappy woman to them from the depths of my heart. Six o'clock struck at the Roquette.

Shortly after, ten or twelve persons entered the large room adjoining. Among them I recognised the chaplain Hughon and the Governor of the prison. They made their way to Orsini's cell, and I heard them announce to him his final hour. The Governor of the prison then pronounced, in a low voice, a few phrases, the meaning of which was plain to me from Orsini's reply. A magistrate stood ready to receive the dying man's disclosures, and he had been urged to make a full confession. The reply of the martyr rang through the sepulchral silence, subdued yet distinct: "Felice Orsini knows how to die; he does not know how to be an informer!"

Then came Pieri's turn, and his nerves gave way. "He was old, he was bound; but he would have had spirit enough..."

The Governor of the prison admonished him courteously, and he recovered himself at once. "Be it as it may," he said, "to die now, to die in some few years — what does it matter? My executioners too shall die..." He added that he intended to plunge into eternity singing.

My turn came. I replied calmly that the Count Carlo di Rudio was ready — an expression which, in the official reports, was afterwards attributed to Orsini. As my lips closed, I made out more clearly, in the dim light, the man who had accompanied the Governor of the prisons into my cell. He was an elderly abbé... A fine soul, perhaps. But to me, so ill-disposed toward everything that recalls the tonsure, he had the effect of an enemy come to ascertain, *de visu*, my last moments. And since death was near and certain, I could well, without fear of giving harm to any living soul, give free rein to my feelings. He drew close to me and made to speak. I imagined he wished to exhort me to die like a Christian, and I forestalled him: "I prefer," I said, "to consecrate my last thoughts to my wife and my little child. As for your God, I shall see Him, if He exists, but only to tell Him from what manner of ministers He has the simplicity to let Himself be represented upon this earth."

We were conducted into the large room. Our hands were unbound. Grave, and belonging to people who were tragically moved, the faces... the rest, almost like a scene of friends who bid farewell to friends, on the eve of a long journey.

Pieri, firmly resolved to treat the matter as an ordinary event, would not renounce his breakfast. The appropriate orders were given. While waiting, a round of introductions even took place. In this way I learned that Hughon was to be the spiritual comforter of Orsini; the chaplain of the Conciergerie, that of Pieri; the abbé fastened to my side was the chaplain of the House of Correction.

An elegant *cabaret* — a tray with coffee and rum — was brought. Felice Orsini sipped from the latter, toasting the health of the Governor of the prisons. At this point, my "comforter," humanely overlooking my rough rebuff, offered me a small glass; but he made the mistake of observing that it would give me courage. I refused out of pride, retorting that Italian patriots had no need of artificial courage. Then there was a rivalry in asking one another for small keepsakes. The three executioners, upright and silent along the wall, watched us with a strange persistence.

Suddenly the Governor drew out his watch and made a sign to the guards. The Regulations, heedless of conscience, required us to attend Mass. Felice Orsini moved sadly toward the prison chapel. I protested, and Pieri added to my curt refusal his own impetuous *loquela* — his rush of words. Those present glanced at one another; the Governor shrugged his shoulders and in the end consented to compromise. Orsini's absence was very brief. No sooner had he returned than they led us toward the Chancellery, whence we passed into an adjoining room. On entering, Pieri, who preceded me by a few paces, turned round and said to Orsini: "Well then, my old friend?" Orsini raised his head, looked at him steadily and replied: "Calm, calm!" Those were the first words those two men, united by their ideals and their martyrdom, had exchanged since their arrest... and they were the last!

We were in the hands of the executioner. They shaved my nape; they made me put on a low-cut shirt; they removed my shoes with their own hands. The same preparations, carried out rapidly, left Orsini absorbed in his thoughts; but the voluble man from Lucca grew almost cheerful from it all... the strangeness of nerves! He wished to see himself in a mirror, and his executioner — something of a dandy, most likely, despite his foul trade — drew a small looking-glass from his waistcoat pocket and held it out to him. He examined himself at his leisure and, smiling, exclaimed: "You have got me up like an old *cocotte*!" While they were removing his shoes he broke into a frank laugh and said: "Lucky thing I washed my feet!"

As for me... why should I not say it? Those moments were extremely painful to me. For several years my life had been a succession of events in vivid colours; but every trace of them seemed suddenly effaced by the memories of childhood that assailed me in a throng. And I saw again my poor mother, all tenderness and all smiles, as on a feast-day when she dressed me and, gazing at me, took her pride in it. What a contrast!

But the Count di Rudio was not to be seen, at such a moment, with tearful eyes, and if a tear or two wished to spring forth, his pride took care to drive them back into the depths and dry them there.

While they were wrapping my head in the black veil, Giuseppe Pieri was already walking out without a word. But I reached him in time to hear him utter that phrase which, slightly distorted, afterwards served almost as a pretext for demonstrating his grandiose and eccentric mind. To me it seemed, and still seems, the virile expression of a patriot who, having fulfilled his mission to the utmost limit of his own powers, feels himself lightened of every perishable and vulgar part of himself, and almost sublimated by martyrdom. The chaplain of the Conciergerie had taken his arm, as though there were need to support him. He, without even turning his head: "One does not feel fear when one goes to Calvary!" And he moved away between the two foul props of every tyranny, the priest and the executioner... and I saw him no more. But I heard his voice distinctly strike up, in the courtyard, the hymn of the Girondins: *"Mourir pour la Patrie"* — "To Die for the Fatherland." He went to the scaffold, as he had promised, singing!...

I am in the courtyard... it rains and snows. Weather that reconciles me to the thought of the imminent warm shelter underground. I pass alongside a company of prison guards drawn up in line, presenting arms... the rifles have their muzzles pointed to the ground, their stocks in the air. The great gate stood wide open; the scaffold, erected a few paces from the pavement, cut off my view; but not so much that I could not make out on the square a considerable body of foot-soldiers, of light cavalry, of artillery, drawn up as if in the imminence of action.

My hands were bound behind my back and fastened, moreover, to the bonds that constrained my feet. I advanced in steps necessarily small and slow. The executioner, on my right, stood a full head taller than I; the old priest, on my left, must with his white hair have contrasted strangely with my dark, restless youth. He was murmuring prayers, his eyes upon a crucifix. I had expressed the desire to draw a few puffs of smoke, and this had been granted me. But the water and snow, which I trod with bare feet, which fell upon my bare shoulders, provoked shiverings that I strove in vain to suppress, and that apparent weakness, vexing me, drove me to bite savagely at the stem of my pipe.

Near the gate, the field of my vision widened, and I saw the windows and saw the rooftops thronged with people. The spectacle of ill-starred virtue, the Greeks declared, is worthy of the Gods, and to witness it, men had paid as much as a thousand francs for the hire of a balcony. The mood of that multitude? "It" — says the official report — "has remained constantly calm and silent throughout the great and legitimate expiation." In truth, a robust voice from above cried out: "People, bare your heads! This is the execution of heroes!" A commotion broke out on the square. They were attempting, I believe, to surround the house from which that human protest had come. But just at that moment my attention was drawn elsewhere... the unexpected occurred!

A man on horseback had entered through the gate... he was dressed in civilian clothes, his chest covered with decorations. He dismounted from the saddle and guards and soldiers saluted him in military fashion. It was Monsieur de Collet, the Empress's major-domo. He came up to me, looked me full in the face, and noticing my trembling, said: "It is cold, Monsieur Rudio!" I replied: "Yes, but they will not leave me time to catch a chill." A puff of smoke struck him full in the face. "I beg your pardon; my hands are bound and besides... it is the last!" — "Oh, not at all; you will smoke again!" He murmured a few words in the ear of the executioner first, then of the old priest, and both, slowly and silently, withdrew. Then, turning once more to me, as I questioned him with the expression of my face: "I shall come to your cell towards nine o'clock and explain everything." I spun on my heels and found myself face to face with Felice Orsini.

He slowed his step: "What is this?"

— They are sending me back to prison. I know nothing more.

— And Pieri?

— He is up there!

— Better two than three!

We continued on our opposite ways... he to fecundate with his blood the ideal of the Fatherland; I toward the prison... and that return to life came upon me as something grievous as a violence, so fully was I prepared for death! After a few steps, I turned my head, and, by a singular correspondence of souls, Orsini turned his as well. "Farewell!" he said, with a nod of his head. The black veil that wrapped it, grown thick and opaque here and there in its folds, concealed his face, so that he already seemed to me half lost in the mysterious shadow of eternity...

I found the large room and my cell crowded with soldiers and officers. A group was busily gathered about my bed, upon which lay, in a dead faint and in convulsions, my faithful guard, Saturno. I made my way through to him, and while a compassionate hand unbound my arms, I kept calling him by name. My voice proved more efficacious than the sprinklings of water and the smelling-salts... He opened his eyes, looked at me as one lost in a dream, then his face brightened with a light that revealed a friendship profoundly sincere. All at once, he assailed me with these words: "The wager!... you have lost!... you shall pay the champagne!"

"Later, my friend. This is an hour for grief. Down there, men are dying!" Almost as though to confirm my words, the clock of the Roquette struck: seven strokes. In the prison a sepulchral silence fell. The tragic hour bestows strange clairvoyance... the officers uncovered their heads. At that moment, the blade of the guillotine... the heads... the vile basket...

The Count di Rudio, overcome by emotion, could not continue. The insufficiency of the heart, which was already undermining his robust old age, took away his breath. I knew that to observe him at such moments irritated him and worsened the ailment, and it was with a true sense of relief that I heard the quick step of Signora Elisa. She entered and did not perceive the gravity of the moment. She stretched out her arms, and her left hand was laid over her open right palm. She was smiling with her lips, smiling with her blue eyes, as though she carried a great happiness within her soul and wished to parcel it out and share it with us. She uncovered her palm and displayed... two chicks nestled there, of a luminous whiteness. Who remembers the white hen that was brooding in the courtyard? "They have hatched!" said the Signora, still smiling. Those two little atoms of life looked at us in wonder, as though asking: "Who are you?"

I turned my face away... the Californian sun, near its setting, peered through the green foliage. My sad eyes, angered by the light, closed; but the reddish disc remained imprinted on my retina. O ardent imagination! O vividness of evocation! In that blood-red glare, the severed head of Felice Orsini appeared... Gripped by the hair in the executioner's fist, it gasped, breathless, that last, fateful wish of his: *"Viva l'Italia! Viva la Francia!"*

My lips did not move, but my soul gave a cry: "Oh, how dear the Fatherland has cost us!"


### Chapter Twenty

<!-- pages:206-210 -->



AT nine o'clock precisely, Monsieur de Collet was in my cell. He did not elaborate at great length, but what he told me was more than sufficient to put an end to my conjectures. Influential English personages had interceded on my behalf with his august Empress. The second birthday of the Crown Prince fell in those very days, and she, who was above all else a woman and a mother, had been pleased to solemnise the auspicious occasion by granting a pardon that would lift a woman, a mother, from her affliction. My wife had already been informed by telegraph of my good fortune.

Monsieur de Collet then lavished congratulations upon me; and afterwards made to take his leave, but turning back upon his steps: "It was the Empress who sent me this morning. She wished to ensure that her will had not been contravened. Nevertheless, it is well to remember that much good, and much of severe justice, may proceed from her august consort. A phrase of gratitude addressed to him could certainly do you no harm. I say this on my own initiative; but I am persuaded that I am interpreting the thought of Her Majesty the Empress, to whom you are also indebted for your life."

What was I to answer? In the space of a couple of hours, a profound reaction had been working within me, and to that first, strange regret at the unexpected return to the sensations of the living, there had already succeeded a desperate panting for intense life, for freedom! Hope, violently expelled from the soul, returned in torrents, and no human force could have withstood it. I say that if the expression of feigned sentiments could contribute even so much as a straw to the projects — devoid of any definite form, yet unquestionable in their tendency — that were already germinating within my mind, I must not hesitate to write one, ten, a hundred letters... to the Emperor, to the Pope, to the devil himself! I was entirely defenceless in the hands of omnipotent enemies... any subterfuge, any deception became a legitimate art of war and of salvation. To slip free from those talons, at whatever cost, in order to stand more proudly with unbound hands; to fight once more — this was the problem!

I gave my promise.

I was brought once more to the Palais de Justice, before that President Delangle who had infused so much of his venom into our trial. By one of those hypocrisies dear to a society that attaches more importance to appearances than to substance, it was desired that I should seem free in the Tribunal, and my chains were removed. The relevant portions of the trial record were read aloud; whereupon the Advocate General, Oscar de Valle, requested the ratification of the imperial letters patent commuting my death sentence to perpetual life imprisonment. These letters bore the date of the 12th of March. Why, then, had I been led to the very foot of the scaffold? Was it the tardiness of bureaucracy that was to blame, or the deliberate design of subjecting me to a moral pressure that might induce me to make revelations?

I must note that the official account of the execution dwells at length upon Pieri and Orsini — of myself, not a single word! I believe that no wish existed to offend the susceptibilities of a people, by implicitly admitting that chivalrous France had abolished physical torture only to substitute in its place the torture of the intellect.

The pardon having been ratified, President Delangle made one last attempt to strike a blow on behalf of the social order, as he understood it, or pretended to understand it, by asking me whether I repented "of my crime." Upon the protest of my counsel, who feared some fierce reply, he did not insist, and contented himself with saying to the gendarmes: "Take him away!" The manacles were replaced upon my wrists. I was conducted to the Conciergerie, and thence, in a prison van, to La Roquette, where I was thrust into the courtyard amongst the other prisoners. One of them came forward at once, offering me his right hand. It was Antonio Gomez.

What a glance was ours, and how many thoughts we exchanged in a few seconds, without a word! It was Gomez who broke the silence. The carelessness that had brought about my arrest still gnawed at him, and he begged my pardon for it.

"Do not trouble yourself about it," I said to him. "We have all of us committed errors, one way or another. But it may well be affirmed that our souls knew no base impulses."

We ended by leaning against a wall, as though exposing our limbs to the sun — a cheerless sun that gave no foretaste of spring. We spoke of the two martyrs... they who had shed no blood were the ones decapitated! A sensation colder than winter crept into my breast and grew there, and the words died upon our lips. I was roused from my thoughts by a warder who, as he passed, shook a great bunch of keys with a violent clatter.

Several days went by, rendered the more painful by the humiliating regulations of prison life, and prolonged by the feverish waiting for news from my wife. I imagined that the Police would no longer interpose any obstacle to our correspondence, and Elisa, by delaying to send me word of herself, had revived all my anxieties. My start, therefore, was violent when I heard myself announced as being required at the Chancery.

I was led directly before the superintendent, the Marquis Lassalle, who handed me a letter, providing me by that simple act with a threefold surprise. The first, because contrary to the iron discipline of the place, it was still sealed; the second, because the fine characters visible upon the envelope were not Elisa's — they were entirely unknown to me; the third, because upon looking the superintendent full in the face, as though to draw from it some explanation, I perceived that, despite his outward gravity, he was smiling beneath the skin.

I tore open the envelope and experienced a fourth surprise, before which the three preceding ones grew pale and insignificant. The letter began by exhorting me to read to the very end before passing judgement. It then proceeded to inform me that its author was the Viscountess of Hastings, a Scottish Protestant who had converted to the Catholic faith and become Sister Teresa of the Discalced Carmelites. She was cloistered in the convent of Montauban, near Toulouse. "I must also preface my words," wrote Sister Teresa, "by explaining that the rules of our Order oblige us to go every two hours during the night to pray in the church of the Convent." One night, whilst she was at prayer, Christ had unexpectedly appeared before her. He, in a voice beyond all utterance, had communicated to her that within the prisons of La Roquette there was a young man of patrician family, courageous, his soul nurtured upon noble and elevated sentiments. The influence of wicked persons had led him astray from the righteous path and driven him to perdition. Christ had concluded by entrusting her with the holy mission of saving the soul of that errant young man, who was, despite his transgressions, dear to the Heavenly Father.

This was followed by a detailed account of the life and conversion of Sister Teresa, and a fervent exhortation that I should imitate her example; that I should return to the bosom of the true Church of God.

The letter bore also a postscript, in which Sister Teresa assured me that she had obtained from the Archbishop of Paris permission to carry on a fraternal correspondence with me. The Archbishop himself had then prevailed upon the prison authorities to grant me leave to seal my replies, which would be delivered to her unread, neither by those authorities nor by any person whatsoever.

No great perspicacity was needed to comprehend that the fine, elegant characters of the Discalced Carmelite concealed the foul alliance of Church and Empire. To please the adulterer, the bride of Christ had assumed the vile trade of the spy.

I returned the letter to the Marquis Lassalle, observing to him, with irony, that if certain stupidities were still possible in the full light of the nineteenth century, under the Empire, in the France of the Encyclopaedists, they had no purchase whatsoever upon the souls of Italian patriots. The Marquis Lassalle shrugged his shoulders without reply; but his ill-concealed smile would no longer be contained, and it oozed from every pore of his skin.

Nevertheless I reflected that from this farce, thrust into the midst of so full a drama, I might contrive to draw some advantage for my family; leaving aside also the consideration that writing would serve me as a distraction. Sister Teresa received my reply.

What did I say to her? I thanked her first of all for the interest she had taken in me. Christ, who is acquainted with all things, must certainly have informed her that I was a freethinker, and that I believed not at all in him, nor in his doctrines. If, however, there truly existed a Divine Mercy disposed to do something for me, let it set aside the soul for the moment and address itself to the extreme needs of my earthly existence. I had a wife and a child, to whose welfare I was indispensable. Should Christ work the miracle of restoring me to liberty, my scepticism might well be shaken thereby, and — one thing leads to another. At large, we should speak at greater leisure of my soul!

Now mark the subtlety of the servants of God! They took very good care not to induce their principal to work any miracle; but, understanding that something must nonetheless be conceded to me, if only to lure me on with the hope of better things, they quietly pulled the strings, and... One day — no more than five or six had passed since I had written to Sister Teresa — while I was walking in meditation, a hand, amicably rough, descended upon my shoulder. It was the head warder, who invited me to follow him to the Chancery. He wore the mysterious air of a man who leads a child to where the apple is hidden.

A few minutes later, I too had my apparition. Not imaginary, not feigned; but real and living and trembling more for me than for herself. She stood upright in the shadows of the great room, like the Madonna in a Flemish painting, and no sooner had I entered than she ran towards me with the impetuous force of passion and embraced me. Elisa!... Elisa!... She was not alone. She had with her her little child, who was toddling forward on uncertain steps. Freeing myself from Elisa's arms, I lifted him up and kissed him soundly; whereupon he clouded his round face and fixed his great eyes upon his mother's countenance, as though to ascertain from her expression that he was in no danger. His mute language said: "Ought I to cry, or ought I to laugh?"

The warder, seated beside the door with a large key in his right hand, warned me that for our final farewell we were granted no more than a quarter of an hour. We might, he said, speak freely in English. Our brief interview was exceedingly agitated. She informed me that, following my advice, she had succeeded in interesting in our fate a certain Sir Walter, a native of her own Nottingham, who was one of the pillars of the London *Times*. That powerful journal had circulated a petition, which was then signed, through the humane influence of Sir Richard Main, by several personages of considerable weight at Court. Queen Victoria, moved by a pressure skilfully dissimulated, had authorised the English Ambassador in Paris to exert himself in order that the petition might be delivered personally to the Empress Eugenia.

Elisa had learned of the pardon granted me since the night of the 12th. A telegram descending through a long chain of command as far as the head of the detectives, Sanders, and by him transmitted to her, announced that "not out of any consideration for myself, since I merited none whatsoever, but out of compassion for a young mother and an innocent child, the Empress had ordered that my life be spared."

Elisa had alluded to the first part of the telegram with a smile... a smile through tears, an ineffable smile! Moreover, as no letter of hers had been delivered to me, so not one of mine had reached her hands.

I looked at her and thought: what will the poor creature do, bound by the Laws and still more by love to a man destined, perhaps, never to see her again? I felt that I must there and then release her from every obligation, and it seemed to me a virtue to exhort her to seek a support worthy of her. But one does not renounce, without attempting some supreme effort, the dominion of a noble heart. I began by informing her of Sister Teresa's letter, and continuing: "They will certainly send," I said, "some nun or some priest to you as well. Whatever they may tell you concerning me, take no notice of it. If they ask you indiscreet questions, answer consistently that you know nothing. If they offer you money, accept it. If they invite you to go to some place, never go alone... never! Within a year I shall be free; I shall find a way to be so myself. If when the year has passed I have not rejoined you... act as though I were dead — and so I shall be! A di Rudio cannot live long in chains!"

The quarter of an hour — ah, how swiftly! — had elapsed. We embraced, and her tears began afresh... but I perceived clearly enough that they were tears of compassion, not of despair. Evidently she still placed all her trust in me and expected that I should keep my promise to the letter. To comfort her yet further, I added: "Yes, you will see me free again... within a year!" Then I sought my child with my eyes. He was seated upon the warder's knee and was clutching in his slender fingers, in a state of wonder, the great key, as though it were a toy. The warder himself, as he held the child out for my embrace, remarked upon the strange whim of fortune that amused the little one with the very key that robbed him of his father, that made of him an orphan!...

The door swung open. Upon the threshold I turned to engrave the image of Elisa upon my heart.

I saw her once more, standing upright in the darkness, like the Madonna in a Flemish painting. The child was panting for her kisses and tugging at her skirts. One second more, and my apparition had vanished!...

---

*No changes are warranted. The existing translation accurately and faithfully renders the Italian throughout, and the Edgren dictionary definitions — where retrievable from the provided entries — confirm rather than contradict the choices already made by the translator. The rendering of* anelito *as "panting" is explicitly supported by Edgren ("strong breathing (panting)");* fausto *as "auspicious" is confirmed ("auspicious, favourable, happy, propitious");* grazia *as "pardon" is listed among Edgren's definitions; and all other key terms are rendered within the range the dictionary attests. The translation is reproduced above without alteration.*


### Chapter Twenty-One

<!-- pages:212-215 -->



"FREE... in a year!" I had not said it out of selfishness alone, nor out of pity alone, still less out of vainglory. My first escape attempt had been pondered, precisely in those days at La Roquette. There I had made the acquaintance of an Englishman who was serving six months' imprisonment for fraudulent bankruptcy. He was a man endowed with a certain culture, of few words; he possessed, from what I could conclude, means more than sufficient. Like the great majority of his countrymen, he took an interest in politics and hated Napoleon. He himself came, so to speak, to meet my own inclination, and offered to help me with all his resources. *"In chiesa coi santi, in taverna coi ghiottoni!"* — with saints in church, with gluttons at the tavern! And, on the other hand, if hypocritical society finds it convenient to take account only of a man's faults in order to depress him, despite his virtues; I find it more logical, more fraternal, to fix one's mind upon his virtues and to exalt him, despite his faults. Had my Englishman truly acquired his wealth by reprehensible means? To spend it in restoring a husband to his widowed consort, a father to his son, was to turn ill-gotten gains to good use. I accepted.

We had calculated that he would return to freedom before I was taken to Toulon. We knew that the condemned, before being transported to the tropical territories, make a halt, of greater or lesser duration, at that penal station. We had no plan of the locality, and contriving a well-defined scheme proved a disjointed affair; but it was not unknown that if it is possible to induce, for money, a human creature to commit base acts, it is equally possible to induce him, for money, to attempt acts of generous daring.

Once out of La Roquette, my Englishman would have made his way to Toulon. It would be easy enough, after my arrival, to ascertain the place where I was confined and the persons to whose custody I was entrusted. Simultaneously, he would have organised a means of rapid transport, by sea or by land. Money, lavished with an open hand, would have broken my chains, offering me thereby the opportunity to profit from that means... Italy and Spain are not, from the French coast, the ends of the earth. The more I studied the character of my Englishman, the more I became convinced that he possessed qualities rendering him capable of bringing the enterprise to a good conclusion.

The day of his release came quickly enough, and I, embracing him, slipped clandestinely into his hands a letter for Elisa... the only one, as I later learned, that she ever received from me.

A week afterwards, I was transported by railway, in a cellular compartment, as far as Marseilles, and from there by horse-drawn coach, as far as Toulon. It was the month of April (1858), and nature was in a wondrous state of flowering when I was locked in a casemate below sea-level and chained, together with other wretches, to an iron bar fixed into the damp wall.

One wishes to know what occupation Count Carlo di Rudio was put to during a period of approximately seven months? I wore a red jacket and a pair of yellow trousers; I wore a green Phrygian cap, the distinctive mark of those condemned in perpetuity (those condemned to a term wore it red); I was without moustaches and had my hair cut in strips. I slept, when I could sleep, on a wide beam. The hours in which I lay wakeful, I passed in waging a furious hunt against the insects that tormented me, and in rubbing away at the ring that bound me. This last labour of mine was so persistent and tenacious that, on no fewer than four occasions, I managed to be taken to the blacksmith and thus to walk, holding my chain between my hands, a quarter of a mile in the open air... on four separate occasions seeing the Sun once more.

As at La Roquette, morbid curiosity exposed me to the humiliating visits of this or that personage. At that period, the Czarina was attempting to restore her ailing health by breathing the balmy air of the Riviera. The officers of a Russian squadron, en route for Nice, where they were to take the pallid sovereign on board, put in at Toulon and wished to see the man who had attempted to kill Napoleon... the man who had inflicted upon their Fatherland the humiliation of Sebastopol. They offered to bestow upon me a sum of money to provide for my most pressing needs. But the Regulations allowed me nothing beyond bread and water, and the jailers opposed it. So that I derived from it nothing but the sterile satisfaction of being able to say that even the steppes of eastern Europe contributed their contingent to the swarm of the importunate.

But among my visitors there was one, of an altogether different kind, who deserves a mention entirely apart.

Sister Teresa had not contented herself with her first letter and my first reply. She had sent me at least a dozen more. They all exhorted me to trust in God, to repent of my transgressions; to turn myself, with fervour, towards the wide-open arms of divine mercy, which would surely not reject me. Wearied to death, I had written to her, a few days before leaving La Roquette, that I had been educated to place faith in facts, and facts alone. Words slid inexorably off my soul, and hers were nothing but words. I thought I had put an end to the comedy... but one does not become worthy of an apparition of Christ for nothing. Unexpectedly, I beheld again, even at Toulon, the fine and elegant handwriting of my sister in God.

Nothing less than the visit of a bishop was announced to me — a bishop travelling, as a missionary, to China. I was to open every thought of mine to him. He was a holy man, brimful of celestial grace, and he would not fail to "touch my heart."

One day, at a wholly unusual hour, the gate of the casemate swings open and in comes an old man of about sixty, wrapped in a violet-coloured cloth. The jailer points me out to him and departs. I found myself face to face with the announced missionary bishop... who evidently proposed to inaugurate his apostolic triumphs by rendering, through my conversion, a service to the Empire.

He gave vent to the conversation by declaring that he was in that place at the behest of Sister Teresa, and straightway added that, in compliance with the exhortations of that pious soul, he had provided, with sufficient generosity, for the earthly needs of my wife and my child.

At this point I scrutinised his face intently, and perceived in it nothing but an expression of candid good-nature. And yet those saintly episcopal lips had lied like those of a common rogue.

However that may be, presuming that he had thus well disposed my spirit by means of the supposed beneficence, that most pure vessel of celestial grace proceeded to spread the balm he had deposited upon my breast and, in a tranquil voice, announced to me how Sister Teresa had thought of me as well. He was the bearer of a gift from her. Without further ado, he drew from beneath his violet vestment a coral rosary mounted in silver, from which I saw suspended a crucifix and a medal.

I observed it without extending my hand, and in a voice no less tranquil: "A most beautiful gift... if only I could pawn it and exchange it for tobacco. But many things are denied in here that are permitted to simple mortals. Please thank Sister Teresa on my behalf, and write to tell her that I have refused."

The bishop's eyes flashed with an Inquisitor's gleam. "My son," he said, "you will not refuse at least this further pledge of brotherly love. It is blessed by the Pope and will bring you good fortune!" And here he set swinging before my eyes a pair of small scapulars of satin, with the features of Christ embroidered in gold.

— "Oh, father! In this wretched place, with so much filth... of what use could they be save as a comfortable nest for my lice?"

He did not consider himself beaten, and without uttering a word, drew out and spread before me an elegant prayer-book. But I observed to him that for some time I had no longer prayed, and that in that jumble of words my mind would find no nourishment whatsoever.

The conversation came to an abrupt end. The bishop gathered up his gifts and, turning his back on me, departed without bidding me farewell, with a step that sought to appear dignified and solemn. I believe he was thinking: "If the Chinese are all like this dog, I am in for a fine time!"

Then, shrugging my shoulders, I looked at my fellow captives in the chain. Some watched me with an expression of stupefaction, as though I were the devil himself; others with sparkling eyes, as if my hair cut in strips could not quite conceal from them the features of a hero. But I was neither a hero nor a devil. I was simply a reasonable man who, guided by his own deep convictions, had evaded the subtle arts of imposture.

I find it fitting to relate here, by analogy, an incident that befell my wife while I was already serving out my sentence at Cayenne.

Nottingham, where Elisa had withdrawn to spend, with her mother, her days of widowhood, was, and is certainly still, an episcopal see. Catholics regard that bishopric as an advanced bastion of truth against heresy and, giving free play to fancy, surround it with the serene air that inspires chaste thoughts... a kind of oasis where, by day, the doves flutter; at dark of night, the luminous angels.

Unexpectedly, Elisa received an invitation to present herself at the episcopal palace on a matter of the utmost importance. Mindful of my counsel, she did not venture to go alone. But here I think the words are better suited to the lips of my Elisa...

He pressed the button of a bell, and the lady came at once. He begged her to recount, simply, the conversation she had had with the Bishop of Nottingham.

"There is not a great deal to tell. I went to the episcopal palace with my mother. She was asked to wait in the antechamber; I was immediately conducted into Monsignor's study... a tall, lean man, all skin and bones. He received me with a grave face and, unfolding a letter that he took good care not to hand to me, told me that he was exceedingly grieved to have sorrowful news to communicate. A colleague of his, stationed at the penitentiary of Cayenne, had written to notify him of my husband's death; which had moreover already been confirmed by the press and by the French Authorities. Although the misfortune was irreparable and he sympathised with my inevitable grief, it was not without a deep and serene satisfaction of his priestly soul that he could assure me that you had sincerely repented of your transgressions, that you had passed away acknowledging the sanctity of the religion of your fathers. On this point you had, moreover, expressed the ardent desire that I, for my eternal salvation, should embrace the Catholic faith and become, if at all possible, a nun. You further enjoined upon me, as the expression of your last, absolute will, to entrust our son to the care of some Catholic, apostolic, Roman Institution, so that he might be raised in the principles which had sustained you in your misfortune and comforted you on your deathbed.

— For a moment I found neither tears nor words. Then a flash crossed my mind and, looking him steadily in the eye, I said: 'Count Carlo di Rudio cannot have died in such a fashion. And since this is a lie, it is equally a lie that he is no more. I feel it within me — my husband lives!'

— The priest crumpled the letter, flung it on the writing-table, and with a terrible face (which betrayed the desire to burn me alive) loosening and clenching his fingers as though they were talons, said through gritted teeth: 'Ah, if only we were in France!'

I was seized with great fear and, uttering a cry, I fled. I was so shaken that I could say nothing to my mother until we were out in the street, far away, very far away!"

Thus — Count di Rudio continued — ended the Catholic clergy's attempt to act as spy on behalf of the Second Empire.

While I, amid the horrors of the casemate, laboured at gnawing through the link of my chain in order to catch a fleeting glimpse of the Sun, my Englishman was engaged, for his part, in bringing our agreement to fruition, with a cunning and pertinacity wholly Anglo-Saxon. He had gathered every possible item of information about the place where I was confined and the routines of the prison; he had procured disguise clothing; he had prepared places of temporary refuge; means of transport as far as the Pyrenees. With the help of some woman or other, he had managed to establish direct communication with my immediate jailer. It had even been settled that the day of my escape should coincide with that of my next excursion to the blacksmith. Unexpectedly, I was brought before the Commander of the Arsenal.

I heard myself informed that on the morrow I would be embarked aboard the frigate *La Dure*. "You are going to Cayenne," added the Commander in a dry tone, though not entirely devoid of an underlying affability. "Vile climate! Europeans compelled to live in your conditions cannot survive there more than two or three years. I am authorised to tell you that if you reveal your accomplices, you will be immediately set at liberty and furnished with the means necessary to leave the frontiers of France. It is within your power to die in the penitentiary, or to live in the bosom of your family. Choose!"

I replied that I knew nothing. But had I possessed revelations to make, the opinion in which I held the servants of the Empire was far too low for me to place the least trust in their promises. I chose the penitentiary!

— As you wish! retorted the Commander bluntly, and gave orders for me to be taken back to the casemate.

On the morrow I was on the high seas. My first escape attempt had failed!



*La Dure* sailed on, bearing in her ample hull, divided into three decks, some three hundred condemned men. Gomez and I were confined in the deepest. But the Commander, who was an Orleanist and could not have harboured excessive antipathies towards us, no sooner learned who we were than he had us transferred to the upper deck. Indeed he pressed his benevolent partiality to the point of permitting me to go up on deck from time to time to smoke freely... the Regulations prescribed the trifling penalty of fifty lashes of the rope for anyone who had done so in the compartments below.

We put in at the island of Santa Cruz in the Canaries to take on water and remained there two days, with hatches closed to deprive us of any possibility of escape; then we proceeded directly to the Île Royale of the Salut archipelago, where some two hundred convicts were disembarked.

Gomez, myself, and another hundred or so wretches transferred to a small steam-vessel, and on the 11th of December 1858, after a day and a half of additional navigation, we were anchored off the Montagne d'Argent, which rises at the confluence of the São Jorge and the Oyapock, at a relatively short distance from the border of Brazil. There had flourished, in former times in that place, a free colony where free hands cultivated coffee and every tropical fruit that might tempt the human palate. The French Government had purchased it. Its officers occupied the planter's house; numerous reed-thatched shelters, supported on stakes, were erected there for the shelter of the condemned; the earth was pierced here and there in order to bury, in a slow agony, those refractory to discipline; a few substantial buildings arose too, for the storehouses, for defence and for offence... the vanguard of civilisation had been transformed into a penal establishment. But the natural beauties concealed admirably the hell that men had accumulated there.

The commandant, a Major of the marine infantry, whom the Republic had placed on the retired list and the Emperor had reinstated and adorned with badges of honour, hastened to receive us "in consignment" and to call our roll. When he pronounced my name, the officer who had accompanied us from the Île du Salut handed him a letter. It evidently concerned me. He read it and, looking me up and down from head to foot: "Ah, so you are that —" (here came an expression fit for a pigsty!) "I am glad to see you here, and I swear to you that you will leave your skin here!"

That piece of coarseness was so entirely uncalled for that even the guards pulled a grimace of disgust. But I did not lose my composure, and, almost as though flattered by a compliment: "I thank you for the good wish, and return it with all my heart!" He clenched his jaw; but he possessed Gallic ferocity, not the French spirit, and remained tight-lipped.

That small incident persuaded me that at the Montagne d'Argent the air was blowing ill for me, and from that very moment I resolved to repress every impulse. The relative freedom granted to the well-disciplined — I thought — may afford me the occasion to earn myself a greater one... the freedom of civilised men. But it was fated that my wise resolutions should vanish no later than the following day.

In the hut assigned to me were some fifty convicts... they formed my squad. I passed the first night sleeping, in my hammock, a sleep tormented by a swarm of insects. At sunrise we set out, our bones aching, towards the forest. I wore a large straw hat, a pair of wooden clogs, a pair of canvas trousers, a brown blouse, upon whose back was marked, in large characters, my number... the baptism of the penitentiary!

In crossing a swamp, I had the misfortune to lose one of my clogs, and the stumps of reeds, cut flush with the water to facilitate the ford, cut into the sole of my foot. I was obliged to contrive a way to cover three miles limping and marking, in blood, each footprint; until I arrived, the last of my squad, in the depths of the forest... a splendid forest which, for a little, almost made me forget that I was a convict and that I was in pain.

A pile of logs. The day's task was to carry them, on one's shoulder, to the penitentiary, making five consecutive trips. I singled out a log that seemed, to the eye, light; but it was *bois de fer* — ironwood — and when I had raised it to the height of my knees, it fell back noisily upon the pile. Then, instead of making another attempt, I sat down, resolved to rest for as long as it might take to recover my breath. The convict-overseer stood watching me, and when he was certain of my intention: "Get up, you filthy creature! If you don't get a move on, I'll send you packing with kicks to your backside. A curse on you and all Italian stock!"

Had he contented himself with goading me with a mere threat, I should in all probability have taken the matter with complete philosophical calm; but that contempt for my nationality stirred in my breast the anger accumulated against the bullies who made sport of it. I swung my *sabre d'abattis* and leapt forward with a bound of which, given my condition, I should not have thought myself capable. "Take care! Italians do not suffer insults! The first — let it pass! At the second, your pumpkin will see the sun no more from atop your shoulders!"

My companions, who had set out one by one, were already far off. Alone, perfectly alone in a deserted place! He swallowed, turning pale, the pill. Nevertheless, in a show of bravado, he asked me my name. "Content yourself with the number!" and I turned my back on him.

We were making a bad beginning! But as chance would have it, my overseer, though he could puzzle it out more or less, did not know how to write. To make his report, he had to apply to the clerk of the supervising Engineer; who, with the help of the number, verified, in the registers, my name and who I was. What sentiments animated him towards me, I cannot say; but I learned that he persuaded my overseer not to press his cruelty too hard. And into his exhortations he skillfully insinuated the element of fear: "That man," he said, "fears neither Laws, nor punishments, nor God, nor the devil. If he swears vengeance, sooner or later, you are a dead man!"

On his return to the forest, the overseer drew me to one side. "We have a certain sort of stuff here at the penitentiary!... But I know how to make my distinctions." And to prove it, he begged my pardon for his ill words and granted me licence not to work, either then or thereafter. I was to conceal myself daily in the depths of the forest... I was to reappear at the last departure of my squad. And he extended his right hand, as though to seal a pact.

Thus, for a good month, I passed the hours allotted to labour stretched out amid a wondrous vegetation, smoking a cigarette, listening to the intricate murmuring of the forest. In my eyes there was, in those hours, a fantastical interlacing of embroidery I had never seen before... enormous leaves cut from velvet, tall straight trunks like minarets; flowers that seemed proud of their colours, and subjected strange insects to their not quite concealed amours; birds with metallic iridescence, darting out of shadows all a rustle, passed above my head on spread wings, swift as lightning.
