There were caverns of a deep green, full of mystery; there were majestic vaults, dripping dew and fragrance, through which the Sun gazed placidly, lordly, as though keeping watch that every blade of grass had what it needed... the Sun which, worshipped consciously or unconsciously in all ages by all peoples, had with secular energy drawn into itself that temple, worthy of a God. Oh how beautiful is the Earth when it is not defaced by the cupidity of man!... A temple worthy of Elisa, too!... Her image, intensely evoked, held dominion over me as though she were present.

It was in that forest, and in those hours, that I pondered my second escape attempt. The plan to which I finally resolved was suggested to me by the primitive art of the Indians ranging the southern regions of Guiana: to fell a great tree, to hollow it out so as to fashion a serviceable dug-out canoe. With it I would have ascended, by night, the Oyapock for a stretch of fifty miles, and then crossed the São Jorge, to make landfall on Brazilian soil. Having escaped the pursuit of the French Authorities, I should then have been obliged to face, in all probability, the ferocity of savages, and beyond any doubt the nearly insuperable obstacles of unexplored regions and of the climate. But the prize was worth the risk.

None of this could even be begun without the help of several accomplices. I sounded the spirit of Antonio Gomez. But the ardent political conspirator, satisfied with his position as assistant canteen-keeper and probably dreading the worst, feigned not to understand me. I was obliged to have recourse to less noble material. By what criteria was I to choose it? How was I to acquire over it the ascendancy necessary to bind it to my purposes?

In this case too, my good star, more than my own wisdom, came to my aid.

The penitentiary received its supplies of fresh meat from Brazil. At intervals more or less regular, a sailing vessel would arrive with a cargo of cattle. The shore, which shelved gradually into the ocean, did not afford sufficient depth to permit it to moor close to land, so the anchor was cast offshore, at a distance of some three miles. The overseer would then choose one convict from each squad of ten and form them into a special party which made its laborious way, wading with water above the waist, up to the side of the vessel. The wild bullocks, maddened with rage, were thrown into the sea, and the men drove them, with great shouts and great blows, into a stockade within the penitentiary.

Once that work had begun, it proceeded without interruption until the unloading was complete, skipping, as needs required, even a meal or two. At the Montagne d'Argent, meals were taken by squads of ten. The squad-leader received in charge the provisions, diminished by the sordid speculation of the officers, and set them out, upon a sort of table, in the ten mess-bowls. Each convict ran to his own and devoured it, seated on some step, or on a tree-trunk, or on the bare ground. On days of unloading, it fell to the squad-leader to keep the absent convict's ration and hand it to him on his return.

Having been chosen to lend a hand at that frenzied work, one day I made my way back at eight o'clock in the evening, when my squad was already snoring in its hammocks. Hunger, sharpened by incessant exertion, by the prolonged bath, by the keen sea breeze, had become ferocious. I shake the squad-leader, and cause a half-commotion, and he, showing me his fist instead of the mess-bowl: "*Tais-toi, Italien de m...*" (the word of Cambronne).

I chew on nothing and hold my tongue. On the evening of the following day, I take my place in the rank with the squad and wait around the table. When the squad-leader arrives, holding up the pot brimming with soup, I lunge at it and give it a mighty kick. The leader stands there dumbfounded, arms outstretched, hands empty. "It takes," I say, "nine Frenchmen to make an Italian go hungry, but a single Italian is enough to make nine Frenchmen go hungry!"

Now it is the others who raise the commotion. The overseer, a certain Cosson, comes running, and having been informed of the matter, asks me curtly: "Why, instead of taking your revenge, did you not file your report?"

— "Because Italians do not inform."

Cosson, stealing Salomon's trade, condemned both the squad-leader and Count Carlo di Rudio to fifty lashes of the rope. But my resoluteness and, above all, my fierce reply, had instantly raised me in the estimation of those wretches. They surrounded the overseer and spoke to such effect that he consented to swallow his sentence. From that day, the convicts condescended, in their goodness, to treat me fraternally as one worthy of their fellowship; from that day I found it a relatively easy undertaking to study the various temperaments, to select eight individuals sufficiently enamoured of freedom to face cheerfully, in the attempt to regain it, whatever danger might arise.

We left our hut by night, crawling out like beasts emerging from their lair, in order to deceive any eyes that might still be awake. We proceeded by separate paths to a pre-arranged spot on the edge of the forest; then, united, we pressed on into its labyrinths. From the very first night, we had marked our route with signs nearly invisible to other mortals, averting in that way the danger of losing ourselves.

A tree of gigantic proportions was felled at its base with comparatively little effort; but in falling it caught in the dense undergrowth and remained propped upon it. We had to climb up and hollow it out while clinging to it... a complication that consumed precious time. We finally resolved to have recourse to fire. The light could not be seen from the penitentiary, and the smoke was rendered invisible by the night; but amid so much resinous vegetation, amid so many dead shrubs, amid so much kindling, amid the debris accumulated over centuries, the operation demanded great circumspection, lest one were to feed a conflagration so swift and so vast that even the tropical deluges would have found difficulty in mastering it.

That nocturnal enterprise of ours constitutes, for me, one of those incidents whose impression only death can efface. We were not in that land as pioneers of civilisation, and someone might even remark, not without irony, that we were not of the stuff of missionaries. But the setting — oh the grandeur of that setting, rendered ineffable by the silent night — how it penetrated, together with its own, into our very being, and how it transformed us! All about us, there was nothing that had not been born and grown in the most boundless freedom, and freedom became almost palpable in every atom of air we breathed. It seemed to me that my labouring muscles, in their very exertion, exhaled from every fibre something luminous, as though giving back to things the unstained life that things lent to us. There were moments in which I could have sworn I was as vigorous and eternal as the virgin forest. And we became so thoroughly a homogeneous part of the setting that, after the first few nights, numberless troops of monkeys, drawn by the glow of the resin, came to contemplate and almost to encourage, brightening it with their presence, our work.

After a couple of months, or a little more, we succeeded in dragging into a bend in the river, and in concealing among the lianas, a canoe seven feet wide and twenty-five feet long. It floated admirably. "And now," I exclaimed, when we parted full of enthusiasm, "to the oars and the sail!"

Unexpectedly, an event to which, at its first manifestation, no one had paid serious attention, threw the entire penal colony into disorder, and with it every hope of mine.

In broad daylight, beneath a cloudless sky that is a whole lyric poem in itself, with a sun capable of hard-boiling ostrich eggs in its direct rays... a sentry is seized with violent trembling. Relieved as quickly as possible, he was seen to make his way with slow steps, still trembling, to the antediluvian hut that bore the pompous name of "cabinet of decency." He emerged from it ashen, undone. He declared that try as he might, he had been unable to pass a single drop of water. The convicts laughed loudly... the thing seemed to them so comical. By evening, the poor wretch was vomiting clotted blood, black as coal... two days later he was a corpse.

But before he had drawn his last breath, five, seven, ten convicts, while showing at the thermometer a high temperature, were trembling like leaves. The penitentiary had been invaded by yellow fever.

The catastrophe was swift beyond all telling. In those days, it was held that the seat of the disease lay in the intestines; the remedies, scarce in the extreme and wholly inadequate to the vastness of the mortality, were applied, for greater misfortune, on erroneous principles. The Commander gave orders to throw open the warehouses and the cantina and to leave them at the full disposal of the able-bodied. From that cantina, flung wide and black with shadow, there seemed to issue the sensation of fatality. If yellow fever was not crouching in there, it had there some mysterious ally. The convicts were inexorably drawn into it by vice and by fear. They brutified themselves within it and passed from drunkenness to delirium; from delirium to nothingness!... with a death all the more sudden the more robust they were.

The arrangements made for the victims were of the simplest: to cast them, without ceremony, into quicklime.

Each evening, the chief overseer went to make his report at the Commander's house.

One night the Commander lost his wife and two children almost simultaneously, and he ceased to concern himself with my welfare. Not long afterwards, it was the chief overseer who ceased to make his customary report. He had too easily yielded to the temptations of the cantina, and when he was found stretched out on the beaten earth, it was at first suspected that he was simply drunk. But his gums were coated with bloody froth... he died without having recovered either speech or the faintest glimmer of consciousness, and he was one of the many whom I was obliged to seize by the feet. "One, two, three..." Before casting him in, I should have wished to say a *Requiescat* over him!... he had spared me fifty lashes of the rope. But down there in that pit... it would have sounded like irony.

I lent my hands to the burying, one by one, in that fashion, of my eight fellow-conspirators in the escape attempt, and of others, and still others... I counted past a hundred; then I lost count and counted no more. Some wretch who already seemed to have crossed the boundary beyond which one suffers no more, hardly thrown into that horrible common grave, was seen to revive and writhe in agony.

When the fever, finding no more flesh to gnaw upon, at last disappeared, the French Deputies raised in Paris the customary humanitarian clamour. But the Minister answered them, in a tone of triumph, that he had already given the appropriate orders to transfer the entire colony to the Île du Salut. And indeed one evening, standing out against the crepuscular light, we saw the dark trail of smoke from a vessel proceeding at full steam towards our shore. It was the *Abeille*, bringing us His Excellency's orders. On the following day the entire colony was on board... Of more than six hundred — convicts, military men, civil servants, and attendants alike — we were reduced to sixty-three! The greater part of the survivors had already suffered from yellow fever at other times and on other shores; which had, it seems, rendered them resistant, if not absolutely invulnerable; several, shadows of their former selves, showed signs of having been but recently touched, if not cut down, by it... among these, Antonio Gomez... Oh, how transformed was that good-natured priestly face of his! Three alone had not contracted the infected blood, either then or before. Among the three — the convict di Rudio!

From the deck of the *Abeille*, already churning the broken ocean about her bows, I cast a last glance towards the Montagne d'Argent and think, with profound regret, of my canoe floating solitary and motionless beneath the dense lianas. The landscape dwindles rapidly. The hell that men had accumulated there was no more; what remained was one of its most baleful consequences, the vast charnel pit from which we had miraculously escaped. Nature, indifferent, spread over so much horror her enchanting hues, and in the scintillating air vibrated her mysterious amourings.


### Chapter Twenty-Two

<!-- pages:215-222 -->

THE *Durr* sailed on, carrying in her wide belly, divided into three decks, some three hundred condemned men. Gomez and I were confined in the lowest of these. But the Commander, who was an Orleanist and could not have harboured excessive antipathy toward us, no sooner learned who we were than he had us transferred to the upper deck. Nay, he pushed his benevolent partiality so far as to permit me, from time to time, to go up on deck to smoke freely — the Regulations prescribed the trifling matter of fifty strokes of the rope for anyone who did so in the compartments below.

We put in at the island of Santa Cruz (*Car marìe*) to take on water, and remained there two days with the portholes sealed, to deprive us of any possibility of escape; then we proceeded directly to the Île Royale du Salut, where some two hundred convicts were disembarked.

I, Gomez, and another hundred derelicts boarded a small steamer, and on the eleventh of December 1858, after a day and a half of additional sailing, we were anchored before the Montagne d'Argent, which rises at the confluence of the Saint-Georges and the Oyapock, at a comparatively short distance from the Brazilian border. In that place there had once flourished a free colony where free hands had cultivated coffee and every tropical fruit that might tempt the human palate. The French Government had purchased it. Its officers occupied the planter's house; numerous reed shelters, supported by poles, were erected to house the condemned; the earth was pierced here and there to bury within it, in slow agony, those who resisted discipline; a few more substantial buildings also arose for the warehouses, for defence and for offence: the vanguard of civilisation had been transformed into a penal colony. But the natural beauties of the place marvellously dissembled the hell that men had accumulated there.

The commandant, a Major of the marine infantry whom the Republic had placed on half-pay and whom the Emperor had reinstated and decorated with honorary insignia, hastened to receive us "in custody" and to call our roll. When he pronounced my name, the officer who had accompanied us from the Île du Salut handed him a letter. It evidently concerned me. He read it and, looking me up and down: "Ah, so you are that — —" (down came an expression fit for a pigsty!) "I am glad to see you here, and I swear to you that here you will leave your skin!" ...

That coarseness was so entirely uncalled for that even the guards pulled a grimace of disgust. But I did not lose my composure, and, as though flattered by a compliment: "Thank you for the good wish — I return it with all my heart!" He clenched his jaws; but he possessed the Gallic ferocity, not the French wit, and he kept his mouth shut.

That little incident persuaded me that at the Montagne d'Argent the air was, for me, unwholesome, and from that moment I resolved to restrain every impulse. The relative freedom accorded to the disciplined — I reflected — may offer me the means of winning a greater one: the freedom of civilised men. But it was fated that my wise resolutions should vanish no later than the following day.

In the hut assigned to me there were some fifty convicts who formed my work gang. I passed the first night sleeping, in my hammock, a sleep tormented by a swarm of insects. At sunrise we set off, our bones aching, toward the forest. I wore a wide straw hat, a pair of wooden clogs, a pair of canvas trousers, a brown blouse, on the back of which was marked, in large characters, my number: the baptism of the penal colony!

Crossing a swamp, I had the misfortune to lose one of my clogs, and the stumps of the reeds, cut level with the water to ease the ford, lacerated the sole of my foot. I was obliged to make my way over three miles limping and bloodying every footprint, until I arrived, last of my gang, in the depths of the forest — a splendid forest that, for a little while, almost made me forget that I was a convict and forget the pain.

A pile of logs. The day's task was to carry them on one's shoulder to the penitentiary, making five consecutive journeys. I singled out a log that looked light; but it was *bois de fer* — ironwood — and when I had it at knee height, it fell back noisily onto the pile. Then, rather than make another attempt, I sat down, resolved to rest just long enough to recover my strength. The convict-guard stood watching me, and when he was certain of my intention: "Get up, you filth! If you don't get moving, I'll kick you all the way back. A curse on you and the whole Italian breed!"

Had he been content to goad me with a simple threat, I should probably have taken the matter with the greatest philosophy; but that contempt for my nationality stirred in my breast the anger accumulated against all the tyrants who had trampled upon it. I swung my *sabre d'abattis* and gave a leap of which, considering my condition, I should not have thought myself capable. "Take care! Italians do not suffer insults. The first one — let it pass! At the second, that head of yours will no longer see the sun from the top of your shoulders!"

My companions had set off one by one and were already far away. We were alone, perfectly alone, in a deserted place! He swallowed the pill, turning pale. Nevertheless, by way of a show of bravado, he asked my name. "Be content with the number!" and I turned my back on him.

We were making a bad start! But chance would have it that my guard, though he could spell out letters more or less correctly, did not know how to write. To make his report, he had to resort to the clerk of the superintendent engineer, who, guided by the number, verified my name and identity in the registers. What feelings this clerk harboured toward me I do not know; but I learned that he persuaded my guard not to be cruel. And into his exhortations he skillfully inserted the element of fear: "That man," he said, "fears neither Law, nor punishment, nor God, nor the devil. If he swears vengeance, sooner or later you are done for."

On his return to the forest, the guard called me aside. "We have in the penitentiary certain customs!... But I know how to make my distinctions." And to prove it, he begged my pardon for his ill words and granted me leave never to work, then or thereafter. I was to conceal myself each day in the dense forest... I was to reappear at the last departure of my gang. And he extended his right hand, as if to seal a compact.

Thus, for a good month, I passed the hours assigned to labour stretched out amid a prodigious vegetation, smoking a cigarette, listening to the complex murmur of the forest. In those hours my eyes beheld a fantastic interweaving of embroideries seen no more: enormous leaves cut from velvet, trunks tall and straight as minarets; flowers that seemed proud of their own colours pressed strange insects into service for their not-quite-hidden loves; birds with metallic sheen, darting from the shadows all in a rustle, passed above my head on outstretched wings, swift as lightning. There were caverns of a deep green, full of mystery; there were majestic vaults dripping with dew and perfume, through which the Sun gazed down calmly, regally, as if watching over every blade of grass to see that it had its due. The Sun, which all peoples in all ages have worshipped, consciously or unconsciously, had with its centuries-long energy raised up for itself that temple, worthy of a God. Oh, how beautiful is the Earth when it is not defaced by the cupidity of man! ... A temple worthy of Elisa too! ... Her image, intensely evoked, dominated me as though she were present!

It was in that forest and in those hours that I meditated my second attempt at escape. The plan to which I finally fixed myself was suggested by the primitive craft of the Indians who roam the southern regions of Guiana: to fell a great tree, hollow it out and fashion a serviceable pirogue. With it I would have ascended the Oyapock by night for a stretch of fifty miles, then crossed the Saint-Georges, to land on Brazilian soil. Escaped from the pursuit of the French authorities, I should have had to confront, in all likelihood, the ferocity of the savages, and, indubitably, the almost insurmountable obstacles of unexplored regions and of the climate. But the prize was worth the risk!

None of this could even be begun without the help of several accomplices. I sounded the mind of Antonio Gomez. But the ardent political conspirator, content with his position as assistant canteen-keeper and fearing, in all probability, something worse, feigned not to understand me. I would have to resort to less noble material. By what criteria to choose it? How to acquire over it the ascendancy necessary to bind it to my purpose?

In this instance too, my good star, more than my own wisdom, came to my aid.

The penitentiary received its supplies of fresh meat from Brazil. At intervals, more or less regular, a sailing vessel arrived with a cargo of cattle. The beach, which shelved gradually into the ocean, afforded it no depth sufficient to moor close to the shore, so that the anchor was cast at a distance of some three miles out. The guard would then select one convict from each *decuria* — a squad of ten — and form a special team which made its laborious way, with the water above the waist, out toward the vessel. The wild cattle, maddened, were thrown into the sea, and the men drove them, with great shouts and great blows, all the way into a stockade within the penitentiary.

Once that work had begun, it was carried on without interruption until the unloading was complete, skipping a meal or two when necessary. At the Montagne d'Argent, the men ate by *decurie*. The squad-leader received the provisions — already depleted by the sordid speculation of the officers — and set them out, on a sort of table, in the ten mess-tins. Each convict ran to his own and devoured his portion sitting on some step, or on a log, or on the bare earth. On the days of unloading, it was the duty of the squad-leader to guard the ration of the absent convict and deliver it to him on his return.

Having been chosen to lend a hand at that frantic labour, I made my final return one evening at eight o'clock, when my *decuria* was already snoring in its hammocks. Hunger, sharpened by the incessant exertion, the long wading, and the keen sea-breeze, had grown fierce. I shook the squad-leader and raised something of a commotion, and he showed me, instead of the mess-tin, his fist: "*Tais-toi, Italien de m...*" (the word of Cambronne).

I chewed on nothing and held my tongue. The following evening I took my place in the ranks with the *decuria* and waited at the table. When the squad-leader arrived, bearing the pot brimming with soup, I sprang forward and delivered it a mighty kick. The squad-leader stood stupefied, arms outstretched, hands empty. "It takes nine Frenchmen," I said, "to make one Italian go hungry; but one Italian alone is enough to make nine Frenchmen go hungry!"

Now it was the others who raised the commotion. The guard, a certain Cosson, came running, and having inquired into the matter, demanded of me curtly: "Why, instead of taking your revenge, did you not submit your report?"

— Because Italians do not turn informer!

Cosson, stealing Salomon's trade, sentenced to fifty strokes of the rope both the squad-leader and Count Carlo di Rudio. But my resolution and, above all, my proud reply had raised me suddenly in the estimation of those wretches. They gathered round the guard and pleaded so earnestly that he consented to swallow his sentence. From that day, the convicts condescended, in their generosity, to treat me as a brother, as one worthy of their brotherhood; from that day I found it a comparatively easy undertaking to study the various temperaments, and to select eight individuals sufficiently enamoured of liberty to face cheerfully, in the attempt to recover it, whatever danger might arise.

We left our hut by night, creeping like beasts from their lair, so as to deceive any eyes that might still be open. We proceeded by different routes to an agreed place on the edge of the forest; then, united, we pushed on into its labyrinths. From the very first night we had marked our path with signs practically invisible to other mortals, thus averting the danger of losing our way.

A tree of gigantic proportions was felled at the base with comparatively little effort; but in falling, it struck against the dense growth and lodged there, supported. It was necessary to clamber up and hollow it out while clinging to it — a complication that consumed precious time. We finally decided to resort to fire. The light could not be seen from the penitentiary, and the smoke was rendered invisible by the night; but amid so much resinous vegetation, so many dead shrubs, so many dried canes and so much debris accumulated over centuries, the operation demanded the greatest vigilance, or one would have fed a conflagration so swift and so vast that even the tropical downpours could scarcely have subdued it.

That nocturnal enterprise of ours constituted, for me, one of those incidents whose impression only death can efface. We were not in that land as pioneers of civilisation, and someone might even observe, not without irony, that we were not the stuff of which missionaries are made. But the surroundings — oh, the grandeur of those surroundings, made ineffable by the silent night, how deeply it penetrated, together with its own essence, into ours, and how greatly it transformed us! All around us, there was nothing that had not been born and grown in the most boundless freedom, and freedom became almost tangible in every atom of air that we breathed. It seemed to me that my muscles, as they toiled, exhaled from their every fibre something luminous, as though they were rendering back to things the uncontaminated life that things were lending us. There were moments in which I could have sworn I was as vigorous and inward as the virgin forest. And we became so homogeneous a part of our surroundings that, after the first nights, numberless troops of monkeys, attracted by the glow of the resin, came to observe and almost to encourage, lending it their own gaiety, our work.

After two months or a little more, we succeeded in dragging to a bend in the river, and concealing among the lianas, a canoe seven feet wide and twenty-five feet long. It floated admirably. "And now," I exclaimed, when we parted full of enthusiasm, "to the oars and the sail!"

Then, unexpectedly, an event to which, at its first manifestation, no one paid serious attention, threw the entire penal colony into disorder, and with it every hope of mine.

In broad daylight, beneath a clear sky that is itself a lyric, with a sun capable of hard-boiling an ostrich egg in its direct rays, a sentry was seized by violent trembling. Relieved as quickly as possible, he was seen to make his way with slow steps, shaking, to the antediluvian hut that bore the pompous name of "Cabinet of decency." He emerged from it ashen and distraught. He declared that, struggle as he might, he had not been able to pass a single drop of water. The convicts laughed loudly — the thing seemed to them so comical. By evening, the poor wretch was bringing up clotted blood, as black as charcoal. Two days later he was a corpse.

But before he had even drawn his last breath, five, seven, ten convicts, while showing a high temperature on the thermometer, were shivering like leaves. The penitentiary had been invaded by yellow fever.

The catastrophe was rapid beyond all description. In those days, it was believed that the seat of the disease lay in the intestines; the remedies, extremely few and wholly inadequate to the scale of the mortality, were, to make matters worse, administered on erroneous principles. The Commandant gave orders for the warehouses and the canteen to be thrown open and left at the full disposal of the healthy. From that canteen, wide open and black within, there seemed to me to emanate the sensation of fatality. If yellow fever was not crouching inside it, it had some mysterious ally there. The convicts were drawn to it inexorably by vice and by fear. They turned themselves into beasts and passed from drunkenness to delirium; from delirium to nothingness! — with a death all the swifter, the more robust they were.

The arrangements for the victims were of the simplest: to cast them, without ceremony, into quicklime.

Every evening, the head guard went to make his report at the Commandant's house.

— How many today?

— Twenty, thirty, forty! ...

— And the convict Rudio?

— Healthy as a fish!

One night the Commandant lost, almost at the same moment, his wife and two sons, and he ceased to concern himself with my health. Not much later, it was the head guard who ceased to appear at the usual report. He had yielded too readily to the temptations of the canteen, and when he was found stretched out on the beaten earth, the first suspicion was simply that he was drunk. But his gums were covered in bloody foam. He died without having recovered either speech or a glimmer of consciousness, and was one of the many whom I was obliged to seize by the feet: "One, two, three ..." Before casting him down I should have wished to say a *Requiescat* — he had spared me fifty strokes of the rope. But down there in that pit it would have seemed ...

I lent my hands to burying, one by one in that fashion, my eight accomplices in the escape attempt, and others, and others still. I counted past a hundred; then I lost count and counted no more. Some wretch who already seemed to have crossed the threshold beyond which one suffers no more, barely thrown into that horrible common grave, was seen to revive and writhe with agony!

When the fever, finding no more flesh to gnaw, disappeared, the French Deputies raised in Paris the usual humanitarian clamour. But the Minister replied to them, with an air of triumph, that he had already issued the appropriate orders for the transfer of the entire Colony to the Île du Salut. And indeed one evening we saw outlined against the twilight the black strip of smoke from a vessel proceeding at full steam toward our shore. It was the *Abeille*, bringing us the orders of His Excellency. The following day the entire Colony was on board. Of more than six hundred — convicts, military personnel, civilian employees, and servants — we were reduced to sixty-three! The greater part of the survivors had previously suffered yellow fever in other times and on other shores, which had apparently rendered them, if not absolutely invulnerable, at least resistant; many, shadows of their former selves, showed signs of having been touched by it then and there, if not quite carried off — among these, Antonio Gomez. Oh, how greatly transformed was that good-natured priest's face of his! Three alone had not contracted it, then or before, the infected blood. Among the three, the convict Rudio!

From the deck of the *Abeille*, around whose hull the ocean was already foaming and breaking, I cast a last glance at the Montagne d'Argent and thought, with profound regret, of my canoe floating solitary and motionless beneath the dense lianas. The landscape shrank swiftly. The hell that men had accumulated there was no more; what remained was one of its most baleful consequences, the vast charnel-house from which we had miraculously escaped. Nature, indifferent, spread over so much horror its enchanting hues, and in the scintillating air its mysterious courtships vibrated on!


### Chapter Twenty-Three

<!-- pages:222-225 -->





I HAD scarcely flung my canvas sack upon the quay of the Island of Salvation when hope flooded my breast — intense, overpowering.

The island, with a little play of imagination, presented the shape of a figure 8. Upon the larger loop, which was also the higher, stood the dwellings, the hospital, a church; upon the smaller loop were the workshops, and at its outermost curve, the barracks. From that extremity, facing more or less exactly to the northwest, one could toss a stone by hand onto the long islet of the Devil — the very same that was later to become grimly celebrated for the squalid drama of which Captain Dreyfus was the victim. At low tide, it was not difficult, by leaping from rock to exposed rock, to pass from one island to the other with dry feet.

To the east, another small islet rose from the waters, called San Giuseppe — a rough, nearly circular plain, scattered with a few stunted and dwarfish trees.

The island upon which I found myself measured, by rough estimate and along its greatest diameter, an extent of some two miles. Its shores were rocky and full of inlets.

These were circumstances I had noted in passing. What succeeded in capturing my attention and fixing it so as to plunge me into deep reflection was a small rowing bark running over the waves at no great distance from the shore. It carried four or five convicts aboard, who were rowing and singing. I thought to myself: "What is there to prevent these men from making for the open sea? True, one does not cross the Ocean by oar alone. But why do they not procure themselves a sail? I left a canoe in the waters of the Oyapock, and here I find another at the Island of Salvation. Courage, di Rudio — you have lost nothing!"

I was still standing motionless, my eye fixed upon that nutshell of a craft, when I felt a touch upon my shoulder. I turned with a start and found myself face to face with a man wearing the grotesque uniform of a convict.

I studied him, and he broke the silence:

— I have been told you are Italian... are you not?

— And who are you? By your accent you seem a Roman of Rome.

— I am *Trasteverino*, and proud of it!

— Why are you here?

— That is another pair of sleeves entirely. But may I never see my country again and die with the noose about my throat if, given the chance, I would not do it all over again. A French dog seduced my sister and left her in a fix... I put a hole through his heart. Long live Trastevere blood, and to hell with whoever speaks ill of it! For this I lost my Rome... ten years on my back!... but they will pass. My name is Morbioli!

We exchanged, so to speak, our calling cards; then, all at once:

— How long have you been here?

— Seven years!

— Seven years!... You are Italian, Roman, *Trasteverino*, and you have not attempted to escape?

— Impossible! Alone, not even to think of it. With companions, worse than going out at night! In this accursed place, it is not the musket-shots that are granted to cowards who turn informer — it is clemency! Do you understand me? Denouncing a plot leads to freedom more surely than attempting flight.

— And what of it? One can see you are a newcomer to this place and have no precise notion of the difficulties. Ah, do you think there have been no men with the mettle to risk it? But to what end? Listen!...

And he told me that thirteen years before, thirteen convicts from Île Royale — another French penitentiary situated in those parts — had seized a pilot-boat and made for the open sea. The *Abeille* gave chase, but in vain. Some three months passed and men were already beginning to think they had reached free land, when, on one of her crossings from Cayenne to the Island of Salvation, the *Abeille* sighted a boat adrift. She came alongside — and who was found aboard?

— Three men only. Those three had devoured the other ten.

A flight had also been attempted overland, by way of the Montagne d'Argent. There were twenty of them, and once they had penetrated the forest, no one troubled to pursue them further. After several months, a man gone wild made an unexpected appearance at the penitentiary. He was the sole survivor... he confessed that he had kept himself alive up to that point by having, in one degree or another, stripped the flesh from the other nineteen. Having by then nothing left to set his teeth into, he resolved to give himself up. They ruled that he had eaten more than enough, and... cut off his head.

A flight had also been attempted from the islet of the Devil. The brother of Giacomo Pianori — precursor of Felice Orsini in both the attempt and the martyrdom — had been confined there as a suspect. He had observed that the tide cast numerous tree-trunks upon the shore. He gathered them up and managed to put together a raft. The sinuosities of the beach had lent themselves to the secret labour. He set out by night, with twenty companions. On the open sea, they were overtaken by a squall. The waves broke the raft in two. One part drifted long upon the currents until it ran aground somewhere in Dutch Guiana. The fugitives were given aid, and... handed back to the French authorities. Chained in the pits, exposed to the torrid sun and the torrential rains, they soon breathed their last amid their sufferings. Twelve corpses thrown to the sharks!

The other eight, Pianori among them, also reached the Continent upon the remaining part of the raft; but unfortunately they ran upon a *vase*. Do you know what a *vase* is? A bank of bluish mud that grips like cement. The air is thick with *mosquitos* capable of stripping the carcass of a whale to bare bone within a few days; the earth swarms with crabs — the terrible crabs of Guiana, which burrow long tunnels underground, invade the cemeteries, and... fortunately for them, the corpses sleep sound. The *Abeille*, passing through those parts, found the raft and what little remained of Pianori and the other wretches. The crabs and the insects had not even had the consideration to wait until they were dead, and had most certainly made a feast of them then and there.

I had listened without interrupting. Meanwhile the nutshell of a craft had drawn nearer to the island and, skirting its shore, disappeared now and then behind some rock, only to reappear ever closer. The convicts were gathering on the beach and exchanging shouts with the oarsmen. I asked: What is that boat, and what are those men doing?

— They fish on account of the penitentiary, and when the catch is plentiful, they sell, more or less openly, to anyone who wishes to buy.

He scratched his head and added: "Morbioli is no empty gourd. You are thinking that one might make use of that boat to attempt something new. Do your sums without our four gunboats. They would draw a bead on you merrily, as at a shoot. They would laugh to see you go under. And then there is the *Abeille*..."

I was assigned to the anchor workshop. I laboured half-naked — a heat that shrivelled the skin; the sparks leaping from the anvil spotted my body all over. Yet never, as in those days, did I understand the power of the intellect to resist painful sensations and to master them. The stubborn meditation upon a plan of escape transported me elsewhere — among the rocks of the island, upon the open sea — and the impressions of the workshop reached me as though softened by distance.

My plan was taking shape from the very history of the failures of my predecessors, and it seemed to me that its concatenation issued from my mind harder and more resistant than the iron I was hammering.

Pianori had rushed headlong into his dreadful fate by trusting to a raft — *ergo*, no raft! And why, besides, should I undertake the slow and thankless labour of building one, when I had ready to hand a small vessel lacking only a scrap of sail? I calculated that seizing her by a bold stroke need not prove an excessively arduous undertaking for men of resolute temper, the more so since the fishermen would without doubt submit with good grace to a compulsion that would restore them to liberty.

True it was that being aboard a vessel had profited the other fugitives not at all; but evidently they had known nothing of her handling and had been short of provisions. It was therefore necessary to secure the complicity of some seafaring man; it was necessary to lay in a great quantity of stores.

Where? If the low-lying islet of the Devil had offered a safe hiding-place for a raft, the high, rugged shore of the Island of Salvation could surely offer a secure one for a few sacks of bread, a few demijohns of water and rum.

The *Abeille*? She made regular crossings between the island and Cayenne — one must put to sea during her absence.

And the gunboats? Here lay the problem. Every time I set myself to resolve it, my mind became as dry, as dry as...


### Chapter Twenty-Four

<!-- pages:225-228 -->

DURING my hours of rest, I neglected nothing in my efforts to gather ample information about the men who lived and suffered around me. Antonio Gomez, after having been assigned for some time to the service of the Officers, had recovered, though bronzed in complexion, his old air of a good-natured priest. But an unexpected order removed him from all my influence. He was sent, along with others, to the penitentiary at Corù on the Continent, and I had barely time to press his hand. From that day, although I trust that the political upheavals in France brought him better times, I never saw him again, nor heard any further word of him.

On the island, however, there were other Italians — a dozen, perhaps; Romans for the most part, all condemned for having doubled their political hatred by layering upon it a private one; for having avenged, with our own native tyranny, the outrage inflicted upon them by foreign tyranny. Christ forgave much — so it is said — to those who loved much; He ought to forgive far more to those who have hated much and, in their hatred, found the virtue not to be a sheep. Among these Romans was a Morelli, a tailor, condemned for life. Who lends a more ready ear to hope than he who has lost all hope of his own?

I found, moreover, other Italians condemned, or simply relegated, for political reasons. They resided in part on the Île du Diable, but crossed over frequently at low tide, on the pretext, most commonly, of visiting the pharmacy attached to the Hospital. There was Galli, there was Rossini, there was Tebaldi, and we soon formed a close fraternal familiarity. Their story was known to me, and I could rest assured that, even should they not take part in the escape, they would assist it by every means within their power.

Among the men I had met during my first crossing from Toulon and who had disembarked at the island from the very outset, there was a French archpriest, condemned for life for the murder of a child of whom he had made an abominable use. Throughout the entire voyage, he had jealously clutched beneath his arm a large Bible of his own. In a moment of expansion, he had confided to me, his voice trembling, that he had concealed within the binding of that volume something in the order of twenty thousand francs, and that the tormenting thought of being strangled by hands greedy for his treasure gnawed at him more than any remorse. He intended to use that money to procure the means of escape. I called it to mind and reckoned upon it. To save the indispensable victuals from our limited daily ration would have demanded a sacrifice too severe and too prolonged for our hunger and for my impatience. The victuals would have to be purchased gradually at the penitentiary stores, so as not to arouse suspicion, at usurious prices if need be. Why should the archpriest not furnish us with the *conquibus* — the wherewithal?

But my good fortune willed that there should be on the island yet another individual who might prove far otherwise useful to me. He was a former officer of the French navy, a rabid Orleanist, a man all daring and all nerve; he was serving a life sentence, one more of the circumstances which I counted in my favour and should almost call propitious. That man had already done battle with storms; the Ocean held no terrors for him; it was not for him a boundless plain without paths.

All these thoughts, spokes of a disjointed wheel, remained for a long time motionless and as though crystallised in my mind, until all of a sudden they received an extraordinary impulse through the almost spontaneous formation of the very piece that was missing; which, in my long meditations, I had struggled to devise.

One evening, leaving the workshop, I was making my way towards my hut, which stood a good mile distant, when, upon approaching the quay, I caught sight of Morbioli bent over a gunboat, polishing its brasswork with perfect composure. The gunboats were, in truth, modest skiffs carrying at stem and stern a little pop-gun of a cannon. They were launched upon the water early in the morning and hauled ashore again at five in the evening.

Morbioli, a boatman in Rome, was assigned to their upkeep. How many times had I watched him busying himself about those trifles that were my constant torment! But now only, through some singular disposition of my spirit, those his languid rubbings, which struck me as a kind of caress, roused in me a fierce vexation, and I cried within myself: "Stave them in, you great fool; sink them, if you can!" That simple word — "sink them!" — darted through my mind like lightning illuminating a pitch-dark night. It was a revelation entire, and I shivered at it. I drew near to Morbioli, and I believe my face betrayed the secret tremor, for he looked at me with evident unease and said: "What is the matter?"

"Morbioli, when I was back there at the Montagne d'Argent, a hospital was being built and we had already reached the whitewashing stage. A convict who held me in great regard was one day at the top of his ladder, painting the façade, when from a neighbouring window there came a murmur of voices. It was the Commandant, the Chief Warder, and a condemned man in conference. The whitewasher distinctly heard my name spoken, and stood rigid, listening. I will tell you briefly: the Commandant was guaranteeing the immediate freedom of that convict, provided he succeeded in implicating me in some attempt at escape, in such a manner that the sentinels should have the opportunity to bring me down with rifle fire."

"Why do you tell me this?"

"To persuade you that in the registers, beside my name, a cross has been entered, and that even were I a man so contemptible as to draw you into a plot with the intention of denouncing you — what good would it do me? There would be no pardon for me either way. And now listen..."

I confided to him the secret workings of my mind, the circumstances and the persons upon whom I grounded my hopes; the tactics which would without doubt make us masters of the cutter and of the fishermen; the stratagem which he, all unwittingly, by putting an end to my long torments, had inspired in me. This was precisely the part I should entrust to his loyalty and his intelligence. He scratched his head, as was his habit in moments of anxiety; then he raised his face, and I read his full assent in his eyes, which sparkled. On the spot, I received a compliment of a flavour thoroughly *trasteverino* — the strangest that has ever been addressed to me in all my long existence: "You're a devil! Blessed be the woman that bore you, and may you... *murì amazzato!*" — God grant you a warrior's death!

With Morbioli's assent, the first link of the chain was forged. The others were formed one by one, slowly but wonderfully. On a cloudy night, troubled by the uproar of a fierce storm at sea, I slipped silently from my hut and, armed with a chisel, crept along the rocks to the very foot of the storehouses — a wide timber construction resting in part upon piles. I tried, and tried again, and managed to lift a plank of the boarding. I made my way inside, groping and nosing out my path like a rat, and when I emerged I had upon my back six great hammocks of the sturdiest canvas, which Morelli, the Roman tailor, found means to sew with consummate skill. We had our sail!


### Chapter Twenty-Five

<!-- pages:228-232 -->



IT WAS precisely in those days that the frigate *Amazzone* put in at the sun-kissed island and brought us a wave of news from the old Europe.

Men of retrograde ideas and men of the new order had continued their strenuous struggle for the conquest of the future, and great events had pressed upon one another and fixed themselves in history, though Orsini and Pieri and I and so many, many others had been wholly eliminated from the political scene.

What a fine lesson in humility! Magenta had carved its name there in brilliant, blood-red characters. And I was saddened by it — for what did I discern in it? The King of Piedmont had followed the tactics of his predecessors: making use of the French to beat the Austrians; only to ally himself, at a later hour, with the Austrians to put a check upon the French; and in either case, what results?... a niggardly aggrandizement of his Piedmont; a small additional source of revenue for the old dynasty — that was what I discerned in it! Napoleon would never have consented to have upon the borders of the Empire a strong Italy, capable of escaping his influence, his predominance; Napoleon, who exalted the Church and was himself made, in the hands of the Church, an instrument of reaction and oppression, would never have said to Italy: "Go, proceed triumphantly along the high road and reclaim your Rome!"

Vittorio Emanuele had insinuated among the Italian People that element of dissolution which would put an end to the heroic times — those times that regenerate character — and usher in those of vulgar opportunism and cowardly equivocation. I foresaw the profound scepticism and its attendant dissolution, that corrosive force which would invade the Latin soul, so prone to exaggerated despondency; the Latin soul that does not shine without enthusiasm, as blood does not burn and does not live without oxygen. The glorious goal, foretold by so many elect intelligences, the longing and the torment of so many virile consciences, seemed to me fatally receding. In the most favourable hypothesis, between the present and that goal stretched a treacherous wasteland, where liberty could not take root save in a stunted and debased form, warped by the inescapable demands of a Monarchy.

An officer of the *Amazzone*, struck by my sadness, gently asked me the reason for it.

"If Italy," I answered him, "in order to rise, however partially, to liberty, had absolutely needed to resort to foreign arms, I should have preferred to see men descend from the Alps who did not, as we do, bear the weight of chains upon their wrists. Magenta will doubtless mark a memorable date in the annals of Italy; whether equally auspicious, I know not. I know that it would have been auspicious had Napoleon been defeated and undone there!... Oh, what a providential misfortune that would have been! The two Peoples would have extended a hand to one another without hidden meanings, without ulterior designs; the one after having declared the usurper fallen from his throne; the other after having freed itself, through the third defeat of the Monarchy, from the equivocation, the nightmare that possesses and almost denatures it. Who could have resisted their combined impetus? They would have done far more than beat the Austrians and sweep away the petty tyrants that encumber the Peninsula; they would have practically abolished the Alps!"

"A generous dream!" said the officer, bowing his head. Then he leaned close and murmured in my ear words that made me start: "On board, we carry a Decree of amnesty — and it is for the political prisoners!"

An hour of anxiety, and we were drawn up in line before the barracks. The Captain of the frigate pronounced four sentences, alluding to the new glory with which the French arms had "covered themselves," and then followed the obligatory encomiums to the high wisdom and valour of the august Sovereign. Having extolled his clemency to the skies, he passed to the reading of the Decree of amnesty. The Director of the penitentiary pronounced, one by one, with a distressing slowness, the names of the individuals upon whom the unexpected dew was to fall. The name of Galli, the name of Rossini, the name of Tebaldi, my own — these were not pronounced. The Decree concerned French subjects only!

Several officers gathered about us and, expressing their regret, suggested that a petition from us, supported by the Director of the penitentiary, would, in all likelihood, be favourably received by the Emperor, who would thus remedy what was, beyond all doubt, an involuntary omission. Why not draft it at once? The *Amazzone* herself would carry it to France.

Galli, Rossini, and Tebaldi consented, and announced to me on the spot that they placed more trust in their petition than in the escape attempt, which they were naturally renouncing. And this was not, from my own entirely personal point of view, the least among the bitter consequences of the Franco-Piedmontese alliance and the victory of Magenta.

It fell to someone other than me — who did not sufficiently know the other inmates of that house of sorrow — to remedy that defection. I entrusted the task to Cousin, the former officer of Louis-Philippe, who set about it with such circumspection that it took him a couple of months. On the first of December he informed me that everything was ready. On the morning of the ninth, Morbioli and I stood at the pier watching the departure of the *Abeille*, bound for Cayenne. Its white wake was still faintly visible when I turned to the proud *transteverino* — the Roman from Trastevere:

"Morbioli, today we play the last card with resolution. Inform our comrades, with due prudence. I do not know precisely who the latest additions are; but I know they are not of the same mettle as those we have lost. I must, on this point, give you a recommendation I consider important."

And I urged him to leave the archpriest aside. True, he had contributed to the expenses; but the cargo he carried in his Bible — which was no longer much of a secret — might have stirred in others, if not in us, certain perverse instincts. To abandon him to his fate was, in all likelihood, to save his skin. Besides, we could not risk dishonoring, through dishonest actions, an enterprise which our very enemies would have been careful, in their hearts, not to censure.

Morbioli rubbed his hands together and observed that the archpriest would remain a caged bird, precisely on account of that little treasure upon which he had counted so heavily to be able to spread his wings. "It is plain that the holy unction never infused a shred of prophetic spirit into him." And he added: "The idea suits me; for, to tell you the truth, that priest, in so perilous an undertaking, struck me as an ill omen. It was as though we had taken him along to chant the *De Profundis* over us."

I gave him a few further instructions and we parted. At five o'clock, leaving the workshop, I saw him again, busy about the gunboats. An hour later, while the multitude of wretched souls sat in the refectory before their tin plates, I stood alone in a secluded spot, upright on the sand with my feet in the wash of the water, waiting in anxious expectation. In that low place, hemmed in among the rocks, no one could catch sight of me save from the sea.

And from the sea the oarsmen saw me. The desire for supper lent vigour to their muscles, and the little boat darted lightly over the waves, as though barely skimming them. I gave a shout and raised my fist high in the air, showing, pinched between thumb and forefinger, a five-franc piece.

They cursed; but, persuaded by Cousin, who commanded the small crew, they changed course, and in a flash the keel grated and came to rest. "Quick — quick — how many coins?"

For all answer, a whistle — and from the rocks five surly faces emerged, capable of inspiring respect in a band of savages. Before the fishermen could recover from their surprise, they found them alongside, with blades raised.

But the threat proved an almost superfluous precaution. Questioned one by one, the oarsmen had for some time already given expression to their ardent desire to escape. They had feared their companions, and not one had dared to utter an explicit yes. The kernel of my enterprise consisted, therefore, in binding together, with one bold initiative, all those pale individual affirmatives; in transforming them into a strong, collective, and dynamic will.

When I had drawn from their hiding-place the provisions, the mast, and the sail, the hesitant ones looked one another in the face and smiled. They contented themselves with saying: "Let it be understood that we yield to force."

It happens in precisely this fashion in great political and social affairs as well. How many truths are already firmly rooted in the heart of man, how many aspirations already germinate there in full vigour! Yet nothing is realized until the bold arise who dare to be the precursors. And it is this deplorable inertia, this fatal cowardice, that lends such power to the few to tyrannize the many.

Four strokes of the oar and we are as far from the shore as a musket-shot. We were eleven in all. The archpriest was absent; but he was not the only one. Morelli, the Roman tailor, who had likewise rendered us such effective service, had been seized by sudden fever and lay delirious in the infirmary. Morbioli and I were the only Italians.

Now the rocks no longer obstruct our view, nor the view of others, and the unwonted spectacle of a sailing-boat betrays in an instant the attempt so jealously concealed. The entire refectory is in uproar, and in the confused symphony of shouts, of laughter, of curses, borne to us on the wind, one terrible command stands out above the rest: *"Aux canonnières!"* A line of men pours from the refectory and stretches, with sinister windings, toward the pier.

Our little boat was making good speed; but with a manoeuvre I could not comprehend, Cousin, instead of keeping the prow pointed toward the Continent, described a wide arc, as though he intended to round the island. The convicts, who were following on the rocks a short chord of that arc, arrived promptly at the pier and lowered a gunboat in time to cut off our way. In other words, we were running at full speed, and almost deliberately, straight into danger.

I watched with anxious eyes the gunboat as she rode the water, apparently in perfect order; I watched with even greater anxiety Morbioli. He understood and said: "*Sangue transteverino non falla!* — Trastevere blood does not fail! You see? No one goes aboard, because the rowlocks are at the bottom of the sea — they are scrambling now to get new ones from the workshop! And the hull, on the bottom — four mortal holes!" And having said this, he clenched his jaw and pressed outward with outstretched arms against the empty air, as though straining to make the gunboat heavier and to do violence to the sea.

All at once he drives his fist through the air and bursts into laughter! The four holes had done their duty; the gunboat, laden with water, had gone to join the rowlocks where the light of the Sun does not reach. And the swarming mass of humanity darkening the shore acclaimed us with a torrent of cheers, with a feverish waving of rags and caps.

The tormented exulted at the defeat of their tormentors!

Now Cousin completed his mysterious manoeuvre and turned the prow resolutely toward the North. I cried out to him, with agitation: "What are you doing?"

"Be quiet! The *Abeille* will soon give us chase, and it will be along the coasts of Guiana that she will expect to intercept us. Northward, northward! Liberty lies in the midst of the tempests; our safety is in danger!"

We navigated strenuously through the whole of that night, always bearing toward the Pole. Great winds, great billows! The Ocean covered us with brine, roaring in a mighty voice through the darkness.

Was it warning us? Was it claiming us? Our senses, strained — terribly strained — could not make it out. At one moment it flung us upward as though it would hurl us clear of its domain; at the next we were drawn precipitously toward the abyss, as by a mighty, irresistible suction. Our sole assurance was the marble impassivity of the captain. No human voice amid that roaring fury save his — measured, calm, cold, metallic.

Dawn found us soaked and shrivelled. But we had left behind us the waters clouded by the great rivers that empty into the sea from the Continent, and already we were ploughing the deep blue. The Sun and the reborn hope transformed us within a brief hour. Let the Ocean rage, let it roar its unceasing menace. Each one of its waves might swallow us! What of it? Each throb of sail might bring us ever nearer to a final, tragic struggle — for upon every face there appeared the firm resolve to cut throats, or to have one's own cut, if the fruit of our boldness were to be wrested from us. What of it? We felt ourselves free as the wind that sped through the infinite!


### Chapter Twenty-Six

<!-- pages:232-235 -->





I COULD not recount, in perfect chronological order and as though reading from a ship's log, the manifold vicissitudes of our arduous trial. I shall say briefly of those which most properly determined its course.

The storms, invoked by Cousin, came — and came with such fury as to imperil vessels far sturdier than our own. It was not the sea alone we had to contend with; it was sea and sky in league together, battering us with a persistence that seemed almost to betray some deliberate intent.

To comprehend the full intensity of our danger, one would need to be seasoned in the art of navigation. For those who are, no description is needed; for those who are not, no description suffices. For two days and three nights we found ourselves in worse straits than those unhappy souls condemned to labour in those subterranean dungeons where water enters in cunningly measured jets. Either work the pump without respite, or drown.

It was then that I understood in all its weight the phrase: "And the floodgates of heaven were opened." Whoever was not keeping every fibre, every atom of himself grappled to the oars that held us in balance, toiled feverishly to bail out the boat. For this purpose our palmetto hats served us well; but the storm had left several of us bare-headed — these resorted to the only implement still within their reach: their wooden clogs. In such a predicament, no resource, however slight, was to be neglected.

The driving rain, the waves that rendered any distant view impossible, and above all the profound darkness, had yet another grave and unexpected peril in store for us on the third night. Blacker than the night itself, and like a phantom rising up threateningly from the abyss, we found ourselves face to face with a great sailing vessel. Cousin had barely time to wrench the tiller; but the surge was so violent that for an instant we believed ourselves swamped and finished. The fearsome apparition swept past us, vast and swift; then it vanished, as though it had dissolved into the darkness.

Not even in this circumstance did Cousin lose his composure, and after noting that in all probability it had been a Dutch vessel, he added: "Courage, lads! In this darkness, I'll wager I can slip between the legs of Death without letting Him catch hold of us!"

I reflected that we owed our salvation to the fever that had confined Morelli to the hospital — had the little craft been but slightly heavier, no human strength would have sufficed to keep her afloat. After that encounter, my companions saw a phantom on the crest of every wave.

The following day, the storm abated. Hoisting the sail once more, we were at last able to think of restoring our exhausted strength. Through the long hours of danger we had barely swallowed the odd sip of rum, and our vigour had been sustained, such as it was, by the peril itself. Now that the tempest had subsided on every side, a tempestuous hunger rose in its stead. But a terrible disillusionment awaited us: the salt water had partly swept away, and partly ruined, our provisions, rendering them all but useless.

The horrors of cannibalism then darkly flashed across our imaginations, and into the air burst imprecations fit to shame those attributed to the damned of hell. Cousin judged it not merely opportune but urgent to change course, and he turned the prow to the south-west — our natural heading.

That very day we sighted a long streak of dense smoke in the distance, a few spans above the surface of the water — the *Abeille*, perhaps, hastening westward in pursuit of man. We lowered the sail and for an hour crouched in great anxiety at the bottom of the dinghy. At dusk, the evil streak disappeared below the horizon. Two days later we were coasting the Continent.

At what point? Did our anxious eyes scan a hospitable shore, or did it hold greater perils than those we had overcome with such effort?

For a long stretch we saw no trace of human life; then suddenly, rounding a headland, there opened before us a small, tranquil cove, and on the beach a little hut, and around the hut several men — some labouring to move baskets and barrels, others spreading nets. Still others, seated on the sand, were mending meshes.

We bent to the oars and came ashore. While we were hauling the boat up onto dry land, a dozen Black men gathered around us, their bearing not openly hostile, yet that of people who harbour some suspicion. Among them was a white man, dressed in European fashion and armed with a rifle.

I understood at once that I was among slaves set to catching fish and drying them, working under the lash of the white man — of the civilised man, the believer in Christ.

I asked him whether, for payment, it was possible to obtain some provisions. He knew French and understood me. But his entire reply was: "Where is your money?"

I made my few coins clink and, pressing the first question, added: "What land is this?"

— Dutch Guiana! The land that had driven Pianori's associates back to certain death. My companions felt hunger no more, felt weariness no more. They ran to the dinghy and leapt in, ready to face afresh whatever trials might come — so potent, even in them, was the love of liberty!

We sailed westward again: the wind that blew was laden with the fragrances of the Continent. We reckoned that the *Abeille* had already passed that way and was leaving us ever further behind with every stroke of her piston. Cousin calculated moreover that British Guiana was not far distant, and our dinghy ran so swiftly that it seemed more than reasonable to count the end of our torments as near at hand.

Then suddenly the favourable wind dropped; the boat slowed, dipped her prow, and moved no more — as though some mysterious hand, having seized her by the keel, had held her forcibly back. I looked instinctively at Cousin. He was pale, cadaverous. To my silent interrogation he answered: "We are lost!" A simple and terrible thing had occurred — we were caught in a *vase*.

But we were not men to submit to our fate without first fighting with every ounce of our strength. And Cousin himself, after that first moment of dismay, having recovered all his composure, gave us the order to jump into the water and range ourselves around the dinghy to support it with our shoulders. "If the tide goes out," he said, "commend your souls to God — if you believe you have a soul, and if you believe in God. If the tide comes in — perhaps — well, not all hope would be lost!"

For a couple of hours we remained in the tenacious mud; up to our knees at first; then, little by little, up to our waists, as the water alternately rose and fell. The mosquitoes, fortunately, did not swarm; but the crabs were not slow to torment us with their pincers. There were moments of impotent fury bordering on delirium; moments of grim phantoms barely dispelled by my hardy philosophy. To be eaten alive, in small morsels, or to be devoured one by one by the thousand ravening monsters. What became, in that unequal struggle, of human pride? The longer the minutes passed, the more closely the invisible enemy pressed upon us. I had found myself exposed to many and grave dangers, both before and after; but it was reserved for the dagger of a compatriot and for the loathsome crustaceans of Guiana to pierce my flesh so as to leave thereon indelible scars.

It was our good fortune that the tide rose. The boat we had been supporting became at last our support. We clambered in — filthy with mud, bleeding, spent!


### Chapter Twenty-Seven

<!-- pages:235-240 -->

The little vessel glides on, glides on... until there appeared before our weary, harried eyes, drawn against the blue sky, the black hulk of a cutter. It had emerged as if by surprise from the shore, at so short a distance that the mere thought of evading it would have been madness. We flattered ourselves, for a moment, that those on board would take no notice of us; but a cannon shot, fired as an admonishment, blew away every illusion. The little herring moved toward the shark, the shark moved toward the little herring... the distance vanished with such rapidity that before long we were able to make out the flag flying from the sternpost.

Lift up your hearts! It was the symbol of pride and liberty; of humanity and progress... the English flag!

Leaning over the rail of his cutter, a black captain, surrounded by half a dozen black sailors, demanded of us, in a tone of command, who we were and whether we carried on board any goods subject to customs duties. When I had answered him, he straightened up in astonishment, and pointing his finger: "You don't mean to tell me you have crossed more than a thousand miles of sea on that span of hollow wood?"

I pointed in turn to our trousers which, despite the crust of mud that covered them, revealed here and there something of our origins. Once persuaded, he expressed a desire to question us at greater length on board his cutter. It was as though an electric button had been pressed... we all leaped to our feet at once, simultaneously. But whether he had no wish to soil his vessel with that species of ambulant *vase* [cesspit]; or whether our appearance disposed him to stand on his guard, he cried: "Not all, not all! Come aboard, you — you who have spoken." I was the only one who spoke English.

On board, I had ample occasion to recount our vicissitudes to him in detail, and to request of him, by way of conclusion, assistance for our most pressing needs. He listened without interrupting me, expressing his emotion at the dramatic passages only in the manner natural to his race: by laughing uproariously. When I fell silent, he began: "Well, well, well!..." But he interrupted himself and, turning to the sailors, ordered them to lower into the dinghy a sack of ship's biscuit and fresh water.

I asked him for precise directions to reach the nearest English port and, to my intense relief, was informed that we were not far from the mouth of the Berbice and the town that takes its name from it. "When you have rounded the headland that shows dark over yonder, the town will rise suddenly before you. The tide will be running out then. Keep on this side of the buoys and you will run no risk whatever."

The crew of the cutter, composed of sailors who had once been slaves, felt sympathy for us. Once the biscuit had been lowered, they vied with one another in tossing us tobacco and other small gifts, and when the dinghy set off we were sent on our way by a chorus of "good bye" and "good luck" which for some moments surpassed even the resounding voice of the sea. We showed our gratitude by immediately setting upon the hard crusts with the teeth of crocodiles.

And the little vessel glides on, glides on... until it enters the turbid, yellowish waters that announce the imminent mouth of the Berbice. My companions, far from rejoicing at this, began to give voice to the strangest apprehensions. Who could assure us, among other things, that the black captain had not taught us precisely the way to blunder headlong into some trap?

We chanced upon a fisherman's smack. Two small, sun-bronzed men, Portuguese in appearance, were drawing in their nets. "Which course must we follow to avoid the current and enter the river without danger?"

We heard, in different words, instructions identical to those of the black captain. Half an hour later, to our astonished eyes, Berbice, or New Amsterdam, spread itself upon either bank. On one side, low houses set upon piles, between which, evidently, the water crept in at high tide; on the other, the broad and solid government buildings. In the middle, several anchored vessels traced against the sky the spars of their yards.

We remained not long in uncertainty as to which direction to steer our course. Scarcely had we entered when there came swiftly toward us a launch, in which stood upright an old man with long white whiskers. He was surrounded by a dozen policemen. In an instant he was upon us, and the old man, tossing his honest growth of hair: "Who are you? Where do you come from?"

As though determined, once and for all, to have done with us, fate had driven us directly into the path of the Governor himself. Who, perceiving us so utterly wretched upon a vessel flying no flag of any kind, and supposing us to be castaways, had drawn alongside to gather reliable news and render us such assistance as the case required.

I answered him in four brief words, and he, with the tact of the English, who never permit themselves to give a direct contradiction: "You surely cannot mean to say that you have come from Île Royale?" Then he turned to his helmsman, the only one of his retinue of white complexion, the only one dressed in civilian clothes, and said to him: "What do you make of it? In a shell like that! Our old England has taught them the way!"

He set one of his guards aboard our dinghy and, having given the order that a substantial meal be made ready for us at the police station, made a sign, and his launch darted away, nimble as a fish.

The police station was situated on the left bank, near the quay, and four strokes of the oar sufficed to reach it. But from the French oaths that my companions were chewing between their teeth, I was obliged to perceive that the dinghy was proceeding contrary to their wishes. True, the "substantial supper" was a splendid prospect; but the escort of black policemen smacked too much to them of a grim preamble for them not to suspect that they would be slipped, straight from the table, into some large room locked with a double turn of the key. And matters grew worse when, upon arrival, an officious braid-decked officer launched into formal questions, writing in a register the answers that I translated for him. So we had faced death so many times only to tumble thus wretchedly from the frying pan into the fire?

To convince them that they were not prisoners, I feigned a desire to buy tobacco and went out. Then, freed from all excessive anxiety, I had leisure to observe the surroundings more attentively. It was the fifteenth of December, 1859, and the luxuriant vegetation pressed close about the settlement, as though intent upon dominating it and flooding it with sombre green. Superb, impenetrable forests stretched as far as the eye could see along both banks, and the sea water issued from them in little cascades to return, with the ebb, to the sea. The sky, deeply blue, bore in the west the glare of a thousand conflagrations.

The news of our arrival had spread rapidly and I was soon surrounded by a multitude eager for particulars. My convict's garb, filthy as though I had been wallowing in a pigsty, inspired no revulsion whatsoever. No question was put to me that was not discreet; I saw nothing but faces animated by the most unfeigned sympathy. And there was one who offered me a thousand *duros* for the cession of our dinghy.

I broke off the conversation and the handshakes to hasten at the call of a policeman, who announced that supper was ready. At the sight of the profusion of fresh bread, ham, cheese and beer spread upon the table, every trouble seemed to dissolve from the breasts of my companions. Not a crumb was left, all devoured in profound silence, amid the jests of the policemen, astonished and made merry by that swift, phenomenal disappearance.

The dishes were already being cleared away when an unexpected visit plunged us back into painful uncertainty. The old Governor with his long white whiskers had entered silently and stood before us, conducting a mute inspection. Behind him was a group of citizens whose sentiments toward us were perfectly dissembled by their impassive faces.

We remained still and expectant. The Governor began by expressing, in French, the following thoughts: "You have come to a land that does not turn away those who seek asylum in it. Respect the Laws of this Country and the Country will not fail in its duties of hospitality. But a guarantee is required of you — that of work. Work and thrift open the way before any honest man, however low he may have fallen." He was silent for a moment, then proceeded to question us one by one, asking us not our names, not the nature of our sentences, but what occupation we might honourably pursue. As my companions declared their trades, he turned to one or another of the citizens who had accompanied him and said: "This one I entrust to you... this other to you."

My turn came. I replied: "My profession is that of the sword."

— What do you mean by that?

— That I was educated at the military college of San Luca, in Milan.

— Where did you learn English?

— In England, where I have a wife and a son.

— You are Italian, you have a family in England, and you escaped from a French penitentiary. What a tangle is this?

I then revealed to him my name and the reason for my sentence. His face clouded: "I believe you are mistaken. The Count of Rudio is dead!"

I was about to reply when a young man of handsome appearance, notwithstanding the hue of his face, which was suggestive of chocolate — he was a mulatto — interposed and said in perfectly correct French: "If Your Excellency permits, I will take this man into my house. I make myself his guarantor."

That courteous man was the physician of New Amsterdam. He had completed his studies in Brussels, where he had managed to acquire, in addition to his degree, the love and the hand of a charming Belgian young lady. He was a freethinker, the proprietor of a local newspaper and correspondent of the London Times... not, perhaps, the least of the reasons that had impelled him to come to my aid with such warmth.

He assigned me a good bedchamber, where at last I had the opportunity to scrape off — literally! — the crust of the savage, and to restore my natural colour to the surface.

Thus transformed, I was presented to the lady of the house. She struck me by her very fine figure and by the refinement of her cultivation, notwithstanding that she had been educated in a convent. She spoke Italian with an uncommon sweetness for a foreign mouth, and was more than merely accomplished in drawing melodies from the pianoforte.
