The hour before the brief tropical dusk I spent in friendly conversation, recounting, at the request of my hosts and the visitors drawn by the unusual circumstance, now this, now that of my adventures. At a certain point the lady, who was probably minded to deliver me from the excessive importunity of the curious, said aloud: "The Count of Rudio, as an Italian, undoubtedly loves music." And without more ado she sat down at the piano.

What enchantment!... and how adroit the human heart proves itself in lulling the importunate phantoms to rest and in reviving the rosy phantoms that were lulled! I still had in my ear the language of the penal hulks; I seemed still to see, as it were, at my feet, the chain but barely broken; my soul still trembled from the ill-usage endured — much of it unrecounted, for I shrink from the pose of the martyr — those nimble fingers that, materially, moved across the keyboard, were in spirit touching the hidden fibre that ached within me, light as the fingers of a *smagnetizzatore* [magnetist who lifts a trance]. And I experienced the very real sweetness that accompanies the cessation of all torment. There were notes too which, reverberating in the depths, conjured up a malediction, a groan, a blood-red apparition, the roar of a tempest; yet all of it appeared to me softened and made harmonious by time and by hope.

I believe I wept! But I have one thing to say in my defence. I felt the authority of art deeply, because I too belonged to art... I had once opened wide my mouth among the choristers of Drury Lane!

That enchantment was of brief duration. An acquaintance of the doctor's came hurrying in to announce that a French warship had moored at the quay. "Let us go and see her!" was said in chorus. And we set off. A few minutes later, in the fading twilight, the menacing bulk of the *Abeille* towered before my eyes.

The tactic adopted by Cousin of pressing our dinghy northward had produced its effect. When, forced by the spoiling of our provisions, we turned back toward the South American continent, the *Abeille* had already passed beyond the course we had traced. Not finding us at New Amsterdam, she had pushed on into Venezuelan waters, and the captain, disillusioned, had already turned back on his route, when, passing once more before the Berbice, he had fallen in with the smack and the Portuguese fishermen. From them he learned of our arrival and had come up the river to demand my extradition.

On this there was no doubt. At the rail of the *Abeille* stood, one beside the other, two odious figures — the warders of my work gang. For what reason were they on board, if not to identify me? They caught sight of me and were quickened by a sudden ferocity: "Look at him, look at him!" cried one of them, pointing me out with his finger. And he added: "Hey, you — the one with the bombs! Before long you'll be up here and tasting your medicine again!" Such mockery did not suffice him. He came ashore and, approaching me with his hand outstretched, a diabolical smile on his foul mouth, congratulated me upon the skill of our escape. A civility which I repaid by turning my back on him.

That very same evening a policeman presented himself at the doctor's house and announced that the Governor required my immediate presence at the Palace. My host consulted with the friends who had remained until that hour, then said: "Let us go to the Palace in some number. Some act of weakness... one never knows!"

I covered the long way with feverish anxiety. Then, climbing the steep stairs, I saw in the antechamber — a dismal preamble! — my two warders. A few minutes more and I found myself in a great hall in the presence of the Governor.

Beside his writing-table stood a man in uniform... the captain of the *Abeille*.

Said the Governor: "Is this the fugitive you are seeking?"

I protested: I had never sought to conceal my identity!

— I beg you not to speak when you are not questioned.

And the captain: "I do not know him personally. But I have with me two warders who will be able to answer Your Excellency in categorical fashion."

One of them was brought in. Who, imagining that I had lied about my identity, without waiting for any question shouted: "Yes, it is he! The Count of Rudio in person... none other than he!"

— Get out!

The other warder enters and the denunciation is repeated.

The Governor rose: "Count of Rudio, I had doubted your word. I beg your pardon for it." Then, turning to the other: "Captain, England does not grant the extradition of political prisoners. I am sorry... but I am not in a position to give you any other answer!"

I was free, definitively free! And in the excess of joy that seemed to overwhelm me, instead of drawing myself up in all the dignity of the man restored to his rights, returned to his affections, to the holy struggle in behalf of ideals, I became suddenly a child again. At the captain who was withdrawing and casting me a severe glance, I directed that impish derision which expresses itself by placing the thumb upon the tip of the nose and waggling the fingers.

The following morning... two important pieces of news. The *Abeille* had weighed anchor but, departing, left behind at New Amsterdam a spy charged, certainly, with reporting on my movements. Some precaution was necessary; for if the Napoleonic claws had seized me again, even illegally, there was no diplomatic note that would have sufficed to save me.

The other piece of news, though it did not directly concern me, made the greater impression upon me. My companions in flight, terrified by the spectre of extradition, had crept back by night into the dinghy to take to the open sea in mysterious fashion. It was a grave error... the French authorities, having claimed my person, had taken no account whatsoever of them. According to the doctor, they had unquestionably steered their course toward some port of Venezuela. Did they reach it?

I believe that their manly fibre made them worthy of the liberty they so ardently craved. But I received no further word of them, and for me it was as though the Ocean had swallowed them up.


### Chapter Twenty-Eight

<!-- pages:240-245 -->



IN the afternoon, I saw the old Governor once more. Inspired by the boundless democracy of the English — who are, for all that, so aristocratic in their bearing — he had felt it his duty to come down to me in person, to congratulate the political convict reborn to civil life. And having understood, from what the doctor himself had told him, that my departure for England by ordinary means was not without its perils, he offered to engage me a berth on a man-of-war that would set course for the capital of the Empire no later than March.

With that, he took his leave. But before doing so he placed in my hands, without a word of commentary, a large volume of his own, bound in black Morocco leather. It was... a Bible. The good old man did not feel he had done enough by contributing to my material liberation; he wished also to cast into my soul the seeds of a moral fermentation. Although I considered then, and consider still, that his pious wish, had it taken root and flowered, would have, so to speak, unmanned me — nevertheless I could not help but feel a sense of gratitude for the disinterested nobility of his intention. From the window I watched him until he disappeared. Many times since I have wished to see him again, to prove to him that the good he did was not turned to ill. But he was already some seventy years of age at that time, and who can say how long it has been since the virtues and follies of the world have ceased to move him! He left upon me an indelible impression — not only because with him begins a new period of my existence, but also because, in so brief a time, he revealed to me so many facets of his character. His admiration for bold deeds performed at sea; the pride that led him, without denying the merits of others, to proclaim in our presence the superiority of his own race; his humane sentiments; his conception of the nobility of labour and of the regenerating influence of thrift; his faith in the rehabilitation of the fallen; his abhorrence of falsehood; his spontaneous amends for an offence, however slight, done to one who found himself in social circumstances so far inferior to his own; the respect he showed for the rights of the oppressed; even his inclination towards mysticism — all of this was the synthesis of the virtues and the failings of a People... of a great People.

In my head, amid such a kaleidoscope of men and events, a little confusion has crept in among the names. I may be mistaken; but I believe he was called Melville...

To wait until March — a device wholly irreconcilable with my impatience! A careful examination of circumstances led us to formulate the following plan. There was still at Berbice an English brig, the *John Romelly*, Captain Kandel, which had wished to sail for London on Christmas Eve, but could not, for want of the prescribed number of sailors. The doctor would go to parley with the captain and persuade him to enter me on the crew's roster. I would remain, until departure, shut in my room on the pretext of illness and, the better to deceive the watchful eye of the French spy, the doctor would publish in his journal that I had joined a caravan of rubber-gatherers which was, precisely on the following day, to set out on an expedition into the interior.

Christmas Eve arrived. At nightfall I embraced my courteous host and, having begged leave, kissed the hand of the gentle creature who, day after day during those difficult weeks of painful confinement, had soothed my fevers, kindling in my breast a wide tide of poetry. At dawn, the *John Romelly* was far away, and I could see nothing around me but the sea!...

It was calculated that the crossing would require from twenty-two to twenty-five days. Fierce storms drove us off our course. Our daily rations were halved, and it was a piece of good fortune to have been able to signal a steamer that replenished our provisions. We arrived off the Thames after more than two months of sailing.

How many tragic events had occurred since the day I had traversed those same waters in the opposite direction, summoned by the patriotic voice of Felice Orsini! Now, as then, the elements seemed to yearn for primordial chaos, and with such fury as to mark a memorable date in the history of maritime disasters. Death and destruction had gone before us, and we saw unmistakable traces thereof in the masts rising, here and there, above the surface of the water; in the wreckage drifting madly about us.

The caravan departed. Not many days passed before the sorrowful news arrived that it had been annihilated by savages. The doctor seized upon this to publish that I too, drained of blood and flayed, had passed into the company of the departed. The French spy vanished from New Amsterdam. But the *Times* of London republished the tragic little tale, and for the second time poor Elisa was compelled to make a terrible effort not to believe the plausible, if not the true.

Towards ten o'clock in the evening of the 29th of February 1860, I at last set foot upon the docks of the immense capital, where I knew not a single person who still believed me alive. Of the momentous events crowding upon one another at that hour, especially in my own Homeland, I knew nothing. I had in my pocket a couple of shillings borrowed from the second mate of the *John Romelly*; I was wearing the trousers of the prison hulk. From so humble a beginning was to unfold what I might consider my second existence. And in my soul, alongside a confused impulse towards action and an overpowering desire to cast myself into beloved arms, there was the bitterness of having arrived alone. My thoughts tore me away from the infinite sweetness I had so long imagined, to transport me to that fatal country where two martyrs with severed heads endured, unhonoured, the vicissitudes of matter beneath the earth.

An hour later, the miseries of life and political hatreds had reclaimed me.

My first steps were directed to the offices of the *Times*, to discharge a debt of gratitude and to announce to those friends who might still be interested in the matter my resurrection. Elisa, so I supposed, must be in Nottingham with her mother, and whether I resolved to go there myself or to write asking her to come to London, a few pounds were indispensable. To procure them, I was counting on the doctor Simon Bernard. This thought led me to enter a café in the West End that I knew to be frequented by Frenchmen.

While I was consuming my order, I became aware that my attire — more than shabby — was provoking the smiles and unfavourable comments of the customers, whose astonishment must have reached its height when a young man, entering noisily, ran to my table, crying: "Oh Moretto, you here? And I who have been mourning you for dead!" Hardly were the words out before he was embracing me and kissing me.

It was that Rossi upon whom Foschini had directed the dagger-thrust that had instead passed through my own chest. From the pavement outside, looking through the glass between the green curtains out of sheer curiosity, he had seen and recognised me. The torrent of questions he put to me was ceaseless; but no less ceaseless was the torrent I put to him. From him I learned that Simon Bernard had stood trial; that my wife had appeared as a witness; that the people and the press had protested in terms so forceful against that manifest subjugation of old England to the will of Napoleon III as to provoke not only the acquittal of the accused, but also the fall of the Palmerston Ministry.

From that time, Bernard had swelled with pride and was noisily assuming the pose of a hero. He claimed for himself the principal part in the Orsini conspiracy; which, to hear him tell it, would not have failed if...

At this point Rossi broke off, then resuming the thread of his discourse: "Bernard no longer frequents this café. If you wish to speak with him, I know where to find him. But I cannot understand in what way he could be of use to you... In short, shall I speak plainly? He blames you for having sold yourself to the French Police and denounced your fellow conspirators."

"Take me to him at once."

And so it came about that I found myself before the man. He had organised a small club that met in modest premises off Leicester Square. To go up to it one had to pass through a tobacconist's shop. The proprietress, to whom I applied, looked me up and down and would not let me pass. She did, however, send word to Bernard, who did not keep me waiting long. As he came down the stairs, I noted from his surprise that he had recognised me, and I noted too that, in order to keep himself — as the saying goes — abreast of the times, he had allowed two enormous moustaches to grow, in the manner of Vittorio Emanuele II.

— Good evening, Bernard.
— With whom do I have the honour of...
— You know full well. In any case I shall tell you myself...

And seized by a surge of indignation, I grasped him with both hands by his long moustaches, crying: "This will convince you that you have before you not some ghost of myself, but Carlo di Rudio in flesh and blood!"

It will be readily understood that, after such methods of an eloquence so little suited to the bar, the discussion could not proceed decorously. But the place, and regard for the lady of the establishment, would not allow me to prolong a discussion that resembled too closely a brawl. When he had threatened me with a warning not to make a scene, if I did not wish to risk being expelled from England, and when I had answered him, with a flourish of insults, that I had no need whatsoever to make a scene, since I had already charged the *Times* to make one for me, we ended by establishing by mutual accord that on the following evening at eight o'clock we would meet at the corner of Leicester Square to settle our dispute thoroughly — and coward be he who failed to appear!

I spent the night in Rossi's room, seething and writing. In the morning, resolved to seek out Giuseppe Mazzini, I went to Leather Lane to ask Massarenti for news of him.

He greeted me with extravagant praise; but when, interrupting him, I asked for the Master's address, I received the answer that he had not seen him for a long while and did not know in what corner of the world he had betaken himself.

Then the great Italian revolution, which had flung so many of my friends, so many patriots, upon London, had by now, like a maelstrom, almost entirely absorbed him.

Evening came. In Leicester Square I discerned, through the fog, beneath a street-lamp, Simon Bernard, deep in conversation with two strangers. He saw me and invited me to follow him. "Nasty cold weather!... In a public house — he said — we shan't catch a chill and shall have ample opportunity to come to an understanding."

After walking in silence for a couple of blocks, I suddenly turned round... the two strangers were following us. We walked another couple of blocks; I turned again, and the two were still following. I stopped short, and the moment the two realised they had attracted notice, they planted themselves before a shop window and stood examining it. I resumed my walk, and the two resumed theirs.

Then I moved sharply, and drawing out a concealed weapon that the provident Rossi had slipped into my pocket, I said to Bernard: "What game is this? Who are these fellows? Take care not to trifle with an Italian... If they come closer, I will run you through."

Not content with this, I advanced with sudden resolution towards the two strangers, who had stopped this time before a stationer's shop, and thrusting myself brusquely between them, I stared each of them in turn, fixedly, in the face.

From that moment I was followed no longer, and at the first public house I said to Bernard: "Let us go in here. Now we shall be able to speak freely."

But our conversation was very different from what I had expected. Doctor Bernard — a cunning, wary, double-dealing man, one who shrank from assuming responsibility for his own actions; incapable of confronting an adversary or an equivocal situation — of his own making — with manly directness; a quality that adorns the breast even of brigands! — opposed to my vehemence diplomatic equivocations, extravagant protestations of deep sympathy, lavish offers of assistance. I should pay no heed to the foolish gossip of malicious persons; I should go to Nottingham; I should hasten to bring unexpected joy to my wife, who most certainly stood in great need of it. He would confer with friends and write to apprise me of some definitive plan in my favour.

And he contrived to slip out from under me.

But permit me to pass for a moment beyond these miseries. There stirs in my soul now a memory of a very different nature... before which vulgar passions find no place in the heart.

I had written to my good mother-in-law in Nottingham to announce my arrival. Enclosed in the envelope was a letter for Elisa. The morning after that second interview with Bernard, scarcely had I thrown off the bedclothes when I caught sight on the floor near the door, where it had been pushed underneath, of a letter addressed to me. It was the reply of my mother-in-law, who, having given vent to her own happiness at my escape, informed me that Elisa was in London, at the Mancherini household. She had forwarded my letter to her, giving her my address at the same time.

I was so moved by this that as I dressed, my fingers could not find a buttonhole. And just then, as I was drying my hands, the knocker on the street door sounded with repeated blows. I made out the landlady's step as she went to open it; then a woman's voice asking whether a young man answering such and such a description lived in that house... An harmonious voice... hers!... But why did she not pronounce my name?

Perhaps she doubts — I thought — that I, out of prudence, may have given a name not my own. In one leap I was on the threshold... and I learned that excessive happiness seizes the heart with a rough, tyrannical hand, producing sensations that resemble pain. I saw her... but dimmed and as if in a dream; I wished to cry out to her: "I promised it you... here I am!" but my voice would not come; I made to throw myself forward with outstretched arms, but my arms turned to lead and my feet remained nailed to the floor.

Elisa too remained motionless for a moment... a deathly pallor drained the colour from her face. Suddenly she moved towards me, but after a few steps her head fell upon her breast and her knees buckled beneath her... She fell into my arms insensible. And when she came to herself, as I kissed her and wept:

"Elisa, Elisa! This honest refuge — more glorious... it may not be granted us, and no matter. But ever thine; and in every fibre of my heart, a shrine for thee!"

In that instant a thought flashed through my mind. Would I yet find my Homeland one, free, and great, as I had found my love again?


### Chapter Twenty-Nine

<!-- pages:245-249 -->

HOWEVER vast my desires, I could for the moment do nothing other than seek some means of establishing myself decently in London. Before long, Bernard's machinations placed me in such a position that, far from combating the enemies of Italian integrity, I was compelled to attend, as a matter of urgency, to the defence of my own personal integrity.

The slanders of which Rossi had given me the first intimation were swiftly confirmed to me, with a greater abundance of particulars, by various other persons. Dr. Bernard not only persisted, in private conversation, in the assertions he had launched forth when he believed me already gone to ground; but he attempted to lend them weight by maintaining that I had recovered my freedom, not through a premeditated and boldly executed escape, but because the French Authorities, in recompense for my betrayals, had, so to speak, thrown open for me the gates of the penal colony.

Elisa moreover informed me that certain gentlemen of the Times and others — the very same who had initiated the petition for my pardon — had crowned their efforts by collecting, on her behalf, a small capital of one thousand and one hundred pounds. From so much generosity, and from the kind of allowance that had been settled upon her on the eve of my departure for Paris, she had drawn no relief whatsoever. During my long absence, Bernard had delivered to her one single, solitary pound.

Upon whom was I to lay the blame? Was I to regard Bernard's conduct towards me as inspired by the terror of a guilty conscience? Did he slander me because he found himself incapable of defending himself honestly? To what end did his cowardly manoeuvres tend?

I wrote on this matter to several conspirators of the English Committee; but not one deigned to reply to me — whether because Bernard had forestalled me; whether because secret ties induced them to treat the question solely in closed session; whether because human selfishness counselled them to imitate the worthy Pilate, of traditional memory.

I must make a partial exception for Tomaso Allsop, the man who had lent his name to Felice Orsini. And I say partial because he sent me a letter containing very courteous phrases and... a banknote for five pounds; but not the slightest allusion to the pressing questions I had put to him.

Having weighed my situation thoroughly, I resolved that to press the matter of the thousand and one hundred pounds might cause my agitation to appear motivated by venal instincts. I therefore decided not to raise it again, and to confront Bernard openly, upon the other question alone. I would call the English public to judge between his assertions and mine.

Rossi, who never ceased to take an interest in my fortunes, introduced me to a certain Kinaer, a journalist already brought to the extremity of his strength by tuberculosis, but intellectually still full of vigour. He, guided by the information I supplied, wrote a succinct account of the Orsini trial and of my escape. A German publisher undertook to send a printed copy to the most widely read English newspapers. This preamble accomplished, I embarked with Kinaer upon a lecture tour, beginning at Nottingham — my wife's city — and proceeding thence through Derby, Birmingham, Manchester, Lee, and other manufacturing centres. Kinaer attended, with great dexterity, to the financial side of the undertaking. I had reserved for myself the task of setting before the audience the story of my case and of answering the questions put to me once I had finished speaking.

I never failed to make it clear that I was resolved to speak in public not in order to make money, but to provoke a discussion from which I had nothing to fear. And I challenged my accusers to come forward and oppose, to my defence, their alleged documents and their arguments.

Did this ever come to pass? One Sherman, who had attended one of my lectures, spoke of it at a Freethinkers' Club to which he belonged, observing how contrary it was to the English people's sense of justice to witness the spectacle of a man labouring under the weight of a shameful accusation, and not to lend him a helping hand so that he might stand face to face with his detractors.

The *Reasoner*, the organ of that club, then directed by Holyoake, reproduced that sentiment in one of its articles, which came to my attention in Birmingham. I immediately wrote to Holyoake, declaring myself entirely willing to submit to the judgement of whatever Committee he might be pleased to appoint. At the same time, I expressed to him my desire that Giuseppe Mazzini should be among its members, since he, knowing thoroughly the behind-the-scenes of our revolutionary endeavours, and to some small degree my public life as well, would greatly have facilitated the rendering of a dispassionate and impartial verdict.

Three events, in rapid succession, led me to consider the dispute fully resolved, notwithstanding the well-known proverb: "Slander, slander, and something will always stick..."

Among the convicts I had known on the Île Royale du Salut was one Le Roux, a Frenchman who, shortly after my escape, had been transferred to the penitentiary of Coru. From that latter place he had succeeded in escaping and taking refuge in England. Having learned of the rumours circulating on my account, he had, of his own accord, set about contradicting them, affirming himself to be an eye-witness of my "Coup d'État," as he called it. In recounting its particulars, he added to them those of the aftermath it had upon life in the penal colony and upon the severity of its regime.

Now, however absurd it may appear, the faith that had not been accorded to the words of an Italian patriot was freely granted to those of a French convict. It is somewhat humiliating; but I shall not complain of it... the strangeness worked to the advantage of myself and of the truth. So fell the little tale of the "doors thrown open."

The second incident had its origin in Giuseppe Mazzini. The editor of the *Reasoner*, having conferred with him, wrote to me and published that a jury of honour was superfluous. A dispassionate examination of the circumstances, together with the attestations of the Maestro, had placed my innocence beyond all doubt.

The third was brought about, unexpectedly, by Simon Bernard himself. "A chicken born must scratch." The funds entrusted to his care by a certain secret society disappeared in a manner considered not in the least mysterious. He was abandoned by his own supporters, went mad, and — *si vera sunt exposita* — died in a lunatic asylum. Reticence is rendered necessary by the fact that I never troubled to verify personally the death certificate that concerns him...

Let us refresh ourselves at the spectacle of a very different conspirator. During the wanderings to which I have alluded, I made the personal acquaintance of Baron Egassy di Toroefalda, the inventor of the Orsini bombs, the technical mind behind our conspiracy. If the martyr, in his trial, attributed the original idea to himself, the reason is to be sought in his honest circumspection. I have already remarked that he never named any person not already compromised.

Baron Egassy was a native of the Carpathian mountains and had been a lieutenant-colonel of Austrian artillery. A great liberal and a man of action, but rough, military to the last degree, he detested all those who claimed to vindicate their rights by shouting in the public squares, or to stir up revolutions by preaching the postulates of philosophy. His muscular strength exceeded the bounds of the credible.

He maintained that the best way of ridding oneself of a despot is to approach him on some plausible pretext, to place with lightning swiftness one hand upon his shoulder and the other upon his head, and to snap his vertebrae with a single sharp blow. Unhappily it is exceedingly difficult to call Brutus back to life and to endow him, into the bargain, with the muscles of an Egassy di Toroefalda.

Who possessed, among his many gifts, those of a chemist of no small worth. Not only did he teach Felice Orsini the method of preparing mercury fulminate, but he had also invented a mixture which, on exploding, would asphyxiate all those within a circuit of five hundred feet in diameter. I believe it was something akin to modern lyddite. He refused to reveal its secret to Governments, because they would use it — so he said — to perpetuate the oppression of Peoples.

One day, stigmatising with phrases full of passion the pusillanimity of those politicians who labour so mightily to vindicate the right of free speech, while remaining deaf and dumb and blind to the far more vital question of the freedom of action, he ended by promising me that, sooner or later, he would confide to me the formula of his explosive.

Uttered in a moment of expansive agitation, I attached no great weight to that promise. A few days later we parted, and I thought no more of it. To my surprise, in 1862 I received a letter from him. He begged me to hasten without delay to the hospital at Birmingham. He was there in a grave condition and wished to keep his promise and confide to me "the secret." I departed on the first train; but barely upon entering that sorrowful house, my eagerness was unexpectedly checked by an attendant. "The Baron di Toroefalda? He died this morning."

I wished to see him. The florid vigour, the portentous strength — more than extinguished and destroyed by the onset of a violent malady — seemed to have evaporated... the yellowish skin clung to the bones as though gummed fast. Observing him, I divined the slow work of starvation; although the physicians, taking the final collapse as their pretext, had attributed it to an entirely different cause, distinguished by some name drawn from the Greek which I no longer recall.

And yet that man possessed the secret of a formula which might have restored him to his former wealth. To follow the current... who could have blamed him? For centuries, every great invention has been turned, principally, to profit and to servitude. Progress the exclusive property of the few; science a prostitute, as religion is, as human Justice is. It seems fated!... But not with impunity did the poor Baron enclose within his heart a spark of greatness!

He had preferred to die in penury among the destitute; forgotten, without glory... if glory consists in the applause of men.

To detach myself from that corpse, already almost in dissolution, I was obliged to make a great effort. His eyes were wide open, as though he had died with his thoughts fixed upon my arrival and were still awaiting me. I wished to close them, and felt a shudder pass through me... It seemed to me that, in drawing down the thin veil of his eyelids, I had given the last touches to the work of death; that I had thrust that exceptional man, with my own fingers, into eternity.


### Chapter Thirty

<!-- pages:249-252 -->



In the very year of my arrival in London, the French Police made, so far as I was concerned, its last attempt at corruption. My finances were then at their lowest ebb. The five pounds from Allsop had evaporated; the modest savings accumulated during my lecture tour had likewise vanished. Elisa, on the point of becoming a mother for the second time, required considerable care. Fortunately, the assistance I no longer sought, out of pride, from my fellow countrymen, I received from Le Houx, the former French convict, who, having found employment at a marble saw-mill, had induced his employer to take me on for polishing slabs. My inexperience, however, kept the wages thin.

The step taken by the French Police at such a moment was not without its tactical shrewdness. The Inspector who entered my humble dwelling launched his attack without any preamble, with his cards on the table, with a blunt frankness worthy of a better cause. He dwelt upon my isolation, upon the ingratitude of those at whose "instigation" I had faced the scaffold, upon the indigence betrayed by my lodgings and my appearance. Was it worth sacrificing myself for people who, in the best of circumstances, cared nothing for my sacrifices? He understood perfectly well that I might harbour ill feelings toward the Government of Napoleon III; but that Government harboured none whatsoever toward me. One single word, and all my misery would be at an end, and I would be able to restore my wife to the comfortable circumstances she deserved, to which she was rightfully entitled.

And he gave greater weight to his words by drawing from his pocket a fistful of sovereigns, which would — he said — be periodically renewed.

But cunning had failed to reckon with a subtle and sovereign coefficient: the honest conscience that transfigures the sufferings endured for a worthy cause and transforms them into profound satisfactions; that transfigures the satisfactions born of wicked deeds and transforms them into unutterable torments.

My Elisa? She would without question have scorned the comfort offered at the price of my honour. What was a handful of gold, set against her love? Happy — thrice happy — the man who, in difficult moments, can see himself reflected in the serene eyes of a woman worthy of the name! He passes over the social mire as the Christ of legend walked upon the waters, with feet undefiled and dry.

When the importunate pressure of the Police had ceased, there came the more nauseating pressure of the priest. One day, while I was busy at my writing-table, I found one of them at my feet — affable, insinuating, a master at concealing the state of his soul beneath a smile... that smile irritated me from the very first moment I saw it. And so, cutting short his opening phrases, in which he expressed his pleasure at making my personal acquaintance, I asked him, curtly, in what way I might be of service to him.

— My parishioners are all, in a manner, my children. I have left the ninety-nine sheep safe upon the mountain...

A first interruption on my part, to establish a necessary premise. Of all the four-legged creatures to which, with a figure of speech not altogether of the most genteel variety, he might have compared me, the sheep was the one I found most detestable.

— "For goodness' sake," he said, "let us not quarrel over mere words. The reasons that brought me here are grounded in a sense of duty, and in the name of duty I may well ask for a few minutes' attention, even at the risk of proving tiresome."

At a gesture from me, he sat down and, protesting that he wished to come at once to the heart of the matter, reminded me of my quality as an Italian, which he held to be inseparable from that of a Catholic. Not from any eagerness to meddle in the affairs of others, but from the conscientious discharge of his own ministry, he had come to ask my permission to baptise my child, sustained by the hope that I would not refuse to render that dutiful homage to the religion of my father.

— My father has no faith in priests... that key, therefore, cannot lock or unlock my heart. But even if it could... if children were under an obligation to practise the faith of their ancestors, we should still be casting human victims, here in the full light of the nineteenth century, into the flaming belly of Moloch; we should still be worshipping onions.

With that reply I had furnished the opening for a discussion which it had been my intention to avoid. The priest seized the opportunity on the wing and, still smiling, observed that the ancient forebears had believed in error and practised falsehood. It was therefore incumbent upon their children to seek out the truth, even if by groping in the dark. But from the moment that truth had been revealed to us by Him who is the very essence of the true, why depart from it, why obstinately close one's eyes to the light? And if even, out of a misguided pride, I wished to go headlong into evil, by virtue of what right did I deny redemption to an innocent creature?

And I replied: — If my son is innocent, why would your God hold him responsible for sins not his own?

— These are mysteries of our holy religion; nor is it given to the human mind to fathom them...

— Not even to the men chosen by "holy" vocation and anointed of the Lord?

— That would be to presume too much upon poor clay!

— From which I must infer that you make yourself the herald and teacher of things you do not know; which, by your own confession, you will never be in a position to know. What would you say of me if I claimed to teach Greek, when I am ignorant even of its alphabet?

— Faith, Count, faith!...

— If it is a virtue to believe the absurd, I fail to see what difference there could be between a bishop and the savage who worships the aerolite fallen from the sky.

And here, by my own foolishness, I allowed myself to be drawn into expounding for him the essence of the Brahminic legends, the fertile matrix from which so many other religious legends sprang, the Christian included. Indeed, pressing further back into the succession of centuries, I demonstrated — an easy enough task! — that all the religions which cloud the human intellect derive, in the last analysis, from the astronomical knowledge possessed by the ancients, and that upon the *scienza del Sole* — the science of the Sun — every sacred allegory, every pretended sacred mystery, is precisely founded. He was coming to me, therefore, unconsciously perhaps but unquestionably, to propose that I bend the knee, bow the soul, before the majesty of Apollo, of Bacchus, of Mithras... of the Sun!

He let me speak, endeavouring to assume, here and there, the expression of a learned man who listens, out of benevolent condescension, to the fables woven by the exuberant and wayward fancy of a child. But at that final phrase, his thin lips took on the hardness of sarcasm, and seemed to stretch toward the corners of his ears.

Was that sarcasm sincere? Perhaps!... In the Church, as in an army, the General Staff knows the secret plans; but the multitude of subordinates operates in the dark.

He said: — Count, permit me to employ, in defence of the truth — which has, moreover, no need of defence — the selfsame frankness you employ in defending heresy. Human fancy has indulged in far wider flights than that particular one of yours. But in the field of positive facts, the Holy Catholic, Apostolic and Roman Church has nineteen centuries of good works to her credit, which neither you nor any other unbridled spirit would know how to destroy.

And I replied in kind: — The good existed in theory and in practice even when humanity had not so much as dreamt that, upon a small and picturesque peninsula of the savage West, a Rome would arise, and in Rome, after a long succession of intrigues and outrages, a Catholic coalition of superstitions and cupidity, which would take morality as its sign, or rather, as its picklock. And I maintain that good will continue to be practised even when of the Rome of the Popes there remains, as a pale memory, nothing but the name.

Individually, and despite the habit he wore, even a priest might practise virtue, since goodness lies as a seed within human nature and the day would come when, once the obstacles were broken, it would at last triumph upon the Earth. But the evil which the Catholic Church, regarded in its exclusive essence, had wrought and continued to wreak — that, now, could not be eclipsed with a handful of phrases! Did he truly wish me to recall it, by evoking nineteen centuries of obscurantism, of deception, of tyranny, of torture, of the stake, of tears and of blood?

He lowered his eyes and appeared to reflect. Then came the stroke of the venomous tail. — Let us leave aside for a moment, he said, opinions which, after all, can do little or nothing against the fatal march of events... Could one not, with a little goodwill, find a common ground upon which two men of honour might reach an understanding, notwithstanding their difference of opinion?

He lowered his eyes again and, in a subdued voice: — Allow me, not as a priest, but as a man of feeling who might, by reason of age, be your father... If you were to authorise me to baptise your child, I would undertake to procure for you — upon my word of honour! — a lucrative post, such as would restore you to a decent standing in society. Around me I perceive signs of poverty only too manifest. Forgive me!... Between serious men, between upright consciences, who yet know how to weigh the demands of life... why sacrifice reality to mere abstractions? Does it not seem to you that the greatest of our duties is precisely that of providing decently for those we love? And what greater satisfaction for a father and family man?

It was the noose of necessity flung about my neck! Whether General Staff or common subordinate, the indelible priestly character never belies itself.

I rose sharply to my feet and, in a voice full of anger: — The greatest of my duties is to leave my son a legacy of manly example. In any case, I am not a priest, and I do not traffic in my convictions! And I showed him the door.

That very same year, after an existence of but a few weeks, my second-born child ceased to live. It was Christmas Eve... the anniversary of the day on which I had set sail to voyage at full canvas toward the fellowship of free men... and I had not so much in the house as would allow me to provide for his burial. I gazed with dry eyes upon his small white face, pale as the pillow on which it rested, and, striving to dry Elisa's tears, I said:

— Sooner or later, once the obstacles are broken, the good will triumph upon the Earth. And the credit for it will belong, in great part, to those who, knowing the truth, are not afraid to live it. But now... Peace, peace! An uncompromised conscience expects no reward from men. And perhaps this utter destitution... is the only true glory that is ours.


### Chapter Thirty-One

<!-- pages:252-258 -->

BETWEEN one and another incident of my private life, the History of my Country sends forth gusts laden with renewed hopes and enthusiasm, with bitterness and with anger.

My predictions concerning the fatal consequences of the alliance concluded between the King of Piedmont and Napoleon III, though they had formed themselves in my mind at so great a distance from the theatre of events and whilst I found myself as though shut off from the rest of humanity, tallied with those expressed by Giuseppe Mazzini. Those predictions had come to pass.

Having undertaken to drive the Austrians out from the Ticino to the Adriatic in exchange for a compensation which, wounding our national sentiment, placed in his hands the keys to the western gateways of Italy, Napoleon had unexpectedly halted his victorious army and concluded the pretty Peace of Villafranca, which left to the enemy the Veneto, the Quadrilateral, and part of Lombardy itself.

Thus the pact of alliance was torn up in the very face of Vittorio Emanuele II, who, by greater insult still, was not accorded, in the discussion of the armistice and the peace that followed, so much as a *pro forma* consultative vote.

That reversal of front, that breach of faith, that sudden manifestation of imperial spite were owing to the turn taken by Napoleon's manœuvres, in part confessed, in part only too manifest. Napoleon had descended into the valley of the Po to substitute his own influence for that of Austria. A Piedmont enlarged but weak, defenceless against imperial overbearance; a central remnant governed by Plon-plon and vassalised to France; a Murat at Naples; an Italy still in fragments, under the Presidency of Pius IX; the Pope protected and made the instrument of the man to whom he owed his sceptre — this was, in a more or less veiled but nonetheless substantial fashion, the conquest of the entire Peninsula: the most sorrowful pages of our History reactivated in the full nineteenth century!

The unitary patriotism of the Tuscans and the sagacity of Bettino Ricasoli, who was its supreme exponent, made a mockery of the trafficking which had made a pawn of the King through the vulgar Plon-plon, and by persuading the latter of the futility of any attempt on his part, lifted from the minds of the patriots the newest burden of dread.

Following the example of Tuscany, Umbria, Modena, Parma, Piacenza, Bologna and the Romagne had risen up, had dismissed their petty tyrants — not to bow before foreign sovereigns, but to attest their solidarity with the rest of the Italian family. The long propaganda of Giuseppe Mazzini was bearing its fruits.

Had the Empire possessed a popular foundation; had the ill-suppressed apotheosis of Felice Orsini, made by Napoleon's own subjects, not robbed Luigi Napoleone of every illusion on that score; had the general conditions of Europe permitted it, he would have unquestionably assumed the posture of an absolute master, dictating his own will to the peoples of Italy as supreme law. And the Gallic armies would have drunk the waters of the Po for an indefinite time. He lacked the audacity, and, gravely misjudging our popular forces — forces destined henceforth to expand "with, without, or against" the high foreign and domestic influences — trusting, on the other hand, too greatly in the subtle arts of his diplomacy, he preferred to spy upon events, playing for time.

And he announced it clearly enough with the celebrated phrase: "Now we shall see what the Italians can do on their own!" Which was a warning, was a threat; which, broken down into its component parts and glossed in plain language, signified: "Engaged in the absorbing concerns of a war, you would slip entirely from my grasp. Nor do I stake my crown for the platonic love of your fair skies. Free from excessive cares, at the head of an army strengthened by the prestige of victory, I shall be better able to dominate the situation. And let the Italians take care not to go too far beyond the limit willed by my interests!"

Vittorio Emanuele II — with Austria thirsting for vengeance, well entrenched in Italian soil; with the spectre of revolution seething in every part of the Peninsula; fearful of losing even the little he had gained; constrained to subordinate the unity of the Fatherland to the fortune of his own dynasty (he and Cavour deemed it a dream of exalted minds) — could no longer flatter himself with the hope of attempting anything new, of taking a single step, without the blessing of the "magnanimous ally." In other words, that ally had reduced the great King to the condition of a simple vassal of the Empire, perhaps more, certainly no less than the Pope. And we see that, precisely from the Peace of Villafranca, the Government of the King inaugurates that policy of phenomenal pliancy to the caprices of the fateful man; a policy which may seem practical, and perhaps even necessary, but formative of character, but Italian — never!

And Vittorio Emanuele II, glimpsing this, cried out in bitterness: "Poor Italy!" But here was, at bottom, another great intellect deceiving itself. The unitary patriotism of Tuscany not only added the true cornerstone to our national edifice, but, by forcing Napoleon III to throw off his mask, was destined also to render his much-lamented defection an auspicious event in the annals of Italy. The sublime boldness of the *partito d'azione* (the party of action), impatient of delay, redoubled in intensity and precipitated History!

Two months after my arrival in London, there spread, swift as lightning, the news that Giuseppe Garibaldi had landed at Marsala; then, amid general trepidation, came the most contradictory reports, and even that report claiming he had been captured by the Bourbons and summarily hanged from a tree. To dispel that sinister vision came the announcement of the victory of Calatafimi and of the rapid triumphs that followed. England remained as though dazzled by those bursts of intermittent light, and such and so intense was her enthusiasm as to leave one almost supposing that the legionaries of Garibaldi, more than fighting battles for the redemption of Italy, were fighting for the greatness of the English People.

The entry of Garibaldi into Palermo was met, unhappily, by Parliament's sanction of the cession of Nizza and Savoy; the full price paid for a service not fully rendered. Thus, while the Revolution was sacrificing the blood of heroes to add new provinces to the resurgent Nation, to make her whole, the Savoyard Monarchy was operating in reverse and amputating two of her ancient provinces.

Then suddenly, the superior intellect governing the fortunes of that Monarchy, with a master-stroke, turned from devil to friar. I mean that Camillo Benso di Cavour, for all that he deprecated the arts of the revolutionaries as the supreme calamity of Italy, became in his turn a provoker of revolutions. The Marche, having risen, were traversed and re-traversed by his emissaries, who encouraged the wavering, gathered up the scattered threads, distributed arms, redoubled boldness and enthusiasm by promising the imminent intervention of the royal army. And General Cialdini, charged with making good that promise, invaded the Marche to crush the mercenaries of reaction. This took place on the very same day that Garibaldi entered Naples in triumph.

Naturally, even for his new enterprise, the King's Minister had sought the consent of Napoleon III, to whom he demonstrated the necessity of drawing a cordon of defence about the Papal States, menaced by the red shirts. But the supreme purpose was to cancel, in part, the shame of the cession of Nizza, to which Napoleon himself had shown himself disposed to resign. To cancel that shame and to paralyse the prestige of the *partito d'azione* (which had become almost irresistible after the conquest of the Two Sicilies) — whose republican tendencies he vastly overrated. Monarchist to the point of fanaticism, he could not have desired the unity of Italy when attempting it would have compromised the Monarchy; he had become a unitarian when the Monarchy would have been compromised by inaction. The dynamic force of republican thought had thus, indirectly, drawn even the Marche into the bosom of the Italian family.

On the thirtieth of November of that year, I received from the English newspapers the news that Giuseppe Garibaldi, a new Cincinnatus, had retired, as a private citizen, to the solitude of Caprera. With scanty troops, poorly armed, in a few months and as if by a single impulse, he had conquered a Kingdom. Having been proclaimed its Dictator, from that height which permitted him to treat as an equal with the great King, to dictate terms, he held out to him, that he might gird it upon his brow, the crown of the Bourbons. A gesture entirely new in History! — but not new the bad faith with which he was repaid by the very one who had benefited by him. Vittorio Emanuele II assumed the posture of a man who had known how to forestall usurpation; who recovered, by his own virtue, that which was his by right. The debt of gratitude he would see to paying — later!

The glorious enterprise of the Thousand had been organised by Giuseppe Mazzini, by Rosalino Pilo, who left his life in it, by Agostino Bertani, by Nino Bixio, and by many and many another, with those means and with those arts which were so frequently reproached to the Master as a crime. The last at the time of a series of misunderstood and sublime endeavours which had created an "Italian question," which had compelled the Potentates of Europe to concern themselves with it, it was the most fortunate, but not the most exuberant in boldness and spirit of sacrifice. To it the glory of triumph; to the others, that of having prepared and made it possible. And I shall recall that Francesco Crispi, who had not yet renounced embodying within himself the genius of rebellion, brought to it his own contribution — among other things, with an impressive supply of... Orsini bombs!

The enterprise itself gave wider scope to the various tendencies to define themselves. Napoleon III accentuated ever more openly his Italophobe designs, and Vittorio Emanuele II appeared ever more clearly in his equivocal light: champion of the Italian cause, he conspired with the enemies of Italy; liberator, he opposed liberty. Intent upon obstructing the triumphal advance of Garibaldi, he did not blush to open negotiations with the Bourbon himself. And with the Emperor of the French, he came openly to an understanding to spread, along the Strait of Messina, a snare of iron and lead, to bar the bold legionaries of redemption from the road to the Continent. However, the English Cabinet, sounded on the matter from Paris, responded that the Neapolitans had the right to determine their own destinies, and imposed upon Napoleon the policy of non-intervention.

The greatness of the republican soul shines all the more brilliantly in the light of these comparisons. Let it bear repeating: the monarchical entity never throws itself into the fray without keeping an eye to its own interest, and subordinates the unity of Italy to privileges which it proclaims and will have inviolable; the republican entity, having written its own epic in characters of blood, sacrifices freely, upon the altar of the Fatherland, both itself and its serene convictions.

True! — Vittorio Emanuele II was regarded at that time by Garibaldi as the *Dux* foretold by Dante, as the Prince schooled by the powerful mind of Machiavelli. Giuseppe Mazzini, more sparing in his judgements, contented himself with considering him better than his Ministers.

With the swiftness of lightning came the days of disenchantment. Cavour, overwhelmed by immoderate labours, succumbed before his time to the common fate. In England he was mourned sincerely; though, to shake his popularity, something had already leaked out of those secret pacts, so greatly resented by English liberalism, which, later given concrete form in the ill-omened "Convention of September," led the first King of Italy to the renunciation of Rome and to the defence — against the Italians themselves — of the greatest among the enemies of our Country. The *partito d'azione* hoped instead that, with the disappearance of Cavour, it might be possible to draw the King free from the baneful influence of Napoleon.

To the Piedmontese statesman there had succeeded a most thoroughly Italian figure endowed with wide views, one who desired unity and independence not as an end but as the means of ensuring the political regeneration and the greatness of the People — the Baron Bettino Ricasoli! The ship of State changed its course at once. The edict perpetuating the exile of Giuseppe Mazzini was abrogated; constitutional guarantees were respected; the extreme parties were encouraged to draw together, to give concrete form, with their accustomed boldness, to the good of the Fatherland: a renewed current of enthusiasm, a powerful tremor, a feverish activity shook the entire Peninsula. But when, working in concert with Garibaldi, the proud Baron set himself to making of the Italian collectivity not an inert mass at the mercy of its own governors, but a formidable armed force, capable of imposing upon Privilege the respect for popular rights, the retrograde party — already livid with suppressed rage — took fright and decided to make an end of it. One of those parliamentary crises that cover, beneath the cloak of legality, the odious interest of caste, stripped him unexpectedly of the reins of public affairs. And the antiquated and mendacious Diplomacy, under the auspices of Urbano Rattazzi, sharpened oppression and violence the better to corrupt young Italy.

Rattazzi concealed himself at first beneath the skin of the just man; he assumed the posture of a continuator of his predecessor's work: he promised, encouraged, spurred the *partito d'azione* toward the conquest of the Trentino and of the Alps. At the tactical moment, the news broke of the seizure of arms and the arrest of patriots. Brescia, whose horrible wounds inflicted by the beast Haynau were not yet healed, was made to writhe under those opened in her by Piedmontese bullets. In place of foreign tyranny, domestic tyranny had been installed. And Garibaldi, in a burst of righteous indignation, flung his sarcasm in the faces of these fierce men, proposing a national subscription intended to offer a sword of honour to the Russian Popov who, at Warsaw, had broken his own sword rather than drench it in the patriotic blood of defenceless Poles.

Nor was the measure of the affront yet full. The possibility of acting in the North having been cut off, Giuseppe Garibaldi gathered in Sicily a band of volunteers, crossed the Strait once more, and marched up through Calabria, resolved to give the Fatherland her glorious capital. He had made his own the battle cry launched by the patriotic population of Marsala: "O Roma, o morte! — Rome or death!"

"You shall have death, not Rome!" was the cry from Paris. And Napoleon imposed upon Vittorio Emanuele, who accepted, the rôle of the modern Cain.

We are come to the infamy of Aspromonte. When the Garibaldian Legion and the soldiers of the King encountered one another on the 28th of August 1862, the leader of the Thousand responded to the assault of the Bersaglieri by ordering his men to stand firm without opening fire. This alone should have sufficed to avert the fratricide, had not those men who had labelled the attempt of Orsini an abomination sought to eliminate for ever from the stage of the world the foremost and most beloved of the combatants for the cause of Italy. And perhaps it is to the conscience of Sergeant Cavalli of Ivrea that we owe the failure of that conspiracy, truly abominable in its nature. Stepping out from the ranks and advancing three hundred paces beyond the line of fire, he, a most expert marksman, fired a round into the air. Reprimanded, covered with abuse, he took aim once more —

"Viva l'Italia!" cried the leader of the Thousand, and fell at the foot of a tree which resurgent Italians ought to hold sacred as the wood of Calvary. The ball which had wounded Garibaldi in the ankle of his right foot struck the Fatherland to the heart. Vittorio Emanuele II paid the debt of gratitude in that regal fashion — and the courtiers had all the more reason to bequeath to posterity his name with the epithet of *Galantuomo* — the Man of Honour!

Major De Villata shortly afterwards summarily shot, at Fantina, the legionaries who, wandering in the environs of Messina, had attempted to reach Garibaldi. And the insult of that modern Maramaldo, who presumed to brand their unsullied brows with the mark of the brigand, reached them as something more bitter than death itself. In that lust for reaction, Alfonso Lamarmora — the narrow Lamarmora, the same man who in 1849, to the offers of fraternal assistance from the Triumvirs of Rome, opposed, and boasted of it, the cynical response of the bombardment of Genoa; the same who was to lead us to the humiliation of Custoza — arrested, notwithstanding parliamentary guarantees, three Deputies, three representatives of the Nation — and would have had them shot. But Urbano Rattazzi, frightened by the possible consequences, paralysed by the indignant reprobation which, on the morrow of Aspromonte, rose like a tide in every part of the civilised world, compelled him to release them and to make his apologies.

"The Monarchy," cried Giuseppe Mazzini in those days, "inveighs in Lombardy against patriots, in favour of Austria; in Southern Italy it shoots patriots, in favour of the Pope. The equivocation is no longer possible!" And he broke openly, in full view of all, with the Monarchy.

Too late! No longer the interpreter, even in outward appearance, of the popular will, Vittorio Emanuele II felt himself from that time secure in his position, and boldly proclaimed himself the sole arbiter of the destiny of the annexed Provinces. He cast, like a leaden mantle, over the rest of Italy the pedantic administrative systems which had ill enough suited the small Piedmont.


### Chapter Thirty-Two

<!-- pages:258-263 -->



WHAT sad fatality, seeming almost a punishment! From the crime of Aspromonte to the present day, across half a century of History, the House of Savoy, meritorious as it is from various points of view, has no longer known how to lead Italy to victory — that very victory which continued to smile, again and again, upon the glorious victim of its own fratricidal bullets. (1)

In 1866 Venetia was wrested from the hateful yoke of Austria; yet only at the cost of mysterious defeats, of humiliations that recall all too keenly another dark mystery — the one coupled with the name of Novara. As at Novara, so too at Custoza and Lissa, the enemy, sensibly inferior in strength, marvels at his own victory and is barely able to credit it. Meanwhile, a handful of volunteers, deliberately left deficient in arms, redeems the honour of Italy upon the crags of the Tyrol. Once more, those who arrogated to themselves the monopoly of intelligence and seriousness demonstrated their hollow pomposity, their inferiority before the handful of "madmen" who stubbornly insisted upon an Italy whole and glorious. And the peremptory order issued to Garibaldi to evacuate the redeemed lands, made fertile with the blood of his men, reveals all too plainly the royal resentment at so galling a contrast.

In 1870, Rome was wrested from the baneful yoke of the Pope; yet the Monarchy did no more than gather, reluctantly and almost under violent compulsion, the fruit of the audacity of Mentana. That Mentana which saw the royal army stand motionless whilst witnessing the slaughter of the bold ones who were sacrificing themselves to clear the road to the Capitol for the great King. Where were they that day — the righteous wrath and the generous impulse that make Italians the most frank, the most admirable among the races of old Europe?

As the Fatherland recovered, piece by piece, each fragment of her territory, that portion of the Nation which had settled itself within the leaden mantle lost, fragment by fragment, its finest character. Ideality set like a declining sun, and in its place succeeded a bastard *praticismo* — a creed of mere practicality — distilled from all that smells most vulgar. "We must be practical!" cried, until they were hoarse from it, the *barbassori* — the self-appointed pundits — of Italy, who looked on with folded arms at the fruitful endeavours of the *scapigliati*, the free spirits. And they failed to perceive that for a Nation, to live is less than nothing when it does not know how to live gloriously. I say that the men who pass themselves off as serious and positive are precisely those who perpetuate the evils and the shames that afflict us; for where the effort that strikes and crumbles away the grim reality bearing down upon us is lacking; where one does not aspire constantly, in thought and in deed, to the realisation of "dreams" — progress becomes a fable; whilst the utopia of today, nourished by sacrifice and blood, becomes inexorably the reality of tomorrow.

Be that as it may, the conduct of the royal army at Mentana must have caused a lurid light to flash within the breast of the ruler of Italy.

The maladies of the soul manifest themselves through actions. To take up position on a field of battle as though upon the tiers of an amphitheatre and to contemplate, motionless, the foreigner who, ensconced in the very sanctuary of Italian feeling, bayonets the generous insurgents so as to drive them out — as one drives out thieves who have entered the household sanctuary with a false key; to count the wounds; to hear the death-rattle of the dying; to see the countryside, all red with our own blood, covering itself with corpses — and to persist in that immobility, and not to level one's rifle, not to strike the imploring ones, even from behind if need be... In vain do I search my knowledge of History, ancient and modern... I find therein no more complete an eclipse of every patriotic sentiment; no more abject a renunciation of the rights and duties imposed upon virile souls.

And what Sovereign, even one animated by a vision as clear as the purest expanse of sky, would not have cried out: "Your bodies and your spirits are mine... I have conquered!"

From that servile submission to the hollow prestige of a crown, the Italian People, taken as a whole, has never recovered. Italy is no longer a geographical expression, no longer the land of carnival; but neither is she the land of Brutus and the Gracchi. "His Majesty... His Holiness"... between the two there runs not merely the analogy of rhyme. The one by grace, the other by the will of God; the one sacred and infallible, the other sacred and inviolable; the one inseparable from Truth, the other from Goodness; to speak ill of either... a crime! The heart bleeds at the thought of it. We have cast aside one set of dogmas only to stumble into another of equal weight; our Fatherland is now the Peninsula of the two Pontiffs.

The Monarchy, casting the national programme upon the scrap-heap, boldly placed, after Mentana, its veto upon every aspiration of Italians toward the North. Was it bound to Austria by secret pacts even before it thought to bind itself by open alliances? I cannot say... but I know that from that moment the motto "Italy belongs to Italians" brings it out in fits of hysterical trembling. It dismisses the old generals who dare still to pronounce it; it prepares the manacles for the patriots who cannot bring themselves to regard it as a mere unspoken assumption. It will then attempt, repudiating the principles that were the foundation of our Risorgimento, to refurbish its military glory by conquering, in distant Africa, the land of "the four brigands." But even though it has at its disposal soldiers who are the very incarnation of dash and sacrifice, it will succeed in nothing other than renewing the shame of its own defeat.

And yet, personally, the Savoia possess in the highest degree that military courage which descended to them through a long line of ancestors; and at San Martino, where there was "glory for all," even the great King reaped a rich harvest of it.

Vittorio Emanuele II knew also how to conceal his own weaknesses, assuming before the astonished eyes of Europe attitudes worthy of bronze statues. Yet, despite his essentially aristocratic nature, his exuberant vitality drove him toward pleasures verging upon the vulgar — pleasures which provoked a smile of indulgent complaisance both among the refined of high society, idolatrous and lenient toward him, and among the multitude which, accustomed to picturing Kings as they are depicted in tragedies, admired these excesses as almost the revelation of a sincerely democratic spirit. The times granted him the spectacular rôle of the conqueror, and his robust individuality seemed fashioned by art to play it. He assumed the mission of guiding the Institutions inherited from the past safely through the present storm. A fateful mission... he knew how to accomplish it; but at the cost of sacrificing the future. The common people admire him and will admire him so long as they are schooled in the lesson of prostrating themselves before success. The thinkers who analyse have already reproached him for the subtle, almost parvenu-banker's policy which cast upon the treasury of our enthusiasms the seeds of dissolution.

My sympathies go by preference to his predecessor. Carlo Alberto is ennobled in my eyes by the merit that belongs to forerunners, and by his serene dignity in adversity. He did not gather the fruit of his own good works; the bitter fruit of the works of others he gathered instead. Epic and tumultuous, despite their errors, were his battles upon the field; but these become as nothing beside those fought silently within his own conscience. He betrayed... perhaps; but he was far more betrayed. His weaknesses, his hesitations, his defections, his own intermittent cruelty, reveal the deep suffering of a soul sick and exquisitely sensitive; so that the instinctive aversion for the head of a system of privilege and reaction yields before the rising tide of pity for the man. Yields?... No — it changes its object and flows all the more intensely upon the nefarious faction which, sheltered behind things held sacred, stoops to corrupt natures as elect as his.

"The Hamlet of Italy"... aptly defined! His is a figure that succeeds not in triumphal processions but in passionate tragedy, all shadow and sudden light. Is there, among my countrymen, one who feels himself equal to the attempt?

*(Long silence.)*

It has been written that I have no vision of contemporary Italy — free, united, risen again politically and civilly; advancing with a sure step toward the glorious goal that awaits her... loved and envied anew by all the world.

It has been written that I retain, instead, the vision of the Italy of my own times, enslaved, wretched and sorrowful; that I am still moved and still suffer at the recollection of the evils that afflicted her.

Whoever wrote this shows himself more poet than observer, or, at the very least, grasps only the smallest part of the truth.

I have the vision of an Italy such as was glimpsed by Giuseppe Mazzini; such as we all dreamed, more or less, when between her and us there lay so great a Calvary of suffering. Contemporary Italy resembles that vision all too little... that is why I grieve. Outbursts of virile energy are not wanting in those classes that have least cause to congratulate themselves on the independence that has been gained. But their gaze reaches to a height which the times do not permit us, and passes beyond, almost sketching them in outline, the intermediate demands that would yet be within our reach. It is idle to prattle of universal brotherhood when the dismal plant of servitude still takes root, by force of foreign tyranny, in one's own domestic garden.

And I know that, with the sole exception of the government of Francesco Crispi — marked by an energy not always well applied, but virile — we have had, from a long succession of Ministers, nothing but the manifestations of a neurotic ferocity that betrays fear, or of a myopic and senile cunning that commands no respect whatsoever and arouses the envy of no one.

And I know that insolent Austria still stretches its iron hand this side of the Alps. It could descend, in a single day, to the very heart of the Po valley, the most fertile region of Italy. And while its Emperor, so as not to retreat, even in appearance, from the position it pleased him to assume *ab initio*, refuses to return to Rome the visit paid to him by our Sovereign, our Ministers cheerfully sign the alliances that guarantee him peaceful enjoyment of the usurped territories; that repudiate the integrity and the greatness of the Fatherland. What a contrast!... and how feeble is the fibre of those who govern the destinies of the young Nation compared to that of the old reactionary Monarch and executioner!

I ask myself: Who is to realise the aspirations consecrated by the blood of so many martyrs, if one courts the enemy who has us by the throat? It has come to this: a special word has been invented — *quarantottate!* — to signify, in mockery of it, the desperate heroism of the fathers!

One thought addressed to him who reigns at present, and I have done. For some years now our Monarchy has shown signs of undergoing a partial transformation. The acts of royal humanism aim less at theatrical and ephemeral display than at leaving some trace that will endure. Respect for the individual right is more deeply felt, and political liberty is extended. Perhaps the young King was instructed by the tragic end of his father, violently struck down for his frequent returns to antiquated and tyrannical systems which ought to have been the dismal exclusive property of times long past. The May of 1898, for instance, in its means and in its effects — if not precisely in its aim — recalls the Second of December all too closely for it not to insinuate, in more than one heart, the painful suspicion that it was its sad and ill-contrived imitation.

Yet even while the Monarchy makes concessions to liberalism and professes to wish to advance with it, it retreats, underhandedly and without appearing to do so — as though bent on maintaining its equilibrium by blunting, through the augmented power of the Church, the ever-growing demands of the labouring multitudes.

A Government that seats itself between the parties, moderator of all, superior to all...! This may appear wise to the *praticismo* that cannot discern the future, or that, discerning it, is wrong to dread it. I see in it, rather, the tactic recommended by the little manuals of political philosophy. A "living" Nation is necessarily in perpetual struggle, and the Rulers of a State, monarchical or republican, may temporarily obstruct or divert it, but suppress it — never. In the social conflict that looms, equilibrium is a chimera, and the progress which now completes, ever more swiftly, its fateful course, is the inexorable condemnation alike of economic plunder and of dogma. The labouring multitude will triumph, and will annihilate in the fever of triumph not only the hostile elements, but also the Institutions that will not align themselves openly and sincerely with it.

Let no one think that I am animated by rancour. Distance in time and in space has smoothed away the sharp edges, has smothered many passions. Only love survives, intense. It flashes forth in truth. Nor shall I go to my grave with the remorse of having kept truth silent.

As a republican, I could wish that the Monarchy would commit a thousand errors; for once the measure is full, it would tumble headlong into the pit dug by its own improvidence. But as an Italian educated by my elders to set the integrity of the Fatherland above my political ideals, I do not hesitate to rekindle the last flame still in me in order to give this warning:

"Whoever leans upon the past sets his foot upon ground which the waves are daily undermining and swallowing. Trust in the future and advance boldly to meet it. It is in that way alone that you will succeed in mastering it. The past evolved from barbarism and is saturated with arrogance and crimes; but History tends toward the triumph of Democracy, of equality before rights, duties and opportunities; toward the triumph of justice. Whoever does not follow the tendencies of History cannot enjoy a reign long, blessed, and glorious.

"But in foreign policy the traces of the violence suffered by the Italian people are too manifest and drip with too much blood for your mission not to stand out clear and precise. The Monarchy exists because every patriotic party has furled its own banner before hers — not as the vanquished furl theirs, but by entering into a solemn compact that guaranteed, as the recompense for sacrifice, an Italy independent from the Alps to the uttermost southern shores. The Monarchy, on one pretext or another, has evaded the full observance of that compact. Meanwhile, Austria, notwithstanding the notorious alliance, has not slumbered. She renews and multiplies her arms; she sharpens her claws and points openly at the flesh into which she yearns to sink them. It is urgent to act humanely within the borders of the Kingdom, in order to win over the multitude by that ascendancy which springs from respect — the respect that is rooted in gratitude and in love; it is urgent to rekindle the slumbering enthusiasm and the virile spirit that will not suffer insults — not, however, with empty commemorative pageants, nor with the puerile rhetoric of banqueting speakers... it is urgent, I say, that the Nation be ready to breathe its own soul into the army. Cold and arid discipline is no longer sufficient to hold together armed formations that, at the first disaster, fall to pieces if they are not renewed and sustained by a nobler sentiment. Upon Vittorio Emanuele III falls the most exalted task of completing and correcting Vittorio Emanuele II. Ill does one bind one's head with a crown come down by the ephemeral right of succession, if, arrogating to oneself its privileges and its honours, one sets at naught its obligations. All further delay is now excessive... to prevaricate any longer would mean to usurp, by virtue of an equivocation, the throne of Italy!"

Count di Rudio closed his eyes before our victories in Libya could blind them with their dazzle. What judgement he would have pronounced upon them, the present writer is not in a position to say with certainty. But if one reflects that he disdained material advantages whenever they were not coupled with a moral and regenerative triumph, one would be inclined to wager that those victories would have wrung from him one more of those cries of his that reveal a heart that bleeds — given the manner in which those deeds were officially presented to us as worthy of the ancient Romans... and the peace treaty that recalls the shameful "cession" of Venetia!

But in setting forth the reasons why they had failed to achieve the better, the Advisers of the Crown showed themselves truly without equal.


### Chapter Thirty-Three

<!-- pages:263-268 -->



THE sufferings of the prison-fortress had long been effaced by my lengthy immersion in the free light of day and in the warmth of family affection, which had almost reconciled me to idleness, when the announcement of the crime of Aspromonte came to shake me from it.

The conferences and public meetings organised in London to protest against the Temporal Power of the Popes had become a daily occurrence, and I threw myself into them, so to speak, headlong.

There was one incident which I recall with satisfaction. The Irish element, which obscures its fine qualities by subordinating them all too readily to the watchword of the tonsured camarilla, took offence at this newest agitation. One day, when an imposing international meeting was being held in the Park, the police cordon that was protecting us was broken, and the Irish set upon us furiously. The demonstrators were as though swept away, and the banners brought down. Two alone remained upright and flying: that of the French republican delegation, which stood at my side, and the one I myself held — upon it blazed, in scarlet letters, the motto: *"O Roma o Morte."*

The modest triumph was owed to the short blade concealed from the eyes of the French Police. On that occasion too it remained unstained by blood. The menace of its gleam had been enough!

Resolved to reconsecrate my energies to greater things, I set about finding a way to return to my homeland.
